Q1. Critically review Gomez classification of PEM with relation to Indian 
condition.   [2011] 
 
Introduction 
Protein Energy Malnutrition (PEM) is defined as a range of pathological conditions arising from coincident lack of 
varying proportions of protein and calories, occurring most frequently in infants and young children. Classification 
helps in assessing severity, prognosis, and management. 
 
Gomez Classification (1956) 
Proposed by Dr. Federico Gomez (Mexico). Based on weight-for-age as percentage of expected weight 
(Harvard/NCHS standards). 
Grade 
% of Expected Weight (Wt/Age) 
Clinical Interpretation 
Normal 
>90% 
Well-nourished 
Grade I (Mild) 
75–90% 
Mild underweight 
Grade II (Moderate) 
60–74% 
Moderate PEM 
Grade III (Severe) 
<60% 
Severe PEM / Marasmus 
 
Critical Appraisal 
Advantages: Simple, uses weight and age only; low cost; applicable in field conditions. 
• Weight is easily measurable even in low-resource settings. 
• Gives a quick snapshot of nutritional status at community level. 
• Has epidemiological utility for prevalence estimation. 
Limitations / Criticisms:  
• Does not distinguish between acute and chronic malnutrition. 
• Cannot differentiate between stunting (chronic) and wasting (acute). 
• Edematous malnutrition (Kwashiorkor) — child may appear normal or even overweight due to edema, 
masking true protein deficiency. 
• Depends on accurate knowledge of age — problematic in rural India where birth registration is incomplete. 
• Reference standards (Harvard/NCHS) may not be appropriate for Indian children; Indian Academy of 
Pediatrics (IAP) standards are preferred. 
• No assessment of dietary quality (protein vs calorie deficiency). 
1


--- PAGE 14 ---

swaDesh
 
Relevance to Indian Conditions 
India has a high burden of undernutrition. As per NFHS-5 (2019-21): 35.5% children under 5 are underweight. In 
India, significant proportion are stunted (chronic malnutrition) — Gomez does not capture this. Edema-based 
PEM (Kwashiorkor) may be missed entirely by Gomez as weight appears normal. Indian children have lower birth 
weights and smaller frames, making Harvard standards inappropriate. 
 
Better Alternatives 
Classification 
Parameters Used 
Advantage Over Gomez 
Waterlow 
Ht/Age + Wt/Ht 
Distinguishes stunting from wasting 
Wellcome Trust 
Wt/Age + Edema 
Detects Kwashiorkor 
WHO Z-score 
Wt/Age, Ht/Age, Wt/Ht (Z-scores) 
Most comprehensive; recommended 
for India 
IAP Classification 
Wt/Age (Indian standards) 
Adapted for Indian children 
 
Conclusion 
While Gomez classification has historical significance and simplicity, it has major limitations in Indian context — 
inability to detect Kwashiorkor, distinguish stunting from wasting, and dependence on accurate age data. WHO Z-
score based classification using IAP standards is the currently recommended approach for India. 
 
Q2. Discuss causes of malnutrition in India and strategies to control it.   [2012] 
 
Introduction 
Malnutrition in India is a multidimensional public health problem encompassing undernutrition (stunting, wasting, 
underweight), micronutrient deficiencies, and increasingly, overnutrition. India bears the highest burden of child 
malnutrition globally. 
 
Current Burden (NFHS-5, 2019-21) 
Indicator 
Prevalence (%) 
Stunting (Ht/Age <-2 SD) 
35.5% 
Wasting (Wt/Ht <-2 SD) 
19.3% 
Underweight (Wt/Age <-2 SD) 
32.1% 
Anaemia in children 6-59 months 
67.1% 
Overweight/Obesity (children) 
3.4% 
 
UNICEF Conceptual Framework of Malnutrition Causes 
Immediate Causes:  
• Inadequate dietary intake (both quantity and quality) 
• Disease burden — repeated infections (diarrhoea, ARI, malaria) leading to nutrient losses 
Underlying Causes:  
• Household food insecurity — poverty, food price inflation, subsistence farming 
2


--- PAGE 15 ---

swaDesh
• Inadequate care and feeding practices — poor breastfeeding, delayed complementary feeding 
• Unhealthy environment — poor sanitation, unsafe water, open defecation 
• Limited access to health services and antenatal care 
Basic/Root Causes:  
• Poverty and economic inequality 
• Gender inequity — women's low status, adolescent marriages, maternal malnutrition 
• Illiteracy and low education of mothers 
• Social factors — caste-based food taboos, cultural practices 
• Political and governance failures 
 
Strategies to Control Malnutrition 
1. Nutrition-Specific Interventions (Direct):  
1. ICDS (Integrated Child Development Services) — supplementary nutrition, immunization, health education 
for children 0-6 years and pregnant/lactating women 
2. National Nutrition Mission (POSHAN Abhiyaan, 2018) — reduce stunting by 2% p.a., wasting by 2% p.a., 
anaemia in women/children by 3% p.a. 
3. Mid-Day Meal Scheme (PM POSHAN) — school nutrition for 6-14 years 
4. National Iron + Initiative (NI+) — weekly iron-folic acid supplementation (WIFS) for adolescents 
5. Vitamin A supplementation programme — 9 months to 5 years 
6. National Iodine Deficiency Disorders Control Programme (NIDDCP) 
7. MAM/SAM Management — CMAM (Community-based Management of Acute Malnutrition), NRC 
(Nutritional Rehabilitation Centres) 
2. Nutrition-Sensitive Interventions (Indirect):  
8. Swachh Bharat Mission — sanitation and ODF (Open Defecation Free) villages 
9. Jal Jeevan Mission — safe drinking water 
10. PDS (Public Distribution System) — food security 
11. PM Matru Vandana Yojana — maternity benefit programme (Rs 5000 for first child) 
12. Beti Bachao Beti Padhao — girl child education and empowerment 
 
Conclusion 
Controlling malnutrition in India requires a convergent multi-sectoral approach combining nutrition-specific 
interventions (ICDS, POSHAN Abhiyaan) with nutrition-sensitive actions addressing food security, sanitation, 
women empowerment, and poverty alleviation. Political will and community participation are essential. 
 
Q3. Enumerate methods of assessing nutritional status of a community using 
anthropometric measures.   [2023] 
 
Introduction 
Nutritional assessment of a community involves measuring the nutritional status of individuals in a defined 
population. Anthropometric measurements are the most widely used, non-invasive, and cost-effective methods. 
The term 'anthropometry' refers to the science of measuring the human body. 
 
Anthropometric Indices Used 
3


--- PAGE 16 ---

swaDesh
Measurement 
Index Derived 
Reflects 
Weight + Age 
Weight-for-Age (W/A) 
Overall nutrition (underweight) 
Height + Age 
Height-for-Age (H/A) 
Chronic malnutrition (stunting) 
Weight + Height 
Weight-for-Height (W/H) 
Acute malnutrition (wasting) 
MUAC 
Mid-Upper Arm Circumference 
Acute malnutrition screening 
Head Circumference 
HC/Chest circumference ratio 
Brain growth; < 1 year 
Skin-fold thickness 
% Body fat 
Adiposity, obesity assessment 
BMI 
Wt(kg)/Ht(m²) 
Overweight and obesity in adults 
Waist circumference 
Waist-hip ratio 
Abdominal obesity; CVD risk 
 
Key Methods in Community Assessment 
1. Weight-for-Age (W/A):  
• Indicates underweight; does not differentiate acute vs chronic malnutrition 
• Reference: WHO growth standards (2006) or IAP standards for India 
• Expressed as percentage of median or Z-score 
2. Height-for-Age (H/A) — Stunting:  
• Reflects cumulative nutritional deprivation over time (chronic malnutrition) 
• <-2 SD = Stunted; <-3 SD = Severely Stunted 
• Sensitive to long-term socioeconomic conditions 
3. Weight-for-Height (W/H) — Wasting:  
• Reflects current acute nutritional status 
• <-2 SD = Wasted (acute malnutrition); <-3 SD = Severe Acute Malnutrition (SAM) 
• Used for therapeutic feeding programme entry criteria 
4. MUAC (Mid-Upper Arm Circumference):  
• Most useful field tool for screening acute malnutrition 
• Measured at midpoint between acromion and olecranon 
• < 11.5 cm = SAM; 11.5–12.5 cm = MAM (Moderate Acute Malnutrition); >12.5 cm = Normal (in children 6-
59 months) 
• Advantage: No weight/height required; independent of age after 6 months 
5. BMI (Body Mass Index):  
• Used for adults: <18.5 = Underweight; 18.5–24.9 = Normal; ≥25 = Overweight; ≥30 = Obese 
• For children: BMI-for-age (percentile or Z-score) 
6. Skin-fold Thickness (Triceps/Subscapular):  
• Uses Harpenden calipers; measures subcutaneous fat 
• Used to estimate % body fat; useful in obesity research 
7. Head Circumference and Chest Circumference:  
• HC > CC at birth; become equal at 6 months; CC > HC after 1 year 
• HC < CC after 1 year suggests malnutrition 
 
Expression of Anthropometric Data 
• Percentage of median (Gomez/Waterlow) — simple; commonly used in India 
• Z-scores (SD scores) — recommended by WHO; allows comparison across populations 
• Percentiles — used in clinical settings 
 
4


--- PAGE 17 ---

swaDesh
Community Nutritional Assessment Methods 
13. Growth monitoring (regular weighing of children 0-5 years in ICDS) 
14. Nutrition surveys — cross-sectional surveys using random sampling (e.g., NFHS, CNNS) 
15. MUAC screening camps in villages 
16. School health surveys — height and weight of school children 
 
Conclusion 
Anthropometric assessment is the cornerstone of community nutritional surveillance. MUAC is ideal for rapid field 
screening; Z-scores of W/A, H/A, and W/H are standard WHO indicators. Combined use of multiple indices gives 
comprehensive information on both acute and chronic malnutrition. 
 
Q4. Nutritional challenges in India. How can a multi-sectoral approach help?   
[2022, 2014] 
 
Introduction 
India faces a 'double burden of malnutrition' — coexistence of undernutrition (stunting, wasting, micronutrient 
deficiencies) alongside overnutrition (obesity, diet-related NCDs). Both are driven by interrelated determinants 
that span multiple sectors beyond health. 
 
Major Nutritional Challenges in India

Hidden Hunger 
Iodine Deficiency Disorders 
Endemic in certain regions 
 
Key Nutritional Challenges 
• High maternal malnutrition — low pre-pregnancy BMI, anaemia in pregnancy affecting birth outcomes 
• Poor infant and young child feeding (IYCF) practices — suboptimal breastfeeding, delayed/inappropriate 
complementary feeding 
• Food insecurity and poverty — 22% of India still below poverty line 
• Dietary diversity deficit — dependence on cereals; poor protein, fat, micronutrient intake 
• Sanitation and infection link — poor WASH leading to malabsorption, gut enteropathy 
• Adolescent nutrition gap — especially in girls; intergenerational cycle of malnutrition 
• Rising obesity — ultra-processed food consumption, urbanization, sedentary lifestyle 
 
Multi-Sectoral Approach: Rationale 
5

swaDesh
Malnutrition is caused by factors across agriculture, water, sanitation, education, economy, health, and social 
protection. No single sector can address all determinants. WHO recommends a 'nutrition-sensitive' multi-sectoral 
strategy to address root causes.

POSHAN Abhiyaan as Multi-Sectoral Model 
Launched in 2018, POSHAN Abhiyaan (National Nutrition Mission) is India's flagship multi-sectoral nutrition 
programme. It uses technology (ICDS-CAS application), community mobilization (Poshan Maah — nutrition 
month), and convergence of 42+ government schemes across 8 ministries to address the first 1000 days of life. 
 
Conclusion 
Nutritional challenges in India are multifactorial and require convergent action. A multi-sectoral approach 
addressing food systems, water, sanitation, women's empowerment, and health services simultaneously is 
essential. POSHAN Abhiyaan represents this vision; effective implementation at district level with strong 
monitoring is the key to success. 
 
Q5. Describe Protein Energy Malnutrition (PEM) in detail.   [2015] 
 
Definition 
PEM is a family of related disorders that includes marasmus, kwashiorkor, and intermediate states of marasmic-
kwashiorkor. It results from deficiency of both protein and energy, primarily affecting children under 5 years in 
developing countries. 
 
Classification of PEM 
Type 
Main Deficiency 
Key Feature 
Body Weight 
Marasmus 
Energy + Protein 
Wasting, 'skin and bone' 
Severely low (<60% 
expected) 
Kwashiorkor 
Predominantly Protein 
Oedema, fatty liver 
Low-to-normal (masked by 
oedema) 
6


--- PAGE 19 ---

swaDesh
Type 
Main Deficiency 
Key Feature 
Body Weight 
Marasmic-Kwashiorkor 
Both 
Both wasting + oedema 
Very low 
 
Etiology / Causes 
• Dietary: Inadequate intake of protein and energy; early weaning; diluted formula feeding 
• Infection: Diarrhoea, measles, ARI — increase catabolism, reduce absorption 
• Maternal: Low birth weight, poor breastfeeding 
• Socioeconomic: Poverty, food insecurity, ignorance 
• Environmental: Poor sanitation, contaminated water 
 
Clinical Features of Marasmus 
• Severe wasting — wizened 'old man' appearance 
• MUAC <11.5 cm, W/H <-3 SD (Z-score) 
• Baggy pants appearance — loose skin over buttocks 
• Irritable, ravenous appetite 
• No oedema, no fatty liver 
• Hair changes mild; skin dry and wrinkled 
 
Clinical Features of Kwashiorkor 
• Pitting oedema — starts in feet, ascends 
• 'Flaky paint' dermatosis, depigmented skin 
• Flag sign — alternating bands of pale and dark hair 
• Hepatomegaly (fatty liver) 
• Moon face, misery, anorexia 
• Decreased serum albumin (<2.5 g/dL) 
• W/H may be deceptively normal due to oedema 
 
Biochemical Changes 
Parameter 
Marasmus 
Kwashiorkor 
Serum Albumin 
Normal/slightly low 
Severely low (<2.5 g/dL) 
Blood Glucose 
Low (hypoglycaemia) 
Low 
Serum Transferrin 
Normal 
Reduced 
Insulin 
Very low 
Low-normal 
Cortisol 
Elevated 
Elevated 
 
Assessment 
• W/H <-3 SD (WHO criteria) = SAM 
• MUAC <11.5 cm = SAM 
• Bilateral pitting oedema = SAM (even if weight appears normal) 
• Use F-75 formula (therapeutic milk) initially, then F-100 
 
Management of SAM — 10-Step WHO Protocol 
7


--- PAGE 20 ---

swaDesh
17. Treat hypoglycaemia (10% glucose/sucrose) 
18. Treat hypothermia 
19. Treat dehydration (ReSoMal — special rehydration solution for malnourished) 
20. Correct electrolytes (potassium, magnesium — NOT sodium) 
21. Treat infections (amoxicillin/cotrimoxazole) 
22. Correct micronutrient deficiencies (Vitamin A, zinc, folate, multivitamins) 
23. Initiate cautious feeding (F-75) 
24. Achieve catch-up growth (F-100, RUTF — Ready-to-Use Therapeutic Food) 
25. Provide sensory stimulation and emotional support 
26. Prepare for follow-up after discharge 
 
Community-Based Management (CMAM) 
• MAM: Supplementary feeding programmes (SFP) — Corn Soy Blend 
• SAM without complications: RUTF (Plumpy'nut — peanut-based paste, 500 kcal/92g) — OTP (Outpatient 
Therapeutic Programme) 
• SAM with complications: Inpatient stabilization in NRC (Nutritional Rehabilitation Centre) 
 
Prevention 
• Promote exclusive breastfeeding for 6 months 
• Appropriate complementary feeding from 6 months 
• ICDS supplementary nutrition 
• Growth monitoring and promotion (GMP) 
• Vitamin A supplementation, immunization 
• Infection prevention: ORS, handwashing, safe water 
 
Q6. Describe the nutritional disorders of public health importance in children 
and health programmes to tackle them.   [Dec 2024 — NEW] 
 
Introduction 
Nutritional disorders of public health importance in children include both macronutrient deficiencies (PEM) and 
micronutrient deficiencies ('hidden hunger'). These are responsible for significant morbidity, mortality, and 
impaired development.

Vitamin A Deficiency (VAD) — Detail 
• VAD is the leading preventable cause of childhood blindness globally 
• Night blindness → Bitot's spots → corneal xerosis → keratomalacia → blindness 
• Also causes increased susceptibility to infections, contributes to childhood mortality 
Programme: National Vitamin A Supplementation Programme — Biannual doses: 100,000 IU (6-11 months), 
200,000 IU (12-60 months), every 6 months. 
 
Iron Deficiency Anaemia (IDA) — Detail 
• Most common micronutrient deficiency worldwide; 67.1% children 6-59 months anaemic (NFHS-5) 
• Causes: poor dietary iron, low bioavailability (phytates in cereals), hookworm, repeated infections 
• Consequences: impaired cognitive development, reduced school performance, increased infection risk 
Programme: National Iron + Initiative (NI+) — WIFS (Weekly Iron Folic Acid Supplementation) for 6-59 month 
children, school-age children (6-10 yrs), and adolescents. 
 
Iodine Deficiency Disorders (IDD) — Detail 
• Spectrum: goitre, cretinism, intellectual disability, stillbirth, spontaneous abortion 
• Fetal iodine deficiency is most critical — causes irreversible brain damage 
Programme: National Iodine Deficiency Disorders Control Programme (NIDDCP) — Universal salt iodization 
(iodized salt with 15 ppm iodine at consumption level). Regular monitoring by NIN (National Institute of Nutrition). 
 
National Programmes for Nutritional Disorders in Children

services 
 
Conclusion 
Nutritional disorders in children are preventable and treatable through targeted supplementation, dietary 
diversification, food fortification, and social protection programmes. India's multi-pronged approach through 
POSHAN Abhiyaan integrates all these dimensions, with a focus on the first 1000 days as the 'window of 
opportunity'.

Q7. Discuss the role of nutritional factors in health and disease of a rural 
community.   [2011] 
 
Introduction 
Nutrition is a fundamental determinant of health. In rural India, nutritional factors are closely intertwined with 
agricultural output, poverty, sanitation, and cultural practices. Poor nutrition leads to a vicious cycle of disease, 
reduced productivity, and poverty. 
 
Role of Nutrition in Health (Rural Context) 
1. Growth and Development:  
• Adequate protein and energy intake essential for physical growth of children 
• Micronutrients (iodine, iron, zinc) critical for brain development and school performance 
• Stunting in rural India: 35.5% children under 5 (NFHS-5) 
2. Immunity and Infection Resistance:  
• Vitamin A deficiency increases susceptibility to measles, respiratory infections 
• Zinc deficiency causes immune dysfunction, increases diarrhoea severity 
• PEM markedly reduces thymic function, lymphocyte counts, antibody production 
• 'Infection-malnutrition cycle' is common in rural areas 
3. Maternal and Perinatal Health:  
• Maternal anaemia (haemoglobin <11 g/dL in pregnancy) — associated with preterm birth, LBW 
• Iodine deficiency during pregnancy — fetal brain damage, cretinism 
• Calcium deficiency — osteoporosis in mothers, rickets in neonates 
4. Chronic Disease Risk:  
• 'Barker Hypothesis' / Developmental Origins of Health and Disease (DOHaD): Intrauterine malnutrition 
programs the fetus for metabolic syndrome, diabetes, and cardiovascular disease in adult life 
• Rising rural obesity from refined grain consumption, sugar-sweetened beverages 
 
Nutritional Factors Causing Disease in Rural India 
Nutritional Factor 
Associated Disease 
Rural Prevalence 
Energy-protein deficit 
Marasmus, Kwashiorkor, Stunting 
Very high 
Iron deficiency 
Anaemia 
High — especially women, children 
Vitamin A deficiency 
Xerophthalmia, blindness 
Moderate 
Iodine deficiency 
Goitre, cretinism 
Endemic in some regions

Osteoporosis, rickets 
Common

Pradesh, Rajasthan 
 
Special Considerations for Rural India 
• Seasonal food insecurity — lean seasons (post-harvest gap) worsen malnutrition 
10


--- PAGE 23 ---

swaDesh
• Agricultural dependence — mono-crop cultures reduce dietary diversity 
• Food taboos — avoidance of protein-rich foods (eggs, meat) for children and pregnant women in many 
communities 
• Water contamination — parasitic infections increase nutrient losses 
• Poor access to health services — undetected and untreated nutritional deficiencies 
 
Interventions 
• ICDS supplementary nutrition and growth monitoring at AWC 
• Kitchen gardens (Poshan Vatika) to increase dietary diversity 
• Biofortification — high-iron pearl millet, high-vitamin A orange-fleshed sweet potato 
• IEC on nutrition and infant feeding practices 
• PDS — supply of fortified rice, wheat, pulses 
 
Q8. Criteria for severe acute malnutrition (SAM)? Add a note on its 
management.   [2017] 
 
Definition of SAM 
Severe Acute Malnutrition (SAM) is the most extreme and visible form of malnutrition. It is defined by the 
WHO/UNICEF as a condition in which a child is severely undernourished, identifiable by objective anthropometric 
criteria. 
 
Diagnostic Criteria for SAM 
Criterion 
Cut-off 
Tool Required 
Weight-for-Height (W/H) 
<-3 SD (Z-score) or <70% of median 
Weight scale + height board 
MUAC 
<11.5 cm (children 6-59 months) 
MUAC tape 
Bilateral Pitting Oedema 
Present (any grade) 
Clinical examination 
Note: Any ONE criterion is sufficient to diagnose SAM. Oedema alone qualifies even if W/H or MUAC appears 
normal. 
 
Grades of Oedema 
• Grade +: Feet/ankles only 
• Grade ++: Feet + lower legs + hands + lower arms 
• Grade +++: Generalized (includes face, eyelids) — 'Moon face' in Kwashiorkor 
 
Differentiation: SAM with vs. without Complications

SAM without Complications 
SAM with Complications 
Appetite 
Good (passes appetite test) 
Poor/absent 
Medical complications

Present (infection, dehydration, 
hypoglycaemia) 
Oedema 
Absent or Grade +

swaDesh
Feature 
SAM without Complications 
SAM with Complications 
Management 
OTP (Outpatient) 
NRC (Inpatient) 
 
Appetite Test (RUTF Test) 
• Offer RUTF (Ready-to-Use Therapeutic Food) under supervision 
• Pass: Child eats at least 3 teaspoons of RUTF in 30 minutes 
• Fail: Child refuses — indicates need for inpatient care 
 
Management of SAM — WHO 10-Step Protocol 
Phase 1: Stabilization (Days 1-7)  
27. Treat/prevent hypoglycaemia — 10% glucose (5 ml/kg) orally or NGT if unconscious 
28. Treat/prevent hypothermia — warm environment, kangaroo method, warm blankets 
29. Treat/prevent dehydration — ReSoMal (reduced osmolarity ORS with added potassium and magnesium) 
30. Correct electrolyte imbalance — give extra potassium (3-4 mmol/kg/day) and magnesium (0.4-0.6 
mmol/kg/day). AVOID sodium supplements 
31. Treat and prevent infections — broad-spectrum antibiotics: Amoxicillin 40 mg/kg/day x 5 days + 
Metronidazole for giardiasis if suspected 
32. Correct micronutrient deficiencies — Vitamin A (200,000 IU on Day 1 except if given recently), folic acid, 
zinc, copper, multivitamins. Note: Iron NOT given during stabilization — risk of free radical injury 
33. Begin cautious feeding — Start with F-75 formula (75 kcal/100 ml, 0.9 g protein/100 ml); small frequent 
feeds 
Phase 2: Rehabilitation (Weeks 2-6)  
34. Achieve catch-up growth — Transition to F-100 (100 kcal/100 ml, 2.9 g protein/100 ml); increases 
appetite and weight gain. Target: 10-20 g/kg/day weight gain. Iron supplementation started here 
35. Sensory stimulation and emotional support — play therapy, structured activity, maternal involvement 
36. Prepare for discharge — train mother, educate family on feeding practices, follow-up schedule 
 
RUTF (Ready-to-Use Therapeutic Food) 
• Composition: Peanut paste, milk powder, vegetable oil, sugar, vitamins and minerals 
• Energy: 500 kcal per 92g sachet 
• India: Balbhog (groundnut-based), Plumpy'Nut (imported) 
• Used in OTP — given 2-3 sachets/kg/week based on weight 
 
Discharge Criteria from NRC 
• W/H >-2 SD on two consecutive measurements 
• No oedema for 2 weeks 
• Good appetite, well, and alert 
• Weight gain >5 g/kg/day for 3 consecutive days 
 
Q9. Describe aetiology, screening, management and prevention of iron 
deficiency anaemia.   [2018] 
 
Definition 
12


--- PAGE 25 ---

swaDesh
Anaemia is defined as haemoglobin below the defined threshold for a given age, sex, and physiological status. 
Iron deficiency anaemia (IDA) results when iron stores are depleted, impairing haemoglobin synthesis. 
Group 
Anaemia Threshold (Hb g/dL) 
Children 6-59 months 
<11.0 
Children 5-11 years 
<11.5 
Children 12-14 years 
<12.0 
Non-pregnant women >15 yrs 
<12.0 
Pregnant women 
<11.0 
Men >15 years 
<13.0 
 
Burden 
IDA is the most common nutritional deficiency globally. In India (NFHS-5): Anaemia prevalence — 67.1% in 
children 6-59 months, 57.0% in women 15-49 years, 25.0% in men 15-49 years. India contributes 40% of global 
anaemia burden. 
 
Aetiology 
A. Dietary causes:  
• Inadequate iron intake — predominantly cereal-based diet (low in haem iron) 
• Poor bioavailability — phytates (cereals), polyphenols (tea, coffee) inhibit non-haem iron absorption 
• Ascorbic acid deficiency — vitamin C enhances iron absorption; low fruit intake 
B. Increased demand:  
• Rapid growth in infants and adolescents 
• Pregnancy (iron required for fetal development + expanded maternal blood volume) 
C. Increased losses:  
• Menstrual blood loss (heavy menstruation) 
• Hookworm (Ancylostoma duodenale) — leading cause of IDA in adult men in rural India 
• GI bleeding (peptic ulcer, haemorrhoids, malignancy) 
D. Impaired absorption:  
• Malabsorption syndromes (coeliac disease, IBD) 
• Post-gastrectomy 
 
Pathogenesis — Stages of Iron Deficiency 
37. Iron depletion — serum ferritin falls; no anaemia 
38. Iron-deficient erythropoiesis — serum iron falls, TIBC rises, transferrin saturation falls 
39. Iron deficiency anaemia — Hb falls below threshold; microcytic hypochromic picture 
 
Screening for IDA 
Community Screening:  
• Clinical pallor assessment — conjunctiva, palms, tongue, nail beds 
• HemoCue — field-deployable haemoglobin meter; recommended for ASHA/ANMs 
• Digital haemoglobinometer — non-invasive 
Laboratory Screening:  
• Hb estimation (Sahli's, Cyanomethaemoglobin method — gold standard) 
13


--- PAGE 26 ---

swaDesh
• Peripheral blood smear — microcytic hypochromic RBCs, anisocytosis, poikilocytosis 
• MCV <80 fL, MCH <27 pg, serum ferritin <12 ng/mL confirms IDA 
 
Management 
Mild-Moderate IDA (Hb 8-11 g/dL):  
• Oral iron — Ferrous sulphate 60 mg elemental iron + 0.5 mg folic acid daily x 100-180 days 
• Children 6 mos-5 yrs: 1-2 mg/kg/day elemental iron 
• Continue for 3 months after Hb normalizes to replenish stores 
Severe IDA (Hb <7 g/dL):  
• IV iron sucrose / ferric carboxymaltose — when oral not tolerated 
• Blood transfusion — only if symptomatic (cardiac failure, severe dyspnoea) or Hb <5 g/dL 
 
Prevention — National Iron Plus Initiative (NI+)

1 tablet daily x 180 days 
 
Dietary Advice 
• Consume iron-rich foods: green leafy vegetables, jaggery, meat, eggs, legumes 
• Enhance absorption: eat with vitamin C-rich foods (amla, lemon, guava) 
• Avoid inhibitors: don't take tea/coffee with meals 
• Food fortification: fortified rice, wheat flour under PDS 
 
Q10. Enumerate Iodine Deficiency Disorders (IDD) and preventive strategies by 
GOI.   [2021] 
 
Introduction 
Iodine Deficiency Disorders (IDD) refer to a spectrum of conditions caused by insufficient iodine intake. Iodine is 
essential for thyroid hormone synthesis (T3, T4), which regulates growth, brain development, and metabolism. 
The consequences are particularly severe during pregnancy and early childhood. 
 
Daily Iodine Requirements 
Group 
Recommended Daily Intake (mcg/day) 
Infants 0-12 months 
90 
Children 1-8 years 
90 
Children 9-13 years 
120 
Adults 
150 
14


--- PAGE 27 ---

swaDesh
Group 
Recommended Daily Intake (mcg/day) 
Pregnant women 
220 
Lactating women 
290 
 
Spectrum of Iodine Deficiency Disorders 
Fetus:  
• Stillbirth, spontaneous abortion, congenital anomalies 
• Endemic cretinism — irreversible brain damage; IQ deficit of 13-15 points on average 
• Increased perinatal mortality 
Neonate:  
• Neonatal goitre, neonatal hypothyroidism 
• Delayed brain development, psychomotor delay 
Child and Adolescent:  
• Goitre (most visible manifestation) 
• Impaired mental function, poor school performance 
• Retarded physical development 
Adult:  
• Goitre (diffuse → nodular → toxic multinodular) 
• Hypothyroidism, impaired mental function 
• Increased risk of thyroid cancer 
• Decreased fertility 
 
Classification of Goitre (WHO/PAHO) 
Grade 
Description 
Grade 0 
No palpable or visible goitre 
Grade 1 
Goitre palpable but not visible when neck in normal 
position 
Grade 2 
Goitre visible and palpable with neck in normal position 
 
Assessment of IDD in Community 
• Total Goitre Rate (TGR) = Grade 1 + Grade 2 goitre 
• TGR >5% = public health problem 
• Urinary iodine excretion (UIE) — <100 mcg/L indicates deficiency; <20 mcg/L severe deficiency 
• Neonatal TSH (>5 mIU/L in >3% neonates = iodine deficiency in population) 
 
Government of India Strategies — NIDDCP 
National Iodine Deficiency Disorders Control Programme (NIDDCP):  
• Formerly: National Goitre Control Programme (1962); renamed NIDDCP in 1992 
• Objective: Reduce IDD prevalence to <10% in all districts 
40. Universal Salt Iodization (USI): Iodine fortification of all edible salt at production level (30 ppm at 
production, 15 ppm at consumption level). Prevention of Food Adulteration (PFA) Rules mandate iodized 
salt 
41. Monitoring and surveillance: Regular urinary iodine surveys, neonatal TSH screening 
42. Promotion of iodized salt: IEC campaigns, social mobilization 
15


--- PAGE 28 ---

swaDesh
43. Strengthening laboratories: State IDD surveillance units for urinary iodine and salt iodine testing 
44. Goitre mapping surveys: National surveys every 5 years to assess burden 
 
Challenges 
• Salt smuggling from non-iodized sources in border areas 
• Non-availability of iodized salt in tribal/remote areas 
• Storage losses — iodine evaporates with heat and light 
• Perception that iodized salt is inferior or more expensive 
 
Conclusion 
IDD is entirely preventable through universal salt iodization. India's NIDDCP has made substantial progress — 
goitre prevalence has declined nationally. However, pockets of high endemicity persist, particularly in the 
Himalayan belt and hilly areas. Continued surveillance and strong implementation of USI are essential. 
 
Q11. Rationality behind supplementary nutritional programmes in India.   [2011] 
 
Introduction 
Supplementary Nutrition Programmes (SNPs) provide additional food to nutritionally vulnerable groups to bridge 
the gap between dietary intake and recommended nutrient requirements. The rationale is rooted in India's 
enormous unmet nutritional burden. 
 
Rationale and Justification 
1. Evidence of Nutritional Gap:  
• NNMB surveys show average calorie and protein intake of the bottom quartile falls short of RDA by 30-40% 
• NFHS-5: 35.5% stunting, 67.1% anaemia in under-5 children — indicating massive dietary insufficiency 
2. Critical Period Concept:  
• First 1000 days (conception to 2nd birthday) — window of opportunity for nutrition impact on brain 
development, immunity, and growth 
• Irreversible consequences of malnutrition during this period make early supplementation essential 
3. Economic Rationale (Cost-Effectiveness):  
• Copenhagen Consensus (2008, 2012): Nutrition interventions (micronutrient supplementation, 
breastfeeding promotion) rank among the highest return-on-investment development interventions 
• Every Rs 1 invested in nutrition yields Rs 34-38 in economic returns 
• Malnutrition reduces GDP by 2-3% through lost productivity (World Bank estimate for India) 
4. Failure of Individual Diet:  
• Poverty and food prices prevent adequate dietary intake at household level 
• Dietary diversification alone insufficient in extreme poverty 
• Complementary foods for infants are expensive and culturally limited 
5. Infection-Nutrition Cycle:  
• Malnutrition increases susceptibility to infection, which worsens malnutrition — supplementation breaks this 
cycle 
 
Major Supplementary Nutrition Programmes in India 
16

ICDS — Core SNP 
• Provides 300 kcal and 8-10 g protein to children 6 months-6 years daily 
• Severely malnourished children: double ration — 600 kcal, 20-25 g protein 
• Pregnant/lactating women: 600 kcal, 18-20 g protein 
• Take-Home Rations (THR) during COVID-19 — preshelled groundnuts, eggs, energy-dense foods 
 
Criticism and Limitations 
• Leakage and corruption in food supply chains 
• Poor quality and nutritional inadequacy of food provided 
• Low coverage and targeting inefficiency 
• Dependence without addressing root causes 
• Does not address all micronutrient deficiencies 
 
Conclusion 
Supplementary nutrition programmes are justified by the magnitude of India's nutritional burden, the critical period 
concept, and the cost-effectiveness evidence. Their rationality lies in closing the dietary gap that poverty and food 
insecurity create. Strengthening quality, coverage, and community ownership is essential for maximum impact. 
 
Q12. Factors influencing nutritional anaemia among adolescents and 
preventive strategies.   [2020] 
 
Introduction 
Adolescence (10-19 years) is a period of rapid growth and development with markedly increased nutrient 
demands. Nutritional anaemia — primarily iron deficiency anaemia — is highly prevalent among adolescents in 
India. NFHS-5 shows 59.1% of adolescent girls and 31.1% of adolescent boys are anaemic. 
 
Factors Influencing Nutritional Anaemia in Adolescents 
A. Biological Factors:  
• Rapid growth spurt — increased iron demand for expanding blood volume and muscle mass 
• Onset of menstruation in girls — average 30-40 mg iron loss per cycle; heavy menstruation (menorrhagia) 
increases this 
• Early pregnancy — adolescent pregnancy depletes already limited iron stores 
17


--- PAGE 30 ---

swaDesh
• Low birth weight and pre-existing iron deficiency from childhood 
B. Dietary Factors:  
• Predominantly cereal-based diet — high phytate content reduces iron bioavailability 
• Low consumption of meat, poultry, fish (haem iron sources) due to cost or cultural practice 
• Inadequate ascorbic acid (vitamin C) intake — reduces non-haem iron absorption 
• Frequent tea/coffee consumption — polyphenols bind dietary iron 
• Skipping meals, dieting (especially girls), irregular eating patterns 
• Junk food preference — low nutrient density 
C. Socioeconomic Factors:  
• Poverty — inability to afford iron-rich foods 
• Low literacy of parents — poor nutritional awareness 
• Gender discrimination — girls receive less food, have lower food priority in family 
• Early marriage and early pregnancy in girls 
D. Infection and Parasitic Causes:  
• Hookworm infestation — significant cause of iron loss in rural adolescent boys 
• Malaria — haemolytic anaemia in endemic regions 
• Repeated gut infections — malabsorption of iron 
• Schistosomiasis (some endemic areas) 
E. Social and Cultural Factors:  
• Exclusion of girls from meals during menstruation (food taboos) 
• School dropout — loss of mid-day meal as iron source 
• Ignorance about iron-rich foods 
 
Preventive Strategies 
1. Weekly Iron Folic Acid Supplementation (WIFS) — National Programme:  
• 100 mg elemental iron + 0.5 mg folic acid — weekly for adolescents 10-19 years 
• Girls: 52 tablets/year; Boys: 52 tablets/week through schools and AWCs 
• Supervised administration in schools under National Health Mission 
• Concomitant biannual deworming (Albendazole 400 mg) 
2. Dietary Modification and Counselling:  
• Promote iron-rich foods: green leafy vegetables (spinach, drumstick leaves), jaggery, meat, eggs, legumes 
• Encourage vitamin C-rich foods alongside iron-rich foods 
• Discourage tea/coffee with meals 
• School nutrition education programmes 
3. Deworming:  
• National Deworming Day (NDD) — biannual Albendazole 400 mg for 1-19 year age group 
• Reduces hookworm-related iron loss 
4. Infection Control:  
• ORS and zinc for diarrhoea 
• Malaria control in endemic areas 
• Safe drinking water and sanitation (Swachh Bharat) 
5. Food Fortification:  
• Fortified rice and wheat flour through PDS — Iron + Folic Acid 
• Double fortified salt (DFS) — iodine + iron 
6. Behaviour Change Communication (BCC):  
• Adolescent peer educators (Kishori Shakti Yojana) 
18


--- PAGE 31 ---

swaDesh
• Health and Wellness Clubs in schools 
• Mass media campaigns — Eat Right India (FSSAI) 
7. Addressing Menstrual Health:  
• Access to sanitary products — PM Overarching Scheme for Holistic Nourishment 
• Menstrual hygiene management education 
• Treatment of menorrhagia 
 
Q13. Describe the ecology of malnutrition in India.   [Nov 2023 — NEW] 
 
Introduction 
The 'ecology of malnutrition' refers to the complex web of biological, environmental, social, economic, and political 
factors that interact to produce malnutrition. The term was popularized by Jelliffe (1966) and encompasses the 
relationship between the host (human), agent (nutrients), and environment. 
 
Ecological Triad in Malnutrition 
Component 
Factors 
Host (Human) 
Age, sex, physiological state (pregnancy), genetics, pre-
existing illness, immunocompetence 
Agent (Nutritional) 
Energy, protein, micronutrients — their quantity, quality, 
and bioavailability 
Environment 
Food availability, socioeconomic status, sanitation, 
cultural practices, climate, agriculture 
 
UNICEF Conceptual Framework — Ecological Levels 
Immediate Level:  
• Inadequate dietary intake — insufficient food quantity, poor quality, monotonous diet 
• Disease — diarrhoea, ARI, fever increase catabolism; reduce appetite and absorption 
Underlying Level:  
• Household food insecurity — seasonal food scarcity, crop failure, poverty 
• Inadequate care practices — poor IYCF, lack of maternal time, child neglect 
• Poor WASH (Water, Sanitation, Hygiene) — pathogen exposure, environmental enteropathy 
• Inadequate health services — unimmunized children, no ANC 
Basic/Root Level:  
• Poverty — inability to afford nutritious food 
• Gender inequality — women's disempowerment, low maternal education 
• Political and governance failures — food policy gaps, corruption in welfare schemes 
• Climate change — droughts, floods affecting food systems 
 
Specific Ecological Factors in Indian Context 
Agricultural:  
• Green Revolution emphasis on wheat/rice — dietary monotony, reduction in pulses and oilseed cultivation 
• Loss of traditional food biodiversity (millets, indigenous vegetables) 
• Climate vulnerability — 60% farmers dependent on rain-fed agriculture 
19


--- PAGE 32 ---

swaDesh
Socio-cultural:  
• Caste-based food restrictions — avoidance of animal protein foods 
• Food taboos during pregnancy — restriction of certain fruits, meat, eggs 
• Early weaning — replacement with starchy, low-nutrient gruels 
• Female feticide/foeticide — malnourished adolescent girls → malnourished mothers → LBW infants 
Environmental:  
• Open defecation — environmental enteropathy; gut pathology reducing absorption 
• Water contamination — diarrhoeal diseases accelerating malnutrition 
• Pollution — reduced physical activity, reduced outdoor food gathering 
Economic:  
• 30% of calorie deficit among rural poor attributable to income poverty 
• Food price inflation — affects protein-rich foods disproportionately 
• Urban-rural nutrition divide — urban slums developing dual burden 
 
Intergenerational Cycle of Malnutrition 
A malnourished girl → becomes malnourished adolescent → malnourished mother → delivers LBW baby → 
malnourished infant and child → grows into malnourished adult. This cycle is the ecological basis of India's 
persistent malnutrition burden. Breaking this cycle requires multi-generational, multi-sectoral action. 
 
Conclusion 
Ecology of malnutrition in India is a complex interplay of individual, household, community, and structural factors. 
Effective intervention requires addressing all ecological levels simultaneously — food systems, health, education, 
sanitation, and governance — rather than isolated nutrition interventions. 
 
Q14. Role of micronutrients in diet.   [2012] 
 
Introduction 
Micronutrients are vitamins and minerals required by the body in small quantities (milligrams to micrograms per 
day) but essential for vital physiological functions. Deficiency causes 'hidden hunger' — impaired function without 
obvious visible signs.

Micronutrient Interactions 
• Vitamin C enhances iron absorption (reduces Fe3+ to Fe2+) 
• Vitamin D is required for calcium absorption in gut 
• Vitamin A and zinc interact — zinc required for retinol-binding protein synthesis 
• Excess zinc inhibits copper absorption (competitive inhibition) 
21


--- PAGE 34 ---

swaDesh
• Folate masks vitamin B12 deficiency (haematologically) 
 
Conclusion 
Micronutrients are indispensable for life despite being required in small amounts. Deficiencies are widespread 
globally, particularly in developing countries. India's public health nutrition strategy addresses this through 
targeted supplementation (NI+, VA programme), food fortification (iodized salt, fortified rice), dietary 
diversification, and biofortification. 
 
Q15. Describe methods of food fortification.   [Nov 2023 — NEW] 
 
Definition 
Food fortification is the deliberate addition of one or more essential nutrients (micronutrients) to food, whether or 
not normally contained in the food, for the purpose of preventing or correcting demonstrated deficiency of one or 
more nutrients in the population (WHO/FAO, 1994). 
 
Rationale for Food Fortification 
• Reaches large populations through commonly consumed foods without changing eating habits 
• Cost-effective compared to supplementation programmes 
• Sustainable — does not require individual compliance 
• Suitable for both deficient and non-deficient populations (universal) 
• Technological feasibility — modern food technology allows stable addition of micronutrients 
 
Types/Methods of Fortification 
1. Mass/Universal Fortification:  
Addition of micronutrients to widely consumed staple foods. 
• Universal Salt Iodization (USI) — iodized salt (15 ppm at consumption level); India's NIDDCP 
• Flour fortification — wheat flour with iron, folic acid, Vitamin B12 (FSSAI standards: Fortified Atta — Iron 14-
21 mg/kg + Folic acid 75-125 mcg/kg + Vitamin B12 0.75-1.25 mcg/kg) 
• Rice fortification — iron-fortified extruded rice kernels blended with regular rice (1:100); Pradhan Mantri 
Garib Kalyan Anna Yojana (PMGKAY) — fortified rice through PDS and MDM 
• Milk fortification — Vitamin A and D added to double-toned milk 
• Edible oil fortification — Vitamin A and D (FSSAI standard) 
2. Targeted/Selective Fortification:  
Fortification of foods targeted to specific vulnerable groups. 
• Complementary food supplements (Bal Shakti, Suji, corn-soy blend) for malnourished children 
• Ready-to-Use Supplementary Food (RUSF) — for MAM children 
• Therapeutic milks F-75 and F-100 for SAM management 
• Energy-dense nutritional supplements for pregnant women (e.g., Lipid-based Nutrient Supplement — LNS) 
3. Market-Driven/Commercial Fortification:  
Voluntary fortification by food industry to meet market demand for 'healthy' foods. 
• Fortified breakfast cereals (iron, B vitamins) 
• Fortified biscuits (multiple micronutrients) 
• Energy drinks with B vitamins, electrolytes 
22


--- PAGE 35 ---

swaDesh
• Baby foods and infant formula — mandatorily fortified per FSSAI Infant Foods Standards 
4. Home Fortification:  
Addition of micronutrient preparations to foods at the household level. 
• Micronutrient Powders (MNPs) / 'Sprinkles' — sachets containing iron, zinc, Vitamin A, folic acid; added to 
home-cooked complementary foods 
• Used in India under ICDS and ASHA programmes for 6-23 month children 
• Crushable tablets and drops (Iron drops for children) 
5. Biofortification:  
Improving nutritional quality of food crops through plant breeding or agronomic practices. 
• High-iron pearl millet (Bajra) — developed by ICRISAT; 80-100 ppm iron vs normal 20-40 ppm 
• High-zinc wheat and rice 
• Vitamin A-rich cassava (orange-fleshed) 
• Vitamin A-rich sweet potato (orange-fleshed sweet potato — OFSP) 
• Advantage: Self-propagating, no recurring cost, culturally accepted food 
 
Key Principles of Effective Fortification 
Principle 
Explanation 
Food vehicle selection 
Widely consumed by target group; consumed regularly 
and in consistent amounts 
Micronutrient selection 
Based on documented deficiency in the population 
Fortification level 
Must meet dietary gap without risk of toxicity; based on 
EAR and UL 
Stability 
Nutrient must be stable in food during processing, 
storage, and cooking 
Organoleptic acceptability 
No change in taste, colour, or texture 
Cost 
Addition cost should be minimal (<1% of food cost 
ideally) 
Regulatory framework 
FSSAI in India sets standards for all fortified foods 
Monitoring 
Regular quality control at production; survey-based 
impact assessment 
 
FSSAI '+F' Logo 
Foods meeting FSSAI fortification standards display the '+F' (Plus F) logo — a blue-and-orange logo — to help 
consumers identify fortified foods. India mandates +F logo for all commercially sold fortified staples. 
 
Conclusion 
Food fortification is one of the most cost-effective and scalable interventions for addressing micronutrient 
deficiencies. India's strategy combines universal salt iodization, rice and wheat flour fortification, and 
biofortification. The regulatory framework under FSSAI, combined with targeted supplementation through NI+, 
provides a comprehensive approach to eliminating 'hidden hunger'. 
 
Q16. Measures to promote food safety in hostels of educational institutions.   
[Dec 2024 — NEW] 
 
23


--- PAGE 36 ---

swaDesh
Introduction 
Food safety in institutional settings such as educational hostels is a critical public health concern. Hostels serve 
large numbers of students daily under shared kitchen facilities, making them high-risk settings for foodborne 
illnesses. Food safety measures aim to prevent contamination, ensure hygiene, and maintain nutritional quality of 
food served. 
 
Magnitude of Problem 
• Foodborne diseases affect an estimated 600 million people annually globally (WHO 2015) 
• Common outbreaks in Indian hostels: typhoid, hepatitis A, cholera, staphylococcal food poisoning, bacillary 
dysentery 
• Risk factors: bulk cooking, long storage, inadequate refrigeration, poor personal hygiene of food handlers 
 
Common Causes of Foodborne Illness in Hostels 
Source 
Examples 
Biological 
Salmonella, S. aureus, E. coli O157, Hepatitis A virus, 
Bacillus cereus 
Physical 
Insect/rodent contamination, foreign bodies (glass, 
metal) 
Chemical 
Pesticide residues, adulterants (FSSAI concern), 
cleaning agent residues 
Improper handling 
Undercooking, cross-contamination, food held at danger 
zone (5-60°C) 
 
Measures to Promote Food Safety 
A. Infrastructure and Physical Standards:  
45. Kitchen design — separate areas for raw and cooked food; adequate ventilation; impervious, washable 
floors and walls 
46. Adequate refrigeration — separate refrigerators for raw and cooked food; maintain <5°C (cold chain) 
47. Pest-proof construction — rodent-proof, mosquito mesh on windows 
48. Potable water supply — treated, tested water; regular bacteriological testing 
49. Waste management — covered bins, daily removal, no stagnant water near kitchen 
50. Adequate storage — dry goods stored off floor; FIFO (First In, First Out) system 
B. Personal Hygiene of Food Handlers:  
51. Regular medical check-up — food handlers screened for typhoid, hepatitis B, skin infections, tuberculosis; 
certificates maintained 
52. Hand hygiene — mandatory handwashing with soap before handling food, after toilet use; hand sanitizer 
stations at kitchen entry 
53. Appropriate clothing — clean aprons, hair nets, no jewellery; no smoking/eating in kitchen 
54. Training — food safety and hygiene training for all kitchen staff 
C. Food Handling Practices (HACCP Principles):  
55. Proper cooking temperatures — meat/poultry cooked to ≥75°C (core temperature); eggs fully cooked 
56. Avoid danger zone (5-60°C) — hot food stored >60°C; cold food <5°C; prepared food not left at room 
temperature >2 hours 
57. Avoid cross-contamination — separate cutting boards/knives for raw and cooked foods; colour-coded 
equipment 
58. Prevent reheating more than once — single reheating to ≥75°C 
59. Source verification — purchase food from approved, licensed suppliers only 
24


--- PAGE 37 ---

swaDesh
 
D. Regulatory and Institutional Measures 
1. FSSAI (Food Safety and Standards Authority of India) Compliance:  
• All hostel kitchens serving >50 students must register under FSSAI 
• FSSAI's 'Eat Right Campus' initiative — promotes healthy and safe food in educational institutions 
• Display FSSAI registration certificate prominently 
2. Regular Inspections:  
• Periodic inspection by Food Safety Officers (FSO) under FSSAI Act, 2006 
• Internal audits by hostel administration monthly 
• Surprise inspections by university health teams 
3. Food Quality Testing:  
• Routine bacteriological testing of drinking water (monthly) 
• Swab tests of kitchen surfaces and utensils (quarterly) 
• Food sample preservation for 48 hours — retained sample for investigation if outbreak occurs 
4. Menu Planning and Nutrition:  
• Balanced, nutritionally adequate meals planned by a qualified dietitian 
• Display calorie content and allergen information 
• Avoid use of banned food additives and colours 
5. Student Participation:  
• Student food committee — representatives involved in menu planning, hygiene monitoring 
• Complaint box for food quality grievances 
• Awareness campaigns — food safety week, nutrition fairs 
6. Emergency Preparedness:  
• Outbreak investigation protocol — immediately report cluster of GI illness cases to hostel warden and 
health team 
• Food handler removal during illness 
• Stock of ORS, antimotility agents, contact with nearest hospital 
 
FSSAI 'Eat Right Campus' Programme 
FSSAI launched the Eat Right Campus initiative to make educational institutions models of healthy eating and 
food safety. Components include: Safe and hygienic food environment, nutritious and diverse menu, food 
fortification awareness, reduced junk food availability, and engagement of students as food safety ambassadors. 
 
Conclusion 
Food safety in educational institution hostels requires a comprehensive approach integrating infrastructure 
standards, personal hygiene of handlers, HACCP-based food handling, regulatory compliance under FSSAI, and 
active student engagement. Regular training, monitoring, and inspection are the cornerstones of safe institutional 
food service.

TOPIC 2: IEC / HEALTH COMMUNICATION / SKILLS FOR IPC 
 
Q1. As MO i/c of PHC, plan an IEC campaign in villages. Evaluate its effectiveness. (2014) 
Introduction 
As Medical Officer in-charge of a PHC, planning an IEC (Information, Education, and Communication) campaign 
requires a systematic, participatory, community-centered approach. IEC campaigns aim to change knowledge, 
attitudes, and practices (KAP) of the community regarding health issues. 
Steps in Planning an IEC Campaign (PRECEDE-PROCEED / COMBI Framework) 
STEP 1 – Situational Analysis / Needs Assessment 
• 
Conduct rapid epidemiological survey: prevalence of target disease/behaviour 
• 
Community health survey: identify KAP gaps, socio-cultural barriers 
• 
Stakeholder analysis: AWW, ASHA, ANM, Panchayat, SHG leaders 
• 
Resource inventory: manpower, funds, materials available at PHC level 
STEP 2 – Goal and Objective Setting (SMART) 
• 
Specific: e.g., 'Increase ORS use in diarrhoea cases by 60% in 6 months' 
• 
Measurable: quantifiable baseline from KAP survey 
• 
Achievable within PHC resource constraints 
• 
Relevant to local health priority (determined by HMIS data) 
• 
Time-bound: phased calendar with milestones 
STEP 3 – Target Audience Segmentation 
• 
Primary audience: Women of reproductive age, children 0–5 years, adolescents 
• 
Secondary audience: Family influencers — husbands, mothers-in-law 
• 
Tertiary audience: Community leaders, PRIs, religious leaders 
STEP 4 – Message Development 
• 
Develop culturally appropriate, linguistically correct messages 
• 
Use SMOG formula: Simple, Measurable, Obtainable, General 
• 
Pretesting of messages with focus group discussions (FGDs) 
• 
Positive reinforcement messaging; avoid scare tactics

Evaluation of IEC Campaign Effectiveness 
Evaluation should be built into the plan from the beginning — not an afterthought. 
A. Process Evaluation (Formative): 
• 
Were activities conducted as planned? (coverage, reach, frequency) 
• 
Number of IEC materials distributed, events held, persons reached 
• 
Quality of message delivery — supervisory checklists 
B. Output Evaluation: 
• 
Number of educational sessions conducted 
• 
Attendance registers at VHNDs, group meetings 
• 
IEC material distribution records 
C. Outcome Evaluation (Impact on KAP) — Most Important: 
• 
Pre–post KAP survey comparison using validated questionnaire 
• 
Indicators: % increase in awareness, change in attitude scores, behaviour change 
• 
Specific: ORS use, immunization uptake, ANC registration, condom use rates 
D. Impact Evaluation (Long-term): 
• 
Change in morbidity/mortality indicators (IMR, MMR, disease incidence) 
• 
Assessed via HMIS data, 3–5 years post-campaign 
E. Evaluation Tools: 
• 
KAP surveys (pre and post), FGDs, key informant interviews 
• 
HMIS/RHC data analysis, supervisory field visits 
• 
Rapid assessment surveys by external evaluators 
Conclusion 
A well-planned IEC campaign at PHC level integrates systematic needs assessment, targeted messaging, mixed-
channel delivery, and built-in evaluation mechanisms. Continuous feedback loops and community participation 
are essential for sustained behaviour change.

Q4. What is health communication? Describe in detail the methods of health communication. 
(2021, 2018) ★★★★★ 
Definition 
Health Communication (WHO definition): The study and use of communication strategies to inform and influence 
individual and community decisions that enhance health. It encompasses interpersonal, organizational, and mass-
media strategies to disseminate health information, promote behavior change, and advocate for health-promoting 
policies. 
Models of Health Communication 
• 
Shannon-Weaver model: Source → Message → Channel → Receiver → Feedback 
29


--- PAGE 42 ---

swaDesh
• 
Berlo's SMCR model: Source credibility + Message + Channel + Receiver characteristics 
• 
Transtheoretical model: Stages of change (Pre-contemplation → Contemplation → Preparation → Action 
→ Maintenance) 
• 
Health Belief Model: Perceived susceptibility + severity + benefits − barriers → behaviour 
Methods of Health Communication 
1. Interpersonal Communication (IPC) 
• 
Definition: Face-to-face two-way communication between individuals 
• 
Examples: Doctor-patient counselling, ASHA's home visits, AWW nutrition counselling 
• 
Advantages: Immediate feedback, tailored messages, trust-building, addresses individual barriers 
• 
Disadvantages: Time-intensive, limited reach, dependent on provider skills 
• 
Skills required: Active listening, empathy, non-verbal cues, motivational interviewing 
2. Group Communication 
• 
Methods: Lecture, group discussion, panel discussion, symposium, workshop, demonstration 
• 
Examples: VHND group education, SHG health talks, school health assemblies 
• 
Advantages: Peer learning, cost-effective, group dynamics reinforcement 
• 
Disadvantages: Individual needs may be missed, dominated by vocal members 
3. Mass Communication 
• 
TV/Radio: Doordarshan health spots, AIR health talks, Red Ribbon Express 
• 
Print media: Newspapers, magazines, pamphlets, posters, billboards 
• 
Digital: Social media (Facebook, WhatsApp, YouTube, Twitter), mobile health (mHealth) 
• 
Advantages: Wide reach, simultaneous coverage, social norm setting 
• 
Disadvantages: One-way mostly, cannot tailor to individual, digital divide 
4. Folk / Traditional Media 
• 
Methods: Nukkad natak (street play), puppetry, folk songs, dance drama, Burra katha 
• 
Target: Low-literacy, rural, tribal populations 
• 
Advantages: Culturally resonant, high recall, entertainment-education 
• 
Used in: Family planning, malaria, TB, nutrition campaigns 
5. IEC Materials (Print/Visual Aids) 
Material 
Best Use 
Limitation 
Poster 
Health centres, schools 
Literate audience only 
Pamphlet/Leaflet 
Mass distribution 
Often discarded 
Flip chart 
Field workers (ASHA/ANM) 
Limited to small groups 
Flash cards 
Teaching aids 
Need trained user 
Model/Specimen 
Anatomical demos 
Costly, fragile 
 
6. Digital and mHealth Communication 
• 
SMS/IVR: Mother and Child Tracking System (MCTS), Kilkari programme 
• 
Apps: Aarogya Setu, NHM mobile applications for ASHA workers 
• 
Telemedicine: eSanjeevani for remote health communication 
• 
Social media campaigns: #QuitTobacco, Poshan Maah digital drives 
Principles of Effective Health Communication (SMOG + ACADA) 
• 
Simple: Plain language, appropriate literacy level 
• 
Meaningful: Relevant to target audience's life context 
30


--- PAGE 43 ---

swaDesh
• 
Obtainable: Actionable recommendations within reach 
• 
Graphic: Visual appeal, appropriate graphics 
• 
Accuracy, Consistency, Availability, Decisiveness, Appropriateness (ACADA) 
Barriers to Health Communication 
• 
Language and literacy barriers 
• 
Cultural beliefs and superstitions 
• 
Noise (physical, semantic, psychological) 
• 
Digital divide and low media access 
• 
Credibility of source 
• 
Information overload / health misinformation ('infodemic') 
Conclusion 
Effective health communication requires matching the method to the target audience, message, and context. A 
multi-channel integrated approach using IPC + group + mass media ensures maximum reach and deepest 
behavior change. 
 
Q5. How will you plan, organize, implement and evaluate a Health Communication 
Programme? (2020) ★★★★★ 
Framework: GOBSMEEP / PRECEDE-PROCEED 
Planning a Health Communication Programme (HCP) follows a systematic management cycle: 
1. Goal and Situation Analysis 
• 
Epidemiological analysis: What is the health problem? Who is affected? 
• 
Behavioural analysis: What behaviours contribute? What needs to change? 
• 
Social/cultural context: Beliefs, literacy, language, existing knowledge 
• 
Resource analysis: Budget, manpower, infrastructure (SWOT) 
2. Objective Setting (SMART) 
• 
Programme objectives: e.g., 70% of mothers to know 3 danger signs of neonatal illness 
• 
Communication objectives: Awareness → Attitude change → Practice change 
3. Target Audience Analysis 
• 
Segmentation: Demographic, geographic, psychographic, behavioural 
• 
Audience profiling: literacy, preferred media, influencers 
4. Message Development and Pretesting 
• 
Core message concept development 
• 
Pretesting with 25–50 representatives of target audience 
• 
Revision based on feedback before finalizing 
5. Media / Channel Selection and Materials Development 
• 
Mix of IPC + group + mass media + folk media 
• 
Develop, pretest, finalize IEC materials 
• 
Build media production schedule 
6. Implementation (Organizing + Executing) 
• 
Operational plan: Activities, timelines, responsibilities (who does what, when, where) 
• 
Capacity building: Train ASHAs, ANMs, school teachers as communicators 
• 
Roll-out: Phased — pilot district → scale-up 
• 
Monitoring: Monthly review meetings, field supervision, MIS 
7. Evaluation 
31


--- PAGE 44 ---

swaDesh
• 
Formative: Pre-testing materials, process feedback during implementation 
• 
Summative: Pre–post KAP survey; service utilization data 
• 
Impact: Long-term morbidity/mortality trends (HMIS/SRS) 
Evaluation Framework 
Level 
What to Measure 
Tool 
Input 
Resources used, materials produced 
Financial records 
Process 
Activities completed, reach achieved 
Supervisory reports 
Output 
Number of sessions, persons reached 
Registers, MIS 
Outcome 
KAP change, service uptake 
KAP survey 
Impact 
Disease reduction, health status 
HMIS, SRS

Q7. Enumerate different barriers of health communication. Role of audio-visual aids. (2018) 
★★★★ 
Barriers of Health Communication 
A. Source-related Barriers: 
• 
Low credibility of communicator (trust deficit) 
• 
Poor communication skills, jargon use 
• 
Cultural/linguistic mismatch between communicator and audience 
B. Message-related Barriers: 
• 
Complex, technical language inappropriate for target literacy 
• 
Inconsistent or contradictory messages across channels 
• 
Information overload — too much data, low retention 
• 
Culturally inappropriate content 
C. Channel-related Barriers: 
• 
Poor reach of media in remote/tribal areas (digital divide) 
• 
Unreliable electricity, internet connectivity 
• 
Language barrier in mass media (regional content deficit) 
D. Receiver-related Barriers: 
• 
Low health literacy (India adult literacy ~77.7%, NFHS-5) 
• 
Deep-rooted beliefs, myths, and superstitions 
• 
Psychological barriers: denial, fear of stigma, fatalism 
• 
Socioeconomic barriers: poverty, work pressure 
E. Environmental/Situational Barriers: 
• 
Physical noise (crowded OPD, market IEC) 
• 
Semantic noise (same word, different meaning) 
• 
Time constraints in busy OPD settings 
Role of Audio-Visual Aids (AVAs) in Health Communication 
Dale's Cone of Experience: People retain 20% of what they hear, 30% of what they see, and 50% of what they 
see and hear simultaneously — AVAs dramatically enhance retention. 
AVA Type

Slides, PowerPoint, OHP, filmstrips

Benefits of AVAs: 
• 
Overcome language/literacy barriers through visual demonstration 
• 
Standardize messages — same content delivered uniformly 
• 
Increase engagement, retention, and emotional impact 
• 
Demonstrate procedures (e.g., ORS preparation, condom use) 
• 
Useful in health worker training — skill demonstration 
 
Q8. Skills for good interpersonal communication. (Oct 2025) ★NEW★ 
Definition 
Interpersonal communication (IPC) is a face-to-face, two-way communication process between two or more 
individuals that enables sharing of information, feelings, and meaning. In health settings, IPC is the cornerstone of 
doctor-patient and health worker-community relationships. 
Core Skills for Good IPC 
1. Active Listening 
• 
Give undivided attention; avoid interruptions 
• 
Maintain eye contact (culturally appropriate) 
• 
Use verbal affirmations ('I see', 'Go on') and non-verbal nods 
• 
Paraphrase and reflect: 'So you're telling me that...' 
2. Empathy 
• 
Acknowledge and validate the patient's feelings 
• 
'I understand this must be difficult for you' 
• 
Distinguish sympathy (feeling for) from empathy (feeling with) 
3. Non-verbal Communication 
• 
Body language: Open posture, facing the patient, not crossing arms 
• 
Proxemics: Appropriate personal space (intimate <0.5m for clinical; social 1–3m for counselling) 
• 
Facial expressions: Warm, non-judgmental expression 
• 
Touch: Therapeutic touch (with consent) — handshake, shoulder pat 
4. Clear and Simple Language 
• 
Avoid medical jargon; use patient's vocabulary level 
• 
Short sentences, one idea at a time 
• 
Check understanding: 'Can you tell me in your own words what we just discussed?' 
5. Questioning Skills 
• 
Open-ended questions: 'Tell me about your symptoms' — elicits narrative 
• 
Closed questions: 'Do you have fever?' — useful for specific info 
• 
Probing: 'Can you tell me more about that?' 
• 
Avoid leading questions: 'You don't smoke, do you?' 
6. Feedback and Reflection 
• 
Summarize and reflect back key points 
• 
Correct misunderstandings gently 
• 
Invite questions: 'Do you have any questions for me?' 
7. Motivational Interviewing (MI) 
• 
OARS: Open questions, Affirmation, Reflective listening, Summarizing 
• 
Explore ambivalence without confrontation 
34


--- PAGE 47 ---

swaDesh
• 
Roll with resistance; support self-efficacy 
8. Cultural Competence 
• 
Respect cultural beliefs, gender norms, language preferences 
• 
Avoid assumptions; ask about preferences 
• 
Use trained interpreters when language barrier exists 
AIDET Framework (Healthcare Communication Model) 
Letter 
Meaning 
Example 
A 
Acknowledge 
Greet by name, make eye contact 
I 
Introduce 
'I am Dr. X, your treating physician' 
D 
Duration 
'This procedure will take about 10 minutes' 
E 
Explanation 
Explain in simple terms what you will do 
T 
Thank you 
Thank patient for their time and cooperation 
 
Q9. What are the principles of health education? (2023) ★★★ 
Definition 
Health education is any combination of learning experiences designed to help individuals and communities 
improve their health by increasing knowledge or influencing attitudes. (WHO) 
Principles of Health Education 
1. Credibility: Information must come from a trusted, authoritative source. Credibility increases compliance 
and adoption of health messages. 
2. Interest: Education must be relevant and meaningful to the learner. Irrelevant content leads to 
disengagement. 
3. Participation: Community members must be involved in planning and implementing health education. 
Active participation ensures ownership and sustainability. 
4. Comprehension: Messages must be pitched at the literacy level and comprehension capacity of the 
audience. Use simple language, visuals, and demonstrations. 
5. Reinforcement: Repeated exposure to health messages across multiple channels over time reinforces 
learning and sustains behaviour change. 
6. Learning by doing: Practical demonstration (e.g., ORS preparation, breastfeeding technique) is more 
effective than passive listening. 
7. Known to unknown: Start from what is familiar to the audience, then build toward new concepts. 
8. Specific and realistic: Recommendations must be specific, actionable, and achievable within the 
audience's means. 
9. Cultural appropriateness: Content, language, and delivery must respect local cultural norms, beliefs, and 
practices. 
10. Feedback: Two-way communication enables the educator to assess understanding and correct 
misconceptions. 
11. Motivation: Positive reinforcement (praise, recognition) sustains behaviour change better than fear or 
coercion. 
12. Individual differences: Learning pace, style, and literacy vary — individualize education accordingly. 
Application in Practice 
• 
ASHA's counselling: Uses known-to-unknown, participation, reinforcement principles 
• 
VHSND: Group education employs comprehension, interest, and reinforcement 
• 
BCC materials: Designed applying credibility, cultural appropriateness, specificity 
35


--- PAGE 48 ---

swaDesh
 
Q10. What are the barriers in Doctor-Patient communication? (2012) ★★★ 
Importance of Doctor-Patient Communication 
Effective doctor-patient communication (DPC) is associated with better diagnosis accuracy, greater patient 
adherence, higher satisfaction, and improved health outcomes (KALAMAZOO Consensus 2001). 
Barriers — SENDER (Doctor) Side 
• 
Use of medical jargon and technical terminology 
• 
Time pressure in busy OPD (average consultation <3 min in public health facilities) 
• 
Poor communication training in medical curricula (AETCOM module — recent initiative) 
• 
Cultural/language mismatch with patient 
• 
Paternalistic attitude — not encouraging questions 
• 
Burnout, fatigue, emotional exhaustion 
• 
Implicit bias (social class, gender, caste prejudice) 
Barriers — RECEIVER (Patient) Side 
• 
Low health literacy — cannot understand medical information 
• 
Fear, anxiety, shame causing reluctance to share full history 
• 
Cultural taboos — discussing sexual/reproductive health, mental illness 
• 
Language barrier — dialect differences 
• 
Memory limitations — forgetting instructions post-consultation 
• 
Low assertiveness — 'not daring to ask the doctor' 
Barriers — Environment/System Level 
• 
Physical: Crowded OPDs, lack of privacy, excessive noise 
• 
Structural: No interpreters, inadequate consultation time 
• 
Hierarchical: Power imbalance in doctor-patient relationship 
• 
Electronic: EHR focus leading to 'screen time' replacing eye contact 
Overcoming Barriers 
• 
AETCOM (Attitude, Ethics & Communication) module in CBME — MCI mandated 2019 
• 
Communication skills training: Role play, standardized patients, OSCEs 
• 
Patient-centred care: Calgary-Cambridge framework 
• 
Use of communication aids: Visual materials, teach-back technique 
• 
System: Designated consultation time, translation services 
 
Q11. Enumerate teaching learning methods for AETCOM in CBME curriculum. (Dec 2024) 
★NEW★ 
Background: AETCOM in CBME 
AETCOM (Attitude, Ethics and Communication) is a mandatory, thread-based module in the Competency-Based 
Medical Education (CBME) curriculum introduced by the National Medical Commission (NMC) in 2019. It runs 
across all phases of MBBS and aims to develop professional values, ethical reasoning, and communication 
competencies. 
Teaching-Learning Methods for AETCOM 
1. Case-Based Learning (CBL) 
• 
Presentation of real/hypothetical clinical ethical dilemmas 
36


--- PAGE 49 ---

swaDesh
• 
Small group discussion of competing ethical principles 
• 
Develops: Critical ethical reasoning, empathy, professional identity 
• 
Example: Case of informed consent refusal; breaking bad news 
2. Role Play 
• 
Students enact doctor-patient scenarios in pairs/small groups 
• 
Immediate feedback by peers and facilitator 
• 
Develops: IPC skills, empathy, conflict resolution 
3. Standardized Patients (SP) / Simulated Clinical Encounters 
• 
Trained actors portray patients with specific communication challenges 
• 
Provides safe environment to practice without harming real patients 
• 
Assessed via OSCE stations 
4. Reflective Writing / Journaling 
• 
Students write reflective accounts after clinical postings 
• 
Guided questions: 'What did I feel? What would I do differently?' 
• 
Develops: Self-awareness, professional identity, emotional intelligence 
5. Small Group Discussion (SGD) 
• 
Facilitated structured discussions on ethical dilemmas, professionalism 
• 
Promotes peer learning, democratic dialogue 
6. Lecture-Demonstration 
• 
Didactic for foundational ethics principles (beneficence, non-maleficence, autonomy, justice) 
• 
Demonstration of communication techniques by faculty with feedback 
7. Portfolio-Based Learning 
• 
Students compile evidence of their communication and professional growth 
• 
Includes reflective logs, peer assessments, supervisor feedback 
8. Bedside Teaching with Communication Focus 
• 
Faculty model effective doctor-patient communication at bedside 
• 
Students observe, then practice under supervision 
• 
'See one, do one, teach one' modified for communication skills 
9. Arts and Humanities (Narrative Medicine) 
• 
Literature, films, artwork used to explore illness experience 
• 
Develops empathy, reduces burnout, enhances humanistic medicine 
Assessment of AETCOM Competencies 
• 
Formative: OSCE, mini-CEX, peer assessment, reflective portfolios 
• 
Summative: Communication OSCE stations, professionalism portfolio 
• 
360-degree feedback: Patient, peer, supervisor

TOPIC 3: INDICATORS OF HEALTH (MORBIDITY / MORTALITY / 
DISABILITY / HEALTH SYSTEMS / FERTILITY) 
 
Q1. Morbidity and mortality indicators of health — describe in detail with examples. (2022, 
2018) ★★★★★ 
Introduction — Health Indicators 
Health indicators are quantitative measures that describe the health status of a population and allow comparisons 
across time, place, and sub-groups. They are essential for health planning, monitoring, and evaluation (WHO 
Health Statistics). 
A. MORBIDITY INDICATORS 
Morbidity = burden of illness, disease, and injury in a population. 
1. Incidence Rate 
• 
Number of NEW cases of a disease per 1000 population per year 
• 
Formula: (New cases / Population at risk) × 1000 
• 
Use: Epidemic investigation, vaccine efficacy studies 
• 
Example: TB incidence in India = 199/1,00,000 (2022, WHO GTB) 
2. Prevalence Rate 
• 
Number of ALL cases (old + new) at a given time per 1000 population 
• 
Point prevalence: At a specific point in time 
• 
Period prevalence: During a defined period 
• 
Example: Hypertension prevalence India ~28% adults (ICMR-INDIAB) 
3. Attack Rate 
• 
Incidence proportion during limited epidemic period 
• 
Formula: (Cases during epidemic / Exposed population) × 100 
• 
Use: Outbreak investigation (food poisoning, cholera) 
4. Secondary Attack Rate (SAR) 
• 
Proportion of susceptible household contacts who develop disease after index case 
• 
Measures transmissibility within households 
5. Disability Rates 
• 
Days of disability per person per year 
• 
DALY = Disability Adjusted Life Year (YLL + YLD) 
• 
Example: India's disease burden — 29% from NCDs expressed in DALYs 
B. MORTALITY INDICATORS 
Indicator 
Formula 
India Value 
Crude Death Rate (CDR) 
Deaths / mid-yr pop × 1000 
6.2/1000 (SRS 2020) 
Infant Mortality Rate (IMR) 
Deaths <1yr / LB × 1000 
28/1000 LB (SRS 2020) 
Under-5 Mortality Rate (U5MR) 
Deaths <5yr / LB × 1000 
32/1000 LB 
Neonatal Mortality Rate (NMR) 
Deaths <28d / LB × 1000 
20/1000 LB 
Maternal Mortality Ratio (MMR) 
Maternal deaths / 1,00,000 LB

Q3. Elaborate on Disability rates used as indicators of health. (2022, 2018, 2014) ★★★★★ 
Definition 
Disability refers to limitations in functioning resulting from health conditions — it encompasses impairments, 
activity limitations, and participation restrictions (ICF 2001, WHO). 
Types of Disability Measures 
1. Days of Disability (National Health Survey method) 
• 
Restricted activity days: Days when person cannot perform usual activities 
• 
Bed disability days: Days spent in bed >50% of the day due to illness 
• 
Work-loss days: Days absent from work due to illness 
• 
Average: 2–4 restricted activity days per person per year (USA-NCHS) 
2. Disability-Adjusted Life Year (DALY) 
DALY = Years of Life Lost (YLL) + Years Lived with Disability (YLD) 
• 
YLL = N (deaths) × L (standard life expectancy at age of death) 
• 
YLD = Number of cases × Disability weight × Duration 
• 
One DALY = one year of healthy life lost 
• 
Global Burden of Disease Study uses DALY as primary summary measure 
• 
India: TB causes 2.7 million DALYs; lower respiratory infections — 2.5M DALYs (GBD 2019) 
3. Quality-Adjusted Life Year (QALY) 
• 
Used in health economics and technology assessment 
• 
QALY = Years lived × Quality weight (0–1 scale, 1 = perfect health) 
• 
Used to calculate Cost per QALY gained — threshold ~$50,000/QALY (WHO-CHOICE) 
4. WHO Disability Assessment Schedule (WHODAS 2.0) 
• 
36-item interview-administered scale 
• 
Domains: Cognition, Mobility, Self-care, Getting along, Life activities, Participation 
• 
Provides disability score 0–100 (0 = no disability, 100 = maximum disability) 
5. Sullivan's Index (Disability-Free Life Expectancy, DFLE) 
• 
Combines life expectancy and disability prevalence 
• 
DFLE = Years expected to be lived WITHOUT disability 
• 
More sensitive measure than life expectancy alone 
40


--- PAGE 53 ---

swaDesh

Q7. List the Morbidity Indicators. Add a note on International Classification of Diseases. (2015) 
★★★★ 
Morbidity Indicators — Summary 
• 
Incidence rate: New cases/at-risk population × 1000 
• 
Prevalence rate (point and period) 
• 
Attack rate and Secondary attack rate 
• 
Hospitalization rate: Admissions/1000 population/year 
• 
Sickness absence rate: Days lost/employed person/year 
• 
Disability rates: DALY, QALY, restricted activity days 
• 
Notification rate: Reported cases of notifiable diseases 
International Classification of Diseases (ICD) 
Background: 
• 
ICD is the WHO standard for coding diseases, symptoms, and causes of death 
• 
Developed in 1893 (Bertillon Classification), revised 11 times 
• 
Current edition: ICD-11 (adopted by WHO in 2019, effective Jan 2022) 
• 
India uses ICD-10 in most facilities; transitioning to ICD-11 
Structure of ICD-11: 
• 
22 chapters covering all diseases, injuries, external causes, and Z-codes (factors influencing health) 
• 
Digital-first design: web-based, multilingual, linkable to clinical terminologies (SNOMED CT) 
• 
Separate chapters for Traditional Medicine (Chapter 26) — reflects Ayurveda, TCM 
• 
ICD-11 codes: e.g., TB pulmonary = 1B10, HIV = 1C62 
Uses of ICD: 
• 
Morbidity coding in hospitals — DRG-based payment, quality benchmarking 
• 
Mortality statistics — cause of death certification 
• 
Epidemiological research — disease burden studies 
• 
Health system management — resource allocation, planning 
• 
International comparisons of disease burden 
 
Q8. Explain quality of life and its calculation. (2020, 2015) ★★★ 
Definition 
Quality of Life (QoL): 'An individual's perception of their position in life in the context of the culture and value 
systems in which they live and in relation to their goals, expectations, standards, and concerns.' — WHO, 1997 
Health-Related Quality of Life (HRQoL) focuses on the impact of health and disease on physical, psychological, 
social, and functional well-being. 
Domains of QoL (WHOQOL Framework) 
• 
Physical: Pain, energy, sleep, mobility, ADL 
• 
Psychological: Emotions, cognition, body image, self-esteem 
• 
Social relationships: Personal relationships, social support, sexual activity 
• 
Environmental: Safety, finances, transport, leisure 
• 
Level of independence: Mobility, communication, work capacity 
• 
Spiritual/religion: Personal beliefs, meaning of life

QALY Calculation 
QALY = Years of life × Utility weight (health state value 0–1) 
Example: Patient lives 10 years with moderate angina (utility 0.7): 
QALY = 10 × 0.7 = 7 QALYs 
Used in health technology assessment — NICE UK threshold: £20,000–30,000/QALY 
 
Q9. Enumerate IMR causes. Why is IMR considered a sensitive indicator of health? (2017) ★★ 
Definition 
Infant Mortality Rate (IMR) = (Deaths in infants <1 year / Live births in same year) × 1000 
India IMR: 28/1000 live births (SRS 2020) — down from 80 in 1990. Target: <20 by 2025 (NHM). 
Causes of Infant Mortality 
Neonatal deaths (0–28 days) — 67% of IMR: 
• 
Preterm birth complications (35%) 
• 
Birth asphyxia and trauma (25%) 
• 
Neonatal sepsis/infections (15%) 
• 
Congenital anomalies (5%) 
Post-neonatal deaths (29 days–<1 year) — 33% of IMR: 
• 
Pneumonia/ALRI (leading cause) 
• 
Diarrhoeal diseases 
• 
Malnutrition (underlying cause in 45% of child deaths) 
• 
Malaria, measles in endemic areas 
Why IMR is a Sensitive Health Indicator 
13. Reflects overall health system quality: Captures MCH services, immunization, nutrition, sanitation 
simultaneously 
14. Sensitive to socioeconomic conditions: Poverty, literacy, maternal education all directly impact IMR 
15. Rapidly responsive to interventions: Improvements in ANC, skilled delivery, immunization reflect quickly in 
IMR 
16. International comparability: Standardized definition enables cross-national comparison 
17. Equity marker: Wide urban-rural, state-wise variations in IMR reveal health inequities (Bihar 46 vs Kerala 
6 — SRS 2020) 
18. Policy target: Central to NHM, SDG 3.2 (U5MR <25/1000 LB by 2030)

Q1. Describe various levels of prevention and modes of intervention with examples. (2018, 
2015, 2014) ★★★★★ 
Introduction 
Prevention is the cornerstone of public health. Clark and Leavell (1965) described levels of prevention based on 
the natural history of disease and the host-agent-environment triad.
