Detailed Description 
1. PRIMORDIAL PREVENTION 
• 
Concept: Prevent emergence of risk factors before disease begins (Strasser, 1978) 
• 
Target: Entire population (healthy individuals), societal determinants 
• 
Modes: Policy, legislation, urban planning, taxation 
• 
Examples in India: 
◦ 
COTPA: Cigarette & Other Tobacco Products Act — restricts tobacco advertising 
◦ 
Swachh Bharat Mission: Sanitation to prevent diarrhoeal disease risk factors 
◦ 
Junk food taxation to prevent obesity 
◦ 
Building cycle lanes to promote physical activity 
2. PRIMARY PREVENTION 
A. Health Promotion: 
◦ 
Health education, nutrition promotion, maternal and child care 
◦ 
Adequate housing, recreation, genetic counselling 
B. Specific Protection: 
◦ 
Immunization (UIP: BCG, OPV, DPT, Measles, HPV) 
◦ 
Use of specific nutrients: Iodized salt (IDD), iron supplementation (anaemia) 
◦ 
Environmental sanitation: Safe water, food safety, vector control 
◦ 
Protection from occupational hazards: Mask, gloves, safety equipment 
◦ 
Chemoprophylaxis: Malaria — primaquine; TB — INH prophylaxis 
3. SECONDARY PREVENTION 
• 
A. Early Diagnosis and Prompt Treatment: 
46


--- PAGE 59 ---

swaDesh
◦ 
Objective: Halt progression, reduce severity, prevent complications 
◦ 
Methods: Screening (population/individual), case-finding 
◦ 
Screening tests: VIA for cervical cancer, mammography for breast cancer, CBE 
◦ 
Neonatal screening: TSH for congenital hypothyroidism, PKU 
◦ 
TB: DSTB — 2HRZE/4HR; drug sensitivity testing by CB-NAAT 
B. Disability Limitation: 
◦ 
Treat to prevent complications: DM — glucose control to prevent nephropathy 
◦ 
Adequate treatment to prevent disability — leprosy MDT to prevent nerve damage 
4. TERTIARY PREVENTION 
• 
A. Disability Limitation (advanced) 
• 
B. Rehabilitation: Restore maximum possible function 
• 
Medical rehabilitation: Dialysis, prosthetics, surgery 
• 
Psychological: Counselling, support groups 
• 
Social: Vocational training, employment support 
• 
Vocational rehabilitation: NIMH, National Leprosy Eradication Programme (NLEP) support 
• 
Example: Physiotherapy + vocational training for post-stroke disability 
Modes of Intervention — Additional 
Besides Leavell & Clark's levels, WHO adds: 
• 
Health promotion (Ottawa Charter 1986): Build healthy public policy, create supportive environments, 
develop personal skills, strengthen community action, reorient health services 
• 
Specific protection: Immunization, chemoprophylaxis, chemical protection 
• 
Health education: KAP modification at all levels 
 
Q2. Define natural history of disease. Apply levels of prevention to natural history with 
examples. (2023) ★★★★★★ 
Natural History of Disease 
Natural history of disease (NHD) refers to the progression of a disease process in an individual over time, in the 
absence of any therapeutic intervention. It encompasses the entire spectrum from initial exposure to an etiological 
agent to recovery, disability, or death. 
Stages of Natural History of Disease (Leavell & Clark Model) 
STAGE 1: Pre-pathogenesis Period (Before disease onset) 
• 
Interaction between host, agent, and environment occurs 
• 
No clinical signs; disease not yet established in host 
• 
Stimulus = etiological agent; host susceptibility determines outcome 
• 
Example: Person exposed to M. tuberculosis from a smear-positive contact 
STAGE 2: Pathogenesis Period (Disease established in host) 
a) Early pathogenesis (Subclinical/Presymptomatic): 
◦ 
Agent multiplies/spreads; pathological changes begin 
◦ 
No symptoms yet; pathological lesion present 
◦ 
Example: Primary TB complex developing in lung without symptoms 
b) Discernible early lesion: 
◦ 
Earliest detectable clinical/lab signs 
◦ 
Horizon of symptoms = point when symptoms first appear 
c) Advanced disease: 
47


--- PAGE 60 ---

swaDesh
◦ 
Full clinical disease with symptoms, signs, complications 
d) Outcome: 
◦ 
Recovery (complete/partial), Disability, Death, or Carrier state 
Application of Levels of Prevention — Tuberculosis (TB) Model 
Stage of NHD 
Level of Prevention 
Intervention 
Pre-pathogenesis (pre-
exposure) 
Primordial 
Nutrition, poverty reduction, housing 
improvement

disability assessment, vocational rehabilitation 
 
Application to Type 2 Diabetes — NHD Model 
• 
Pre-pathogenesis: Sedentary lifestyle, obesity, family history → Primordial (activity promotion, junk food 
tax) + Primary (lifestyle counselling, weight loss) 
• 
Subclinical (impaired fasting glucose): Secondary — OGTT screening, lifestyle intervention 
• 
Clinical DM: Secondary — metformin, insulin, HbA1c monitoring 
• 
Complications (retinopathy, nephropathy): Tertiary — laser therapy, dialysis, foot care 
 
Q3. How do you relate levels of prevention to natural history of disease? (Nov 2023) ★★★★★ 
Conceptual Bridge 
The natural history of disease and levels of prevention are inextricably linked in the Leavell & Clark (1965) model. 
Each stage of natural history corresponds to a specific level of prevention and mode of intervention. 
Key Concept — The Horizon of Symptoms: 
• 
Separates the 'iceberg' of subclinical disease from clinically apparent disease 
• 
Secondary prevention targets the subclinical phase (before the horizon) — most impactful 
• 
The 'iceberg phenomenon' shows most disease is subclinical — screening is essential

Q4. Describe the modes of intervention in the natural history of Type 2 Diabetes Mellitus. (Dec 
2024) ★NEW★ 
Natural History of T2DM 
T2DM has a prolonged natural history spanning 10–20 years from initial insulin resistance to full clinical disease 
and complications, offering multiple opportunities for preventive intervention. 
Stage 1: Pre-pathogenesis — Risk Factor Phase 
• 
Risk factors: Obesity (especially central), sedentary lifestyle, family history, ethnicity (South Asians at 
higher risk at lower BMI) 
• 
Host factors: Genetic predisposition, age, sex 
• 
Environmental: Dietary patterns, urbanization, stress 
• 
Primordial prevention: Policy — junk food taxation, urban planning for walkability, school nutrition 
programmes 
• 
Primary prevention: Individual — lifestyle modification (500 kcal/day deficit, 150 min/week moderate 
exercise = DPP trial: 58% risk reduction) 
Stage 2: Subclinical — Impaired Glucose Metabolism 
• 
Impaired Fasting Glucose (IFG): FPG 100–125 mg/dL 
• 
Impaired Glucose Tolerance (IGT): 2-hr OGTT 140–199 mg/dL 
• 
Prediabetes phase — no symptoms but metabolic derangement present 
• 
Secondary prevention (early diagnosis): NHM HWC screening protocol (opportunistic screening >30 yrs 
with risk factors) 
• 
Metformin for high-risk prediabetes (ADA recommendation) 
Stage 3: Clinical T2DM 
• 
Symptoms: Polydipsia, polyuria, polyphagia, fatigue, blurred vision 
• 
Diagnosis: FPG >126 mg/dL OR 2-hr OGTT >200 mg/dL OR HbA1c >6.5% 
• 
Secondary prevention (prompt treatment): MNT + oral hypoglycaemics + lifestyle 
• 
SMBG (self-monitoring blood glucose), HbA1c target <7% 
Stage 4: Complications — Disability Limitation 
Complication 
Intervention 
Level 
Diabetic retinopathy 
Annual ophthalmological review, laser 
photocoagulation 
Tertiary 
Diabetic nephropathy 
ACEI/ARB, dialysis, transplant 
Tertiary 
Diabetic neuropathy 
Gabapentin, foot care clinics 
Tertiary 
Diabetic foot 
Wound care, podiatry, amputation prevention 
Tertiary 
CVD 
Statins, anti-hypertensives, cardiac rehab 
Tertiary 
 
Q5. Discuss the role of primordial prevention in control of Non-communicable diseases. (2013) 
★★★★ 
Concept of Primordial Prevention 
Primordial prevention (Strasser, 1978; WHO 1998) aims to prevent the emergence and establishment of social, 
economic, and cultural patterns of living that are known to increase the risk of disease. It acts BEFORE risk 
factors develop — preventing the conditions that give rise to risk factors. 
Rationale for Primordial Prevention in NCDs 
• 
NCDs (CVD, diabetes, COPD, cancers) now account for 63% of global deaths (WHO 2023) 
49


--- PAGE 62 ---

swaDesh
• 
India: NCDs responsible for 66% of deaths — premature NCD deaths 4.5 million/year 
• 
Common NCD risk factors: Tobacco, physical inactivity, unhealthy diet, harmful alcohol use 
• 
Primordial prevention targets the upstream determinants — more cost-effective than treatment 
Modes of Primordial Prevention for NCDs 
1. Policy and Legislative Interventions: 
• 
COTPA (Cigarettes & Tobacco Products Act, 2003): Ban on tobacco advertising, smoking in public places 
• 
FSSAI regulations: Front-of-pack nutrition labelling, trans fat elimination, junk food standards 
• 
Motor vehicle safety laws: Seat belt, helmet mandates — prevent trauma 
• 
Alcohol policy: Liquor licensing restrictions, minimum legal drinking age 
• 
Building codes: Mandatory staircase access to reduce sedentary behaviour 
2. Environmental and Urban Planning: 
• 
Cycle lanes, pedestrian footpaths — Active transport infrastructure 
• 
Urban green spaces — Parks, exercise areas 
• 
Air quality management — NCAP (National Clean Air Programme) to reduce COPD risk 
• 
Safe water and sanitation — prevent infection that triggers NCD exacerbations 
3. Fiscal Measures: 
• 
Sugar-sweetened beverage tax (India GST on aerated drinks 28%) 
• 
Tobacco excise duties — price elasticity of demand reduces consumption 
• 
Subsidies on fruits, vegetables, whole grains 
4. Health Promotion and Education (Population Level): 
• 
School-based programmes: Healthy diet, physical activity habits before risk factors emerge 
• 
Media campaigns: Fit India Movement, Eat Right India, Poshan Maah 
• 
Community-level social norms change — denormalize tobacco, alcohol 
5. Intersectoral Action (HiAP — Health in All Policies): 
• 
Agriculture: Support production of healthier foods 
• 
Education: Integrate health literacy into school curriculum 
• 
Transport: Promote public transport, cycling infrastructure 
• 
Workplace: No smoking zones, healthy canteens, mental health support 
India-Specific Primordial Prevention Initiatives for NCDs

Q6. Write in detail about the Pre-pathogenic period with examples. (2015) ★★★★ 
Pre-Pathogenic Period — Definition 
50


--- PAGE 63 ---

swaDesh
The pre-pathogenic period refers to the phase of natural history of disease that occurs BEFORE the disease 
process begins in the host. During this stage, the interplay between the host, agent, and environment 
(epidemiological triad) creates conditions favorable for disease development, but the pathological process has 
NOT yet been established. 
Ecological Interactions in Pre-pathogenesis 
Epidemiological Triad: HOST ↔ AGENT ↔ ENVIRONMENT 
1. Host Factors: 
• 
Genetic: Sickle cell trait, BRCA1/2 mutation, HLA-B27 
• 
Age and sex: IMR higher in males; breast cancer higher in females 
• 
Nutritional status: Malnutrition → susceptibility to TB, measles 
• 
Immune status: Immunocompromised → opportunistic infections 
• 
Behaviour: Smoking, alcohol, promiscuity 
• 
Pre-existing disease: DM increases TB risk 3-fold 
2. Agent Factors: 
• 
Biological: Infectivity, pathogenicity, virulence, antigenicity 
• 
Chemical: Dose, route of entry, metabolic activation 
• 
Physical: Duration of exposure, intensity (radiation, noise) 
• 
Nutritional: Deficiency (vitamin A → xerophthalmia) or excess (fat → obesity) 
3. Environmental Factors: 
• 
Physical: Climate, season (malaria peaks in monsoon), geography 
• 
Biological: Vector density (Aedes mosquito), reservoir hosts 
• 
Social: Overcrowding (TB), poverty, education 
• 
Economic: Poverty → malnutrition → increased susceptibility 
Examples of Pre-pathogenic Period 
Example 1 — Tuberculosis: 
• 
Host: Malnutritioned adolescent, HIV co-infection 
• 
Agent: M. tuberculosis — high infectivity, moderate virulence 
• 
Environment: Overcrowded urban slum, poor ventilation 
• 
Pre-pathogenesis: Person breathes in droplet nuclei from smear-positive patient 
• 
Stage: Ecological phase — agent not yet established in host tissues 
• 
Prevention: BCG, ventilation, nutritional support (all PRIMARY prevention) 
Example 2 — Coronary Heart Disease (CHD): 
• 
Host: Middle-aged male, sedentary, family history of DM 
• 
Agent: Risk factors — hypercholesterolaemia, hypertension, diabetes 
• 
Environment: Urban lifestyle, junk food availability, work stress 
• 
Pre-pathogenesis: Risk factors accumulating silently over years before first MI 
• 
Prevention: Lifestyle modification, statin, BP control (PRIMARY + PRIMORDIAL) 
Example 3 — Cervical Cancer: 
• 
Host: Young woman, multiple sexual partners, low socioeconomic status 
• 
Agent: HPV type 16/18 — carcinogenic 
• 
Environment: Poor hygiene, lack of screening access 
• 
Pre-pathogenesis: HPV exposure before any cellular change 
• 
Prevention: HPV vaccine 9–14 yrs, safe sex education 
Significance of Pre-pathogenic Period for Public Health 
• 
The MOST cost-effective time to intervene — before disease is established 
51


--- PAGE 64 ---

swaDesh
• 
All primordial and primary prevention measures are applicable here 
• 
Identification of high-risk groups enables targeted prophylaxis 
• 
Genetic counselling can alter host susceptibility

below.) 
Additional Aspects 
Historical Context: 
• 
Described by Strasser (1978) as the 4th level of prevention — added after Leavell & Clark's original 3 
levels 
• 
Formally adopted by WHO in 1986 and reinforced in Ottawa Charter 
• 
Gained prominence in cardiovascular epidemiology — preventing risk factors for heart disease

Q8. Differentiate between control, elimination and eradication. (2014) ★★★ 
Definitions and Comparison 
1. Control 
• 
Definition: Reduction of disease incidence, prevalence, morbidity, or mortality to a locally acceptable level 
• 
Goal: Reduce disease burden; no specific endpoint — ongoing effort required 
• 
Agent: Remains in the environment/reservoir 
• 
Example: Malaria control in India — reduced cases but not eliminated 
• 
Methods: Vector control, chemoprophylaxis, case management 
2. Elimination 
• 
Definition: Reduction to zero new cases in a defined geographic area (but pathogen/agent may still exist 
elsewhere) 
• 
Requires sustained measures to prevent re-introduction 
• 
Two sub-types: 
◦ 
Elimination of disease: Zero cases in region (e.g., India eliminated polio 2014, neonatal tetanus) 
◦ 
Elimination of infection: Zero transmission in region 
• 
Examples in India: India eliminated guinea worm disease 2000, polio-free certification 2014 
3. Eradication 
• 
Definition: Permanent reduction to zero of worldwide incidence + extinction of agent — NO intervention 
needed thereafter 
• 
Most difficult — requires global action, highly specific tools (vaccine/control method) 
52


--- PAGE 65 ---

swaDesh
• 
Only ONE disease eradicated in history: Smallpox (1980, WHO) 
• 
Near-eradication: Guinea worm (dracunculiasis) — <15 cases globally (2023) 
Criterion 
Control 
Elimination 
Eradication 
Incidence 
Reduced 
Zero in region 
Zero globally 
Agent exists? 
Yes 
Yes (elsewhere) 
No (extinct) 
Interventions

Not needed

Malaria, TB 
Polio (India), NNT 
Smallpox 
 
India-specific examples: 
• 
Eradicated: Smallpox (1977 last case in India; global 1980) 
• 
Eliminated: Polio (India certified polio-free March 2014); Guinea worm 
• 
Elimination target: Leprosy (grade 2 disability <1/million by 2030 — NLEP target) 
• 
Controlled: Malaria, Kala-azar (target: elimination by 2023 — ongoing), Lymphatic filariasis

Public Health Significance of Man-Made Disasters & Bioterrorism

Mass casualties overwhelming health systems

Triage (French 'to sort') is the process of rapidly sorting casualties based on severity of injury and 
likelihood of survival, to allocate limited medical resources most effectively.

Applied Example: Cholera Outbreak Investigation
•
Case definition: Person with acute watery diarrhoea (3+ stools/day) ± vomiting in the affected

•

•
Hypothesis: Contaminated municipal water supply after flooding
•

•
Control: Chlorination of water supply, ORS distribution, chemoprophylaxis with doxycycline for 
contacts

6. EPIDEMIOLOGICAL TRIAD / WEB OF CAUSATION / ADVANCED 
MODEL
Frequency: 11 Times Asked | Priority: GRADE A
Q1. Describe the Ecological Concept of Disease Causation — Epidemiological 
Triad. (2022) ★★★★★
Ecological Concept of Disease Causation
Disease is not caused by a single factor; it results from a dynamic interaction between the host, agent, 
and environment — the three components of the Epidemiological Triad (also called the 
Epidemiological Triangle).
Components of the Epidemiological Triad
Component
Definition
Examples / Sub-types
HOST
The living organism (usually 
human) in which the agent 
causes disease
Demographic: Age, sex, race, occupation Biological: 
Immunity (innate/acquired), genetics, nutritional 
status, concurrent illness Behavioural: Diet, smoking, 
exercise, sexual behaviour
AGENT
A factor whose presence (or 
absence) is necessary for 
disease production
Biological: Bacteria, viruses, parasites, fungi (e.g., 
Mycobacterium tuberculosis) Chemical: Toxins, 
drugs, heavy metals (e.g., lead) Physical: Radiation, 
heat, trauma Nutritional: Deficiency (e.g., iodine → 
goitre) or excess (obesity)
ENVIRONMEN
T
All external conditions and 
influences affecting the host 
and agent
Physical: Climate, geography, temperature, water 
Biological: Vectors, reservoir hosts, sanitation Socio-
economic: Poverty, overcrowding, education, health 
services
The Triad — Equilibrium and Disease
•
In health: Host, Agent, Environment are in dynamic equilibrium (balance)
•
Disease occurs when balance is disrupted in favour of the agent (e.g., increased virulence, 
decreased host immunity, deteriorating environment)
•
Prevention targets can be identified for each vertex: host (vaccination), agent (antimicrobials), 
environment (sanitation)
Application — Tuberculosis (Epidemiological Triad)
Component
Factors
Agent
Mycobacterium tuberculosis — virulent, acid-fast bacillus, airborne droplet nuclei
Host
Malnutrition (↓ cell-mediated immunity), HIV co-infection, diabetes, 
overcrowding, age extremes, un-vaccinated (BCG)
Environment
Poverty, poor ventilation, overcrowded housing, lack of health services, migrant 
60


--- PAGE 73 ---

swaDesh
populations
Exam Tip: The Triad is most suitable for infectious diseases with a specific biological agent. For 
chronic non-communicable diseases, Web of Causation is more appropriate — be ready to state this 
distinction.
Q2. Discuss the Model of Web of Causation with Reference to CAD. (2015, 2011) 
★★★★★
Web of Causation — Brian MacMahon (1960)
Proposed by Brian MacMahon & Pugh (1960), the Web of Causation acknowledges that diseases 
(especially NCDs) result from a complex network of interacting causes rather than a single agent.
Key Concepts
•
Multiple causation: No single cause is sufficient or necessary for most diseases
•
Causal chain: Each cause itself has antecedent causes, creating a web-like network
•
Component cause: Any factor that contributes to the sufficient cause
•
Sufficient cause: Minimal combination of factors that inevitably produces disease
•
Necessary cause: A component present in every case (e.g., M. tuberculosis for TB)
Web of Causation for Coronary Artery Disease (CAD)
Level
Causal Factors
Distal / Societal causes
Urbanisation, Westernised lifestyle, Socioeconomic status, Cultural factors, 
Globalisation
Intermediate causes
Physical inactivity, High-fat diet, Tobacco use, Psychosocial stress, Alcohol
Proximal / Biological 
risk factors
Hypertension, Hyperlipidaemia (↑LDL, ↓HDL), Diabetes mellitus, Obesity 
(central), Metabolic syndrome
Genetic / Host factors
Family history, Genetic polymorphisms (e.g., APOE genotype), Age (♂>45 
yrs, ♀>55 yrs), Male sex
Pathological pathway
Endothelial injury → Atherosclerotic plaque → Plaque rupture → Thrombus 
→ MI / Angina
Advantages of Web of Causation
•
Suitable for multifactorial diseases (CAD, diabetes, cancer)
•
Identifies multiple intervention points — breaking any strand weakens the web
•
Explains why risk factor reduction (e.g., BP control) reduces disease even without removing the 
'root' cause
61


--- PAGE 74 ---

swaDesh
Limitations
•
Does not address socio-political root causes explicitly (unlike "Wheel of Causation")
•
Can be overly complex; difficult to quantify all strands
•
Lacks temporal sequencing of causes
Exam Tip: For NTRUHS: always draw a schematic web diagram conceptually in text — list factors at 
societal → intermediate → proximal → biological levels. Examiner expects structured hierarchy.
Q3. Explain Epidemiological Triad in Relation to COVID-19 Infection. (2022) 
★★★★★
Component
COVID-19 Specifics
AGENT — SARS-
CoV-2
RNA betacoronavirus; spike protein binds ACE-2 receptors; transmission: 
respiratory droplets, aerosols (airborne), fomites (lesser); incubation 2–14 
days (mean 5.1 days); multiple variants (Alpha, Delta, Omicron) with altered 
transmissibility & immune escape
HOST — Human
All ages susceptible (highest severity in elderly, immunocompromised, co-
morbidities: DM, HTN, CKD, obesity) Immunity: Natural infection or vaccine-
induced (BNT162b2, ChAdOx1, COVAXIN) Genetics: Blood group A — 
higher risk; O — lower risk Behaviour: Mask compliance, social distancing, 
hand hygiene
ENVIRONMENT
Physical: Cold, dry indoor air → prolonged aerosol viability; crowded enclosed 
spaces Biological: Animal reservoir (bats, pangolin); wet markets Socio-
economic: Urban density, poverty, healthcare access Health system: ICU 
capacity, ventilators, PPE availability
Disease Control Strategies Mapped to Triad
•
Agent control: Antiviral drugs (Remdesivir, Paxlovid), Disinfection, Decontamination
•
Host protection: COVID-19 vaccination (PM COVID-19 vaccine drive), mask use, early 
treatment, co-morbidity management
•
Environmental control: Ventilation, HEPA filtration, social distancing, lockdowns, travel 
restrictions (IHR 2005 PHEIC declared Jan 2020)
Exam Tip: COVID-19 demonstrated the importance of all three triad components simultaneously — 
highlighting why single-component interventions alone are insufficient.
Q4–Q5. Multifactorial Causation of Disease & Bradford Hill Criteria for Causality. 
(2021, 2022) ★★★★
Multifactorial Causation — Rothman's Sufficient-Component Cause Model
Kenneth Rothman (1976) proposed the "Pie" model: disease is caused when a sufficient cause is 
completed. Each sufficient cause is a 'pie' made up of component causes (pie slices). Removing any 
component prevents that sufficient cause from being completed.
62


--- PAGE 75 ---

swaDesh
Bradford Hill Criteria for Causality (1965) — 9 Criteria
Criterion
Description
Example (Smoking & Lung 
Cancer)
1. Strength of 
Association
High RR/OR → stronger causal 
inference
RR = 10–30 for heavy smokers vs 
non-smokers
2. Consistency
Finding replicated in different 
populations, times, by different 
researchers
Confirmed across 29+ countries in 
multiple studies
3. Specificity
One exposure → one specific 
disease (less important for NCDs)
Smoking specifically associated 
with squamous cell lung cancer
4. Temporality
Cause must precede effect — ONLY 
essential criterion
Smoking precedes cancer by 20–
30 years
5. Biological Gradient 
(Dose-Response)
↑ exposure → ↑ risk — supports 
causation
Pack-years of smoking linearly 
related to lung cancer risk
6. Plausibility
Biologically plausible mechanism 
known
Carcinogens in tobacco (PAHs, 
nitrosamines) → DNA mutation → 
oncogenesis
7. Coherence
Association consistent with known 
natural history and biology
Animal studies confirm tobacco 
carcinogenesis
8. Experiment
Reduction in exposure reduces 
disease risk
Smoking cessation → 50% 
reduction in lung cancer risk over 
10 years
9. Analogy
Similar cause-effect relationships 
already established
Radiation causing cancer is 
analogous
Exam Tip: Temporality is the ONLY sine qua non (essential) criterion among Bradford Hill's 9. Strength 
of association is the most important but not essential. Memorise this — it is a favourite examiner 
question.
Advanced Model / Triangle of Epidemiology (May 2025) — NEW
The Advanced Epidemiological Triangle expands the classic triad by adding a 4th component: the 
Social/Structural Environment (also called the 'super-environment'). This acknowledges that 
socioeconomic determinants (poverty, inequality, governance) operate above the classical environment 
to influence all three triad vertices simultaneously.
•
Social determinants (WHO Commission — Marmot 2008): income, education, employment, 
social support, early childhood development, structural violence
•
This model underpins the Social Determinants of Health (SDH) framework and the Health in All 
Policies approach
•
Relevant to: NHP 2017, SDG 3 (Good Health & Well-being), Ayushman Bharat, Pradhan Mantri 
Jan Arogya Yojana (PMJAY)

Health Effects of Air Pollution
•
Respiratory: COPD, asthma, lung cancer, acute respiratory infections (leading cause of under-5

•
Cardiovascular: Ischaemic heart disease, stroke (responsible for 25% of all IHD deaths

64

Neurological: Lead exposure → cognitive impairment in children
•

•
Pregnancy: Low birth weight, preterm birth, stillbirth
Indicators of Air Pollution
•
Air Quality Index (AQI) — India: 6 categories (Good → Severe); sub-indices for PM2.5, PM10, 
NO₂, SO₂, CO, O₃
•
Dust fall index — kg/km²/month
•
Sulphation rate — mg SO₃/100 cm²/day
•
Biological indicators: Lichen growth (sensitive to SO₂); absence indicates pollution
Control Measures
•
Legislative: Air (Prevention & Control of Pollution) Act 1981, Environment Protection Act 1986, 
National Clean Air Programme (NCAP) 2019
•
Vehicular: Bharat Stage (BS-VI) norms, odd-even traffic schemes, EV promotion (FAME II 
scheme)
•
Industrial: Stack emission standards, electrostatic precipitators, scrubbers, relocation of 
industries
•
Domestic: Pradhan Mantri Ujjwala Yojana (PMUY) — free LPG to BPL households replacing 
biomass → reduces indoor pollution
•
Agricultural: Ban on stubble burning, promotion of Happy Seeder technology
•
Urban planning: Green belts, tree plantation, cycle lanes
Exam Tip: NCAP 2019 target: 20–30% reduction in PM2.5 and PM10 concentrations by 2024 
(baseline: 2017). India has ~140 non-attainment cities — commonly asked in MCQs.
Q2. Define Safe Water. Elaborate Water-Related Diseases. (2018, 2014) ★★★★★
Definition of Safe Water
Safe/potable water is water that is free from pathogenic organisms, harmful chemicals, and excessive 
dissolved solids; it is pleasant to taste, smell, and appearance; and is available in adequate quantity 
(WHO). It should be free from pollution at the source, protected from contamination during distribution, 
and available at a reasonable cost.

Water Treatment Process — Brief Overview
8.
Storage & Sedimentation — large particles settle; sunlight UV kills some organisms
9.
Coagulation & Flocculation — Alum (Al₂SO₄) added → flocs form → colloidal particles removed
10. Sedimentation of flocs
11. Filtration — Slow sand filter (biological; removes 99.9% bacteria) or Rapid sand filter
12. Disinfection — Chlorination: residual free chlorine 0.5 mg/L at consumer end; contact time 30 
min; pH <8; Cl demand = Cl applied − residual
Exam Tip: Breakpoint chlorination: The point at which all chlorine demand is satisfied and free residual 
chlorine begins to appear — always mention in chlorination questions.
Q5. Green House Effect and Kyoto Protocol — Discuss with Implications for 
India. (2015, 2010, 2017) ★★★
Greenhouse Effect
The Greenhouse Effect is the trapping of heat in the Earth's atmosphere by greenhouse gases 
(GHGs), causing global warming. Solar radiation passes through the atmosphere, warms the Earth's 
surface; re-emitted infrared radiation is absorbed by GHGs and re-radiated downward.
Greenhouse Gas
Sources

Health Implications of Climate Change / Global Warming
•
Vector-borne diseases: Geographic expansion of malaria, dengue, chikungunya, Lyme disease 
as temperature rises
•
Extreme weather events: Heatwaves (heat stroke), floods (water-borne diseases, drowning), 
droughts (malnutrition, famine)
•
Air quality: Increased ground-level ozone; aggravated respiratory diseases
•
Food security: Reduced crop yields → undernutrition especially in South Asia
•
Sea-level rise: Displacement → refugee health crises, mental health impact
International Agreements

Limit global warming to <2°C (preferably 1.5°C) above

India's Position & Commitments
•

50% electricity from non-fossil sources by 2030

Energy Efficiency, Sustainable Habitat
•
India 3rd largest emitter of CO₂ globally (after China, USA) but per capita emissions far lower
Exam Tip: Kyoto vs Paris: Kyoto — top-down, legally binding for developed nations only. Paris — 
bottom-up, NDCs for all nations, voluntary. Examiners frequently ask this distinction.

Role of Family in Health Promotion
13. Health behaviours: Diet, exercise habits, tobacco/alcohol use — all shaped primarily within 
family
14. Immunisation: Family's awareness and acceptance determines child immunisation coverage
15. Antenatal care: Family support (especially mother-in-law) critical for ANC attendance in India
16. Mental health: Strong family ties protect against depression, suicidality; isolation increases risk
17. Elderly care: Geriatric health — joint family provides home-based care, reducing 
institutionalisation
18. Child development: Early stimulation, nutrition, attachment within family determines long-term 
health outcomes (1000 days concept)
Role of Family in Disease Causation
•
Genetic diseases: Haemophilia, sickle cell anaemia, thalassaemia — clustered in families
•
Infectious disease clustering: TB, typhoid, scabies, hepatitis — shared exposure within 
household
•
Consanguineous marriages: Common in South India (Andhra Pradesh, Telangana) — ↑ risk of 
autosomal recessive disorders
•
Family stressors: Domestic violence, poverty, broken families → ↑ mental illness, substance 
abuse, child maltreatment
•
Role modelling of unhealthy behaviours: Parents who smoke → children more likely to smoke
Exam Tip: Family is the fundamental unit of epidemiology — the family pack concept (Cassel, 1972) 
shows that social support from family modifies host resistance. Cite this for high-scoring answers.

Q2. Role of Family in Health and Disease. (Dec 2024) 
 
NEW
★★★★★★
★
(This question asks for a focused essay — cover both health-promoting and disease-causing roles of 
family, incorporating recent evidence.)
Introduction: The family is universally recognised as the most fundamental social unit and primary 
determinant of health. It acts simultaneously as a health-promoting institution (protective role) and a risk 
environment (disease-causing role), depending on its structure, function, and dynamics.
A. Protective / Health-Promoting Role
•
Biological: Breastfeeding (↓ infant morbidity & mortality), family planning (birth spacing improves 
maternal & child health)
•
Nutritional: Family food system — balanced diet, food security, home cooking vs processed 
food
•
Psychological: Emotional support buffers stress response (HPA axis); strong family attachment 
→ lower cortisol → better immune function
•
Care-seeking: Families identify illness early, facilitate health service utilisation, support 
adherence to treatment
•
Socialisation: Transmission of positive health behaviours — handwashing, dental hygiene, sleep 
habits
•
Economic safety net: Pooled family resources reduce catastrophic health expenditure
B. Disease-Causing Role
•
Shared exposure: Household TB transmission — secondary attack rate 10–15% in contacts
•
Genetic transmission: Autosomal recessive diseases (sickle cell, thalassaemia); X-linked 
(haemophilia); autosomal dominant (Huntington's disease, familial hypercholesterolaemia)
•
Consanguinity: South Indian communities — increases homozygosity → recessive disease 
expression
•
Domestic violence: IPV affects mental and physical health of women and children — GBV as a 
public health crisis (WHO 2021: 1 in 3 women experience physical/sexual violence)
•
Dysfunctional family dynamics: Child abuse, neglect → adverse childhood experiences (ACEs) 
→ lifelong health consequences (diabetes, depression, substance use — Felitti 1998 ACE 
study)
•
Problem family: Family with persistent dysfunction (alcoholism, psychiatric illness, domestic 
violence) — Park's definition
C. Family Medicine Approach
•
Family-centred care: ICMR recommends family physician as first point of contact under 
Comprehensive Primary Health Care
•
Family health index (FHI): Tool to assess overall family health status for targeted interventions
•
Health & Wellness Centres (HWCs) under Ayushman Bharat — deliver comprehensive care to 
family as a unit
Exam Tip: Dec 2024 is a recent paper — this is predicted to be a 15-mark essay. Open with definition, 
cover protective + disease role systematically, close with public health implications for India.

Q3. Problem Family and Broken Family — Discuss in Detail. (2022) ★★★★
Problem Family (Park's Definition)
A problem family is one that fails to maintain a standard of health and social behaviour acceptable 
to the community due to one or more of: mental deficiency, psychopathy, alcoholism, chronic ill-health, 
social incapacity, domestic violence, or persistent poverty.
Characteristics of a Problem Family
•
Persistent poverty and economic instability despite available support
•
High rates of illness — disproportionate health service utilisation
•
Child neglect, abuse, malnutrition, poor school attendance
•
Domestic violence and antisocial behaviour
•
Social isolation — excluded from community networks
•
Substance abuse — alcoholism, drug dependence as central features
Broken Family
A broken family is one that has lost its structural integrity due to absence of one or both parents, 
through: divorce/separation, death, desertion, imprisonment, long-term hospitalisation, or migration.
Health Consequences of Broken and Problem Families
Impact Area
Consequences
Child health
Malnutrition, recurrent infections, developmental delay, poor immunisation, 
higher infant mortality
Mental health
Depression, anxiety, conduct disorder, suicidal behaviour in children and 
adults; PTSD from domestic violence
Adolescent health
School dropout, early sexual debut, substance abuse, teenage pregnancy, 
juvenile delinquency
Maternal health
Unsafe abortion, poor ANC, postnatal depression
Elderly
Neglect, elder abuse, institutionalisation, increased morbidity and mortality 
(social isolation)
Public Health Interventions
•
Identification: ASHA workers / ANMs conduct household surveys to identify problem families for 
targeted support
•
Family welfare services: Integrated Child Development Services (ICDS), POSHAN Abhiyaan
•
Legal protection: Protection of Women from Domestic Violence Act 2005, Prohibition of Child 
Marriage Act 2006
•
Counselling services: Family counselling centres under National Mental Health Programme 
(NMHP)
•
Social support: Social welfare schemes — PM Awas Yojana, Aadhaar-linked cash transfers

•
Community-based rehabilitation: For substance abuse — de-addiction centres

TOPIC 8: ROLE OF FAMILY IN HEALTH AND DISEASE 
  Frequency: 9 Times Asked  |  High Priority Topic 
 
  Q1. Classify family. Broad functions of a family. Discuss its role in health and disease.  
★★★★★  (2022, 2021, 2019, 2018) 
 
DEFINITION OF FAMILY 
A family is a group of persons directly linked by kin connections, the adult members of which assume 
responsibility for caring for children. (Park's Textbook, 27th ed.) 
 
CLASSIFICATION OF FAMILY 
A. Based on Structure: 
• Nuclear Family: Husband, wife, and their unmarried children living together. Most common unit in urban 
India. 
• Joint/Extended Family: Three or more generations living together. Common in rural India; provides social 
support. 
• Three-generational Family: Grandparents, parents, and children. 
• Single-parent Family: Only one parent with children (due to divorce, death, or abandonment). 
• Reconstituted/Blended Family: Formed after remarriage. 
 
B. Based on Marriage Type: 
• Monogamous: One husband, one wife. 
• Polygamous: Polygyny (one man, multiple wives); Polyandry (one woman, multiple husbands). 
 
C. Based on Descent/Authority: 
• Patriarchal: Authority with father; Matriarchal: Authority with mother. 
• Patrilineal: Descent through father; Matrilineal: Descent through mother. 
 
BROAD FUNCTIONS OF FAMILY 
Function 
Details 
Biological / Reproductive 
Continuation of species; regulation of sexual behavior; rearing of 
children. 
Economic 
Unit of production and consumption; financial security for members. 
Psychological 
Emotional support, love, affection, sense of belonging; mental 
health. 
Educational / Socialization 
Primary socialization; transmission of culture, values, norms to 
children. 
Protective / Welfare 
Care during illness, old age; protection from external threats. 
Religious 
Transmission of religious beliefs, rituals, and practices. 
Recreational 
Leisure activities; family bonding. 
Status Conferring 
Assigns social position and role in society. 
 
75


--- PAGE 88 ---

swaDesh
GOLD MEDAL MODEL ANSWERS — MD SPM (Community Medicine) | NTRUHS    Topics 8, 9 & 10 | Paper I 
Page 3 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
ROLE OF FAMILY IN HEALTH 
Positive (Protective) Roles: 
• Health Promotion: Families instill healthy behaviors — diet, hygiene, exercise, avoidance of 
tobacco/alcohol. 
• Preventive Care: Ensures immunization, antenatal care, family planning utilization. 
• Early Diagnosis: Family members notice early signs of illness and facilitate healthcare seeking. 
• Rehabilitation: Post-illness recovery and compliance with treatment is supported by family. 
• Mental Health: Strong family bonds prevent depression, anxiety, substance abuse. 
• Nutrition: Family determines dietary practices, breastfeeding, complementary feeding. 
 
ROLE OF FAMILY IN DISEASE 
Negative (Disease-promoting) Roles: 
• Genetic Transmission: Hereditary diseases (hemophilia, thalassemia, diabetes) cluster in families. 
• Communicable Disease Spread: Common source of infection for TB, enteric fever, COVID-19, hepatitis. 
• Lifestyle Diseases: Unhealthy dietary habits, sedentary lifestyle, obesity shared within families. 
• Domestic Violence & Abuse: Physical, sexual, emotional abuse causing physical & mental morbidity. 
• Socioeconomic Deprivation: Low-income families face malnutrition, poor sanitation, limited health access. 
• Problem Family: Dysfunctional family — social isolation, neglect, substance abuse, mental illness, poor 
childcare. 
 
CONCEPT OF PROBLEM FAMILY (Park) 
A problem family is one that fails to maintain the health and social standards expected of a reasonable 
household. Characteristics: 
• Dirty, dilapidated home; poor housekeeping. 
• Evidence of social incompetence: persistent poverty, debt, truancy, juvenile delinquency. 
• Mental illness, alcoholism, or criminal history in family members. 
• Repeated hospitalizations; multiple chronic illnesses. 
 
  EXAM TIP: In 10-mark answers: Write Definition → Classification (with table) → Functions (table) → Role in 
Health (5–6 points) → Role in Disease (5–6 points) → Conclusion. This structure ensures all marks. 
 
  Q2. Role of family in health and disease.  ★★★★★  (Dec 2024 — NEW) 
(Refer to Q1 above for comprehensive answer. Additional points for newer exam:) 
 
ADDITIONAL CONTEMPORARY ASPECTS 
• COVID-19 Pandemic: Family clustering of infection; family as quarantine unit; pandemic-related domestic 
violence surge. 
• Nuclear Family Trend: Urbanization and nuclearization reduces extended family support → increased 
elderly neglect, increased institutional childcare need. 
• Digital Health Literacy: Tech-savvy younger family members act as health navigators for elderly members. 
• Family as Unit of Care: WHO emphasizes family-centered care in primary health care. 
 
  Q3. Problem family and broken family — discuss in detail.  ★★★★  (2022) 
 
PROBLEM FAMILY

GOLD MEDAL MODEL ANSWERS — MD SPM (Community Medicine) | NTRUHS    Topics 8, 9 & 10 | Paper I 
Page 4 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
Definition: A family that persistently fails to achieve a minimum standard of health, hygiene, and social 
functioning expected from a reasonable household (J.H. Hutchinson, 1948). 
 
Features of a Problem Family: 
• Substandard housing — dirty, overcrowded, poorly maintained home. 
• Inadequate nutrition — irregular meals, nutritional deficiencies in children. 
• Financial irresponsibility — chronic indebtedness despite adequate income. 
• Social incompetence — inability to use available social services. 
• Mental or emotional disorders in one or more family members. 
• History of alcoholism, drug abuse, or criminal behavior. 
• Repeated child neglect, truancy, juvenile delinquency. 
 
Health Implications of Problem Family: 
• High infant mortality rate and malnutrition in children. 
• Increased rates of communicable diseases due to poor hygiene. 
• Higher rates of mental illness, depression, anxiety. 
• Delayed immunization; non-compliance with treatment. 
• Increased domestic violence; child abuse and neglect. 
 
BROKEN FAMILY 
Definition: A family in which the marital bond is disrupted due to death of a spouse, divorce, desertion, or 
prolonged separation. 
 
Types of Broken Family: 
• Death of a spouse (widowhood). 
• Divorce or legal separation. 
• Desertion or abandonment. 
• Imprisonment of a parent. 
• Prolonged illness (physical/mental) causing functional separation. 
 
Health Consequences: 
• Children: Emotional insecurity, poor academic performance, behavioral problems, increased risk of 
substance abuse. 
• Single Parent: Overwork, financial stress, physical and mental exhaustion. 
• Elderly: Social isolation, depression, neglect. 
• Increased risk of child abuse and domestic violence. 
 
  EXAM TIP: Differentiate: Problem family = functional failure (dysfunctional but intact). Broken family = 
structural disruption (absent member). Examiners expect both compared.

  Q5. Effects of changes in family structure on maternal and child health care.  ★★★  (2022) 
 
CHANGING FAMILY STRUCTURES IN INDIA 
• Shift from joint to nuclear families due to urbanization, industrialization, migration. 
• Rise of single-parent families due to divorce, migration, conflict. 
• Increasing number of dual-income households. 
• Declining family size due to effective family planning. 
 
EFFECTS ON MATERNAL HEALTH 
• Reduced Antenatal Support: Nuclear families lack experienced elder women for guidance during 
pregnancy. 
• Decreased Breastfeeding: Working mothers in nuclear families have lower breastfeeding duration. 
• Delayed Healthcare Seeking: No elders to recognize warning signs; single mother may delay seeking care. 
• Mental Health: Increased postpartum depression in isolated nuclear mothers. 
• Domestic Violence: Isolated mothers in nuclear families are more vulnerable. 
 
EFFECTS ON CHILD HEALTH 
• Nutrition: Without extended family knowledge, complementary feeding practices may be suboptimal. 
• Immunization: Nuclear families dependent on healthcare system; no elder reminder system. 
• Childcare: Dual-income families use institutional childcare — exposes children to infections. 
• Psychological Development: Nuclear family children may have reduced social skills, increased screen time. 
• Positive: Smaller family size → more resources per child → better nutrition, education outcomes. 
 
  EXAM TIP: Structure as: Introduction → Changed family structures → Effect on Maternal Health (5 points) → 
Effect on Child Health (5 points) → Public Health Implications → Conclusion.

TOPIC 9: DIMENSIONS / DEFINITION OF HEALTH / POSITIVE 
HEALTH 
  Frequency: 8 Times Asked  |  High Priority Topic 
 
  Q1. Define health. Describe the various dimensions of health.  ★★★★★  (2022, 2019, 2018) 
 
DEFINITIONS OF HEALTH 
Source / Year 
Definition 
WHO, 1948 
Health is a state of complete physical, mental, and social well-being 
and not merely the absence of disease or infirmity. 
WHO, 1984 (revised) 
The extent to which an individual or group is able to realize 
aspirations and satisfy needs and to change or cope with the 
environment. Health is a resource for everyday life, not the object of 
living. 
Park (Social perspective) 
Health is a condition and quality of the organism which expresses 
an adequate functioning under given genetic and environmental 
conditions. 
Ecological view 
Health is a dynamic equilibrium between man and his environment. 
Biomedical model 
Health is the absence of disease — reductionist, organ-focused. 
 
DIMENSIONS OF HEALTH 
Health is multidimensional. The major dimensions are: 
 
1. Physical Dimension 
• Perfect functioning of the body; all organs working optimally. 
• Indicators: good complexion, body weight, posture; no disease or defect; high work capacity. 
 
2. Mental Dimension 
• Ability to think clearly, make rational decisions, cope with stress. 
• Absence of mental illness; positive self-image; emotional stability. 
• WHO definition of mental health: A state of well-being where individuals realize their potential, cope with 
normal stress, work productively, and contribute to community. 
 
3. Social Dimension 
• Ability to maintain satisfying interpersonal relationships; participate in social activities. 
• Social health implies harmony with family, community, and society. 
• Indicators: Role fulfillment, social adaptability, community participation. 
 
4. Spiritual Dimension 
• Recognized by WHO in 1984 as important dimension. 
• Sense of meaning, purpose, inner peace, and moral values in life. 
• Resilience during adversity; ethical behavior. 
 
5. Emotional Dimension 
79


--- PAGE 92 ---

swaDesh
GOLD MEDAL MODEL ANSWERS — MD SPM (Community Medicine) | NTRUHS    Topics 8, 9 & 10 | Paper I 
Page 7 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
• Ability to express emotions appropriately; handle joy, sorrow, fear, anger constructively. 
• Emotional health → better coping; reduces psychosomatic illnesses. 
 
6. Vocational Dimension 
• Ability to engage in productive, fulfilling work appropriate to one's abilities. 
• Work gives meaning, income, and social identity. 
 
7. Other Dimensions — as per Dunn's High Level Wellness: 
• Intellectual: Capacity for learning, using information. 
• Environmental: Living in clean, safe, supportive environment. 
• Cultural: Health is shaped by cultural values, beliefs, practices. 
 
SPECTRUM OF HEALTH (Leavell & Clark) 
Health exists on a spectrum from optimal health → sub-clinical → clinical disease → death. Public health aims 
to push the population toward the optimal end through primordial, primary, secondary, and tertiary prevention. 
 
  EXAM TIP: In 10-mark questions: write 2 WHO definitions + table of dimensions (7 dimensions, each with 
definition + indicator) + spectrum concept. Avoid listing only physical/mental/social — this fetches low marks. 
 
  Q2. Explain the concept of Positive Health.  ★★★★  (2014) 
 
CONCEPT OF POSITIVE HEALTH 
Positive health is not merely freedom from disease but attainment of a state of physical, mental, and social well-
being at the highest possible level. It represents an active pursuit of health, not a passive state. 
 
Key Features of Positive Health: 
• Optimal functioning of body and mind, not just absence of disease. 
• High physical fitness: strength, endurance, flexibility, coordination. 
• Emotional stability: coping ability, positive outlook, resilience. 
• Social integration: satisfying relationships; community participation. 
• Spiritual well-being: sense of purpose, meaning, and ethical living. 
 
DUNN'S HIGH LEVEL WELLNESS (1961) 
H.L. Dunn coined 'High Level Wellness' as an integrated method of functioning oriented toward maximizing the 
individual's potential within the environment. It has two axes: 
• Health axis: from peak wellness to death. 
• Environmental axis: from very favorable to very unfavorable. 
The goal is to be in the 'High Level Wellness Zone' — peak wellness in a favorable environment. 
 
POSITIVE HEALTH INDICATORS 
Domain 
Indicators 
Physical 
BMI 18.5–24.9; BP <120/80 mmHg; fasting blood glucose <100 
mg/dL; no anemia. 
Mental 
Absence of mental illness; GHQ score; resilience; positive affect.

Domain 
Indicators 
Social 
Social support networks; role satisfaction; participation in 
community. 
Functional 
Work capacity; activities of daily living; quality of life (QOL) 
measures. 
 
POSITIVE HEALTH vs BIOMEDICAL MODEL 
Biomedical model focuses on disease diagnosis and treatment (curative). Positive health model focuses on 
enhancing well-being (promotive). Modern public health has shifted from disease prevention to health 
promotion (Ottawa Charter, 1986). 
 
  EXAM TIP: Link Positive Health to Ottawa Charter 1986 for Health Promotion: 5 action areas — Build healthy 
public policy, Create supportive environments, Strengthen community action, Develop personal skills, Reorient 
health services. 
 
  Q3. What impact does Industrialization and Urbanization have on health?  ★★★★  (May 2024 — 
NEW) 
 
INDUSTRIALIZATION AND HEALTH 
Adverse Effects: 
• Occupational Hazards: Pneumoconiosis, silicosis, byssinosis, noise-induced hearing loss, chemical toxicity. 
• Environmental Pollution: Air, water, soil pollution from industrial effluents → respiratory diseases, cancers. 
• Lifestyle Changes: Sedentary work, stress, shift work → cardiovascular disease, diabetes, mental illness. 
• Child Labor & Exploitation: Child labor in industries → injury, malnutrition, denied education. 
• Social Disruption: Migration; weakening of family bonds; urban slums. 
 
Positive Effects: 
• Economic Growth: Increased income → better nutrition, housing, healthcare access. 
• Medical Technology: Industrial development drives pharmaceutical and diagnostic advances. 
• Infrastructure: Roads, electricity, sanitation improve with industrialization. 
 
URBANIZATION AND HEALTH 
Adverse Effects: 
• Slum Formation: 40–50% of urban population in India lives in slums — overcrowding, poor sanitation. 
• Water & Sanitation: Inadequate safe water → diarrhea, typhoid, cholera. 
• Air Pollution: Vehicular and industrial emissions → ARI, COPD, asthma, lung cancer. 
• Urban Heat Islands: Higher temperatures → heat stress, vector breeding. 
• Mental Health: Isolation, anonymity, poverty, violence → depression, anxiety, substance abuse. 
• Non-Communicable Diseases: Sedentary lifestyle, fast food, stress → hypertension, diabetes, obesity. 
• Road Traffic Accidents: Increased motorization → leading cause of trauma in urban India. 
 
Positive Effects: 
• Better access to hospitals, specialists, diagnostic services. 
• Higher female literacy and employment → better reproductive health. 
• Improved water supply and sanitation systems in planned urban areas.

PUBLIC HEALTH RESPONSE 
• National Urban Health Mission (NUHM): Addresses healthcare needs of urban poor. 
• Smart Cities Mission: Promotes healthy urban environments. 
• Pollution Control Boards: Regulate industrial and vehicular emissions. 
 
  EXAM TIP: This NEW question (May 2024) — structure as: Introduction → Industrialization adverse/positive 
→ Urbanization adverse/positive → SDG linkage → Conclusion. Mentioning NUHM and NCAP (National Clean 
Air Programme) adds marks. 
 
  Q4. Give an account of changing concepts in Public Health.  ★★★  (2018, 2015) 
 
The concept of public health has evolved through several phases: 
 
Era / Period 
Concept & Focus 
Miasma Theory (pre-1850) 
Disease caused by 'bad air' (miasma). Focus on environmental 
sanitation — drainage, sewage. 
Germ Theory (1850–1900) 
Pasteur, Koch — specific microorganisms cause specific diseases. 
Focus on infection control, vaccines. 
Epidemiological Triad (early 
20th c.) 
Host–Agent–Environment interaction. Multifactorial causation 
recognized. 
Social Medicine (1940s–50s) 
Health determined by social and economic factors. Virchow: 
'Medicine is a social science.' 
Biomedical Model (1950s–70s) 
Dominant curative model; organ/disease-focused; hospital-centered 
care. 
Comprehensive PHC (1978) 
Alma Ata Declaration: 'Health for All by 2000.' Primary Health Care 
as cornerstone. 
Health Promotion (1986) 
Ottawa Charter: Health as resource for everyday life. 
Empowerment, policy, environment. 
Sustainable Development 
(2000s–) 
MDGs → SDGs. Health as integral to development. SDG 3: Good 
Health and Well-being. 
Universal Health Coverage 
(UHC) 
Ensuring all people have access to quality health services without 
financial hardship. 
Planetary Health (2020s) 
One Health, climate change, antimicrobial resistance as health 
threats. 
 
  EXAM TIP: Conclude with: 'The concept has evolved from a negative (absence of disease) to a positive 
(optimal well-being), from individual to community, from biomedical to socioecological model.' This theoretical 
framing earns distinction marks. 
 
  Q5. Discuss — 'Health is not Stethoscope and Pills'.  ★★★  (2011)

Page 10 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
This statement critiques the biomedical reductionist model of health, which equates health with clinical 
diagnosis (stethoscope) and pharmacological treatment (pills). 
 
LIMITATIONS OF THE BIOMEDICAL MODEL 
• Focuses on disease rather than health promotion. 
• Neglects social, psychological, environmental determinants of health. 
• Hospitalo-centric and curative in orientation — addresses only 20% of health burden. 
• Fails to address root causes: poverty, illiteracy, malnutrition, poor sanitation. 
 
WHAT HEALTH TRULY REQUIRES 
• Social Determinants: Income, education, employment, housing, food security. 
• Primary Prevention: Immunization, sanitation, safe water, health education. 
• Community Participation: Empowered communities take ownership of health. 
• Intersectoral Action: Agriculture, education, infrastructure, finance — all impact health. 
• Mental & Spiritual Well-being: Not addressed by any pill or stethoscope. 
 
McKeown Thesis: The major decline in infectious disease mortality in England (18th–19th century) occurred 
BEFORE the introduction of vaccines and antibiotics — attributed to better nutrition, sanitation, and living 
conditions. This proves health is more than medicine. 
 
  EXAM TIP: Strong answers quote McKeown Thesis + Virchow ('Medicine is social science; politics is nothing 
but medicine on a large scale') + WHO 1984 revised definition. These are gold medal differentiators.

TOPIC 10: COLD CHAIN / IMMUNIZATION / VACCINES / AEFI / 
SURVEILLANCE 
  Frequency: 9 Times Asked  |  High Priority Topic 
 
  Q1. Define cold chain. Describe cold chain equipment and maintenance. What is AEFI?  
★★★★★  (2022, 2021, 2018) 
 
DEFINITION: COLD CHAIN 
Cold chain is the system of storage and transport of vaccines at recommended temperatures (between +2°C 
and +8°C for most vaccines; -15°C to -25°C for OPV) from the point of manufacture to the point of 
administration, to maintain their potency and efficacy. 
 
WHY COLD CHAIN IS CRITICAL 
• Vaccines are heat-labile biological products — exposure to heat irreversibly destroys potency. 
• Some vaccines (OPV) are also sensitive to freezing — freeze-sensitive vaccines include DPT, TT, Hep-B, 
DT. 
• Failure of cold chain is a major cause of vaccine failure and re-emergence of vaccine-preventable diseases. 
 
COLD CHAIN EQUIPMENT AT EACH LEVEL 
Level 
Equipment 
National / State 
Cold rooms (+2°C to +8°C) and Frozen rooms (-15°C to -25°C). 
Capacity: months of supply. 
Regional / District 
Deep Freeze (-15°C to -25°C) for OPV; Ice-Lined Refrigerators 
(ILR) for other vaccines. Refrigerated vans for transport. 
CHC / PHC Level 
ILR (Ice-Lined Refrigerator) — maintains temp even during power 
failure for 6–8 hrs. Deep freezer for OPV. 
Sub-centre / Village 
Vaccine Carrier (4 icepacks; maintains 4°C for 12 hrs). Cold Box 
(20–22 icepacks; up to 48–72 hrs). 
Health Worker Level 
Vaccine Carrier with icepacks. Hub cutter for syringe destruction. 
 
ICE-LINED REFRIGERATOR (ILR) — KEY FACTS 
• Lined with ice-water mixture along the walls. 
• Temperature maintained at +2°C to +8°C. 
• Maintains cold for 6–8 hours after power failure — critical for power-deficit areas. 
• Top-opening design prevents cold air escape when door opened. 
 
VACCINE VIAL MONITOR (VVM) 
A small adhesive label on vaccine vial with a heat-sensitive inner square: 
• Stage 1: Inner square lighter than outer ring — USABLE. 
• Stage 2: Inner square = outer ring colour — USABLE but use first. 
• Stage 3: Inner square darker than outer ring — DO NOT USE. 
• Stage 4: Inner square very dark — DISCARD immediately. 
84


--- PAGE 97 ---

swaDesh
GOLD MEDAL MODEL ANSWERS — MD SPM (Community Medicine) | NTRUHS    Topics 8, 9 & 10 | Paper I 
Page 12 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
 
COLD CHAIN MAINTENANCE 
• Daily: Check temperature twice daily; record in temperature log chart; ensure proper stacking. 
• Weekly: Defrost ILR when ice layer >0.5 cm (reduces efficiency). 
• Monthly: Stock inventory, expiry date check, VVM inspection. 
• Yearly: Annual maintenance contract (AMC) for cold chain equipment. 
• General: No vaccines in door shelves; use basket/wire rack; cold box with icepacks for transport; FIFO 
(First In First Out) principle. 
 
WHAT IS AEFI? 
AEFI = Adverse Event Following Immunization. 
Definition: Any untoward medical occurrence which follows immunization and which does not necessarily have 
a causal relationship with the usage of the vaccine. (WHO, 1999 — Glossary of Vaccine Safety Terminology.) 
 
CLASSIFICATION OF AEFI (WHO 2012 revised) 
Category 
Description / Examples 
Vaccine product-related 
Caused by intrinsic properties of vaccine. Vaccine-associated 
paralytic polio (VAPP) from OPV. 
Vaccine quality defect-related 
Improper manufacture — clumping, wrong concentration. Sterility 
failure. 
Immunization error-related 
(programmatic) 
Wrong dose, site, technique; contamination; reconstitution error; 
cold chain failure. 
Immunization anxiety-related 
Vasovagal syncope, hyperventilation. Not caused by vaccine itself. 
Coincidental 
Temporal association only; would have occurred regardless of 
vaccination. SIDS following vaccine. 
 
COMMON AEFIs AND MANAGEMENT 
AEFI 
Management 
Local reaction (pain, swelling, 
redness) 
Cold compress; analgesics; usually self-limiting. 
Fever post-immunization 
Paracetamol; monitor; usually <38.5°C and resolves in 24–48 hrs. 
Anaphylaxis 
Adrenaline 1:1000 IM (0.01 mg/kg); IV fluids; hospitalize. 
Persistent crying (>3 hrs) — 
DPT 
Monitor; usually resolves; next dose use DT. 
Febrile convulsion 
Antipyretics; if recurrent — consult neurologist. 
Vaccine-Associated Paralytic 
Polio 
Notify; shift to IPV; supportive rehabilitation. 
 
  EXAM TIP: AEFI classification (5 categories per WHO 2012) is frequently asked. Learn: Vaccine product → 
Quality defect → Immunization error → Anxiety-related → Coincidental. Use this order in exams for maximum 
marks.

  Q3. Surveillance of AEFI — describe in detail.  ★★★★★  (May 2025 — NEW) 
 
OBJECTIVES OF AEFI SURVEILLANCE 
• Detect and investigate AEFIs promptly to identify cause (vaccine, program, or coincidental). 
• Ensure vaccine safety and maintain public confidence in immunization. 
• Identify immunization errors and strengthen program quality. 
• Provide data for regulatory decisions on vaccine licensing. 
 
AEFI SURVEILLANCE SYSTEM IN INDIA 
India follows the WHO-recommended AEFI Surveillance framework, operational under the Universal 
Immunization Programme (UIP) and overseen by MoHFW. 
 
Key Components: 
• Case Detection: Health workers, caregivers, clinicians report AEFIs. 
• Reporting: Online reporting through Co-WIN portal and AEFI reporting forms. 
• Investigation: District AEFI Committee investigates serious/cluster AEFIs. 
• Causality Assessment: State AEFI Committee and National AEFI Committee. 
• Feedback & Action: Findings shared back to field; corrective action taken. 
 
AEFI REPORTING CRITERIA (Serious AEFIs must be reported within 24–48 hrs) 
Category 
Report Timeline 
Serious AEFI (hospitalization, 
death, life-threatening) 
Within 24 hours to district/state. 
Cluster AEFI (≥2 cases same 
session/batch) 
Immediate — same day. 
Non-serious AEFI (local 
reactions, minor fever) 
Within 7 days, in monthly tally. 
 
AEFI INVESTIGATION PROCESS 
1. Case Identification: History, clinical examination, onset timing after vaccination. 
2. Lab Investigation: Where needed — blood culture, CSF, histopathology. 
3. Causality Assessment: Using WHO 2012 classification algorithm. 
4. Report Preparation: AEFI investigation form completed; batch number, storage checked. 
5. Corrective Action: If immunization error found → training, supervision, cold chain review. 
6. Regulatory Report: Pharmacovigilance — ADR reporting to CDSCO (Central Drugs Standard Control 
Organisation). 
 
CAUSALITY ASSESSMENT — WHO 2012 Algorithm 
Step 1: Characterize the event (clinical diagnosis, timeline). 
Step 2: Review evidence (biologic plausibility, similar cases in literature). 
Step 3: Classify: 
Classification 
Meaning 
Consistent causal association 
Evidence supports vaccine → AEFI.

Classification 
Meaning 
Inconsistent causal association 
(coincidental) 
Evidence favors another cause. 
Indeterminate 
Evidence insufficient to attribute or deny causal link. 
Unclassifiable 
Insufficient evidence to classify. 
 
NATIONAL LEVEL — AEFI COMMITTEE STRUCTURE 
• Health Worker Level: Reports AEFI; provides first aid. 
• District AEFI Committee: Chaired by CMHO; investigates serious AEFIs; submits report to state. 
• State AEFI Committee: Expert committee; causality assessment for serious AEFIs. 
• National AEFI Committee (NAEFI): Ministry level; reviews national trends; advises regulatory action. 
 
PHARMACOVIGILANCE LINKAGE 
• AEFI data feeds into Pharmacovigilance Programme of India (PvPI) under CDSCO. 
• Adverse Drug Reaction (ADR) Monitoring Centres across India collect vaccine safety data. 
• WHO Collaborating Centre for Adverse Reaction Monitoring: Uppsala Monitoring Centre (UMC), Sweden. 
 
  EXAM TIP: NEW question (May 2025) — examiners expect: Objectives → Components of surveillance → 
Reporting criteria (with timeline table) → Investigation process (numbered) → Causality assessment (WHO 
2012) → Committee structure → Pharmacovigilance. This comprehensive structure earns gold medal marks. 
 
  Q4. Describe principles of Immunization. What diseases have been eradicated and 
eliminated?  ★★★★  (2021) 
 
PRINCIPLES OF IMMUNIZATION 
A. Immunological Principles: 
• Primary Response: First exposure → lag phase → antibody production (IgM first, then IgG). Slow but 
establishes immunological memory. 
• Secondary (Anamnestic) Response: Re-exposure → rapid, large, sustained antibody production (IgG). 
Basis of booster doses. 
• Herd Immunity: When a critical proportion of population is immune, even non-immune individuals are 
protected. Threshold varies by R0 of disease. 
Herd immunity threshold = 1 − (1/R0). For measles (R0=12–18): ~92–95% coverage needed. 
 
B. Practical Principles: 
• Age: Given at optimal immunological age — not too young (maternal antibodies interfere) or too late 
(disease risk window missed). 
• Schedule: Multiple doses needed for some vaccines (e.g., OPV) to ensure adequate response despite 
interference. 
• Route and Dose: Oral (OPV), Intradermal (BCG), Intramuscular (DPT, Hep B), Subcutaneous (MR). Wrong 
route → failure or adverse reaction. 
• Cold Chain: Essential for heat-labile vaccines to maintain potency. 
• Simultaneous Administration: Multiple vaccines at same visit do not interfere — recommended by WHO. 
 
ERADICATED DISEASES 
88

Page 16 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
Disease 
Year Eradicated / Details 
Smallpox 
Globally eradicated in 1980. Last natural case in Somalia, 1977. 
Certified by WHO. Only human disease fully eradicated. 
Rinderpest 
Animal disease. Eradicated in 2011. Relevant to One Health 
concept. 
 
ELIMINATED / NEAR-ELIMINATION 
Disease

India certified polio-free in 2014. Globally — only WPV1 in Pakistan 
and Afghanistan as of 2025.

ongoing.

Not yet eliminated — India targets elimination by 2026. 
 
  EXAM TIP: Distinguish: Eradication (global, permanent, no intervention needed) vs Elimination (regional, 
ongoing surveillance needed) vs Control (reduction in incidence). Smallpox = only eradicated human disease. 
This distinction always gets extra marks. 
 
  Q5. Immunization in Primary Health Care.  ★★★  (2015) 
 
PRIMARY HEALTH CARE AND IMMUNIZATION 
Alma Ata Declaration (1978) identified immunization against the major infectious diseases as one of the 8 
essential components of PHC. UIP (Universal Immunization Programme) is the backbone of immunization 
delivery through PHC in India. 
 
UIP — STRUCTURE AND DELIVERY 
• Launched: 1985 (Expanded Programme on Immunization started 1978 by WHO). 
• Coverage target: >90% for all antigens in all districts. 
• Delivery points: Sub-centres, PHCs, outreach sessions, Urban Health Posts. 
• Healthcare worker: ANM (Auxiliary Nurse Midwife) — primary vaccinator at village level. 
 
KEY STRATEGIES FOR PHC-BASED IMMUNIZATION 
• Routine Immunization: Fixed-site and outreach sessions at sub-centre level; ANM-led. 
• Pulse Polio Immunization (PPI): Mass campaigns on National Immunization Days (NIDs) — house-to-
house follow-up. 
• Mission Indradhanush: Launched 2014; targets children missed by routine immunization; >90% coverage 
goal. 
• Intensified Mission Indradhanush (IMI 5.0): Ongoing; focuses on zero-dose children and underserved 
populations. 
89


--- PAGE 102 ---

swaDesh
GOLD MEDAL MODEL ANSWERS — MD SPM (Community Medicine) | NTRUHS    Topics 8, 9 & 10 | Paper I 
Page 17 of 18  |  For Academic Use Only 
• Electronic Vaccine Intelligence Network (eVIN): Real-time cold chain monitoring and vaccine stock 
management. 
• Co-WIN Platform: Digital vaccine tracking, beneficiary management (originally for COVID-19; now 
expanded). 
 
CHALLENGES IN PHC IMMUNIZATION 
• Cold chain breaks in power-deficit rural areas. 
• Vaccine hesitancy — misinformation, cultural beliefs. 
• Hard-to-reach populations: Tribal, migrant, urban slum dwellers. 
• Shortage of ANMs; high absenteeism. 
• Drop-out rates between doses (DPT1 to DPT3). 
 
INDICATORS OF IMMUNIZATION COVERAGE 
Indicator 
Formula / Definition 
Immunization Coverage 
(Vaccinated / Target) × 100. Target >90%. 
Drop-out Rate 
[(DPT1 − DPT3) / DPT1] × 100. Target <10%. 
Effective Coverage 
Coverage × Vaccine Efficacy. 
Zero-dose children 
Children who have not received even one dose of any routine 
vaccine. 
 
  EXAM TIP: Mentioning Mission Indradhanush, eVIN, and zero-dose children in this answer differentiates 
distinction from pass answers. Conclude: 'Immunization is the most cost-effective public health intervention — 
$1 invested yields $44 in returns (WHO, 2019).'

  TOPIC 11: SOCIAL / CULTURAL FACTORS IN HEALTH 
(incl. Acculturation) 
Grade A | Asked 9 Times | Highest Priority 
  Q1. Role of Social and Cultural Factors in Health and Disease  ★★★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Asked in 2023, 2018, 2015, 2011 — Master this answer thoroughly. 
 
◆ INTRODUCTION 
Health is determined not only by biological factors but also by a complex interplay of social and cultural 
determinants. The WHO defines health as a complete state of physical, mental, and social well-being. 
Social and cultural factors constitute major determinants that profoundly influence patterns of health and 
disease in communities. 
◆ SOCIAL FACTORS AFFECTING HEALTH 
1. Socioeconomic Status (SES) 
SES is one of the strongest determinants of health. It encompasses income, education, and occupation. 
Low SES is associated with: 
● Higher prevalence of infectious diseases (TB, malaria, cholera) 
● Poor nutrition and higher rates of malnutrition 
● Limited access to healthcare services 
● Increased exposure to environmental hazards 
● Higher infant and maternal mortality rates 
2. Education 
Education directly influences health literacy, health-seeking behavior, and disease prevention: 
● Educated mothers have lower rates of infant mortality 
● Higher education correlates with better hygiene practices 
● Education enables understanding of health information and compliance with treatment 
92


--- PAGE 105 ---

swaDesh
3. Occupation and Working Conditions 
Occupational factors influence both physical and mental health: 
● Hazardous occupations: Silicosis (miners), Byssinosis (cotton workers), Mesothelioma 
(asbestos workers) 
● Psychosocial stress in high-pressure occupations leads to hypertension and cardiovascular 
diseases 
● Agricultural workers exposed to pesticides face neurotoxicity risks 
4. Housing and Living Conditions 
● Overcrowding facilitates transmission of respiratory diseases (TB, influenza) 
● Poor sanitation increases diarrheal diseases 
● Indoor air pollution from biomass fuels causes COPD and respiratory infections 
● Lack of safe drinking water leads to waterborne diseases 
5. Social Support and Networks 
● Strong social networks act as protective factors against mental illness 
● Social isolation increases risk of depression, cardiovascular disease, and premature death 
● Community cohesion improves disaster preparedness and health outcomes 
6. Gender and Inequality 
● Women face gender-based discrimination affecting access to healthcare 
● Gender inequality leads to nutritional neglect of girl children 
● Female genital mutilation (FGM) practiced in certain societies causes severe health 
complications 
 
◆ CULTURAL FACTORS AFFECTING HEALTH 
1. Food Habits and Dietary Practices 
Cultural beliefs profoundly influence dietary patterns: 
● Vegetarianism (practiced in many Hindu communities): May lead to iron, B12, zinc deficiency 
● Halal and Kosher dietary restrictions may limit access to certain nutrients 
● Festivals with feasting: Contribute to obesity and metabolic syndrome 
● Taboo foods during pregnancy (e.g., avoiding eggs, meat in some tribes): Can cause maternal 
malnutrition 
● Colostrum discarding in many rural communities denies newborns passive immunity 
2. Religious Beliefs and Practices 
● Blood transfusion refusal (Jehovah's Witnesses): Can be life-threatening 
● Faith healing over modern medicine: Delays treatment of serious conditions 
● Pilgrimage gatherings (Kumbh Mela, Hajj): Can facilitate mass disease outbreaks (cholera, 
meningitis) 
● Cow urine consumption and other religious remedies may cause harm 
● Fasting during Ramadan / Navratri: Can affect diabetic control 
3. Traditional Medicine and Folk Beliefs 
● Preference for traditional healers (Vaids, Hakims) over allopathic doctors delays definitive 
treatment 
93


--- PAGE 106 ---

swaDesh
● Use of certain herbs (e.g., Aristolochia): Can cause nephropathy 
● Heavy metal-containing Ayurvedic preparations: Risk of lead/mercury toxicity 
4. Marriage Practices 
● Consanguineous marriages (cousin marriages): Increase risk of autosomal recessive genetic 
disorders (thalassemia, sickle cell anemia) 
● Child marriage: Associated with early pregnancy, maternal and neonatal complications 
● Polygamy: Increases risk of STIs including HIV/AIDS 
5. Hygiene and Sanitation Practices 
● Open defecation practices (prevalent in rural India): Cause fecal-oral transmission of diseases 
● Use of shared utensils: Spreads hepatitis A, typhoid 
● Non-use of latrines due to cultural habits: Perpetuates soil-transmitted helminthiasis 
6. Birth Practices 
● Home deliveries by untrained dais: Lead to neonatal tetanus, birth injuries 
● Applying cow dung on umbilical stump: Causes neonatal tetanus 
● Early introduction of solids: Can cause diarrhea and malnutrition in infants 
7. Death and Burial Practices 
● Open-air cremation near water sources: Can contaminate drinking water 
● Communal burial practices during epidemics: Can spread diseases if infected bodies are not 
handled properly 
 
◆ MECHANISMS BY WHICH SOCIAL/CULTURAL FACTORS INFLUENCE HEALTH

◆ CONTROL MEASURES 
● Health education targeting cultural myths and misconceptions 
● Involvement of community leaders, religious heads, and traditional healers in health programs

  Q2. Culture, Social Structure & Community Health  ★★★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Dec 2024 NEW — Focus on definitions + structure with examples. 
 
◆ DEFINITION OF KEY TERMS 
Culture: The integrated pattern of human knowledge, beliefs, behaviors, arts, morals, laws, customs, 
and habits acquired and transmitted by members of a society. (E.B. Tylor, 1871) 
Social Structure: The organized pattern of social relationships and social institutions that together 
compose society — including family, caste, religion, class, and gender. 
Community: A group of people living in the same locality with shared interests, values, and 
governance. 
 
◆ COMPONENTS OF SOCIAL STRUCTURE RELEVANT TO HEALTH 
A. Family Structure 
● Nuclear family: More privacy, better child spacing; but less support during illness 
● Joint/extended family: Better support system but overcrowding risk; more disease transmission 
● Matriarchal vs. Patriarchal families: Affect decision-making for healthcare 
B. Caste System 
● Lower castes historically denied access to health resources 
● Caste-based discrimination affects mental health of marginalized groups 
● Scheduled caste communities have higher rates of malnutrition and poverty 
C. Religion and Community 
● Religious affiliation affects dietary practices, birth rates, and disease patterns 
● Community identity influences vaccine acceptance and health-seeking behavior 
D. Social Class 
● Lower social class = higher disease burden (inverse care law — Julian Tudor Hart) 
● Social class determines living conditions, nutrition, and access to medical care 
 
◆ HOW SOCIAL STRUCTURE AFFECTS HEALTH — KEY PATHWAYS

◆ CULTURE AND ITS INFLUENCE ON HEALTH 
● Cultural norms define what is considered 'healthy' or 'sick' — affecting illness behavior 
● Culture determines healthcare-seeking patterns (traditional vs. modern medicine) 
● Cultural practices can promote health (yoga, meditation, balanced diets) or harm health 
(tobacco chewing, purdah system reducing female mobility) 
● Stigma around mental illness, HIV/AIDS — deeply rooted in cultural beliefs — hinders treatment 
◆ IMPLICATIONS FOR PUBLIC HEALTH

● Community participation is essential for sustained behavioral change 
● Social structure should be mapped during needs assessment of communities 
● Laws must address structural inequities (Reservation system, PESA Act for tribals)

  Q4. Social and Cultural Factors: Women and Children  ★★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Asked 2023 — Focus on gender-specific factors + child health. 
 
◆ WOMEN: HEALTH-INFLUENCING SOCIO-CULTURAL FACTORS 
A. Gender Discrimination 
● Female foeticide and infanticide: Declining sex ratio (Sex Ratio at Birth: 930 per 1000 males in 
India — Census 2011) 
● Girl child nutrition deprivation: Higher rates of stunting and wasting in females 
● Early marriage and early pregnancy: Leading cause of maternal mortality; <18 years marriage 
increases obstetric risk 
● Limited reproductive autonomy: Low contraceptive use, high unmet need 
B. Cultural Factors Affecting Women 
● Purdah system: Restricts mobility, limits access to healthcare 
● Dowry system: Causes domestic violence, affecting mental and physical health 
● Widow restrictions: Nutritional and psychosocial deprivation 
97


--- PAGE 110 ---

swaDesh
● Preference for male children: Leads to sex-selective abortion (PC-PNDT Act, 1994 to combat 
this) 
 
◆ CHILDREN: HEALTH-INFLUENCING SOCIO-CULTURAL FACTORS 
A. Nutritional Factors 
● Colostrum discarding: Increases neonatal mortality 
● Late breastfeeding initiation: WHO recommends within 1 hour of birth 
● Inappropriate complementary feeding: Diarrhea, growth failure 
● Use of feeding bottles: Increases infection risk (bottle-mouth syndrome) 
B. Traditional Practices Affecting Children 
● Tying of fontanelle with cloth (to 'close the soft spot'): No benefit, can cause head injury 
● Use of kajal (Kohl) in eyes: Risk of lead poisoning 
● Ghutti (honey/herbal preparation): Botulism risk in neonates 
● Delayed vaccination due to cultural beliefs: Increases susceptibility to VPDs 
C. Social Factors 
● Poverty: Main driver of child malnutrition and mortality 
● Mother's education: Most powerful predictor of child health outcomes 
● Birth order: Higher-order births have worse nutrition and healthcare 
● Spacing of children: <2 years leads to 'sibling competition' for nutrition 
◆ GOVERNMENT INITIATIVES 
● PC-PNDT Act (1994): Prevention of sex-selective abortion 
● Beti Bachao, Beti Padhao: Addressing girl child neglect 
● ICDS (Integrated Child Development Services): Nutrition, immunization, preschool education 
● IMNCI (Integrated Management of Neonatal and Childhood Illness) 
● Mid-Day Meal Scheme: Nutrition and school retention

  Q6. Acculturation — Definition and Health Implications  ★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: May 2024 NEW — Define clearly + give the Berry Model + health effects. 
 
◆ DEFINITION 
Acculturation is the process of cultural and psychological change that occurs when two cultures come 
into sustained contact. It involves changes in values, behaviors, attitudes, and social structures of one or 
both cultures. 
(Redfield, Linton & Herskovits, 1936): 'Acculturation comprehends those phenomena which result when 
groups of individuals having different cultures come into continuous first-hand contact, with subsequent 
changes in the original culture patterns of either or both groups.' 
◆ BERRY'S MODEL OF ACCULTURATION (4 Strategies) 
Strategy 
Maintain Heritage 
Culture? 
Adopt Host 
Culture? 
Health Outcome 
Integration 
Yes 
Yes 
Best mental health outcomes 
Assimilation 
No 
Yes
