◆ ACCULTURATIVE STRESS 
When individuals cannot adapt to a new culture, they experience 'acculturative stress' (Culture Shock), 
characterized by: 
● Anxiety, depression, confusion, identity crisis 
● Psychosomatic complaints: Headache, insomnia, fatigue 
● Increased risk of substance abuse and psychiatric disorders 
● Alienation from both heritage and host cultures 
◆ HEALTH IMPLICATIONS OF ACCULTURATION 
100


--- PAGE 113 ---

swaDesh
A. Nutritional Changes 
● Shift from traditional to Western diet: High fat, high sugar, processed foods 
● Increase in obesity, T2DM, cardiovascular disease among immigrants 
● Loss of traditional protective dietary practices (e.g., Mediterranean diet) 
B. Behavioral Changes 
● Adoption of risky behaviors: Alcohol, tobacco, drugs — common in acculturating youth 
● Change in sexual behavior norms: Increased STI risk 
● Physical inactivity: Sedentary Western lifestyle replaces active traditional living 
C. Mental Health Impact 
● 'Immigrant Paradox': Recent immigrants often have BETTER mental health than native-born or 
long-settled immigrants (due to social cohesion of original community) 
● Second-generation immigrants: Higher rates of depression, identity conflicts 
● Acculturation-related domestic violence: Cultural conflicts in family settings 
D. Healthcare-Seeking Behavior 
● Language barriers reduce access to healthcare 
● Distrust of host country medical system 
● Cultural incompetence of healthcare providers: Poor patient-provider communication 
E. Positive Effects of Acculturation 
● Adoption of healthy lifestyle habits from host culture 
● Improved sanitation and hygiene practices 
● Increased vaccination rates and preventive care utilization 
● Access to modern medical treatments 
◆ ACCULTURATING GROUPS IN INDIA 
● Tribal populations integrating into mainstream society (e.g., Adivasis in urban areas) 
● Rural-to-urban migrants: Face dual burden of traditional diseases + urban NCDs 
● Diaspora communities abroad 
● Refugee populations (e.g., Rohingya, Sri Lankan Tamils) 
◆ HEALTH INTERVENTIONS 
● Culturally sensitive health services — 'Cultural Competency' training for health workers 
● Community health workers from same ethnic backgrounds 
● Bilingual health education materials 
● Mental health support for acculturating populations 
● WHO Social Determinants framework to address structural barriers 
★ EXAM TIP: Memorize Berry's 4 acculturation strategies — they appear as 1-mark questions. Integration = 
best health. Marginalization = worst health.

  TOPIC 12: SAMPLING METHODS / PROBABILITY 
SAMPLING TECHNIQUES 
Grade B | Asked 7 Times | High Priority 
  Q1. What is Sampling? Methods of Sampling with Examples  ★★★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Asked 2021, 2018 — The master answer covering all sampling types. 
 
◆ DEFINITION OF SAMPLING 
Sampling: The process of selecting a representative subset (sample) from a larger group 
(population/universe) for the purpose of a study, such that inferences drawn from the sample can be 
generalized to the population. 
Universe / Population: The entire group of individuals or items that meet the criteria for a study 
Sample: A subset of the population selected for study 
Sampling Frame: A list of all elements in the population from which the sample is drawn (e.g., voter 
list, school register) 
Sampling Unit: The unit selected at each stage of sampling (individual, household, village) 
◆ WHY SAMPLING? 
● Cost-effective: Studying entire population is expensive 
● Time-saving: Faster data collection 
● Feasibility: Some populations are infinite or inaccessible 
● Greater accuracy: Intensive study of fewer subjects yields better quality data 
● Destructive testing: Some tests destroy the subject (food quality testing, drug testing) 
 
◆ CLASSIFICATION OF SAMPLING METHODS 
SAMPLING METHODS 
Probability Sampling 
Non-Probability Sampling 
Simple Random Sampling (SRS) 
Convenience Sampling 
Systematic Random Sampling 
Purposive / Judgmental Sampling 
Stratified Random Sampling 
Quota Sampling 
Cluster Sampling 
Snowball Sampling 
Multi-stage Sampling 
Accidental Sampling 
 
◆ A. PROBABILITY SAMPLING METHODS 
In probability sampling, every element in the population has a known, non-zero probability of being 
selected. It allows for statistical inference and generalization. 
1. Simple Random Sampling (SRS) 
102


--- PAGE 115 ---

swaDesh
Each unit in the population has an equal and independent chance of selection. 
● Methods: Lottery method, Random number tables, Computer-generated random numbers 
● Example: Selecting 50 students from 500 by assigning numbers and using a random number 
table 
Advantages: Unbiased, simple, gold standard 
Disadvantages: Requires complete sampling frame; impractical for large, geographically dispersed 
populations 
2. Systematic Random Sampling 
Elements are selected at regular intervals from the sampling frame after a random start. 
● Sampling interval (k) = Population Size (N) / Sample Size (n) 
● Example: Selecting every 10th patient from an OPD register after randomly choosing a starting 
point between 1-10 
Advantage: Simple, no complete list needed, spread across population 
Disadvantage: Periodicity bias — if there is a periodic pattern in the list 
3. Stratified Random Sampling 
Population is divided into homogeneous subgroups (strata) based on a relevant characteristic, then SRS 
is applied within each stratum. 
● Proportionate stratification: Same proportion sampled from each stratum 
● Disproportionate stratification: Higher sampling fraction from smaller/important strata 
● Example: Stratifying by gender, age group, socioeconomic class, then sampling within each 
group 
Advantage: Ensures representation of all subgroups; reduces sampling error 
Disadvantage: Requires knowledge of stratification variable for all population members 
4. Cluster Sampling 
Population is divided into natural groups (clusters — e.g., villages, schools, blocks). Clusters are 
randomly selected and ALL individuals within selected clusters are studied. 
● Single-stage cluster: All units within selected cluster are studied 
● Two-stage cluster: Random sample within selected cluster 
● Example: Selecting 10 villages randomly from 100 villages and studying all households in 
selected villages 
● USED IN: EPI coverage surveys (WHO 30x7 cluster sampling method) 
Advantage: Cost-effective, no need for complete sampling frame of individuals 
Disadvantage: Cluster members tend to be similar (intracluster correlation) — less precise; requires 
larger sample size 
5. Multi-stage Sampling 
Sampling is done in multiple stages with different units at each stage. 
● Stage 1: Districts → Stage 2: Blocks → Stage 3: Villages → Stage 4: Households → Stage 5: 
Individuals 
● Used in NFHS (National Family Health Survey), NSSO surveys 
Advantage: Practical for large-scale national surveys; cost-effective 
Disadvantage: Complex design; errors may accumulate at each stage; requires expertise 
 
103


--- PAGE 116 ---

swaDesh
◆ B. NON-PROBABILITY SAMPLING METHODS 
Selection is based on non-random criteria. Not all elements have a known probability of selection. Cannot 
generalize to the population. 
1. Convenience Sampling 
● Selecting subjects based on availability and willingness 
● Example: Surveying patients in OPD, passersby on street 
● Use: Pilot studies, exploratory research 
2. Purposive (Judgmental) Sampling 
● Researcher selects subjects based on their own judgment about who will be most informative 
● Example: Selecting expert health administrators for a policy study 
● Use: Qualitative research, key informant interviews 
3. Quota Sampling 
● Population divided into subgroups; interviewer fills predetermined quotas 
● Example: 'Interview 20 males and 20 females between ages 18-65 from this area' 
● Used in market research, opinion polls 
4. Snowball Sampling 
● Existing subjects recruit future subjects from their network 
● Example: Studying IV drug users — hard-to-reach population; one user refers others 
● Used for hidden/stigmatized populations: IV drug users, sex workers, LGBTQ+ communities 
 
◆ COMPARISON: PROBABILITY vs. NON-PROBABILITY SAMPLING 
Feature 
Probability Sampling 
Non-Probability Sampling 
Selection basis 
Random (chance) 
Non-random (judgment) 
Representativeness 
High 
Low 
Generalizability 
Yes 
No 
Sampling error 
Can be calculated 
Cannot be calculated 
Cost 
Higher 
Lower 
Time 
More 
Less 
Best use 
Epidemiological surveys 
Qualitative/exploratory studies 
 
★ EXAM TIP: In exam, always define sampling → classify → explain each type → give example → 
advantages/disadvantages. End with a comparison table. This ensures full marks. 
 
  Q2. Various Methods of Sampling in Medical Research  ★★★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Asked 2023 — Same as Q1 but emphasize medical research applications. 
 
104


--- PAGE 117 ---

swaDesh
◆ SAMPLING IN MEDICAL RESEARCH: CONTEXT 
Medical research involves studying populations to understand disease patterns, evaluate interventions, 
or assess healthcare needs. Appropriate sampling ensures validity, reliability, and generalizability of 
research findings. 
◆ SAMPLING METHODS IN MEDICAL RESEARCH CONTEXT 
Clinical Trials 
● Convenience or purposive sampling for Phase I (safety) trials 
● Stratified randomization to ensure balanced treatment groups 
● Block randomization: Ensures equal allocation in subgroups 
Epidemiological Surveys 
● Simple random or systematic sampling for disease prevalence studies 
● Multi-stage cluster sampling for national surveys (NFHS, DLHS) 
● Stratified sampling by age, sex, region for representativeness 
Case-Control Studies 
● Cases: All incident cases from a defined population (census of cases) 
● Controls: Random sample from the source population (incidence density sampling) 
Cohort Studies 
● Defined cohort selected from a population (occupational cohort, birth cohort) 
● Follow-up of entire cohort — no sampling within; but sampling of the initial cohort 
Health Service Evaluation 
● Cluster sampling of health facilities 
● Systematic sampling of patient records 
◆ SAMPLE SIZE DETERMINATION 
Before sampling, adequate sample size must be calculated using: 
● Prevalence of condition in population (P) 
● Desired precision (d) — allowable error 
● Confidence level (Z — usually 1.96 for 95% CI) 
● Formula (for proportions): n = Z² × P × (1-P) / d² 
● Add 10-20% for non-response / attrition 
★ EXAM TIP: In medical research, mention: Sampling bias (selection bias), response bias, non-response 
bias. Examiner expects awareness of bias in context of each method. 
 
  Q3. Probability Sampling Techniques in Research Study  ★★★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Dec 2024 NEW — Focus purely on PROBABILITY sampling — all 5 types in detail. 
 
◆ DEFINITION 
Probability sampling is a sampling technique where every unit of the population has a known, non-zero 
probability of being selected. It is the foundation of scientific, unbiased epidemiological research. 
105


--- PAGE 118 ---

swaDesh
◆ CHARACTERISTICS OF PROBABILITY SAMPLING 
● Randomness: Selection based on chance, not investigator's choice 
● Representativeness: Sample mirrors the population 
● Quantifiable error: Sampling error can be estimated 
● Inferential: Allows generalization from sample to population 
● Unbiased: Eliminates selection bias 
◆ THE FIVE PROBABILITY SAMPLING TECHNIQUES 
Method 
Key Feature 
Best Used For 
Example in Health Research 
Simple Random 
Equal probability 
for all units 
Small, well-listed 
populations 
Selecting patients from hospital register

selected 
Populations

strata

randomly 
selected

geographically

EPI coverage survey (WHO 30×7) 
Multi-stage 
Multiple levels of 
sampling 
National/state-level 
surveys 
NFHS, NSSO, DLHS surveys 
 
◆ WHO EPI CLUSTER SAMPLING (30×7 Method) 
The WHO Expanded Programme on Immunization (EPI) uses a specific cluster sampling methodology 
for vaccine coverage surveys: 
● Step 1: Obtain population list of all villages/areas in the region 
● Step 2: Calculate cumulative population and sampling interval 
● Step 3: Use PPS (Probability Proportionate to Size) to select 30 clusters 
● Step 4: In each cluster, interview 7 children aged 12-23 months 
● Total sample = 30 × 7 = 210 children 
● Household selection: 'Modified EPI method' — random direction spin from center of village 
◆ SOURCES OF SAMPLING ERROR 
● Random sampling error: Due to chance (reduced by increasing sample size) 
● Systematic error / bias: Due to faulty sampling design (cannot be corrected after sampling) 
● Non-response bias: When non-respondents differ from respondents 
● Frame error: Sampling frame does not match target population 
★ EXAM TIP: For probability sampling, learn the formulas: SRS = equal probability; Systematic k=N/n; For 
Stratified: Proportionate allocation ni = n × Ni/N. These formulae show depth of knowledge. 
 
  Q4. Define Sampling. Write about Cluster Sampling in Detail  ★★★★ 
106


--- PAGE 119 ---

swaDesh
★ EXAM TIP: Asked 2018 — Half the answer is on cluster sampling specifically. 
 
◆ DEFINITION OF SAMPLING 
Sampling is the process of selecting a representative subset from a defined population, with the intention 
that conclusions drawn from the sample will be applicable to the population as a whole. 
◆ CLUSTER SAMPLING — DETAILED DESCRIPTION 
Definition 
Cluster sampling is a probability sampling technique in which the population is divided into groups 
(clusters), clusters are randomly selected, and all units within selected clusters are studied (single-stage) 
or a random sample is drawn from within selected clusters (two-stage). 
When to Use Cluster Sampling 
● Population is geographically spread and a complete sampling frame of individuals is unavailable 
● Cost-effective study of widely distributed populations 
● When clusters (natural groups) exist in the population 
Types of Cluster Sampling 
Type 
Description 
Example 
Single-stage cluster 
All units in selected clusters 
studied 
All households in 5 randomly selected 
villages 
Two-stage cluster 
Sample drawn within selected 
clusters 
30 random households from each of 10 
selected villages 
Multi-stage cluster 
Multiple levels of sampling 
within clusters 
NFHS survey design 
PPS Cluster 
Clusters selected with 
probability proportional to size 
WHO EPI 30×7 method 
 
Steps in Cluster Sampling 
● Step 1: Define the population and identify natural clusters (e.g., villages, school classes, 
hospital wards) 
● Step 2: List all clusters with their sizes 
● Step 3: Calculate sampling interval if using PPS 
● Step 4: Randomly select required number of clusters 
● Step 5: Study all (or a sample of) units in each selected cluster 
Advantages of Cluster Sampling 
● No need for a complete individual-level sampling frame — only cluster-level list required 
● Cost-effective — resources concentrated in selected clusters 
● Logistically feasible for large, geographically dispersed populations 
● Suitable for EPI surveys, community health surveys 
Disadvantages of Cluster Sampling 
● Loss of statistical efficiency — members of same cluster tend to be similar (intracluster 
correlation = ICC) 
107


--- PAGE 120 ---

swaDesh
● Design Effect (DEFF): Must multiply sample size by design effect (usually 1.5-2.0 for cluster 
sampling) 
● More complex analysis required 
● Clusters may not be homogeneous — some very large, some very small (PPS addresses this) 
Design Effect (DEFF) 
DEFF = Variance of cluster sample / Variance of SRS 
● DEFF > 1 means cluster sampling is less efficient than SRS 
● To achieve same precision: Sample size from cluster design = SRS sample size × DEFF 
★ EXAM TIP: Cluster sampling is tested repeatedly. Always mention: ICC (Intracluster Correlation 
Coefficient), Design Effect (DEFF), PPS selection, and WHO EPI 30×7 method. 
 
  Q5. Sampling Methods for Community-Based Research  ★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Nov 2023 NEW — Focus on practical community health research applications. 
 
◆ COMMUNITY-BASED RESEARCH: OVERVIEW 
Community-based research aims to study health problems in natural community settings. It includes 
prevalence surveys, intervention studies, surveillance, and health needs assessments. Sampling in 
community research must balance scientific rigor with practical constraints. 
◆ MOST COMMONLY USED METHODS IN COMMUNITY RESEARCH 
1. Multi-stage Stratified Cluster Sampling 
Most widely used in large community surveys (NFHS, DLHS, AHS, NNMB): 
● Stage 1: Select districts (strata = rural/urban) 
● Stage 2: Select villages/wards using PPS 
● Stage 3: Select households using systematic sampling 
● Stage 4: Select individuals within households 
2. Simple Random Sampling 
● Used when community is small and a complete sampling frame exists (e.g., school children, 
registered patients) 
3. Systematic Sampling 
● Selecting every kth household along a street / from a PHC register 
4. Stratified Sampling 
● Stratify by rural/urban, gender, age group, socioeconomic class 
● Ensures proportionate representation of all groups 
5. Community-Based Participatory Sampling 
● Community members involved in sampling design 
● Uses local knowledge to identify hard-to-reach groups 
◆ PRACTICAL CHALLENGES IN COMMUNITY SAMPLING 
108


--- PAGE 121 ---

swaDesh
Challenge 
Solution 
No updated census/list 
Use satellite maps, GPS enumeration 
High non-response rate 
Multiple follow-up visits; incentives 
Seasonal migration 
Stratify by season; note seasonal population 
Language barriers 
Local language questionnaires, local surveyors 
Hard-to-reach populations 
Snowball or RDS (Respondent-Driven Sampling) 
 
◆ QUALITY CONTROL IN COMMUNITY SAMPLING 
● Pilot study before main survey (5-10% of sample size) 
● Training and standardization of field investigators 
● Supervision and back-checks of completed forms 
● Data entry double-checking 
● Consistency checks during analysis 
★ EXAM TIP: Community-based research also uses Rapid Assessment Procedures (RAP) and Lot Quality 
Assurance Sampling (LQAS) — mention these for extra marks. 
 
  Q6. Define Randomization and Explain Methods of Randomization  
★★★ 
★ EXAM TIP: Asked 2023 — Related to sampling but focuses on experimental design. 
 
◆ DEFINITION OF RANDOMIZATION 
Randomization is the process of randomly assigning subjects to different treatment or comparison groups 
in an experimental study (especially Randomized Controlled Trials — RCTs), such that each participant 
has an equal and independent chance of being assigned to any group. 
Randomization is the hallmark of experimental study design and the most effective method of controlling 
confounding variables. 
◆ PURPOSE OF RANDOMIZATION 
● Eliminates selection bias in group allocation 
● Ensures comparability of groups at baseline (known and unknown confounders balanced) 
● Allows valid statistical inference 
● Strengthens causality of observed associations 
● Foundation of Evidence-Based Medicine (EBM) 
◆ METHODS OF RANDOMIZATION 
1. Simple Randomization 
Each participant is independently assigned to a group based on a random process. 
● Method: Coin toss (Heads = Treatment, Tails = Control) 
109


--- PAGE 122 ---

swaDesh
● Random number table: Even = Group A, Odd = Group B 
● Computer random number generator 
Advantage: Simple, easy to implement 
Disadvantage: Small samples may yield unequal group sizes by chance 
2. Block Randomization (Permuted Block Randomization) 
Participants are randomized in blocks to ensure equal group sizes at any point in the trial. 
● Block size (b): Usually 4, 6, or 8 
● Example (block size 4): All permutations of AABB = AABB, ABAB, ABBA, BABA, BAAB, BBAA 
● Randomly select one permutation per block; ensures groups balanced every b patients 
Advantage: Ensures equal group sizes; important if recruitment may stop early 
Disadvantage: Blinding can be compromised if block size is known 
3. Stratified Randomization 
Randomization is performed within strata defined by important prognostic factors. 
● Strata defined by: Age, sex, disease severity, center in multi-center trials 
● Block randomization applied within each stratum 
● Example: Stratify hypertensive patients by severity (mild/moderate/severe), then randomize 
within each group 
Advantage: Ensures balance across important prognostic variables; especially important in small trials 
Disadvantage: Complex with many strata; limited to 2-3 stratifying variables 
4. Minimization 
An adaptive randomization method that minimizes imbalance across multiple prognostic factors 
simultaneously. 
● Each new participant assigned to the group that minimizes overall imbalance 
● More efficient than stratified randomization for multiple factors 
● Used in large multi-center clinical trials 
5. Cluster Randomization 
Groups (clusters) rather than individuals are randomized. 
● Used in community intervention trials (e.g., health education program) 
● Example: 10 villages randomized to receive intervention or control 
● Advantage: Prevents contamination between groups 
● Disadvantage: Loss of efficiency; needs design effect correction; ICC must be accounted for 
6. Adaptive Randomization 
Randomization probabilities change based on accumulating trial data. 
● Response-adaptive: More patients allocated to better-performing treatment 
● Covariate-adaptive: Minimizes covariate imbalance 
● Ethical advantage: Fewer patients receive inferior treatment 
 
◆ ALLOCATION CONCEALMENT vs. BLINDING 
110


--- PAGE 123 ---

swaDesh
Concept 
Definition 
Purpose 
Allocation Concealment 
Hiding treatment allocation 
sequence from those enrolling 
patients 
Prevents selection bias BEFORE 
allocation 
Blinding 
Hiding treatment allocation from 
participants/investigators AFTER 
allocation 
Prevents performance and 
detection bias 
 
◆ SEQUENCE GENERATION vs. ALLOCATION CONCEALMENT 
● Sequence generation: Creating the randomization sequence (computer, tables) 
● Allocation concealment: Implementing the sequence without bias (sealed envelopes, SNOSE — 
Sequentially Numbered Opaque Sealed Envelopes, central telephone randomization) 
★ EXAM TIP: CONSORT 2010 Statement requires reporting of: randomization sequence generation method, 
allocation concealment mechanism, and implementation details. Mention CONSORT in exam answers for 
extra marks.

Surveillance is the continuous, systematic collection, analysis, interpretation, and dissemination of 
health data for planning, implementation, and evaluation of public health programmes. (WHO) 
★ Mnemonic: "C-A-I-D" — Collect, Analyse, Interpret, Disseminate

Q3. Surveillance of AEFI (Adverse Events Following Immunization)  
⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ (May 2025 — NEW) 
Definition — AEFI 
AEFI is any untoward medical occurrence which follows immunization and which does not necessarily 
have a causal relationship with the usage of the vaccine. (WHO definition) 
★ Mnemonic: "Any Medical Occurrence Post-Immunization" — does NOT imply causation 
Classification of AEFI (WHO 2013 Brighton Collaboration) 
Category 
Cause 
Example 
Vaccine product-related 
Inherent property of vaccine 
OPV — VAPP 
Vaccine quality defect 
Manufacturing defect 
Aggregated particles → 
fever 
Immunization error 
Wrong dose, site, technique 
Abscess at injection site 
Immunization anxiety 
Fear/anxiety about injection 
Vasovagal syncope

AEFI Surveillance System in India 
Legal basis: Drugs & Cosmetics Act; National Pharmacovigilance Programme (PvPI) 
Nodal body: Ministry of Health & Family Welfare; National AEFI Committee 
Levels: Health worker → Block/PHC → District → State → National AEFI Committee 
Reporting Criteria — Which AEFIs to Report? 
ALL serious AEFIs must be reported within 24–48 hours: 
Serious AEFI includes: Death, hospitalisation, life-threatening events, permanent disability, 
anaphylaxis, encephalopathy, VAPP, seizures, sepsis at injection site, abscess 
Non-serious AEFIs (fever, redness, pain) → record in health register; no immediate upward reporting 
needed 
Investigation of AEFI 
1. Verify the event  →  2. Collect patient & vaccine details  →  3. Causality assessment  →  4. Report to 
AEFI Committee  →  5. Corrective action 
Causality Assessment: WHO causality categories: Consistent causal association / Inconsistent / 
Indeterminate / Unclassifiable 
Key Committees 
District AEFI Committee: Chaired by CMO/CMHO; investigates all serious AEFIs 
State AEFI Committee: Reviews district reports; provides technical guidance 
National AEFI Committee: Apex body; issues national alerts; publishes annual report 
114


--- PAGE 127 ---

swaDesh
⚡ High Yield: Anaphylaxis — most severe AEFI. Occurs within 30 min. Adrenaline 0.01 mg/kg IM 
is treatment. Every vaccination point must have adrenaline. 
★ Mnemonic: AEFI Classes: "VP-QE-IA-Anxiety-Coin" — Vaccine Product, Quality, Error, 
Anxiety, Coincidental 
 
Q4. Herd Immunity and its Role in Infection Control  ⭐⭐⭐ (Nov 2023 — NEW) 
Definition 
Herd immunity (community immunity / population immunity) is the indirect protection afforded to 
susceptible individuals when a sufficiently large proportion of a population has become immune to an 
infection — either through vaccination or prior infection — thereby reducing the likelihood of infection 
for individuals who lack immunity. 
Mechanism 
When immune individuals act as 'barriers', the chain of transmission is broken. Even non-immune 
individuals are protected because the pathogen cannot easily find new susceptible hosts. 
★ Mnemonic: "Sea of immune, island of susceptible — infection can't cross the sea" 
Herd Immunity Threshold (HIT) 
HIT = proportion of population that must be immune to halt epidemic spread. 
Formula: HIT = 1 − (1/R₀) 
Where R₀ = Basic Reproduction Number (average secondary cases from 1 primary case in fully 
susceptible population) 
Disease 
R₀ 
HIT (approx.) 
Measles 
12–18 
92–95% 
Polio 
5–7 
80–85% 
COVID-19 (original) 
2–3 
~67% 
Smallpox 
5–7 
83% 
Mumps 
4–7 
75–86% 
 
Role in Infection Control 
Protects vulnerable groups: Neonates, immunocompromised, elderly, pregnant women who 
cannot be vaccinated 
Outbreak prevention: High coverage prevents explosive outbreaks — seen with smallpox 
eradication 
Endemic control: Sustained high coverage maintains endemicity below epidemic threshold 
Programme target: Vaccination programmes aim to exceed HIT to achieve herd immunity 
Achieving Herd Immunity — Methods 
Vaccination: PREFERRED — safe, ethical, no morbidity. Pulse Polio, measles campaigns 
Natural infection: NOT recommended — leads to morbidity, mortality, and risk of more virulent 
strains 
Limitations 
115


--- PAGE 128 ---

swaDesh
• Herd immunity does NOT apply to non-communicable diseases or non-person-to-person infections 
(e.g., tetanus, rabies) 
• Vaccine hesitancy creates susceptible pockets → outbreaks (measles resurgence in Europe 2018) 
• Waning immunity may reduce herd effect over time 
• Antigenic drift (influenza, COVID variants) may evade existing immunity 
⚡ High Yield: Measles requires highest HIT (~95%) due to very high R₀. That is why 2 doses of 
MMR are given in UIP. 
★ Mnemonic: HIT = 1 − 1/R₀  ||  Higher R₀ → Higher HIT needed 
 
TOPIC 14: SCREENING — Applications, Criteria, Advantages & 
Disadvantages 
 
Q1. Define Screening. Uses, Criteria and Validity of Screening Tests  
⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ (2023) 
Definition 
Screening is the presumptive identification of unrecognized disease or defect by the application of 
tests, examinations or other procedures that can be applied rapidly to sort out apparently healthy 
individuals who probably have a disease from those who probably do not. (Commission on Chronic 
Illness, USA, 1951) 
KEY: Screening does NOT diagnose; it sorts into presumptively positive and presumptively negative 
groups. 
Types of Screening 
Mass screening: Whole population screened regardless of risk (e.g., neonatal heel-prick for PKU) 
Selective / High-risk: Target high-risk groups (e.g., smokers for lung cancer) 
Multiphasic: Multiple tests in one visit (e.g., health camp — BP, CBG, BMI together) 
Opportunistic: Screening during unrelated clinical visit (e.g., BP at dental clinic) 
Case finding: Physician-initiated on individual patients 
Uses of Screening 
1. Early detection of disease at preclinical stage (→ better prognosis) 
2. Reduction of disease burden at population level 
3. Cost-effective intervention when treatment at early stage is cheaper 
4. Epidemiological research — measure prevalence, natural history 
5. Evaluation of prevention programmes 
Wilson & Jungner Criteria (WHO 1968) — 10 Classic Criteria 
★ Mnemonic: "SITE-SAFE-TEC" mnemonic or simply WHO 10 criteria 
# 
Criterion 
Details 
1 
Important health 
problem 
Common / severe disease with significant burden 
116


--- PAGE 129 ---

swaDesh
2 
Accepted treatment 
available 
No point detecting if no treatment exists 
3

diagnosis & treatment

Validity of Screening Tests 
Validity = ability of a test to measure what it is intended to measure. Assessed by: 
Measure 
Formula 
Ideal Value 
Sensitivity 
TP / (TP + FN) × 100  [True Positive Rate] 
High (>80%) 
Specificity 
TN / (TN + FP) × 100  [True Negative Rate] 
High (>80%) 
PPV 
TP / (TP + FP) × 100 — depends on prevalence 
Varies 
NPV 
TN / (TN + FN) × 100 
Varies 
Accuracy 
(TP + TN) / Total × 100 
High 
 
Sensitivity vs Specificity trade-off: Increasing sensitivity → decreasing specificity (and vice 
versa). The cut-off is chosen based on programme priorities. 
Youden's Index: Sensitivity + Specificity − 1  (max = 1.0; best cut-off) 
ROC Curve: Receiver Operating Characteristic — plots Sensitivity vs (1−Specificity); larger AUC = 
better test 
⚡ High Yield: Sensitivity is used for RULING OUT disease (SnNout); Specificity for RULING IN 
disease (SpPin). 
★ Mnemonic: "SnNout" — High Sensitivity, Negative result rules OUT. "SpPin" — High 
Specificity, Positive result rules IN.

Q3. Advantages and Disadvantages of Screening  ⭐⭐⭐⭐ (May 2024 — NEW) 
Advantages of Screening

Explanation / Example 
Early detection 
Disease caught at preclinical stage → better 
prognosis

--- PAGE 131 ---

swaDesh
Reduced morbidity 
Treatment before symptoms → less disease

Treating early disease cheaper than advanced 
stage

Disadvantages of Screening 
Disadvantage 
Explanation / Example 
False positives 
Cause anxiety, unnecessary investigations, harm 
False negatives 
False reassurance — miss diseased individuals 
Lead time bias 
Apparent ↑ survival without actual ↑ lifespan 
Length bias 
Slowly progressive (benign) cases over-
represented in screen-detected 
Overdiagnosis

Cost 
Expensive to run population-level screening

Ethical issues 
Labelling effect — anxiety, discrimination, 
insurance issues 
Resource burden 
Screen-positives need follow-up → strains health 
system 
 
Key Biases in Screening — Must Know 
Lead time bias: Survival appears longer because clock starts earlier (at screening), not because 
patient lives longer 
Length bias: Screening preferentially detects slow-growing tumours; aggressive tumours grow too 
fast to be caught between screens 
Volunteer bias (Selection bias): Screened population is healthier than non-screened — inflates 
apparent benefit 
Overdiagnosis bias: Detecting pseudodisease — abnormality that would never cause symptoms 
or death 
⚡ High Yield: Randomized Controlled Trial (RCT) is the GOLD STANDARD to evaluate screening 
programmes — eliminates lead time and length biases. 
★ Mnemonic: Biases: "LLV-O" — Lead time, Length, Volunteer, Overdiagnosis 
 
Q4. Concept of Screening — Types, Criteria and Effectiveness  ⭐⭐⭐ (2018) 
119


--- PAGE 132 ---

swaDesh
Concept of Screening 
Screening is a secondary prevention strategy — does not prevent disease but detects it early to 
improve outcomes. It is applied at the interface between primary prevention (preventing disease onset) 
and tertiary prevention (limiting disability). 
The concept rests on the idea that most chronic diseases have a preclinical detectable phase (PCDP) 
during which intervention is more effective than after clinical disease onset. 
Types of Screening (Detailed Classification) 
By population covered: Mass → High-risk → Individual (case finding) 
By setting: Community-based, hospital-based, workplace-based, school-based 
By number of tests: Single test vs Multiphasic (battery of tests applied simultaneously) 
By timing: Antenatal, neonatal, premarital, occupational, geriatric 
By disease type: Communicable (TB — CBNAAT, sputum), NCD (HTN, DM, cancer), genetic 
(sickle cell) 
Criteria for Effectiveness of Screening 
A screening programme is considered effective when: 
↓ Specific mortality: Death rate from the target disease falls (RCT evidence required) 
↓ Incidence of advanced disease: More cases detected at early stage 
↑ Survival: True survival gain, not just lead time bias 
Cost-effective: Cost per QALY/DALY averted within acceptable threshold 
Acceptable false positive rate: <5–10% to avoid excessive follow-up burden 
Evaluation Designs for Screening 
RCT: Gold standard — randomize to screened vs unscreened; compare cause-specific mortality 
Case-control study: Compare past screening history among cases vs controls 
Cohort study: Follow screened vs unscreened — compare outcomes 
Note: Survival rate is a POOR endpoint due to lead time and length biases — use disease-specific 
MORTALITY RATE 
Examples of Evaluated Screening Programmes 
Cervical cancer (Pap/VIA): Proven to ↓ mortality — adopted in >100 countries 
Breast cancer (mammography): ↓ mortality by ~15–20% in RCTs for women 50–69 yrs 
Colorectal cancer (FOBT): ↓ mortality by ~15–33% in RCTs 
Neonatal PKU: Dietary treatment from birth prevents intellectual disability 
Prostate cancer (PSA): CONTROVERSIAL — high false positives, overdiagnosis; not 
recommended for mass screening 
⚡ High Yield: Wilson & Jungner 10 criteria are the standard for deciding whether to implement a 
screening programme. Memorize all 10. 
★ Mnemonic: Effectiveness = ↓ Mortality (not ↑ Survival) — True benefit shown only in RCT 
 
Q5. Criteria and Validity of Screening  ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ 
(Refer to Q1 for comprehensive answer — Wilson & Jungner 10 criteria and 2×2 validity table)

Sensitivity: a/(a+c)  — avoids false negatives — used for RULING OUT 
Specificity: d/(b+d)  — avoids false positives — used for RULING IN 
PPV: a/(a+b)  — rises with higher prevalence 
NPV: d/(c+d)  — falls with higher prevalence 
Reliability / Repeatability: Consistency of test results — measured by kappa statistic 
⚡ High Yield: PPV and NPV depend on PREVALENCE; Sensitivity and Specificity are intrinsic to 
the test (prevalence-independent).

UNIT 15: DEMOGRAPHIC CYCLE / TRANSITION / DIVIDEND / 
FERTILITY INDICATORS 
 
Q1. Discuss demographic cycle with suitable examples. ★★★★★ (2022, 2012) 
 
Introduction 
The Demographic Cycle (also called Demographic Transition Theory) describes the sequential change 
in birth rates and death rates in a society as it progresses from a pre-industrial to an industrialized 
economy. It was proposed by Warren Thompson (1929) and elaborated by Frank Notestein (1945). 
 
Stages of the Demographic Cycle 
The theory identifies FIVE stages (some sources describe 4), each characterized by distinct patterns of 
birth rate (BR), death rate (DR), and natural growth rate (NGR = BR - DR):

Very Low 
(<10/1000) 
Low 
Negative 
Germany, Japan, Italy 
 
Detailed Description of Each Stage 
Stage I – High Stationary (Pre-Industrial): Both birth rate and death rate are high (40–50/1000). 
Population remains stable/stagnant. High mortality due to famines, epidemics, and wars. No family 
planning. Example: Pre-colonial India, Medieval Europe. 
Stage II – Early Expanding (Industrial Revolution Phase): Death rate falls due to improved 
sanitation, nutrition, and medical advances. Birth rate remains high. Population EXPLODES. This is 
the most dangerous phase for resource management. Example: India in 1920s–1970s. 
Stage III – Late Expanding (Transitional): Birth rate begins to fall due to urbanization, education 
of women, use of contraception, rise in cost of living. Death rate is already low and stable. Growth 
decelerates. Example: India currently (BR ~20, DR ~7/1000). 
Stage IV – Low Stationary (Post-Industrial): Both birth and death rates are low. Zero population 
growth. Aging population. Example: Western Europe, North America. 
Stage V – Declining: Birth rate falls BELOW death rate → negative natural growth rate → 
population shrinks. Concerns: labour shortage, pension burden. Example: Germany (BR 9, DR 
12/1000), Japan. 
122


--- PAGE 135 ---

swaDesh
 
India's Position in Demographic Cycle 
India is currently in STAGE III (Late Expanding) of the Demographic Transition: 
• 
Birth Rate: ~20/1000 (2020 SRS data) 
• 
Death Rate: ~6.2/1000 
• 
Natural Growth Rate: ~13.8/1000 
• 
TFR: 2.0 (National Family Health Survey-5, 2019-21) — near replacement level 
• 
15 states have already achieved TFR ≤ 2.1 (replacement level) 
 
Demographic Gap 
The gap between the falling death rate and the (slower-falling) birth rate is called the 'Demographic Gap.' 
This gap = period of maximum population growth. India's demographic gap was widest in 1960s–1980s. 
 
★ The Demographic Transition Theory explains population dynamics through 5 stages. India 
is in Stage III with a TFR of 2.0, approaching replacement level. Key determinants: education, 
urbanization, female literacy, family planning programs. 
✔ Exam Tip: Draw the classic S-curve/J-curve diagram showing BR and DR trends across stages. 
Examiners reward visual representation.

swaDesh
Q2. What are the different processes of demography? Discuss fertility indicators. 
★★★★ (2023) 
 
Processes of Demography 
Demography is the scientific study of human populations. The three fundamental demographic processes 
are: 
 
• 
1. Fertility: The reproductive performance of a population — refers to actual live births occurring in 
a population. 
• 
2. Mortality: Deaths occurring in a population. Measured by death rate, IMR, MMR, etc. 
• 
3. Migration: Movement of people into (immigration) or out of (emigration) a geographic area. 
Internal migration (rural-to-urban) is a major driver of demographic change in India. 
 
These three processes determine: 
• 
Population size 
• 
Population composition (age, sex distribution) 
• 
Population distribution (urban vs rural) 
 
Fertility Indicators — Detailed Classification 
 
A. Crude Birth Rate (CBR) 
Definition: Number of live births per 1000 mid-year population in a given year. 
Formula: CBR = (Number of live births / Mid-year total population) × 1000 
Normal range: High: >40; Moderate: 20–40; Low: <20 
India (2020): ~20/1000 
Limitation: Includes entire population in denominator (including non-women of reproductive age) 
— hence crude. 
 
B. General Fertility Rate (GFR) 
Formula: GFR = (Live births / Women aged 15–49 years) × 1000 
More refined than CBR as denominator is restricted to women of reproductive age (WRA). 
 
C. Age-Specific Fertility Rate (ASFR) 
Formula: ASFR = (Live births to women in age group X / Mid-year women in age group X) × 1000 
Calculated for each 5-year age group: 15–19, 20–24, 25–29, 30–34, 35–39, 40–44, 45–49. 
Use: Identifies peak fertile age; basis for calculating TFR 
 
D. Total Fertility Rate (TFR) — MOST IMPORTANT 
Definition: Average number of children a woman would have if she lived through her entire reproductive 
period (15–49 years) at currently observed ASFR. 
124


--- PAGE 137 ---

swaDesh
Formula: TFR = 5 × Σ(ASFR for all 7 age groups) — multiplied by 5 as each age group spans 5 years. 
TFR Value 
Interpretation 
2.1 
Replacement Level Fertility (population stable over generations) 
>2.1 
Growing population 
<2.1 
Declining population (long-term) 
India TFR (NFHS-5, 2019-21) 
2.0 — Below replacement level for the first time! 
Bihar (highest) 
3.0 
Sikkim (lowest) 
1.1 
 
E. Gross Reproduction Rate (GRR) 
Average number of DAUGHTERS a woman would bear during her lifetime, assuming no mortality. 
Formula: GRR = TFR × (proportion of female births) ≈ TFR × 0.488 
Replacement value: GRR = 1.0 
 
F. Net Reproduction Rate (NRR) 
Average number of daughters that would replace each woman in the next generation, after accounting 
for mortality. 
Formula: NRR = GRR × (probability of surviving to mean age of childbearing) 
Interpretation: NRR = 1: replacement; NRR > 1: growing; NRR < 1: declining population 
India: NRR ≈ 0.9 (NFHS-5) 
 
★ TFR is the single most important fertility indicator. India's TFR = 2.0 (NFHS-5) — below 
replacement level of 2.1 for the first time. NRR and GRR account for sex composition and 
mortality respectively. 
 
 
 
125


--- PAGE 138 ---

swaDesh
Q3. Significance of Demographic Cycle and Demographic Gap. ★★★★ (May 2024) 
★NEW★ 
 
Significance of the Demographic Cycle 
The demographic cycle has wide-ranging implications for public health policy, economic planning, and 
resource allocation: 
 
1. Policy Planning: Governments use the stage of demographic transition to design policies (family 
planning, old-age security, healthcare infrastructure). 
2. Health System Planning: Stage I/II: Maternal & child health infrastructure needed. Stage IV/V: 
Geriatric care services required. 
3. Economic Implications: Population growth in Stage II/III strains resources. Aging population in 
Stage IV creates dependency burden. 
4. Predicting Population Trends: Helps forecast future population size, age structure, and 
dependency ratios. 
5. Family Planning Programs: Understanding the stage guides contraceptive acceptance 
campaigns and reproductive health priorities. 
 
Significance of Demographic Gap 
The Demographic Gap is defined as the period/interval between the decline in death rate and the 
(lagging) decline in birth rate. It represents the phase of MAXIMUM population explosion. 
 
Key significance: 
• 
Explains why population explodes even without increasing birth rates — it is the FALLING DEATH 
RATE that drives it. 
• 
India's demographic gap: Largest in 1960s–1980s; currently narrowing as birth rate falls. 
• 
A wide demographic gap = high NGR = more pressure on food, education, employment, healthcare. 
• 
Guides urgency of family planning interventions to accelerate birth rate decline. 
• 
Countries with a narrowing demographic gap have potential for a 'Demographic Dividend'. 
 
★ The demographic cycle informs health and economic policy at every stage. The 
demographic gap explains population momentum and the urgent need for fertility control 
measures in developing nations. 
 
 
 
126


--- PAGE 139 ---

Q5. Population Transition and Impact on Health Indicators. ★★★★ (Oct 2025) ★NEW★ 
 
Population Transition — Definition 
Population Transition refers to the shift from a regime of high fertility and high mortality to one of low 
fertility and low mortality. It encompasses the Demographic Transition Theory but focuses specifically on 
health consequences. 
 
Impact on Health Indicators 
 
1. Epidemiological Transition (Omran's Theory, 1971) 
Parallel to the demographic transition, the pattern of disease changes: 
Stage 
Dominant Diseases 
Example 
Age of Pestilence & 
Famine 
Infectious diseases (cholera, plague, 
TB) 
Pre-1900 India 
Age of Receding 
Pandemics 
Declining infections, rise of chronic 
diseases 
1900–1970 India 
Age of Degenerative 
& Man-made 
Diseases 
NCDs: CVD, cancer, diabetes 
dominate 
Present India, Western nations 
Age of Delayed 
Degenerative 
Diseases 
NCDs still dominate but at older 
ages 
USA, Japan today

Infectious Diseases 
New pathogens (HIV, COVID-19) 
Global — ongoing 
 
2. Impact on Maternal & Child Health 
• 
Falling TFR → Fewer high-risk pregnancies → Improved MMR and IMR 
• 
India's MMR: 97/100,000 live births (SRS 2018-20) — significant improvement 
• 
India's IMR: 28/1000 live births (SRS 2020) 
• 
Neonatal Mortality Rate: 20/1000 (SRS 2020) 
 
3. Aging Population — New Health Burden 
• 
Proportion of elderly (60+ years) rising: 8.6% (2011 census) → projected 20% by 2050 
• 
Rise of age-related conditions: hypertension, dementia, COPD, osteoporosis, falls 
• 
Need for geriatric health services, old-age homes, palliative care 
• 
National Programme for Health Care of the Elderly (NPHCE) — launched 2010 
 
4. Life Expectancy at Birth 
• 
India: 69.7 years (2015-19); Male: 68.2; Female: 71.1 
• 
Increasing life expectancy → more years lived with chronic disease (disability-adjusted life)

Q7. Describe Significance of Demographic Dividend in India's Development. ★★★ 
(2023) 
 
Definition of Demographic Dividend 
Demographic Dividend is the accelerated economic growth potential resulting from a shift in a 
population's age structure — specifically, when the proportion of working-age population (15–64 years) 
is LARGER than the non-working age population (children + elderly). This creates a 'window of 
opportunity' for rapid economic growth. 
 
India's Demographic Window of Opportunity 
• 
India's working-age population (15–64 yrs): ~65% of total population 
• 
Median age: 28.4 years (one of the world's youngest) 
• 
Dependency ratio: ~48 per 100 working-age persons (improving) 
• 
Window of Opportunity: Estimated 2005–2055 — India has 3 DECADES remaining 
 
Significance for India's Development 
 
1. Economic Growth: More workers → higher production → higher GDP. East Asian 'Tiger' 
economies gained 25–40% of their GDP growth from demographic dividend (1960s–1990s). 
2. Higher Savings Rate: Working-age adults save more than children/elderly → capital 
accumulation → investment. 
3. Human Capital Development: Smaller families = more investment per child in education and 
health → higher productivity. 
4. Women's Empowerment: Lower fertility → more women in workforce → double income 
households. 
5. Innovation and Entrepreneurship: Young workforce drives technological innovation (IT, 
startups — India's strength). 
6. Better Health Outcomes: Working-age adults generally healthier → reduced healthcare burden. 
 
Conditions for Realizing Demographic Dividend 
• 
Quality education and skill development (NEP 2020, Skill India Mission) 
• 
Adequate job creation (need 8-10 million jobs/year) 
• 
Healthcare investment to keep workforce healthy 
• 
Women's participation in workforce 
• 
Good governance and rule of law 
 
Challenges 
• 
Unemployment and underemployment — youth bulge without jobs = liability not asset 
• 
Skill mismatch — education not aligned with market needs 
• 
Regional disparity — North India still has young population, South aging 
• 
COVID-19 setback on education and employment 
 
130


--- PAGE 143 ---

swaDesh
UNIT 16: PRIMARY HEALTH CARE / COMMUNITY 
PARTICIPATION 
 
Q1. Define PHC and Discuss Community Participation in Health and Its Advantages. 
★★★★★ (2020) 
 
Definition of Primary Health Care (PHC) 
As per the Alma-Ata Declaration (1978): 'Primary Health Care is essential health care based on practical, 
scientifically sound and socially acceptable methods and technology made universally accessible to 
individuals and families in the community through their full participation and at a cost that the community 
and country can afford to maintain at every stage of their development in the spirit of self-reliance and 
self-determination.' 
 
Key Elements of PHC (8 Pillars — SAFE CARE) 
Element 
Description 
Safe water & sanitation 
Clean drinking water, basic sanitation infrastructure 
Adequate nutrition 
Food security and nutritional supplementation 
Family welfare 
Maternal & child health, family planning services 
Education about health 
Health education and health promotion 
Control of endemic diseases 
Malaria, TB, leprosy, etc. 
Appropriate treatment 
Treatment of common diseases and injuries 
Referral services 
Link to secondary and tertiary care 
Expanded Programme on 
Immunization 
Immunization of children and mothers 
 
Community Participation in Health — Definition 
Community participation refers to the process by which individuals and communities take an active role 
in identifying health problems, planning and implementing solutions, and evaluating their effectiveness. 
It is a CORNERSTONE of PHC philosophy. 
 
Levels of Community Participation (Arnstein's Ladder) 
• 
Level 1 — Manipulation: Community is manipulated to accept top-down decisions. Lowest form — 
NOT true participation. 
• 
Level 2 — Information/Consultation: Community is informed or consulted but decisions remain 
with authorities. 
• 
Level 3 — Placation: Community advice is considered but may not be acted upon. 
• 
Level 4 — Partnership: Community co-manages programs — shared decision-making. 
• 
Level 5 — Delegated Power: Community controls major aspects of the health program. 
131


--- PAGE 144 ---

swaDesh
• 
Level 6 — Community Control: Community has full autonomous control. Highest form — TRUE 
empowerment. 
 
Advantages of Community Participation in Health 
 
1. Relevance and Appropriateness: Health programs designed with community input address 
actual felt needs rather than perceived needs of planners. 
2. Increased Utilization: Services designed with community involvement have better uptake — 
people use what they helped create. 
3. Sustainability: Community ownership ensures programs continue even after external support 
ends. 
4. Cost-effectiveness: Community resources (labor, local knowledge, volunteers like ASHA) 
reduce program costs dramatically. 
5. Social Mobilization: Collective action addresses social determinants of health (water, sanitation, 
nutrition) more effectively. 
6. Empowerment: Communities develop capacity to identify and solve problems independently. 
7. Health Equity: Marginalized communities can advocate for their specific health needs. 
8. Monitoring and Accountability: Community members can monitor program quality and hold 
providers accountable. 
 
Examples in India 
• 
ASHA (Accredited Social Health Activist) — community health worker embedded in villages; trained 
local woman; 1 per 1000 population 
• 
Village Health Sanitation & Nutrition Committees (VHSNCs) — community governance of health 
• 
Jan Arogya Samitis — hospital-level community committees 
• 
Mahila Arogya Samitis — women's health groups under NHM 
 
★ PHC cannot succeed without community participation. The Alma-Ata Declaration's vision of 
'Health for All by 2000' and the Astana Declaration (2018) renewal both emphasize community 
participation as non-negotiable. India's ASHA program is the world's largest community 
health worker program. 
 
 
 
132


--- PAGE 145 ---

swaDesh
Q2. Role of Community in PHC. Differentiate Community Involvement vs Community 
Participation. ★★★★ (2021) 
 
Role of Community in PHC 
 
1. Needs Assessment: Community members identify health priorities relevant to their local context 
(not just epidemiological data). 
2. Planning: Contribute local knowledge, cultural perspectives, and resource assessment to health 
program design. 
3. Resource Mobilization: Contribute labor, land, money, and materials; reduce dependency on 
government funding. 
4. Implementation: ASHAs, traditional birth attendants (TBAs), village health committees 
implement programs at ground level. 
5. Monitoring & Evaluation: Community monitors quality of services received; provides feedback; 
holds system accountable. 
6. Advocacy: Communities advocate for their rights to health services and voice grievances. 
7. Social Norms Change: Community leaders and groups promote health-seeking behavior, 
immunization acceptance, hygiene practices. 
 
Community Involvement vs Community Participation — Differentiation 
 
Parameter

designed by external agencies

evaluation

the health center

★ Community involvement = community as MEANS to achieve externally set goals. 
Community participation = community as END in itself — empowerment and self-reliance. 
PHC demands participation, not merely involvement.

Q3. How Can Communities Be More Involved in Making Decisions About Their Own 
Health and Wellbeing? ★★★★ (Dec 2024) ★NEW★ 
 
Strategies for Greater Community Decision-Making in Health 
 
1. Strengthening Community Health Institutions 
• 
Village Health, Sanitation and Nutrition Committees (VHSNCs) — decentralize health decisions to 
gram panchayat level 
• 
Rogi Kalyan Samitis (Patient Welfare Committees) at health facilities — community oversight of 
hospital governance 
• 
Mahila Arogya Samitis — women-led groups for reproductive and child health decisions 
 
2. Community Needs Assessment and Participatory Research 
• 
Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) tools: Focus Group Discussions (FGDs), social mapping, 
seasonal calendars 
• 
Community diagnosis involving local people in data collection and priority-setting 
• 
Community scorecards — self-assessment tools for monitoring health program quality 
 
3. Training and Capacity Building 
• 
ASHA training modules include community mobilization and advocacy skills 
• 
Panchayati Raj members trained in health governance 
• 
Digital health literacy — enable communities to access health information 
 
4. Legal and Policy Mechanisms 
• 
Right to Information (RTI) Act — communities can demand health data 
• 
Jan Sunwai (Public Hearings) — community forums to voice health grievances 
• 
Social audit of health schemes (MGNREGA model applied to health) 
 
5. Intersectoral Action 
• 
Health decisions cannot be separated from water, sanitation, education, livelihood 
• 
Multi-sectoral convergence at village level: ASHA + Anganwadi worker + school teacher + ANM 
• 
Swachh Bharat Mission — community decision on toilet construction 
 
6. Addressing Barriers to Participation 
• 
Include marginalized groups: women, SC/ST, elderly, migrants 
• 
Conduct meetings at times/places accessible to all community members 
• 
Use local language — avoid medical jargon 
• 
Address power dynamics — prevent elite capture of community committees 
 
7. Technology-Enabled Community Engagement 
134


--- PAGE 147 ---

swaDesh
• 
mHealth apps in local languages for health information and feedback 
• 
Aarogya Setu, CoWIN — digital community health platforms 
• 
Community radio stations for health promotion 
 
★ The Declaration of Astana (2018) reaffirms that communities must be empowered — not 
just consulted — in health decisions. Key mechanisms: VHSNCs, social audits, PRAs, and 
RTI. Equity requires special focus on women, tribals, and the poor.

Q4. Critical Review of Role and Responsibilities of [Health Workers in PHC] 
 
Key Health Workers in PHC — Roles and Responsibilities 
 
1. ASHA (Accredited Social Health Activist) 
Selection: Literate woman from the village (preferably 10th pass); 1 per 1000 rural population; 
selected by gram sabha 
Training: 23 modules; trained by ANM/LHV; performance-based incentives 
 
Key Responsibilities: 
• 
Community mobilization for immunization, ANC, institutional delivery (JSY) 
• 
Home visits — identify pregnant women, sick children, TB suspects 
• 
Facilitate access to health services — escort and link community to PHC 
• 
Health education on ORS, nutrition, WASH, contraception 
• 
Drug distribution: ORS packets, iron tablets, contraceptives, chlorine tablets 
• 
CBAC form filling for NCD screening 
 
Critical Review of ASHA: 
• 
STRENGTHS: World's largest CHW program (~1 million ASHAs); proven impact on IMR, MMR, 
immunization 
• 
CHALLENGES: Overloaded with multiple programs; inadequate and irregular incentives; lack of 
career growth; burnout 
 
2. ANM (Auxiliary Nurse Midwife) 
Position: Works at Sub-Centre (SC) level; 1 SC per 3000-5000 rural population 
Key Responsibilities: 
• 
Conduct deliveries and antenatal/postnatal care 
• 
Immunization — cold chain maintenance, outreach sessions 
• 
Family planning counselling and method provision 
• 
Malaria slide collection, TB sputum collection 
• 
Maintain vital statistics (births, deaths) 
• 
Supervise and support ASHA workers 
 
3. Medical Officer (PHC) 
Position: 1 MO per PHC (population 20,000-30,000 plains; 20,000 tribal/hilly) 
Key Responsibilities: 
• 
Outpatient consultation — diagnosis and treatment of common diseases 
• 
Minor surgical procedures — suturing, abscess drainage 
• 
Supervision of all health staff at PHC and sub-centres 
• 
Referral decisions to CHC/District Hospital 
• 
National health program implementation and reporting 
136


--- PAGE 149 ---

swaDesh
• 
Community health education and awareness activities 
 
4. Health Inspector / Sanitary Inspector 
• 
Environmental sanitation — safe water, solid waste management, vector control 
• 
Food safety inspections 
• 
Epidemic investigation and reporting

    TOPIC 17: DESCRIPTIVE EPIDEMIOLOGY / TIME TRENDS 
  
 
  Q1. Define epidemiology. Describe procedures involved in conducting descriptive 
studies. 
  ★★★★★   Year: 2021, 2018   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Epidemiology is the cornerstone of public health and preventive medicine. It provides a scientific framework 
for understanding the distribution of diseases and the factors that influence them. The knowledge generated 
by epidemiology guides the planning and evaluation of public health interventions and health care services. 
DEFINITION OF EPIDEMIOLOGY 
The most widely accepted definition is that of John M. Last (2001): "Epidemiology is the study of the 
distribution and determinants of health-related states or events in specified populations, and the application 
of this study to the control of health problems." 
Distribution: refers to the frequency and pattern of health events in terms of Person, Place, and Time. 
Determinants: refers to the causal factors — biological, physical, social, or environmental — that influence 
the occurrence of disease. 
Specified populations: emphasizes the population-based (not individual) nature of epidemiology. 
Application: epidemiology is always goal-directed — to prevent and control disease. 
SCOPE & AIMS OF EPIDEMIOLOGY 
● To describe the distribution of disease in terms of Person, Place, and Time 
● To identify the determinants and risk factors of disease 
139

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
● To study the natural history and prognosis of disease 
● To evaluate preventive and therapeutic measures 
● To aid in health planning and policy formulation 
TYPES OF EPIDEMIOLOGIC STUDIES 
Type

PROCEDURES IN CONDUCTING A DESCRIPTIVE STUDY 
A descriptive study systematically collects and analyzes data to describe the occurrence of a disease or 
health event in a population. The following steps are followed: 
Step 1: Define the Problem 
The health problem to be studied is clearly defined. This includes specifying the disease or health event, 
identifying the affected population, and the time period of interest. Operational definitions of the case must 
be established (e.g., clinical, laboratory, or epidemiological definitions). 
Step 2: Review of Existing Literature 
A comprehensive review of published literature, surveillance data, health records, vital statistics, and 
hospital registers is done to understand what is already known about the disease. This helps identify gaps 
in knowledge and formulate research questions. 
Step 3: Define the Study Population 
The target population is clearly identified. Inclusion and exclusion criteria are established. Sampling 
methods (random, systematic, stratified) are applied to select representative subjects to ensure 
generalizability. 
Step 4: Data Collection 
Data are gathered using primary or secondary methods. Primary data are collected through questionnaires, 
interviews, physical examinations, and biological specimens. Secondary data come from hospital records, 
disease registries, census data, and mortality statistics. Variables collected include: 
● Person variables: Age, sex, race, ethnicity, socioeconomic status, occupation, marital status, 
religion, lifestyle. 
● Place variables: Country, region, urban/rural classification, geographic clusters, work environment. 
● Time variables: Onset of illness, seasonal patterns, epidemic curves, secular trends. 
Step 5: Data Organization & Analysis 
Collected data are tabulated, classified, and analyzed using appropriate statistical methods. Rates and 
ratios (incidence rate, prevalence rate, attack rate, mortality rate, case fatality rate) are calculated. Graphs, 
maps, spot maps, and epidemic curves are constructed to visualize patterns. 
Step 6: Interpretation & Hypothesis Generation 
Patterns identified are interpreted in terms of Person, Place, and Time. From the observed patterns, 
hypotheses about the causative factors are generated. Descriptive studies do NOT test hypotheses — they 
only generate them for further analytical investigation. 
Step 7: Report and Communicate Findings 
140


--- PAGE 153 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Findings are documented and communicated to relevant stakeholders — public health authorities, the 
scientific community, and policy makers. Recommendations for preventive and control measures are made 
based on the findings. 
EXAM TIP: Remember: Descriptive studies answer WHO, WHERE, and WHEN. Analytical studies answer 
WHY and HOW. RCTs answer whether an intervention WORKS. This hierarchy is frequently tested. 
CONCLUSION: Descriptive epidemiology is the foundation of public health practice. By systematically 
collecting, organizing, and analyzing data on Person, Place, and Time, it generates hypotheses that 
analytical studies then test, ultimately leading to disease prevention and health promotion. 
 
 
  Q2. Classify epidemiologic studies. Describe steps in conducting a cohort study. 
  ★★★★   Year: 2023   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Epidemiologic studies are systematic investigations designed to describe or analyze the distribution and 
determinants of diseases in human populations. They are broadly classified into Observational Studies and 
Experimental Studies. 
CLASSIFICATION OF EPIDEMIOLOGIC STUDIES 
I. Observational Studies 
The investigator does not intervene; they merely observe events as they occur naturally. 
A. Descriptive Studies: Describe the frequency and distribution of disease without testing any specific 
hypothesis. 
● Case Report: Detailed clinical description of a single patient with an unusual disease. 
● Case Series: Description of a small group of patients with the same condition. 
● Cross-Sectional (Prevalence) Study: Measures disease and exposure simultaneously at one point 
in time. Gives Prevalence. 
B. Analytical Studies: Test specific hypotheses about the relationship between exposure and disease. 
● Case-Control Study: Begins with disease (cases) vs. no disease (controls); looks backward at 
exposure. Gives Odds Ratio (OR). Best for RARE diseases. 
● Cohort Study: Begins with exposure; follows forward for disease outcome. Gives Relative Risk 
(RR). Best for RARE exposures. 
II. Experimental Studies 
The investigator actively intervenes and controls the exposure or treatment. 
● Randomized Controlled Trial (RCT): Gold standard. Participants randomly assigned to treatment or 
control group. 
● Field Trial: Conducted in the community among healthy people at risk (e.g., vaccine trials). 
● Community Trial: Entire communities assigned to interventions (e.g., fluoridation of water).

(Retrospective)

COHORT STUDY — DEFINITION 
A cohort study is an observational study in which a defined group of people (a cohort) who share a common 
characteristic or experience (the exposure) are followed over time to determine the rate at which disease 
or other outcomes occur. It proceeds from Cause to Effect (Prospective direction). 
Types of cohort studies: (1) Prospective Cohort — exposure assessed at baseline, followed into the future. 
(2) Retrospective (Historical) Cohort — historical records used to identify past exposure and trace outcome. 
STEPS IN CONDUCTING A COHORT STUDY 
Step 1: Selection of the Cohorts 
Two groups are identified at the outset: the Exposed Cohort (individuals with the risk factor or exposure, 
e.g., smokers) and the Unexposed Cohort (individuals without the exposure, e.g., non-smokers). Critically, 
both groups must be FREE FROM THE DISEASE under study at the time of enrollment. Controls should 
be similar to cases in all respects except the exposure. 
Step 2: Collection of Baseline Data 
Detailed information is recorded at baseline including: nature and degree of exposure, demographic details 
(age, sex, occupation, socioeconomic status), potential confounding variables, and current health status. 
Standardized methods (questionnaires, biological markers, environmental measurements) are used to 
minimize information bias. 
Step 3: Follow-up of Both Cohorts 
Both exposed and unexposed groups are followed over a defined period (months to decades depending 
on the disease latency period). Regular contact is maintained through periodic examinations, 
questionnaires, telephone calls, or review of records. Losses to follow-up must be minimized as they 
introduce attrition bias. Methods to minimize loss: reminder letters, incentives, multiple contact methods, 
tracing through records. 
Step 4: Assessment of Outcome 
The occurrence of the disease (outcome) is documented in both groups using standardized, pre-defined 
criteria. Outcome assessors should ideally be blinded to the exposure status to prevent observer bias. 
Step 5: Data Analysis 
The following measures are calculated from a 2x2 contingency table: 
● Incidence in Exposed (Ie) = a / (a + b) 
● Incidence in Unexposed (Iue) = c / (c + d) 
● Relative Risk (RR) = Ie / Iue  — Measures strength of association 
● Attributable Risk (AR) = Ie − Iue  — Measures excess risk due to exposure 
● Population Attributable Risk (PAR) = Incidence in total population − Iue 
● Attributable Risk Percent (AR%) = (AR / Ie) x 100 
Step 6: Interpretation & Conclusion 
If RR > 1: Exposure is a risk factor. If RR < 1: Exposure is protective. If RR = 1: No association. The 
magnitude of RR reflects the strength of the association. Dose-response relationship, consistency, and 
biological plausibility are assessed to determine causality. 
142


--- PAGE 155 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content

EXAM TIP: Cohort = RR. Case-Control = OR. When RR and OR are quoted together, note that OR 
approximates RR only when the disease is rare (rare disease assumption). 
CONCLUSION: Cohort studies provide the strongest observational evidence for causation by establishing 
temporal sequence. While costly and time-consuming, they are indispensable for studying exposures with 
long latency periods and for calculating true incidence rates. 
 
 
  Q3. How do you classify Descriptive Epidemiology? Describe TIME TRENDS. 
  ★★★   Year: 2011   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Descriptive epidemiology systematically characterizes the occurrence and distribution of diseases in a 
population. The three pillars of descriptive epidemiology are Person, Place, and Time — collectively forming 
the epidemiological triad of distribution. Time trends are one of the most informative dimensions of disease 
distribution. 
CLASSIFICATION OF DESCRIPTIVE EPIDEMIOLOGY 
I. By Person 
Person characteristics define who is affected by the disease. These include: 
● Age: The most important determinant; most diseases show age-specific incidence patterns. 
● Sex/Gender: Biological differences (hormones, immunity) and sociocultural roles influence disease 
patterns. E.g., coronary artery disease is more common in males. 
● Race and Ethnicity: Influence both genetic susceptibility and socioeconomic exposure. 
● Socioeconomic Status (SES): Income, education, and occupation determine exposure to risk 
factors and access to healthcare. 
● Occupation: Determines specific environmental and chemical exposures. E.g., silicosis in miners. 
● Marital Status & Religion: Influence lifestyle, reproductive patterns, and dietary habits. 
● Lifestyle habits: Smoking, alcohol use, diet, physical activity. 
II. By Place 
Place characteristics identify geographic variation in disease occurrence: 
● International variation: Comparing disease rates across countries reveals the role of genetic, 
cultural, and environmental factors. 
● National/Regional variation: Disease burden differs between states or regions. 
143


--- PAGE 156 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
● Urban vs Rural: Urban areas have higher rates of NCDs; rural areas may have higher 
communicable disease burden. 
● Local clustering: Spot maps help identify disease clusters around a common source (e.g., water 
supply). 
● Migrant studies: Migrants who adopt the disease pattern of the host country confirm environmental 
over genetic causation. 
III. By Time 
Time trends reveal how the disease frequency changes over different time scales. This is described in 
detail below. 
TIME TRENDS IN EPIDEMIOLOGY 
Time trends (temporal variations) refer to patterns of disease occurrence over time. They are crucial for 
generating hypotheses about disease causation, identifying risk factors, evaluating the impact of public 
health interventions, and predicting future disease burden. 
Time trends are classified into three major types: 
1. Short-Term Fluctuations (Epidemic Variations) 
These represent sudden increases in disease frequency over a short period. They are best represented by 
an EPIDEMIC CURVE (number of cases plotted against time of onset). 
a) Common Source Epidemic (Point Source Epidemic): Occurs when a group of people are all exposed 
to the same source at approximately the same time. The epidemic curve shows a rapid rise and rapid fall, 
often within a single incubation period. Example: Food poisoning at a wedding. 
b) Propagated (Progressive Source) Epidemic: Occurs when disease spreads from person to person 
through multiple generations of transmission. The epidemic curve shows multiple waves, each separated 
by an incubation period. The tail of the curve is longer than the rise. Example: Measles, chickenpox, 
influenza. 
c) Mixed Epidemic: Begins as a common source epidemic but is then propagated person-to-person. 
Example: Cholera (contaminated water source, then fecal-oral spread). 
d) Continuous Source Epidemic: Prolonged exposure to a source over time produces a plateau curve. 
Example: Skin rash from chronic exposure to a contaminated water supply. 
2. Cyclic (Periodic) Variations 
These are predictable fluctuations in disease occurrence over regular intervals: 
a) Seasonal Variation: Disease incidence increases predictably during certain seasons due to 
environmental changes affecting the agent, host, or vector. Examples: Respiratory infections (winter 
months — dry cold air, indoor crowding); Malaria and dengue (monsoon — breeding of mosquitoes); 
Diarrheal diseases (summer — food spoilage); Meningococcal meningitis (dry, dusty season). 
b) Cyclic (Inter-annual) Variation: Some diseases recur in cycles of several years, related to build-up of 
susceptible individuals in the population after previous outbreaks. Examples: Influenza pandemics 
(approximately every 10–15 years); Measles (every 2–3 years in unvaccinated populations). 
3. Secular (Long-Term) Trends 
Secular trends are gradual changes in disease frequency over a period of decades. They reflect the 
cumulative effect of changes in lifestyle, environment, medical advances, socioeconomic conditions, and 
public health programs. 
Examples of rising secular trends: Cardiovascular diseases, type 2 diabetes, obesity, mental health 
disorders — due to sedentary lifestyles, urbanization, and dietary changes. 
Examples of declining secular trends: Tuberculosis (decades before antibiotics — due to improved 
nutrition and living standards); Poliomyelitis (vaccination programs); Smallpox (eradication by 1980). 
144


--- PAGE 157 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Secular trends are extremely valuable for: Evaluating the long-term effectiveness of vaccination programs 
and health policies; Planning and allocation of health resources; Understanding the epidemiological 
transition from communicable to non-communicable diseases.

EXAM TIP: Mnemonics for Time Trends: SPACE = Short-term (epidemic), Periodic (cyclic), And secular 
(long-term) Cyclical Events. Epidemic curves are the most common MCQ topic on Time Trends. 
CONCLUSION: Time trends are powerful tools in epidemiology. Short-term fluctuations help identify and 
control epidemics. Cyclic trends guide seasonal health programs (e.g., vector control, vaccination). Secular 
trends reflect the success of public health programs and signal emerging health challenges like the non-
communicable disease epidemic. 
 
 
  Q4. Discuss Relative Risk vs Attributable Risk. 
  ★★★   Year: 2018   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Relative Risk (RR) and Attributable Risk (AR) are two fundamental measures of disease association 
derived from cohort studies. While both are calculated from the same 2x2 contingency table, they serve 
different purposes. RR measures the STRENGTH of an association (etiological significance), while AR 
measures the PUBLIC HEALTH IMPACT of an exposure (preventive significance). 
THE 2x2 CONTINGENCY TABLE 
 
Disease (+) 
Disease (−) 
Total 
Exposed (+) 
a 
b 
a + b 
Unexposed (−) 
c 
d 
c + d 
Total 
a + c 
b + d 
N 
 
Incidence in Exposed (Ie): a / (a + b) 
Incidence in Unexposed (Iue): c / (c + d) 
RELATIVE RISK (RR) 
Definition 
145


--- PAGE 158 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Relative Risk (also called Risk Ratio) is the ratio of the incidence of disease in the exposed group to the 
incidence of disease in the non-exposed group. It is the primary measure of association in cohort studies 
and clinical trials. 
Formula: RR = Ie / Iue = [a/(a+b)] / [c/(c+d)] 
Interpretation of RR 
● RR = 1: No association between exposure and disease. 
● RR > 1: Positive association — exposure INCREASES the risk of disease (risk factor). Example: 
RR of 10 for smoking and lung cancer means smokers are 10 times more likely to develop lung 
cancer. 
● RR < 1: Negative association — exposure DECREASES the risk of disease (protective factor). 
Example: RR of 0.3 for exercise and heart disease means physically active people have 70% lower 
risk. 
Significance of RR 
● Measures the STRENGTH of association between exposure and disease. 
● Essential for establishing causation (Bradford Hill criteria — strength of association). 
● Used to identify etiological (causative) agents. 
● Applied in clinical risk assessment and evidence-based medicine. 
● RR is not calculated in case-control studies (Odds Ratio is used instead). 
ATTRIBUTABLE RISK (AR) 
Definition 
Attributable Risk (also called Risk Difference or Excess Risk) is the absolute difference in the incidence of 
disease between the exposed and unexposed groups. It represents the portion of disease incidence in the 
exposed group that is attributable to the exposure — the excess risk due solely to the exposure. 
Formula: AR = Ie − Iue = [a/(a+b)] − [c/(c+d)] 
Interpretation of AR 
AR quantifies the actual excess number of cases per unit population that can be attributed to the exposure. 
Example: If Ie = 70 per 1000 and Iue = 10 per 1000, then AR = 60 per 1000 — meaning 60 extra cases per 
1000 are due to the exposure. 
Attributable Risk Percent (AR%) 
Formula: AR% = (AR / Ie) x 100 = [(Ie − Iue) / Ie] x 100 
AR% estimates the proportion of disease in the exposed group that could be eliminated if the exposure 
were removed. Example: AR% of 85% for smoking and lung cancer in smokers means 85% of lung cancer 
cases in smokers are due to smoking. 
Population Attributable Risk (PAR) 
Formula: PAR = Incidence in total population (It) − Iue 
PAR estimates the excess rate of disease in the entire population attributable to the exposure. It helps 
prioritize public health interventions by identifying how much disease burden could be prevented at the 
population level. 
Population Attributable Risk Percent (PAR%): PAR% = (PAR / It) x 100 
COMPARISON: RELATIVE RISK vs ATTRIBUTABLE RISK 
Feature

Definition

Formula 
Ie / Iue 
Ie − Iue
