PRACTICAL EXAMPLE 
A cohort study on smoking (exposed) vs non-smoking (unexposed) and lung cancer: 
● Incidence in smokers (Ie) = 70 per 1,000 
● Incidence in non-smokers (Iue) = 10 per 1,000 
● RR = 70/10 = 7 — Smokers are 7 times more likely to develop lung cancer 
● AR = 70 − 10 = 60 per 1,000 — 60 extra cases per 1,000 are due to smoking 
● AR% = (60/70) x 100 = 85.7% — 85.7% of lung cancer in smokers is attributable to smoking 
EXAM TIP: RR = Strength of causation (important for the researcher identifying causes). AR = Public health 
importance (important for the policy maker planning prevention). Both are derived from cohort studies. OR 
(Odds Ratio) is the case-control equivalent of RR. 
CONCLUSION: RR and AR are complementary measures. A high RR identifies a strong causal link (e.g., 
smoking and lung cancer). A high AR identifies high-priority targets for prevention programs (e.g., how 
many deaths can be prevented by smoking cessation). Together, they provide a comprehensive picture of 
disease causation and prevention potential. 
 
 
 
    TOPIC 18: DETERMINANTS OF HEALTH 
  
 
  Q1. Enumerate determinants of health. Discuss behavioral determinants for prevention of 
COVID-19. 
  ★★★★★   Year: 2020   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Health is a state of complete physical, mental, and social well-being — not merely the absence of disease 
(WHO, 1948). The level of health in any community is determined by a complex interplay of multiple factors 
called the determinants of health. Understanding these determinants is essential for designing effective 
preventive and promotive health interventions. 
DEFINITION OF HEALTH DETERMINANTS 
"Determinants of health are a range of personal, social, economic, and environmental factors that 
determine the health status of individuals or populations." — WHO 
147


--- PAGE 160 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
ENUMERATION OF DETERMINANTS OF HEALTH 
Health determinants are best understood through the Lalonde Model (1974), which categorizes them into 
four broad fields: 
1. Biological and Genetic Factors 
These are intrinsic, largely non-modifiable host factors that influence susceptibility and resistance to 
disease. 
● Age: Disease patterns change throughout life — infections predominate in childhood; NCDs in 
middle age and old age. 
● Sex: Biological differences (hormonal, immunological) influence disease susceptibility. E.g., women 
have stronger immune responses; men have higher cardiovascular risk before menopause. 
● Genetic constitution: Inherited disorders (sickle cell anaemia, thalassemia, hemophilia), genetic 
predisposition to cancer, diabetes, hypertension. 
● Immune status: Natural immunity, vaccination status, immune deficiencies influence disease 
occurrence. 
2. Behavioral / Lifestyle Factors 
These are modifiable determinants related to individual behaviors and choices. They are increasingly 
important given the global rise of non-communicable diseases (NCDs). 
● Smoking: Leading cause of preventable death globally — lung cancer, COPD, cardiovascular 
disease. 
● Diet and Nutrition: High-fat, high-sugar diets → obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular disease. 
Malnutrition → stunting, micronutrient deficiencies. 
● Physical activity: Sedentary lifestyle → obesity, hypertension, depression. Regular exercise is 
protective against NCDs. 
● Alcohol and substance use: Liver cirrhosis, accidents, mental illness, fetal alcohol syndrome. 
● Sexual behavior: Unsafe sex → STIs including HIV/AIDS. 
● Sleep patterns: Inadequate sleep → metabolic syndrome, cardiovascular disease, mental health 
problems. 
● Health-seeking behavior: Delayed care-seeking, non-adherence to treatment worsens health 
outcomes. 
3. Environmental Factors 
Physical Environment: Air quality (air pollution → respiratory disease), water quality (contamination → 
diarrheal disease), sanitation, climate change, housing quality, occupational exposures (chemicals, dust, 
radiation). 
Social Environment: Social cohesion, community norms, social networks, peer influence, family structure, 
culture, and religion all shape behaviors and health outcomes. 
Economic Environment: Income level, wealth distribution, economic stability determine access to 
nutritious food, safe housing, healthcare, and education. 
4. Health Services 
● Availability: Presence of hospitals, clinics, trained health workers. 
● Accessibility: Geographic, financial, cultural accessibility of services. 
● Quality: Adequacy of diagnosis, treatment, referral systems. 
● Preventive services: Immunization programs, screening, antenatal care. 
5. Socioeconomic and Political Factors 
● Education: Higher education → better health literacy, healthier behaviors, better employment. 
● Income and poverty: Poverty is the single greatest determinant of poor health globally. 
148


--- PAGE 161 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
● Employment: Unemployment → stress, poverty, poor nutrition. Hazardous occupations → 
occupational diseases. 
● Governance and policy: Health legislation, welfare policies, social security systems. 
● Globalization: Trade, migration, and cultural exchange create new health challenges. 
BEHAVIORAL DETERMINANTS FOR PREVENTION OF COVID-19 
COVID-19 (caused by SARS-CoV-2) was declared a global pandemic by WHO in March 2020. The virus 
spreads primarily via respiratory droplets, aerosols, and contact with contaminated surfaces. Since specific 
antiviral treatment was initially unavailable, behavioral modification became the PRIMARY intervention for 
disease control. The following behavioral measures were essential: 
1. Hand Hygiene 
Frequent handwashing with soap and water for at least 20 seconds effectively inactivates the virus. Alcohol-
based hand sanitizers (minimum 60% alcohol) were used when soap was unavailable. People were 
instructed to wash hands: after coughing/sneezing, before touching face, after contact with surfaces, before 
and after eating, after using toilet, and after coming from public spaces. 
2. Respiratory Etiquette 
Covering the mouth and nose with the bent elbow or a tissue while coughing or sneezing prevents droplet 
spread. Used tissues were to be discarded immediately in closed bins and hands washed subsequently. 
Spitting in public places was prohibited. 
3. Face Masking 
Wearing a well-fitting mask (N95 respirator, 3-ply surgical mask, or cloth mask in resource-limited settings) 
significantly reduces transmission both from and to the wearer. Masks were mandatory in healthcare 
settings, public transport, markets, and crowded places. Double masking (cloth mask over surgical mask) 
was recommended for higher protection. 
4. Physical/Social Distancing 
Maintaining a minimum distance of at least 1 meter (WHO) to 6 feet (CDC) from others reduces droplet 
transmission. Avoidance of crowded gatherings, parties, weddings, religious congregations, and public 
events was advised. Work-from-home policies were implemented to reduce workplace transmission. 
5. Ventilation and Avoiding Poorly Ventilated Spaces 
SARS-CoV-2 can spread via aerosols in enclosed, poorly ventilated spaces. Opening windows and doors 
to ensure cross-ventilation, using air purifiers with HEPA filters, preferring outdoor settings over indoor for 
meetings were all recommended. Air conditioning systems without fresh air intake were identified as 
transmission risks. 
6. Avoiding Touching Face 
The virus can be transferred from contaminated surfaces to mucous membranes of eyes, nose, and mouth. 
People were educated not to touch their face, especially without hand hygiene. This addressed fomite 
transmission. 
7. Self-Isolation and Quarantine 
Isolation: Confirmed COVID-positive individuals isolated at home or in COVID care facilities for a minimum 
of 10 days (or until symptoms resolved) to prevent spread to others. 
Quarantine: Contacts of confirmed cases quarantined for 14 days (the maximum incubation period) to 
monitor for symptoms. 
8. Vaccination 
COVID-19 vaccines (Covishield, Covaxin, Pfizer-BioNTech, Moderna, Sputnik V, Johnson & Johnson) were 
deployed globally. Vaccination significantly reduced severe disease, hospitalization, ICU admission, and 
149


--- PAGE 162 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
death. Full vaccination including booster doses was promoted. Vaccine hesitancy was addressed through 
community education. 
9. Testing and Contact Tracing 
Early testing (RT-PCR, Rapid Antigen Test) of symptomatic individuals and contacts helped identify and 
isolate cases early. Contact tracing (identifying and monitoring all contacts of confirmed cases) was a 
cornerstone of outbreak control. Digital tools and Aarogya Setu (India) app aided in contact tracing. 
10. Healthy Lifestyle Behaviors 
● Adequate nutrition to support immune function. 
● Regular physical activity (home-based exercises during lockdowns). 
● Stress management and mental health support (pandemic-related anxiety, depression). 
● Adequate sleep (7-9 hours) to maintain immune function. 
● Avoiding alcohol and tobacco which impair immune response. 
EXAM TIP: Remember the COVID prevention framework: HANDS (hygiene) + FACE (mask + no face 
touching) + SPACE (distancing) + VENTILATION + VACCINATION. This 5-point framework covers all 
behavioral determinants comprehensively. 
CONCLUSION: Behavioral determinants were the frontline of defense against COVID-19. A combination of 
hand hygiene, masking, distancing, ventilation, isolation, and vaccination created multiple barriers to 
transmission (Swiss Cheese Model). These non-pharmaceutical interventions (NPIs), though simple, were 
proven to be highly effective in flattening the epidemic curve and reducing mortality. 
 
 
  Q2. What are the determinants of health? 
  ★★★★★   Year: 2022   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
The determinants of health are the broad range of factors that influence the health of individuals and 
communities. Health is not determined by biology or healthcare alone; it is shaped by a complex web of 
interacting factors. Understanding determinants is essential for addressing root causes of ill-health and 
promoting health equity. 
MODELS OF HEALTH DETERMINANTS 
1. Lalonde Model (1974) — Health Field Concept 
Marc Lalonde, Canadian Minister of Health, proposed four determinants of health: Human Biology, 
Environment, Lifestyle/Behavior, and Health Care Organization. This model established that most 
premature deaths are caused by lifestyle and environmental factors, not by biology or lack of healthcare. 
2. Dahlgren and Whitehead's Rainbow Model (1991) 
The most widely cited and examined model. It depicts health determinants as concentric layers around the 
individual, like a rainbow: 
● Core (fixed): Age, sex, and hereditary factors — non-modifiable. 
● Layer 1 — Individual lifestyle factors: Smoking, diet, physical activity, alcohol. These are modifiable 
behaviors. 
● Layer 2 — Social and community networks: Family, friends, social cohesion, social support 
systems. 
● Layer 3 — Living and working conditions: Education, employment, food security, housing, 
healthcare services, work environment, water and sanitation. 
150


--- PAGE 163 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
● Layer 4 (outermost) — General socioeconomic, cultural, and environmental conditions: Economic 
policies, social inequality, cultural norms, environmental quality. 
The model emphasizes that the outer layers (socioeconomic conditions) shape the inner layers (individual 
behaviors), making health a social issue, not just an individual responsibility. 
DETAILED ENUMERATION OF HEALTH DETERMINANTS 
A. Biological and Genetic Factors 
● Age and sex: Determine baseline disease susceptibility. 
● Genetic factors: Hereditary diseases (e.g., thalassemia, PKU), familial predisposition to NCDs. 
● Immune status: Determines susceptibility to infections. 
● Physiological changes: Pregnancy, puberty, menopause — alter health needs and risks. 
B. Behavioral / Lifestyle Factors 
● Tobacco use: Causes lung cancer, COPD, cardiovascular disease, stroke. 
● Diet: Malnutrition causes deficiency diseases; over-nutrition causes obesity and metabolic 
syndrome. 
● Physical activity: Sedentary lifestyle is associated with NCDs. 
● Alcohol and drug abuse: Cause liver disease, accidents, mental illness. 
● Sexual behavior: Risk factor for STIs including HIV. 
C. Physical Environment 
● Air quality: Indoor and outdoor air pollution causes respiratory and cardiovascular disease. 
● Water and sanitation: Contaminated water causes cholera, typhoid, hepatitis A. 
● Climate: Temperature extremes cause heat stroke and hypothermia. Climate change is increasing 
vector-borne disease range. 
● Housing: Overcrowding facilitates spread of TB, scabies, respiratory infections. 
D. Social Environment 
● Social support networks: Strong social ties are protective for mental and physical health. 
● Social norms and culture: Shape behaviors around food, health-seeking, substance use. 
● Social exclusion and discrimination: Marginalized groups have worse health outcomes. 
E. Socioeconomic Determinants 
● Income and poverty: Poverty restricts access to nutritious food, safe housing, and healthcare. 
● Education: Higher education leads to better health literacy and healthier behaviors. 
● Employment: Provides income, identity, and social participation. Unemployment leads to poor 
health. 
● Social security: Social protection programs buffer against health risks of poverty. 
F. Healthcare System 
● Availability: Physical presence of health services in the area. 
● Accessibility: Geographic, financial, cultural, and organizational barriers to care. 
● Quality: Effectiveness, safety, and patient-centeredness of services. 
● Equity: Distribution of services between populations. 
G. Political and Policy Factors 
● Health legislation: Tobacco control laws, food labelling, pollution regulations. 
● Welfare policies: Social safety nets reduce the health impact of poverty. 
● International factors: WHO, World Bank, UNICEF policies influence national health priorities.

EXAM TIP: The Rainbow Model (Dahlgren & Whitehead) is the most frequently tested model for health 
determinants. Know all 5 layers and be able to draw/describe the diagram. The mnemonic: Individual — 
Social — Living — Socioeconomic (ISLS). 
CONCLUSION: Health determinants operate at multiple levels — from the individual cell to global economic 
systems. Addressing health effectively requires a multi-sectoral approach involving health, education, 
environment, employment, and social welfare sectors. The WHO Commission on Social Determinants of 
Health (2008) called for 'closing the gap in a generation' by addressing the root causes of health inequity. 
 
 
  Q3. What are the social determinants of health? 
  ★★★★   Year: 2023   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Social determinants of health (SDOH) are defined by WHO as: "The conditions in which people are born, 
grow, live, work, and age, including the health system. These circumstances are shaped by the distribution 
of money, power, and resources at global, national, and local levels." (WHO Commission on Social 
Determinants of Health, 2008) 
SDOH are responsible for the majority of health inequities observed within and between countries. They 
are the primary drivers of preventable illness, disability, and premature death globally. Unlike biological 
determinants, SDOHs are modifiable through policy action. 
KEY SOCIAL DETERMINANTS OF HEALTH 
1. Income and Social Protection 
Income is the most fundamental social determinant. Low income limits access to adequate nutrition, safe 
housing, health care, and education. Income inequality (the gap between rich and poor) is associated with 
worse population health outcomes even in high-income countries. Social protection programs 
(unemployment benefits, pensions, conditional cash transfers, disability allowances) buffer against the 
health risks of income insecurity. Example: Countries with strong social safety nets (Scandinavian 
countries) consistently report better health outcomes and lower infant mortality. 
2. Education and Literacy 
Education is one of the most powerful determinants of health. It operates through multiple pathways: higher 
education leads to better employment and income, greater health literacy (ability to understand and act on 
health information), healthier behaviors (less smoking, better diet, more exercise), and greater 
empowerment to navigate health systems. Low literacy leads to inability to read prescriptions, difficulty 
understanding health messages, and poor treatment adherence. Female education is particularly important 
152


--- PAGE 165 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
— educated women have fewer children, with better birth spacing, leading to lower maternal and infant 
mortality. Example: A child born to an educated mother is 50% more likely to survive past age 5 (UNESCO). 
3. Unemployment and Employment Conditions 
Unemployment is associated with psychological distress, depression, anxiety, substance abuse, and 
increased mortality. Beyond unemployment, the quality of work matters: hazardous occupations cause 
occupational diseases (silicosis, occupational asthma, lead poisoning). Precarious employment (short-term 
contracts, zero-hour contracts) causes financial insecurity and chronic stress — a significant cardiovascular 
risk factor. Lack of maternity/paternity leave, sick leave, and occupational health services particularly affect 
vulnerable workers. 
4. Housing and Living Conditions 
Adequate, safe housing is essential for health. Poor housing is associated with: Overcrowding — facilitates 
transmission of airborne infections (TB, influenza, meningitis); Damp and mold — causes respiratory 
disease and asthma; Indoor air pollution (biomass cooking fuels) — causes COPD, lung cancer; Unsafe 
water and sanitation — causes diarrheal disease, helminthiasis; Lack of safe outdoor spaces — limits 
physical activity; Living in slums — cumulative disadvantage across multiple determinants. 
5. Food Security and Nutrition 
Food insecurity — uncertain access to adequate, nutritious food — is a major SDOH. Food insecurity leads 
to: Undernutrition, wasting, stunting, and micronutrient deficiencies in low-income settings; Paradoxically, 
obesity and diet-related NCDs in 'food desert' environments where only cheap, calorie-dense, nutrient-poor 
foods are available. Food environments (access to markets, availability of fresh produce, food prices) shape 
dietary behaviors at the community level. Agricultural policies, food subsidies, and social nutrition programs 
directly influence food security. 
6. Social Exclusion and Discrimination 
Social exclusion — the process by which individuals or groups are shut out from full participation in society 
— has profound health effects. Groups facing exclusion include: Racial and ethnic minorities, indigenous 
peoples, migrants and refugees, women and girls, people with disabilities, LGBTQ+ communities, people 
living in poverty. Discrimination causes: Chronic psychosocial stress (activating the HPA axis, elevating 
cortisol) which damages cardiovascular, metabolic, and immune systems; Reduced access to education, 
employment, and healthcare; Fear of discrimination preventing care-seeking (particularly relevant for HIV, 
mental health, TB). 
7. Early Childhood Development 
The first 1,000 days of life (conception to age 2) represent a critical period when nutrition, stimulation, and 
nurturing care lay the foundations for lifelong health. Early childhood deprivation (food insecurity, parental 
stress, lack of stimulation, poverty) produces lasting biological changes (epigenetic modifications, altered 
stress response systems) that increase risk of NCDs, mental illness, and learning difficulties in adult life. 
Investments in early childhood development (ECD programs, maternity/paternity leave, childcare) have 
among the highest returns of any public health intervention. 
8. Social Support and Social Capital 
Social support — the emotional, informational, and practical support provided by family, friends, and 
community — is strongly protective for health. Social capital refers to the norms of trust and reciprocity that 
enable cooperative action within communities. High social capital communities have: Lower mortality, better 
mental health, faster recovery from illness, higher vaccination uptake, and better adherence to health 
behaviors. Social isolation and loneliness are as damaging to health as smoking 15 cigarettes a day (Holt-
Lunstad, 2015). 
9. Gender and Gender Equity 
Gender shapes health through: Biological sex differences (different disease susceptibilities and 
presentations), Gender norms (what is considered 'masculine' or 'feminine' behavior affects care-seeking, 
health risk behaviors), Gender-based violence (domestic violence, sexual assault — major cause of injury, 
153


--- PAGE 166 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
mental illness, reproductive harm in women), Women's autonomy (low decision-making power limits access 
to contraception, antenatal care, and treatment), Men's health (cultural norms of masculinity discourage 
help-seeking, leading to delayed diagnosis of cancer, cardiovascular disease, mental illness). 
10. Globalization, Urbanization, and Migration 
Globalization has accelerated the spread of communicable diseases (e.g., COVID-19 pandemic) and 
diffusion of unhealthy commodities (tobacco, ultra-processed foods, alcohol) globally. Rapid urbanization 
strains health infrastructure and creates megaslums with extreme health disadvantage. Migration creates 
health vulnerabilities: disruption of social networks, exposure to violence, difficulty accessing health 
services due to language barriers and legal status. 
WHO COMMISSION ON SOCIAL DETERMINANTS OF HEALTH (CSDH) — KEY ACTION 
AREAS 
● Improve daily living conditions (nutrition, housing, healthcare, work, education) 
● Tackle the inequitable distribution of power, money, and resources globally and nationally 
● Measure and understand the problem — build a national evidence base on health inequities

EXAM TIP: WHO's Marmot Commission (2008) concluded that most health inequities are avoidable and 
result from social conditions — not biology. The key phrase to quote: 'The conditions in which people are 
born, grow, live, work, and age.' 
CONCLUSION: Social determinants of health are the root causes of health inequities. Addressing them 
requires 'Health in All Policies' — integrating health considerations into education, housing, agriculture, 
transport, and economic policy. Medical care alone cannot close the health gap; structural social change is 
essential for equitable health outcomes. 
 
 
  Q4. Impact of Industrialization and Urbanization on the health of a community. (May 2024) 
★NEW★ 
  ★★★★   Year: 2024   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
154


--- PAGE 167 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Industrialization refers to the process by which an economy transforms from primarily agrarian to one based 
on manufacturing and industry. Urbanization refers to the increasing proportion of a population living in 
urban areas, largely driven by industrialization and rural-to-urban migration. These two processes are 
intimately linked and have profoundly altered the health landscape of communities worldwide. Their impact 
is a double-edged sword — creating both health benefits and significant new health challenges. 
The shift from communicable diseases to non-communicable diseases as the leading cause of morbidity 
and mortality is termed the Epidemiological Transition — driven largely by industrialization and 
urbanization. 
POSITIVE IMPACTS ON COMMUNITY HEALTH 
1. Economic Development and Poverty Reduction 
Industrialization creates employment, raises incomes, and reduces poverty. Higher income allows 
communities to afford nutritious food, better housing, clean water, and healthcare. The dramatic reduction 
in mortality from infectious diseases in the 20th century was largely due to improved living standards from 
economic development. 
2. Improved Infrastructure 
Urban areas drive development of water supply systems, sewage and sanitation facilities, roads and 
transport networks, electricity, and communication systems. These infrastructure improvements have 
enormous public health benefits — clean piped water supply alone dramatically reduced waterborne 
diseases (cholera, typhoid). 
3. Better Healthcare Services 
Urban centers concentrate hospitals, specialist medical services, diagnostic laboratories, blood banks, and 
emergency services. Urbanization improves physical access to healthcare for populations that move from 
rural to urban areas. Pharmaceutical industries (a product of industrialization) have produced vaccines, 
antibiotics, and life-saving medicines. 
4. Education and Health Literacy 
Urban industrial society invests in education institutions. Higher literacy and education in urban populations 
leads to better understanding of disease prevention, hygiene, and health-seeking behavior. Awareness of 
vaccination, family planning, and nutrition is higher in educated urban populations. 
5. Food Security and Supply 
Industrial food production and distribution systems ensure year-round availability of diverse food 
commodities. Cold chain technology (refrigeration — a product of industrialization) reduces food spoilage 
and related foodborne illness. Modern food processing has extended shelf life and made nutritious foods 
accessible. 
NEGATIVE IMPACTS ON COMMUNITY HEALTH 
1. Environmental Pollution 
Air Pollution: Industrial emissions (factories, power plants, vehicles) release particulate matter (PM2.5, 
PM10), sulfur dioxide, nitrogen oxides, and volatile organic compounds. These cause: Respiratory diseases 
(asthma, COPD, bronchitis, lung cancer), Cardiovascular diseases (ischemic heart disease, stroke), 
Developmental problems in children (low birth weight, cognitive impairment). WHO estimates that air 
pollution causes 7 million premature deaths annually globally. 
Water Pollution: Industrial effluents (heavy metals, chemicals, dyes), agricultural runoff (pesticides, 
fertilizers), and untreated sewage contaminate water bodies. Consequences: Waterborne diseases 
(cholera, typhoid, hepatitis A), Chemical toxicity (lead, mercury, arsenic poisoning), Methemoglobinemia in 
children (nitrate contamination). 
Soil Pollution: Industrial chemicals, heavy metals, and persistent organic pollutants (POPs) contaminate 
soil, entering the food chain and causing chronic toxicity, cancer, and endocrine disruption. 
155


--- PAGE 168 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Noise Pollution: Industrial machinery and urban traffic generate noise exceeding safe limits (>85 dB). 
Consequences: Noise-induced hearing loss (occupational hazard), Hypertension and cardiovascular 
disease (chronic noise stress), Sleep disturbances, Cognitive impairment in children. 
2. Occupational Hazards 
Industrial workplaces expose workers to unique health risks: 
● Dust exposure: Silica dust (miners, quarry workers) → Silicosis; Coal dust → Coal Worker's 
Pneumoconiosis (Black Lung disease); Asbestos → Asbestosis, mesothelioma, lung cancer. 
● Chemical exposure: Organic solvents, benzene → Leukemia, liver damage; Pesticides → 
Neurotoxicity, reproductive harm; Heavy metals (lead, mercury) → Neurological damage. 
● Physical hazards: Extreme heat/cold, vibration (HAVS — Hand-Arm Vibration Syndrome), ionizing 
radiation (cancer risk), electrical hazards. 
● Ergonomic hazards: Repetitive strain injuries (carpal tunnel syndrome), low back pain, 
musculoskeletal disorders. 
● Accidents and injuries: Factory accidents, falls, machinery injuries — leading cause of occupational 
mortality. 
3. Lifestyle-Related Non-Communicable Diseases (NCDs) 
Urbanization drives profound lifestyle changes that fuel the NCD epidemic: 
● Physical inactivity: Office jobs, motorized transport, and sedentary leisure → Obesity, type 2 
diabetes, hypertension, cardiovascular disease, certain cancers. 
● Unhealthy diet: Increased consumption of ultra-processed foods, fast food, refined carbohydrates, 
saturated fats, and sugar → Obesity, metabolic syndrome, NCDs. 
● Tobacco and alcohol: Marketing of tobacco and alcohol to urban populations; stress-driven 
consumption → Lung cancer, COPD, liver cirrhosis, accidents. 
● Stress: Competitive urban work environment, job insecurity, long commutes, social isolation → 
Hypertension, depression, anxiety, substance abuse. 
4. Overcrowding and Communicable Diseases in Slums 
Rapid unplanned urbanization creates urban slums characterized by extreme overcrowding, inadequate 
housing, poor sanitation, and lack of clean water. This creates ideal conditions for: Airborne infections 
(tuberculosis — urban slums are the epicenter of the TB epidemic; influenza; measles), Skin infections 
(scabies, dermatophytosis — due to overcrowding and poor hygiene), Vector-borne diseases (malaria, 
dengue — due to stagnant water and poor waste management in slums), Diarrheal diseases (due to poor 
sanitation and contaminated water). 
5. Mental Health Consequences 
Urbanization and industrialization are associated with significantly higher rates of mental illness: Social 
isolation in large anonymous cities vs. tight-knit rural communities; Stress from work pressure, financial 
insecurity, and urban competitive culture; Disruption of traditional social support systems (joint family 
breakdown) when people migrate; Higher rates of depression, anxiety disorders, schizophrenia, and 
substance use disorders in urban populations. Mental health services remain underfunded despite high 
urban need. 
6. Social Fragmentation and Health Inequity 
Industrial capitalism creates social stratification, with wealthy urban elites and impoverished urban poor 
often living in the same city but experiencing vastly different health outcomes. Rural-urban migration breaks 
up traditional joint family structures, leaving the elderly without care in villages and young migrants without 
social support in cities. Social cohesion declines in large urban areas, reducing the protective health effects 
of community networks. 
URBAN vs. RURAL HEALTH COMPARISON

PUBLIC HEALTH RESPONSES 
● Environmental regulations: Pollution control laws, emission standards, industrial effluent treatment. 
● Occupational health legislation: Workplace safety standards, PPE provision, health surveillance of 
workers. 
● Urban planning: Green spaces, cycling infrastructure, walkable neighborhoods, smoke-free zones. 
● Slum upgrading programs: Improved sanitation, clean water supply, waste management in informal 
settlements. 
● Health promotion: NCD prevention campaigns, tobacco control, physical activity promotion. 
● Mental health services: Community mental health centers, employer wellness programs, crisis 
helplines. 
● Social safety nets: Unemployment benefits, affordable housing, food security programs. 
EXAM TIP: Key concept: Epidemiological Transition — the shift from communicable diseases to NCDs as 
the dominant cause of morbidity and mortality. This is driven by industrialization and urbanization. Omran's 
3 stages: Age of pestilence & famine → Age of receding pandemics → Age of degenerative & man-made 
diseases. 
CONCLUSION: Industrialization and urbanization have generated enormous wealth and infrastructure that 
has improved health in many ways. However, they have simultaneously created new environmental, 
occupational, and lifestyle-related health burdens. The challenge for public health is to harness the benefits 
of economic development while mitigating its negative health consequences through evidence-based 
policies and multi-sectoral action. 
 
 
  Q5. What are the social factors influencing treatment seeking behaviour? (Nov 2023) 
★NEW★ 
  ★★★   Year: 2023   |   Frequency: High Priority 
INTRODUCTION 
Treatment-seeking behaviour (TSB) refers to the sequence of actions undertaken by an individual or group 
when faced with a health problem, including the decision to recognize illness, seek care, choose the type 
of provider, and adhere to treatment. It is not a single decision but a complex process influenced by multiple 
interacting social, cultural, economic, and psychological factors. 
157


--- PAGE 170 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Understanding treatment-seeking behaviour is essential for public health because delays or avoidance of 
appropriate care lead to: progression of disease, increased complications, higher mortality, continued 
transmission of communicable diseases, and increased healthcare costs. 
THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK: THE HEALTH BELIEF MODEL (HBM) 
The Health Belief Model (Rosenstock, 1966) explains that people seek treatment based on their beliefs 
about disease and healthcare. The key constructs are: 
● Perceived Susceptibility: 'Am I at risk for this disease?' High perceived risk → more likely to seek 
care. 
● Perceived Severity: 'How serious is this disease?' High perceived severity → more care-seeking. 
● Perceived Benefits: 'Will treatment help me?' High perceived benefits → care-seeking. 
● Perceived Barriers: 'What will stop me?' High barriers (cost, distance, stigma) → delayed care-
seeking. 
● Cues to Action: Triggers (symptoms, reminder from family/health worker, media campaign) → 
prompt care-seeking. 
● Self-Efficacy: 'Can I take action?' High self-efficacy → proactive health behaviour. 
SOCIAL FACTORS INFLUENCING TREATMENT-SEEKING BEHAVIOUR 
1. Socioeconomic Status (SES) 
SES is one of the most powerful determinants of treatment-seeking behaviour. Low income creates direct 
financial barriers: inability to afford consultation fees, laboratory investigations, medicines, or transport to 
health facilities. Poverty also leads to competing priorities — spending limited resources on food rather 
than healthcare. Indirect costs (loss of wages from time off work for consultations) further deter care-
seeking among daily wage earners. High SES individuals benefit from health insurance, awareness of 
disease, and proximity to quality health services. Social class differences in TSB explain much of the health 
inequity observed globally. 
2. Education and Health Literacy 
Education shapes health literacy — the ability to obtain, process, and understand health information to 
make appropriate health decisions. Low education leads to: Failure to recognize symptoms as serious, 
delayed presentation; Inability to read health education materials or prescription instructions; Poor 
understanding of disease etiology and the importance of completing treatment courses; Reliance on 
traditional or informal sources of health information. High education and health literacy lead to earlier care-
seeking, better adherence, and more effective use of preventive services. 
3. Cultural Beliefs and Traditional Practices 
Culture profoundly shapes how illness is perceived, explained, and responded to. Cultural belief systems 
influence TSB through: Explanatory models of illness — attributing disease to supernatural causes 
(witchcraft, karma, God's will) leads to seeking religious healing rather than biomedical care; Use of 
traditional healers — faith healers, ayurvedic/unani practitioners, bonesetters are often the first point of 
contact, causing delays in reaching appropriate care; Self-medication with herbs, home remedies, or over-
the-counter medicines before seeking formal care; Dietary taboos during illness that may worsen nutritional 
status. Example: In many communities, mental illness is attributed to spirit possession, leading families to 
take patients to traditional healers rather than psychiatrists. 
4. Stigma 
Stigma is a powerful social barrier to treatment-seeking. Disease-associated stigma leads to: Concealment 
of illness to avoid social discrimination; Avoidance of health facilities where diagnosis might be disclosed; 
Discontinuation of treatment to avoid being identified as ill; Social isolation of patients that reduces the 
support available to encourage care. Conditions with the highest stigma burden include: HIV/AIDS, 
tuberculosis, mental illness, leprosy, sexually transmitted infections, and epilepsy. Addressing stigma 
158


--- PAGE 171 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
through community education, patient-centred communication, and reducing visibility of diagnosis (e.g., 
confidential testing) is essential to improving TSB. 
5. Gender and Social Roles 
Gender influences TSB through multiple mechanisms: 
Women's TSB: In patriarchal societies, women often lack autonomy to decide to seek care independently, 
requiring husband or in-laws' permission. Financial dependence on male family members restricts their 
ability to afford or access care. However, women are generally more likely to seek care for preventive 
services and child health than men. 
Men's TSB: Cultural norms of masculinity in many societies discourage men from admitting illness ('real 
men don't get sick'). Men are more likely to delay care-seeking, present late with advanced disease, and 
have lower utilization of preventive services. This explains higher mortality in men for many conditions 
(cancer, cardiovascular disease) despite lower morbidity. 
Maternal health: Female education, empowerment, and decision-making autonomy are the strongest 
predictors of maternal and child health service utilization globally. 
6. Social Support Systems 
Strong social support from family, friends, and community significantly facilitates care-seeking. Family 
members often serve as: 'Illness recognizers' — identifying that a person needs care; Decision-makers — 
deciding whether and where to seek care; Caregivers — accompanying the patient and providing transport, 
financial assistance, and emotional support. Conversely, social isolation (single-person households, 
migrants away from family) is associated with delayed care-seeking and poor adherence. Community social 
norms (e.g., communities where going to hospital is normalized and encouraged) increase healthcare 
utilization. 
7. Geographic Accessibility 
Physical distance to health facilities is a critical barrier, particularly in rural and remote areas. The 'three 
delays model' (Thaddeus & Maine, 1994), originally developed for maternal mortality, applies broadly to 
TSB: First Delay — Decision to seek care (socioeconomic, cultural, perceived barriers); Second Delay — 
Reaching the health facility (distance, transport availability, roads, cost of travel); Third Delay — Receiving 
appropriate care at the facility (staff availability, drug stock-outs, waiting times). Urban populations generally 
have better physical access but may face other barriers (long queues, impersonal care, cost). 
8. Healthcare Provider Factors 
The quality of care provided, and the manner of its delivery, profoundly affects future care-seeking 
behaviour: Patient-provider communication — poor communication, medical jargon, and failure to explain 
diagnosis and treatment plan reduce understanding and adherence; Discrimination and disrespect — 
experiences of discrimination based on caste, class, race, gender, or HIV status in health facilities create 
lasting barriers to future care-seeking; Waiting times — excessively long waiting times in public facilities 
deter patients from returning; Costs — informal payments, excessive charges, and lack of medicines in 
public facilities push patients to more expensive private providers or delay care altogether; Continuity of 
care — familiarity with a trusted provider increases follow-up and adherence. 
9. Media and Information Environment 
The media environment shapes public awareness, illness recognition, and care-seeking decisions: Mass 
media campaigns (television, radio, print, social media) can significantly increase awareness of disease 
symptoms and promote timely care-seeking. Example: HIV testing campaigns, TB DOTS awareness. 
Conversely, health misinformation circulated via social media (e.g., COVID-19 vaccine misinformation) can 
deter care-seeking and undermine public health programs. Endorsement of healthcare by trusted 
community figures (religious leaders, celebrities, community leaders) through media channels is a powerful 
facilitator. 
10. Religious and Community Norms 
159


--- PAGE 172 ---

swaDesh
COMMUNITY MEDICINE — GOLD MEDAL LONG ANSWERS  |  Topics 17 & 18: Descriptive Epidemiology & Determinants of 
Health 
For Exam Use Only  |  Prepared with Gold Standard Content 
Religion shapes health beliefs, dietary practices, and attitudes toward biomedical care. Some religious 
communities prefer faith healing and prayer over biomedical treatment, causing delays in care-seeking for 
serious illness. Religious leaders are key influencers of community health behavior — their endorsement 
of vaccination, HIV testing, or family planning has been shown to dramatically increase uptake. Community 
gatekeepers (elders, caste leaders, community heads) whose approval is required before health-seeking 
behavior can be a facilitator or barrier depending on their attitudes.

EXAM TIP: For exams, structure your answer using: SES → Education → Culture/Stigma → Gender →

dimensions for a complete essay-type answer. 
CONCLUSION: Treatment-seeking behaviour is shaped by a complex interplay of social, cultural, 
economic, and health system factors. Improving TSB requires interventions at multiple levels: individual 
(education, empowerment), community (reducing stigma, community health workers), and health system 
(financial protection, geographic access, respectful care). The ultimate goal is to ensure that every person 
who is ill receives timely, appropriate, and quality care.

19. STATISTICAL TESTS OF SIGNIFICANCE 
Frequency: 5 Times Asked | Grade: A (High Priority) 
Q1. Enumerate Statistical Tests of Significance. How are they useful in Research 
Methodology? (2022, 2013, 2012) ★★★★★ 
INTRODUCTION 
Statistical tests of significance are mathematical procedures used to determine whether an observed 
difference or association between groups is statistically significant or merely due to chance. They form 
the cornerstone of inferential statistics in biomedical research. 
FUNDAMENTAL CONCEPTS 
Null Hypothesis (H₀): 
States there is no real difference or association. Example: H₀: Drug A and Drug B have equal efficacy. 
Alternative Hypothesis (H₁/Ha): 
States there is a real difference. Can be one-tailed or two-tailed. 
p-value: 
Probability of obtaining observed results (or more extreme) if H₀ is true. p < 0.05 → statistically significant 
(5% level). p < 0.01 → highly significant (1% level). p < 0.001 → very highly significant. 
Type I Error (α): Rejecting H₀ when it is actually true (false positive). Conventional α = 0.05. 
Type II Error (β): Accepting H₀ when it is actually false (false negative). Power = 1-β. 
Confidence Interval (CI): Range of values within which true population parameter lies with specified 
confidence (usually 95%). 
CLASSIFICATION OF TESTS OF SIGNIFICANCE 
A. PARAMETRIC TESTS (require normal distribution assumption) 
Test 
Use 
Data Type 
Student's t-test (unpaired) 
Compare means of 2 independent groups 
Continuous, normal data 
Student's t-test (paired) 
Compare 
means 
in 
same 
group 
before/after intervention 
Matched pairs 
One-way ANOVA (F-test) 
Compare means of ≥3 independent 
groups 
Multiple groups 
Two-way ANOVA 
Effect of 2 factors simultaneously 
Factorial design 
Pearson's correlation (r) 
Linear association between 2 continuous 
variables 
Bivariate correlation 
Simple linear regression 
Predict one variable from another 
Prediction model 
B. NON-PARAMETRIC TESTS (distribution-free)

--- PAGE 174 ---

swaDesh
Fisher's exact test

Before-after

outcomes 
C. TESTS FOR SURVIVAL DATA 
• Log-rank test: Compare survival curves between groups 
• Kaplan-Meier estimator: Estimate survival function 
• Cox proportional hazards regression: Multivariate survival analysis 
USEFULNESS IN RESEARCH METHODOLOGY 
1. HYPOTHESIS TESTING 
• Provides objective criteria to accept or reject null hypothesis 
• Converts clinical questions into testable statistical hypotheses 
• Standardizes decision-making across researchers globally 
2. SAMPLE SIZE CALCULATION 
• Statistical tests determine required sample size for adequate power (usually 80-90%) 
• Prevents underpowered studies (Type II error) and overpowered studies (waste of resources) 
3. COMPARISON OF INTERVENTIONS 
• RCTs use t-tests, ANOVA to compare treatment groups 
• Chi-square tests compare proportions (e.g., cure rates, event rates) 
4. EXPLORATION OF ASSOCIATIONS 
• Correlation coefficients quantify strength of relationships 
• Regression models identify independent predictors while controlling confounders 
5. VALIDITY OF DIAGNOSTIC TESTS 
• Sensitivity, specificity, PPV, NPV calculated and tested statistically 
• ROC curves and area under curve (AUC) with CIs 
6. META-ANALYSIS 
• Pooled effect sizes with CIs across multiple studies 
• Heterogeneity tested by I² statistic and Q-test 
7. INTERPRETATION OF CLINICAL SIGNIFICANCE vs STATISTICAL SIGNIFICANCE 
• Statistical significance ≠ clinical significance 
• Large samples can make trivial differences statistically significant 
• Confidence intervals provide clinical context to p-values 
EXAM TIP: This is a 5-star repeat question. Always: (1) Define null hypothesis, p-value, errors; (2) Give 
classified table of tests; (3) Discuss uses in research. Mentioning 'parametric vs non-parametric' choice 
criteria earns bonus marks. 
Q2. Discuss the role of Chi-square test in Biomedical Research (2012) ★★★★ 
INTRODUCTION 
The Chi-square (χ²) test, developed by Karl Pearson (1900), is the most commonly used non-parametric 
test in biomedical research for analyzing categorical data. It tests whether observed frequencies differ 
significantly from expected frequencies. 
TYPES OF CHI-SQUARE TEST 
Type 
Use 
Chi-square test of independence 
Tests association between 2 categorical variables 
(contingency table analysis) 
162


--- PAGE 175 ---

swaDesh
Chi-square goodness-of-fit test 
Tests whether observed frequency distribution fits 
an expected distribution 
McNemar's Chi-square test 
Tests paired categorical data (before-after studies) 
Mantel-Haenszel Chi-square 
Tests association controlling for confounding 
variable (stratified analysis) 
FORMULA 
χ² = Σ [(O - E)² / E] 
Where O = Observed frequency, E = Expected frequency 
E = (Row total × Column total) / Grand total 
Degrees of freedom (df) = (r-1)(c-1) where r = rows, c = columns 
CONDITIONS FOR USE 
• Data should be in frequencies (counts), not percentages 
• Expected frequency in each cell should be ≥5 (if not, use Fisher's exact test) 
• Observations should be independent 
• Total sample size should be ≥20 
PROCEDURE — 2×2 CONTINGENCY TABLE 
Step 1: Set up 2×2 or larger contingency table 
Step 2: Calculate expected frequencies for each cell 
Step 3: Calculate χ² = Σ[(O-E)²/E] 
Step 4: Determine degrees of freedom 
Step 5: Compare calculated χ² with table value at appropriate df and significance level 
Step 6: If calculated χ² > table value → reject H₀ → statistically significant association 
YATES' CORRECTION FOR CONTINUITY 
Applied for 2×2 tables when any expected cell frequency is between 5-10: 
χ²(corrected) = Σ [(|O - E| - 0.5)² / E] 
Reduces the χ² value, making the test more conservative and reducing Type I error. 
APPLICATIONS IN BIOMEDICAL RESEARCH 
• Comparing disease frequency between exposed and unexposed groups (cohort studies) 
• Comparing exposure rates between cases and controls (case-control studies) 
• Evaluating vaccine/drug efficacy by comparing outcome proportions 
• Testing association between risk factors and disease (e.g., smoking and lung cancer) 
• Comparing treatment adherence rates across demographic groups 
• Genetic studies: Hardy-Weinberg equilibrium testing 
• Diagnostic test evaluation: comparing test results with gold standard 
LIMITATIONS 
• Cannot determine direction or strength of association (use Cramer's V for strength) 
• Cannot be used for continuous data 
• Cannot establish causality 
• Not suitable for small samples (n<20) or expected cell frequency <5 
• Does not indicate which specific category pairs differ (post-hoc tests needed) 
EXAM TIP: For Chi-square questions: Always give the formula, Yates' correction, conditions for use, 
and at least 5 applications. Fisher's exact test is the alternative for small samples — always mention it. 
163


--- PAGE 176 ---

swaDesh
Q3. Discuss Different Steps in Hypothesis Testing (2020) ★★★ 
DEFINITION 
Hypothesis testing is a formal statistical procedure to make inferences about a population parameter 
based on sample data. It provides a systematic framework for deciding whether the evidence from data 
supports or refutes a specific claim about the population. 
STEPS IN HYPOTHESIS TESTING 
STEP 1: STATE THE HYPOTHESES 
• Null Hypothesis (H₀): No difference/association exists (status quo) 
• Alternative Hypothesis (H₁): A real difference/association exists 
• Two-tailed test: H₁ states difference in either direction 
• One-tailed test: H₁ states difference in specific direction 
Example: H₀: Mean SBP in intervention group = Mean SBP in control group; H₁: Mean SBP differs 
between groups 
STEP 2: SET THE SIGNIFICANCE LEVEL (α) 
• Pre-determined probability of Type I error 
• Conventional levels: α = 0.05 (5%), 0.01 (1%), 0.001 (0.1%) 
• Medical research usually uses α = 0.05 
• For multiple comparisons, use Bonferroni correction: α/k where k = number of tests 
STEP 3: SELECT THE APPROPRIATE STATISTICAL TEST 
• Based on: type of data (nominal/ordinal/continuous), number of groups, independent vs paired, 
distribution assumption 
• Parametric: t-test, ANOVA, correlation for normally distributed continuous data 
• Non-parametric: Chi-square, Mann-Whitney for categorical/ordinal/skewed data 
STEP 4: COLLECT DATA AND COMPUTE TEST STATISTIC 
• Compute the appropriate test statistic (t, F, χ², z, U) 
• This converts the observed data into a standardized value 
STEP 5: DETERMINE THE p-VALUE OR CRITICAL VALUE 
• Compare computed statistic with theoretical distribution (t, F, χ² tables) 
• Determine the p-value: probability of getting the observed result if H₀ is true 
• If p < α → reject H₀; if p ≥ α → fail to reject H₀ 
STEP 6: MAKE A STATISTICAL DECISION 
Decision Criterion 
Interpretation 
p < α (e.g., p < 0.05) 
Reject H₀ — Result is statistically significant 
p ≥ α (e.g., p ≥ 0.05) 
Fail to reject H₀ — Result is not statistically 
significant 
p < 0.01 
Highly significant 
p < 0.001 
Very highly significant 
STEP 7: DRAW A CONCLUSION AND INTERPRET IN CONTEXT 
• Translate statistical decision into clinical/biological terms 
• Report effect size and confidence intervals alongside p-value 
• Acknowledge limitations and potential for error 
• Statistical significance ≠ clinical significance — always interpret magnitude 
ERRORS IN HYPOTHESIS TESTING 
164


--- PAGE 177 ---

Concept 
Definition 
Type I (α) Error 
Reject true H₀ — False positive — Finding 
significance when there is none 
Type II (β) Error 
Accept false H₀ — False negative — Missing real 
significance 
Power (1-β) 
Probability of correctly rejecting false H₀ — Aim for 
≥80%

Quantifies uncertainty — more informative than p-
value alone 
EXAM TIP: 7-step framework: Hypothesis → Significance level → Test selection → Compute statistic → 
p-value → Decision → Conclusion. Always mention Type I/II errors, power, and CI. 
Q4. Write about Meta-Analysis and its Application in Medical Research (2021) 
★★★ 
DEFINITION 
Meta-analysis is a statistical technique that combines the results of multiple independent studies 
addressing the same research question to produce a single quantitative estimate with greater statistical 
power than any individual study. It is the highest level of evidence in the evidence hierarchy (Level Ia — 
systematic review with meta-analysis). 
PROCEDURE OF META-ANALYSIS 
4. Define research question (PICO format: Population, Intervention, Comparison, Outcome) 
5. Systematic literature search (PubMed, Cochrane, EMBASE, Google Scholar) 
6. Inclusion/exclusion criteria definition and PRISMA flowchart documentation 
7. Data extraction and quality assessment (Cochrane Risk of Bias tool, Newcastle-Ottawa Scale) 
8. Statistical pooling using appropriate model 
9. Assess heterogeneity 
10. Perform sensitivity analysis and publication bias assessment 
11. Interpret results and draw conclusions 
STATISTICAL MODELS 
Model 
Description 
Fixed Effects Model 
Assumes all studies estimate same true effect; 
used when I² < 25%; gives more weight to larger 
studies; uses inverse-variance method 
Random Effects Model (DerSimonian-Laird) 
Assumes variability in true effects across studies; 
used when I² > 25%; more conservative and 
appropriate for heterogeneous studies 
KEY STATISTICAL MEASURES 
• Pooled Effect Estimate: Overall OR, RR, MD, or SMD with 95% CI 
• Forest Plot: Visual display of individual and pooled estimates; horizontal lines = CIs; diamond = 
pooled effect 
• Heterogeneity — I² statistic: 0-25% = low; 26-50% = moderate; >75% = high heterogeneity 
• Q-statistic (Cochran's Q): Chi-square test of heterogeneity; significant p suggests heterogeneity 
• Funnel Plot: Scatter plot of effect vs precision; asymmetry suggests publication bias 
• Egger's test / Begg's test: Formal test for publication bias 
165


--- PAGE 178 ---

swaDesh
APPLICATIONS IN MEDICAL RESEARCH 
• Summarizing evidence for clinical guidelines (e.g., WHO, ICMR treatment protocols) 
• Resolving conflicting results from individual studies 
• Estimating overall effect size when individual studies are underpowered 
• Drug efficacy and safety evaluation (pharmacovigilance) 
• Health technology assessment for policy decisions 
• Identifying gaps in evidence to guide future research 
• Comparative effectiveness research 
LIMITATIONS 
• 'Garbage in, garbage out' — quality of meta-analysis depends on constituent studies 
• Publication bias (positive results more likely published) 
• Clinical heterogeneity limits meaningful pooling 
• Ecological fallacy risk when aggregating individual-level data 
EXAM TIP: Meta-analysis question: Must draw/describe a forest plot, mention PRISMA, fixed vs random 
effects, I² statistic, funnel plot for publication bias. 'Systematic review + Meta-analysis = Level Ia 
evidence' is a quotable line.

20. EUGENICS / EUTHENICS / GENETIC ENGINEERING / 
COUNSELLING 
Frequency: 5 Times Asked | Grade: A (High Priority) 
Q1 & Q2. Describe / Discuss Eugenics and Euthenics & Their Role in Control of 
Genetic Diseases (2011, 2013) ★★★★★ 
EUGENICS — DEFINITION 
Eugenics (Greek: 'eu' = good; 'genos' = race/birth) is the science of improving a human population by 
controlled breeding to increase the occurrence of desirable heritable characteristics. The term was coined 
by Sir Francis Galton in 1883. 
TYPES OF EUGENICS 
A. POSITIVE EUGENICS 
Encouraging reproduction among individuals with desirable genetic traits to improve the genetic quality 
of the population. 
• Incentives for marriage and reproduction among genetically 'fit' individuals 
• Education and genetic counseling to promote informed reproductive choices 
• Encouraging academically/intellectually gifted individuals to have more children 
B. NEGATIVE EUGENICS 
Discouraging or preventing reproduction among individuals with undesirable genetic traits. 
• Voluntary or involuntary sterilization of individuals with hereditary disorders 
• Marriage restrictions for those with genetic disorders 
• Segregation in institutions 
NOTE: Negative eugenics led to horrific abuses under Nazi Germany (eugenics programs, racial hygiene 
laws). It is widely condemned today as pseudoscientific, unethical, and a human rights violation. 
HISTORICAL CONTEXT AND ETHICAL ISSUES 
• Galton's eugenics was misused to justify racism, colonialism, and genocide 
• Forced sterilization programs in USA (Buck v Bell, 1927), Nazi Germany 
• Modern genetics has discredited simplistic eugenics — most traits are polygenic and 
environmentally influenced 
• Modern medical ethics: autonomy, beneficence, non-maleficence oppose coercive eugenics 
• Contemporary 'liberal eugenics' — individual choice in reproductive decisions — remains 
controversial 
ROLE IN CONTROL OF GENETIC DISEASES 
• Genetic screening programs identify carriers and affected individuals 
• Prenatal diagnosis (amniocentesis, CVS) enables informed reproductive decisions 
• Voluntary genetic counseling empowers families without coercion 
• Population-based screening for high-frequency disorders (e.g., thalassaemia screening in Cyprus 
— 97% reduction in thalassaemic births through voluntary programs) 
EUTHENICS — DEFINITION 
Euthenics (Greek: 'eu' = good; 'thenos' = strength) is the science of improving human beings by improving 
their environment. It was developed as a counter to eugenics, emphasizing that nurture (environment) is 
as important as nature (genes) in determining human health and potential. 
167


--- PAGE 180 ---

swaDesh
The term is associated with Ellen H. Richards (1910) who emphasized that environmental improvement 
— nutrition, sanitation, housing, education — can optimize human development regardless of genetic 
endowment. 
COMPONENTS OF EUTHENICS 
1. NUTRITIONAL ENVIRONMENT 
• Adequate nutrition in early life (first 1000 days) prevents stunting, optimizes brain development 
• Micronutrient supplementation (iron, folic acid, iodine) prevents neural tube defects, cretinism 
• School feeding programs improve cognitive performance 
2. PHYSICAL ENVIRONMENT 
• Clean water, sanitation (WASH) prevents diarrhoeal diseases and stunting 
• Clean air prevents respiratory diseases 
• Safe housing prevents injuries and reduces communicable disease transmission 
3. SOCIAL ENVIRONMENT 
• Education — especially female literacy — is the most powerful euthenicist tool 
• Social protection programs reduce poverty-induced deprivation 
• Maternal and child health services 
4. PSYCHOSOCIAL ENVIRONMENT 
• Nurturing caregiving environments optimize neurodevelopment 
• Early childhood development programs (ECCE) 
COMPARISON: EUGENICS vs EUTHENICS 
Aspect

Focus 
Genes / Heredity 
Environment / Nurture

ROLE IN CONTROL OF GENETIC DISEASES 
• Eugenics (ethical, voluntary approach): Carrier screening, prenatal diagnosis, preimplantation 
genetic testing (PGT) for severe monogenic disorders 
• Euthenics: Environmental modification can reduce phenotypic expression of genetic disorders (e.g., 
phenylalanine-free diet for PKU) 
• Complementary approach: Genetic counseling (eugenics tool) combined with social support 
services (euthenicist approach) is the modern public health model 
EXAM TIP: Always write the comparison table. Eugenics: Galton, positive/negative types, ethical 
critique. Euthenics: Richards, environmental improvement, nutritional/social interventions. 
Thalassaemia example for eugenics in practice. 
Q3. Genetic Counseling — Discuss in Detail (2012) ★★★ 
168


--- PAGE 181 ---

swaDesh
DEFINITION 
Genetic counseling is the process of helping people understand and adapt to the medical, psychological, 
and familial implications of genetic contributions to disease. It involves communication of genetic 
information, risk assessment, and provision of support to enable informed decision-making. (ASHG 
definition, updated 2006) 
OBJECTIVES 
12. Establish accurate diagnosis of genetic disorder 
13. Estimate recurrence risks for family members 
14. Explain available reproductive options and their implications 
15. Provide psychological support and facilitate coping 
16. Enable autonomous, non-directive informed decision-making 
WHO NEEDS GENETIC COUNSELING? 
• Couples with family history of genetic disorders (haemophilia, cystic fibrosis, thalassaemia) 
• Consanguineous marriages (autosomal recessive risks doubled) 
• Advanced maternal age ≥35 years (Down syndrome risk: 1 in 350 at 35, 1 in 100 at 40) 
• Previous child with chromosomal anomaly or congenital malformation 
• Recurrent miscarriages (≥3) — possible chromosomal translocation 
• Couples who are known carriers of genetic conditions 
• Exposure to teratogens (radiation, drugs, infections) during pregnancy 
• Abnormal prenatal screening results (triple/quadruple test, NIPT) 
PROCESS OF GENETIC COUNSELING 
Step 1: DIAGNOSIS 
• Detailed personal and family history — minimum 3-generation pedigree 
• Physical examination, chromosomal analysis (karyotype), molecular genetic tests 
• Biochemical tests (enzyme assays for inborn errors of metabolism) 
Step 2: RISK ASSESSMENT 
• Calculate recurrence risk based on pattern of inheritance 
Inheritance Pattern 
Recurrence Risk 
Autosomal Dominant 
50% risk for each child 
Autosomal Recessive 
25% risk if both parents are carriers 
X-linked Recessive 
50% of sons affected; 50% of daughters are 
carriers 
Chromosomal (trisomy) 
1-2% recurrence risk above age-specific baseline 
Multifactorial 
Empiric recurrence risk from population data 
Step 3: COMMUNICATION 
• Non-directive counseling — present options without imposing views 
• Use simple language, visual aids (pedigree diagrams) 
• Allow questions and ensure comprehension 
• Written summary of discussion provided 
Step 4: DISCUSSION OF OPTIONS 
• Prenatal diagnosis: Amniocentesis (16-20 weeks), Chorionic Villus Sampling — CVS (10-13 
weeks), NIPT (Non-invasive prenatal testing) from 10 weeks 
• Preimplantation Genetic Testing (PGT) with IVF 
• Accepting risk and proceeding with pregnancy 
169


--- PAGE 182 ---

swaDesh
• Adoption or use of donor gametes 
• Refraining from reproduction 
Step 5: PSYCHOLOGICAL SUPPORT 
• Address guilt, anxiety, denial, grief 
• Refer to support groups and mental health services 
• Follow-up counseling sessions 
PRINCIPLES OF GENETIC COUNSELING 
• Non-directiveness: Counselor facilitates, does not direct reproductive choices 
• Confidentiality: Results not disclosed without consent 
• Autonomy: Respect for individual and family decision-making 
• Informed consent for all genetic testing 
• Beneficence and non-maleficence 
GENETIC COUNSELING IN INDIA 
• Integrated into National Health Mission (NHM) 
• Available at medical colleges, AIIMS, regional genetic centers 
• National Institute of Biomedical Genomics (NIBMG) supports genetic research 
• Integrated Genetic and Rare Disease Portal 
• PCPNDT Act regulates prenatal sex determination to prevent misuse 
EXAM TIP: Genetic counseling is a potential long essay. Cover: Definition → Who needs it → Process (5

Q4. Human Genome Project — Discuss (2013, 2020) ★★★ 
INTRODUCTION 
The Human Genome Project (HGP) was an international scientific research project with the primary goal 
of determining the sequence of nucleotide base pairs that make up human DNA and identifying and 
mapping all of the genes of the human genome from both a physical and a functional standpoint. 
KEY FACTS 
Parameter 
Detail 
Duration 
1990–2003 (13 years)

~US$ 3 billion

National Human Genome Research Institute 
(NHGRI), USA + 18 countries

James Watson (1990-1992); Francis Collins 
(1993-2003)

--- PAGE 183 ---

swaDesh
TECHNOLOGIES USED 
• Sanger sequencing (chain termination method) — initial gold standard 
• Shotgun sequencing — fragmenting genome and reassembling sequences 
• Fluorescence-labeled dideoxy chain terminators 
• Automated sequencing machines 
• Bioinformatics tools for assembly and analysis 
KEY FINDINGS 
• Humans have approximately 20,000-25,000 genes (significantly fewer than the 100,000 predicted) 
• 98.5% of DNA is non-coding ('junk DNA' — now known to have regulatory functions) 
• 99.9% of DNA sequence is identical among all humans 
• 0.1% variation accounts for individual differences in appearance, disease susceptibility 
• Average gene contains ~27,000 base pairs 
• Chromosome 1 has the most genes; Y chromosome the fewest 
APPLICATIONS AND SIGNIFICANCE 
1. MEDICINE AND DISEASE 
• Identification of genes responsible for monogenic disorders (BRCA1/2, CFTR, HTT) 
• Understanding complex disease genetics (CVD, diabetes, cancer, schizophrenia) 
• Pharmacogenomics: tailoring drug doses to individual genetic profiles (precision medicine) 
• Development of gene therapy approaches 
• Companion diagnostics for targeted cancer therapies 
2. DIAGNOSTICS 
• Prenatal genetic testing (NIPT, chromosomal microarray) 
• Newborn screening programs expanded 
• Carrier testing for autosomal recessive conditions 
• Genetic risk assessment for multifactorial diseases 
3. BIOTECHNOLOGY 
• Production of recombinant therapeutic proteins (insulin, erythropoietin, clotting factors) 
• Development of monoclonal antibodies and biologic drugs 
4. FORENSIC SCIENCE 
• DNA fingerprinting for identification (Short Tandem Repeat — STR analysis) 
• Paternity testing 
5. EVOLUTIONARY BIOLOGY AND ANTHROPOLOGY 
• Tracing human migration patterns (mitochondrial DNA analysis) 
• Comparative genomics with other species 
ETHICAL, LEGAL, AND SOCIAL ISSUES (ELSI) 
• 15% of HGP funding allocated to ELSI research 
• Genetic privacy and discrimination by employers/insurers 
• Genetic Nondiscrimination Act (GINA), USA 2008 
• Equitable access to genomic medicine 
• Psychological impact of genetic risk information on patients 
• Consent and ownership of genetic data 
• Designer babies and enhancement via gene editing (CRISPR) 
FUTURE: POST-GENOMIC ERA 
• 1000 Genomes Project: cataloguing human genetic variation 
171


--- PAGE 184 ---

swaDesh
• ENCODE Project: functional annotation of non-coding genome 
• Pan-genome: capturing full human genetic diversity 
• Next-generation sequencing (NGS): whole genome sequencing for <$1000 now possible
