TOPIC 22: HEALTH PROMOTION — Applications & Concepts 
Q1. Applications of Health Promotion ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ [Oct 2025 — NEW] 
Health promotion is one of the cornerstones of modern public health practice. As populations 
increasingly face complex, multi-factorial determinants of disease, health promotion offers a 
comprehensive framework that goes beyond mere disease prevention to actively enhance the quality 
of life of individuals and communities. 
Definition 
The Ottawa Charter (1986, WHO) defines Health Promotion as 'the process of enabling people to 
increase control over and to improve their health.' It is not limited to clinical settings but encompasses 
social, environmental, and policy dimensions. 
Prerequisites for Health (Ottawa Charter) 
• 
Peace 
• 
Shelter 
• 
Education 
• 
Food 
• 
Income 
• 
A stable ecosystem 
• 
Sustainable resources 
• 
Social justice and equity 
180


--- PAGE 193 ---

swaDesh
Five Action Areas of Health Promotion (Ottawa Charter) 
• 
1. Build Healthy Public Policy — Integrating health considerations into all sectors (e.g., 
tobacco taxation, food labeling) 
• 
2. Create Supportive Environments — Physical and social environments that support health 
(e.g., safe housing, clean water) 
• 
3. Strengthen Community Action — Empowering communities through Participatory 
Learning and Action (PLA) 
• 
4. Develop Personal Skills — Health education, life skills training, and capacity building 
• 
5. Reorient Health Services — Shifting focus from curative to preventive and promotive care 
Applications of Health Promotion 
Health promotion is applied across multiple settings and populations: 
• 
School Health Programs: Life skills education, adolescent sexual health, mental wellness 
sessions 
• 
Worksite Health Promotion: Ergonomic interventions, stress management, anti-tobacco 
drives 
• 
Hospital-Based Promotion: Patient counseling, discharge planning, chronic disease self-
management 
• 
Community-Level Campaigns: Pulse polio, Swachh Bharat, National Health Mission 
• 
Digital Health Promotion: Social media campaigns, mHealth apps, telemedicine counseling 
• 
Policy Applications: FSSAI food regulations, Mental Healthcare Act 2017, National Tobacco 
Control Programme 
Modern Approaches 
The Bangkok Charter (2005) extended health promotion to address globalization. It emphasized 
advocacy, investment, capacity building, regulation, and partnerships. The Sustainable Development 
Goals (SDGs 2030) embed health promotion principles in Goal 3 (Good Health and Well-being). 
Health Promotion vs Disease Prevention 
• 
Health Promotion: Positive, proactive, focuses on well-being; addresses all determinants; 
empowers individuals 
• 
Disease Prevention: Reactive, focuses on reducing risk; targets specific diseases; medically 
oriented 
📌 Exam Tip: Questions often ask to 'distinguish health promotion from disease prevention' — 
emphasize the Ottawa Charter's empowerment model. 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Health Promotion Action Areas — 'BCSDR' 
  B — Build Healthy Public Policy 
181


--- PAGE 194 ---

swaDesh
  C — Create Supportive Environments 
  S — Strengthen Community Action 
  D — Develop Personal Skills 
  R — Reorient Health Services 
   
  Memory hook: 'Be Careful, Students Do Revision' 
 
Q2. Community Involvement in Health Decisions ⭐⭐⭐ [Dec 2024 — NEW] 
Community participation is the sine qua non of effective health promotion. The shift from a top-down 
medical model to a bottom-up, participatory model has been a defining feature of modern public 
health practice since the Declaration of Alma-Ata (1978). 
Levels of Community Participation (Arnstein's Ladder) 
• 
Level 1 — Manipulation: Authorities using community as rubber stamp 
• 
Level 2 — Therapy: Engaging community without real power 
• 
Level 3 — Informing: One-way communication 
• 
Level 4 — Consultation: Community opinions sought but not necessarily used 
• 
Level 5 — Placation: Community advises but power remains with authorities 
• 
Level 6 — Partnership: Negotiated planning and decision-making 
• 
Level 7 — Delegated Power: Community has majority power 
• 
Level 8 — Citizen Control: Full community control 
Methods to Enhance Community Involvement 
• 
Participatory Learning and Action (PLA): Rapid rural appraisal, transect walks, social 
mapping 
• 
Village Health Sanitation and Nutrition Committees (VHSNCs): Under NHM, empowers 
village-level decision-making 
• 
Gram Sabhas: Democratic platforms for local health planning 
• 
ASHA Workers: Community health workers as bridges between communities and health 
systems 
• 
Community Advisory Boards: In research and program planning 
• 
Focus Group Discussions (FGDs): For needs assessment and program evaluation 
Barriers to Community Participation 
• 
Low health literacy and education 
• 
Social inequities — caste, gender, religion 
• 
Lack of trust in health systems 
182


--- PAGE 195 ---

swaDesh
• 
Political disempowerment 
• 
Language and communication gaps 
Success Stories 
The SEARCH (Society for Education, Action and Research in Community Health) project in Gadchiroli 
is a landmark example of community-led health decision-making reducing neonatal mortality by over 
70% through participatory approaches. 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Community Participation Methods — 'PVGAF' 
  P — PLA (Participatory Learning and Action) 
  V — VHSNCs (Village Health Sanitation Committees) 
  G — Gram Sabhas 
  A — ASHA Workers 
  F — Focus Group Discussions 
   
  Memory hook: 'People Value Good And Fair participation' 
 
Q3. Participatory Learning and Action (PLA) in Public Health ⭐⭐⭐ [May 
2025 — NEW] 
Participatory Learning and Action (PLA) is an evolving family of approaches and methods aimed at 
enabling local people to share, enhance, and analyze their knowledge of life and conditions, to plan 
and to act. It evolved from Rapid Rural Appraisal (RRA) and is now widely adopted in community 
health programs globally. 
Key Features of PLA 
• 
Participatory: Communities are co-researchers and co-decision makers 
• 
Empowerment-focused: Builds local capacities and confidence 
• 
Visual methods: Mapping, ranking, seasonal calendars reduce literacy barriers 
• 
Iterative: Continuous cycles of learning, reflection, and action 
Core PLA Techniques 
• 
Social/Resource Mapping: Communities map their health resources (hospitals, water 
sources, roads) 
• 
Seasonal Calendars: Identifying disease patterns by season (e.g., malaria in monsoons) 
• 
Transect Walks: Walking through communities to observe health hazards systematically 
• 
Ranking and Scoring: Priority ranking of health problems (problem ranking, pairwise ranking) 
• 
Venn Diagrams: Mapping relationships between community institutions 
183


--- PAGE 196 ---

swaDesh
• 
Focus Group Discussions: Group exploration of health perceptions and needs 
• 
Participatory Epidemiology: Community-generated disease maps and case reporting 
Applications in Public Health 
• 
Maternal & Child Health: SNEHA program (Mumbai) used PLA with women's groups to 
reduce neonatal mortality 
• 
WATSAN Programs: PLA for community-led Total Sanitation (CLTS) 
• 
Malaria Control: Community mapping of breeding sites 
• 
HIV/AIDS Programs: Stigma mapping, participatory needs assessment 
• 
Health System Strengthening: Community scorecards for health service quality 
Evidence Base 
Manandhar et al. (Lancet, 2004) demonstrated that women's group PLA in Nepal reduced neonatal 
mortality by 30% and maternal mortality by 78% — one of the most significant community health 
intervention outcomes documented. 
📌 Exam Tip: PLA questions commonly appear in context of NHM and community health workers — link 
to ASHA, VHSNC, and sub-centre level activities. 
🧠 MNEMONIC — PLA Techniques — 'SMART VF' 
  S — Seasonal Calendars 
  M — Mapping (Social/Resource) 
  A — Analytical Ranking 
  R — Resource Flow Diagrams 
  T — Transect Walks 
  V — Venn Diagrams 
  F — Focus Group Discussions 
   
  Memory hook: 'SMART communities use Very Fair methods' 
 
TOPIC 23: MASLOW'S HIERARCHY / BEHAVIORAL THEORIES 
Q1. Maslow's Hierarchy of Needs Theory ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ [Oct 2025 — NEW] 
Abraham Maslow's Hierarchy of Needs (1943, 'A Theory of Human Motivation') is a foundational 
motivational theory in psychology and public health. It proposes that human behavior is driven by a 
hierarchical set of needs, and that lower-order needs must be substantially satisfied before higher-
order needs become motivating. 
The Five-Level Hierarchy (Classical Model) 
184


--- PAGE 197 ---

swaDesh
• 
Level 1 — PHYSIOLOGICAL NEEDS (Deficiency Needs): Air, water, food, shelter, sleep, 
warmth, clothing. These are survival needs and the most prepotent (powerful). Example: A 
hungry patient cannot focus on health education. 
• 
Level 2 — SAFETY NEEDS (Deficiency Needs): Physical safety, job security, financial 
security, health, property. Example: Communities in conflict zones prioritize safety over 
disease prevention. 
• 
Level 3 — LOVE & BELONGING (Deficiency Needs): Friendship, intimacy, family, sense of 
community. Example: Social isolation during COVID-19 impacted mental health significantly. 
• 
Level 4 — ESTEEM NEEDS (Deficiency Needs): Self-esteem, achievement, recognition, 
status, respect. Example: Patients with chronic illness may have impaired self-esteem. 
• 
Level 5 — SELF-ACTUALIZATION (Growth Need): Achieving full potential, personal growth, 
peak experiences. Example: Motivated health workers pursuing academic excellence. 
Extended Hierarchy (Maslow 1970 — 8-level model) 
• 
Added: Cognitive Needs (knowledge, curiosity) — between Esteem and Self-actualization 
• 
Added: Aesthetic Needs (beauty, order) — above Cognitive 
• 
Added: Transcendence (helping others self-actualize) — above Self-actualization 
Applications in Public Health & Community Medicine 
• 
Health Education: Tailor messages to unmet needs — starving populations cannot prioritize 
health literacy 
• 
Program Planning: NHM addresses physiological (nutrition programs) → safety (JSSK, safe 
delivery) → belonging (ASHA support groups) in sequence 
• 
Patient Counseling: Chronically ill patients may have unmet safety/esteem needs — 
address holistically 
• 
Occupational Health: Motivating health workers using recognition, autonomy (higher needs) 
• 
Mental Health: Loneliness (unmet belonging need) is a primary driver of depression 
• 
Health Equity: Marginalized communities stuck at lower-hierarchy levels cannot access self-
actualization health behaviors 
Criticisms 
• 
Hierarchy is not always sequential — individuals may seek self-actualization despite unmet 
lower needs 
• 
Cultural variation: Collectivist cultures may prioritize belonging over safety 
• 
Limited empirical evidence supporting strict hierarchy 
• 
Later theorists (Alderfer's ERG Theory) compressed it to 3 levels: Existence, Relatedness, 
Growth 
185


--- PAGE 198 ---

swaDesh
🧠 MNEMONIC — Maslow's 5 Levels — 'Please Send Love Each Saturday' 
  P — Physiological 
  S — Safety 
  L — Love & Belonging 
  E — Esteem 
  S — Self-Actualization 
   
  Or: 'Pandas Sleep Like Enormous Snakes' 
  (bottom to top: Physiological → Safety → Love → Esteem → Self-Actualization) 
 
Q2. JOHARI Window — Describe and Its Applications ⭐⭐⭐ [May 2025 — 
NEW] 
The Johari Window is a psychological model developed by Joseph Luft and Harrington Ingham (1955) 
— 'Jo-hari' from the first names of its creators. It is a framework for understanding interpersonal 
communication, self-awareness, and group dynamics, with significant applications in health 
communication and team management. 
The Four Quadrants of Johari Window 
• 
Quadrant 1 — OPEN/ARENA (Known to self, known to others): Information freely shared. 
Example: A doctor's published research, known clinical skills. This area should be 
maximized for effective communication. 
• 
Quadrant 2 — BLIND SPOT (Unknown to self, known to others): Others see things about 
you that you don't. Example: A clinician unaware of their dismissive communication style; a 
patient's observable symptoms they haven't noticed. Feedback reduces this area. 
• 
Quadrant 3 — HIDDEN/FACADE (Known to self, unknown to others): Deliberately 
concealed information. Example: A patient hiding substance use from their doctor. Self-
disclosure reduces this area. 
• 
Quadrant 4 — UNKNOWN (Unknown to self, unknown to others): Unexplored potential, 
unconscious drives. Example: Latent clinical skills, undiscovered personality traits. 
Reflection and therapy unlock this. 
Applications in Community Medicine & Public Health 
• 
Doctor-Patient Communication: Expanding the Open quadrant improves therapeutic alliance 
and adherence 
• 
Team Management: Used in health team development; reduces Blind Spots through 360-
degree feedback 
• 
Health Education: Identifying Hidden knowledge (patients know risks but hide behavior) 
guides motivational interviewing 
186


--- PAGE 199 ---

swaDesh
• 
Community Needs Assessment: Unknown quadrant represents unrecognized community 
health needs — needs participatory methods to uncover 
• 
Leadership Development: PHC Medical Officers use Johari Window in self-improvement 
workshops 
• 
Mental Health Counseling: Core tool in psychotherapy for improving self-awareness 
Strategies to Expand the Open Window 
• 
Self-disclosure: Sharing relevant personal information 
• 
Soliciting feedback: Structured 360-degree peer review 
• 
Active listening and reflection 
• 
Group process sessions: Team workshops and debriefs 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Johari Window — 'OBHiU' 
  O — Open (known self, known others) 
  B — Blind Spot (unknown self, known others) 
  Hi — Hidden/Facade (known self, unknown others) 
  U — Unknown (unknown self, unknown others) 
   
  Memory hook: 'Only Brilliant Hidden Unknowns grow with feedback' 
  Or: Jo-Hari = Joseph + Harrington (creators) 
 
Q3. Types and Management of Conflict ⭐⭐⭐ [May 2025 — NEW] 
Conflict is an inevitable feature of organizational and community life. In healthcare settings, 
unmanaged conflict reduces team effectiveness, patient safety, and staff morale. Understanding 
conflict types and resolution strategies is a critical leadership competency for public health 
professionals. 
Definition 
Conflict is defined as 'a process that begins when one party perceives that another party has 
negatively affected, or is about to negatively affect, something that the first party cares about' 
(Thomas, 1992). 
Types of Conflict 
• 
1. Intrapersonal Conflict: Within an individual (role conflict, approach-avoidance). Example: 
A health worker torn between family duties and night duty calls. 
• 
2. Interpersonal Conflict: Between two individuals. Example: Disagreement between a 
doctor and nurse over patient management. 
187


--- PAGE 200 ---

swaDesh
• 
3. Intragroup Conflict: Within a team. Example: PHC staff disagreement on prioritizing 
outreach vs. OPD. 
• 
4. Intergroup Conflict: Between departments or organizations. Example: Conflict between 
health and sanitation departments over resource allocation. 
• 
5. Organizational Conflict: Structural conflicts within the health system. Example: Vertical vs. 
horizontal program management disagreements. 
Sources of Conflict in Healthcare 
• 
Resource scarcity — inadequate staff, equipment, budget 
• 
Role ambiguity — unclear responsibilities between cadres 
• 
Communication failures — information gaps between levels 
• 
Value differences — professional vs. community health priorities 
• 
Power and authority disputes 
Thomas-Kilmann Conflict Management Styles 
• 
1. Competing (High Assertiveness, Low Cooperation): Win-lose. Used in emergency 
situations requiring quick decisions. 
• 
2. Collaborating (High Assertiveness, High Cooperation): Win-win. Ideal for complex 
problems with creative solutions. 
• 
3. Compromising (Medium both): Give-and-take. Used when time is limited and stakes are 
moderate. 
• 
4. Avoiding (Low both): Withdrawing. Used when issue is trivial or emotions are high. 
• 
5. Accommodating (Low Assertiveness, High Cooperation): Lose-win. Used to preserve 
relationships when wrong. 
Conflict Resolution Strategies 
• 
Negotiation: Direct dialogue between parties to reach agreement 
• 
Mediation: Neutral third party facilitates resolution 
• 
Arbitration: Binding decision by an authority 
• 
Structural approaches: Redesigning roles, reporting structures, resource allocation 
• 
Communication improvement: Regular team meetings, open-door policy 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Conflict Management Styles — 'C3-AA' 
  C — Competing 
  C — Collaborating (best for complex issues) 
  C — Compromising 
  A — Avoiding 
  A — Accommodating 
   
188


--- PAGE 201 ---

swaDesh
  Memory hook: '3 Cs then 2 As — like a school grade C, C, C, A, A' 
  Winning combo: Collaborate first, Compromise if needed 
 
TOPIC 24: ICD-11 / CLASSIFICATION OF DISEASES 
Q1. Structure, Features, and New Elements of ICD-11 ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ [May 
2025 — NEW] 
The International Classification of Diseases, 11th Revision (ICD-11) was adopted by the World Health 
Assembly in May 2019 and came into force on January 1, 2022. It represents the most comprehensive 
overhaul of disease classification in decades, with transformative implications for global health data 
systems, clinical coding, and epidemiological research. 
Historical Background 
• 
ICD-1 introduced in 1900 (Bertillon Classification) 
• 
ICD-10 adopted in 1990, in use for over 30 years 
• 
ICD-11: Developed over 10 years with input from 90+ countries and 10,000+ clinicians 
• 
India transitioned to ICD-11 reporting from 2022 onwards 
Structure of ICD-11 
• 
26 Chapters (vs. 22 in ICD-10), covering all disease domains 
• 
Entirely digital — browser-based (ICD-11 Browser), API-accessible 
• 
Coded with alphanumeric system: Letter + 2 digits + dot + 2 digits (e.g., CA24.0) 
• 
Multiple Parenting: Conditions can appear in multiple chapters without duplication 
• 
Linearizations: Foundation Component (comprehensive) + Linearizations for specific uses 
(morbidity, mortality) 
• 
Extension Codes: Allow granular specification (severity, laterality, temporality, 
histopathology) 
New Features and Elements

SNOMED CT and LOINC

•

(Ayurveda, Chinese medicine)

(homosexuality fully removed since ICD-10)

•

Incidence Rate: New cases per population per time 
•

•

TOPIC 25: ICEBERG PHENOMENON 
190


--- PAGE 203 ---

swaDesh
Q1. Iceberg Phenomenon and Its Application in Community Medicine 
⭐⭐⭐⭐ [2015] 
The Iceberg Phenomenon is one of the most fundamental concepts in epidemiology and community 
medicine. Just as an iceberg has only a small visible portion above water with the vast bulk hidden 
below the surface, most diseases in a community exist in a subclinical, undiagnosed, or unrecognized 
state — with only a fraction becoming clinically apparent. 
Definition 
The Iceberg Phenomenon states that for most diseases, clinical cases (visible above the 'waterline') 
represent only the tip of the total disease burden, while a much larger proportion exists as subclinical 
infections, inapparent cases, and unrecognized disease in the community. 
Components of the Disease Iceberg 
• 
Above the waterline (Diagnosed/Reported Cases): Cases seeking care; officially counted in 
surveillance; represent prevalence as measured 
• 
Below the waterline — Level 1 (Symptomatic but NOT seeking care): Fear, stigma, lack of 
access, cost barriers prevent care-seeking 
• 
Below the waterline — Level 2 (Subclinical/Inapparent Disease): Infection present, 
pathological changes occurring, but no symptoms yet 
• 
Below the waterline — Level 3 (Pre-pathogenesis/Susceptible Stage): No disease yet, but 
risk factors and susceptibility present 
Classic Examples 
• 
Diabetes Mellitus: For every diagnosed case, 2-3 undiagnosed cases exist in the community 
(IDF data) 
• 
Hypertension: Awareness-treatment-control cascade shows massive iceberg — only ~12% 
controlled in India (NFHS-5) 
• 
Tuberculosis: Estimated 2.8 million TB cases in India (2022); only 2.4 million notified — 
significant iceberg 
• 
Poliomyelitis: For every 1 case of paralytic polio, ~200 subclinical infections occur (200:1 
ratio) 
• 
Sexually Transmitted Infections: Majority of chlamydia, HSV cases are asymptomatic 
• 
Mental Health: ~10-15% prevalence in community; majority undiagnosed and untreated 
Differences Between Incidence and Prevalence in Context of Iceberg 
• 
Incidence: New cases per population per time — measures the 'rate of new disease' 
191


--- PAGE 204 ---

swaDesh
• 
Prevalence: All existing cases at a time point — includes BOTH tip and visible part of 
iceberg 
• 
The iceberg is best captured by prevalence surveys (community-based), not routine 
incidence reporting (facility-based) 
• 
Point prevalence vs. period prevalence — both detect more of the iceberg than incidence 
alone 
Implications for Health Care Planning 
• 
Screening Programs: Mass screening (NPCDCS, RBSK) to detect submerged portion 
• 
Resource Allocation: Disease burden underestimated by clinical data alone; surveys reveal 
true need 
• 
Health System Design: Primary care must be strengthened to surface the iceberg 
• 
Surveillance Enhancement: Passive surveillance (facility-based) misses iceberg; active and 
sentinel surveillance needed 
• 
Community Outreach: Reduce barriers to care-seeking through IEC, camps, mobile units 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Iceberg Layers — '4 D's below the waterline' 
  D — Diagnosed cases (ABOVE water) 
  D — Denied/hidden cases (symptomatic, not seeking care) 
  D — Dormant/subclinical cases (infected, asymptomatic) 
  D — Downstream susceptibles (at risk, not yet infected) 
   
  Memory hook: 'Disease Drowns Deep Down' — 4 D's of the iceberg 
  Polio ratio: 1 paralytic : 200 subclinical = a very deep iceberg! 
 
Q2. Describe Iceberg Phenomenon — Differences Between Incidence and 
Prevalence ⭐⭐⭐ [2018] 
(Core content covered above in Q1. Supplement with the following incidence-prevalence 
comparison:) 
Incidence vs. Prevalence — Detailed Comparison 
• 
Incidence Rate: Number of NEW cases of disease occurring in a defined population during a 
specified time period / Population at risk × K. Measures: Risk of developing disease. Use: 
Studying etiology and risk factors. Affected by: Duration of illness. 
• 
Prevalence Rate: Number of EXISTING cases (new + old) at a given point/period / Total 
population × K. Measures: Disease burden and magnitude. Use: Health service planning. 
Affected by: Incidence and duration. 
• 
Relationship: Prevalence ≈ Incidence × Mean Duration of Disease (for stable conditions) 
192


--- PAGE 205 ---

swaDesh
• 
In Iceberg context: Prevalence surveys reveal more of the iceberg than incidence reporting 
alone, as they capture long-standing undiagnosed cases 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Incidence vs Prevalence — 'NEW vs ALL' 
  Incidence = NEW cases only — measures RISK 
  Prevalence = ALL cases (new + old) — measures BURDEN 
   
  I = Incidence = 'I just got sick' (new) 
  P = Prevalence = 'Pool of all sick people' (all existing) 
   
  Formula link: P ≈ I × D (Prevalence ≈ Incidence × Duration) 
 
Q3. Implications of Iceberg Phenomenon on Planning Health Care Services 
⭐⭐⭐ [2023] 
The iceberg phenomenon has profound implications for how health services are planned, funded, 
and delivered. If health planners rely only on clinical/facility data (the visible tip), they will 
systematically underestimate disease burden, misallocate resources, and fail to prevent disease 
progression in the subclinical majority. 
Planning Implications — Detailed Analysis 
• 
1. Screening and Early Detection: NPCDCS (Non-Communicable Disease Control 
Programme) screens for DM, HTN, cancers at PHC level. RBSK (Rashtriya Bal Swasthya 
Karyakram) screens children for 30+ conditions. Objective: Surface the submerged portion 
of the iceberg early. 
• 
2. Strengthening Primary Health Care: PHCs and sub-centres serve as the 'net' to capture 
the submerged iceberg. Functional sub-centres with ASHAs can identify unreported cases 
through home visits. 
• 
3. Active Surveillance: Sentinel surveillance, periodic surveys (NFHS, DLHS) provide 
iceberg-level data. Notifiable disease reporting must be improved to capture more of the 
iceberg. 
• 
4. Health Workforce Planning: True disease burden (including iceberg) requires more 
frontline health workers than facility-based data suggests. ASHA-ANM-MO triad serves to 
address the full iceberg. 
• 
5. Budget and Infrastructure: NHM budgeting must account for actual community disease 
burden, not just reported cases. Drug inventories, diagnostics, and beds must be iceberg-
calibrated. 
193


--- PAGE 206 ---

swaDesh
• 
6. IEC/BCC Programs: Reduce demand-side barriers (stigma, lack of awareness) that keep 
the iceberg submerged. Mental health anti-stigma campaigns, TB awareness drives serve 
this function. 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Iceberg Planning Implications — 'SEARCH' 
  S — Screening programs (NPCDCS, RBSK) 
  E — Enhanced surveillance (active, sentinel) 
  A — Active outreach (ASHAs, mobile camps) 
  R — Resource planning (calibrated to true burden) 
  C — Community awareness (IEC/BCC) 
  H — Health workforce expansion 
   
  Memory hook: We must SEARCH below the waterline! 
 
TOPIC 26: CASE-CONTROL / COHORT / RCT STUDIES 
Q1. Steps in Conducting a Case-Control Study & Types of Biases ⭐⭐⭐⭐ 
[2018] 
Case-control studies are retrospective, observational studies that are particularly valuable for 
studying rare diseases and multiple exposures. They are among the most efficient epidemiological 
designs, forming the backbone of analytical epidemiology in community medicine. 
Definition 
A case-control study selects individuals with a disease (cases) and a suitable comparison group 
without the disease (controls), then compares the frequency of exposure to putative risk factors in 
both groups. The measure of association is the Odds Ratio (OR). 
Steps in Conducting a Case-Control Study 
• 
Step 1 — Define the Research Question: Clear PICO framework (Population, Exposure, 
Comparison, Outcome) 
• 
Step 2 — Select Cases: Define inclusion/exclusion criteria. Cases should be newly 
diagnosed (incident cases preferred over prevalent to avoid survival bias). Sources: Hospital 
records, disease registries, surveillance data. 
• 
Step 3 — Select Controls: Match to cases on potential confounders (age, sex, 
socioeconomic status). Sources: Hospital controls, neighborhood controls, community 
controls. Maintain 1:1 to 1:4 case:control ratio for efficiency. 
194


--- PAGE 207 ---

swaDesh
• 
Step 4 — Matching Strategy: Individual matching (pair each case to a control) or frequency 
matching (match distributions). Over-matching can reduce statistical power — avoid 
matching on variables in the causal pathway. 
• 
Step 5 — Measure Exposure: Structured questionnaire, medical records, biological 
specimens. Exposure must be assessed for the relevant exposure period BEFORE disease 
onset. 
• 
Step 6 — Minimize Bias: Use standardized data collection; train interviewers; blind 
interviewers to case/control status where possible. 
• 
Step 7 — Calculate Odds Ratio (OR): 2×2 contingency table; OR = (a×d)/(b×c). OR >1: 
positive association; OR <1: protective; OR = 1: no association. 
• 
Step 8 — Analyze Confounders: Stratified analysis (Mantel-Haenszel), logistic regression for 
multiple confounders. 
• 
Step 9 — Interpret and Report: STROBE checklist for observational studies. 
Advantages 
• 
Efficient for rare diseases 
• 
Relatively inexpensive and quick 
• 
Can study multiple exposures simultaneously 
• 
No long follow-up required 
Disadvantages 
• 
Cannot calculate incidence or relative risk directly (only OR) 
• 
Subject to recall bias and selection bias 
• 
Poor for rare exposures 
• 
Temporal relationship (exposure → disease) difficult to establish 
Types of Bias in Research Studies 
• 
SELECTION BIAS: Systematic error in choosing study participants. Types: Berkson's bias 
(hospital-based controls); Admission rate bias; Healthy worker effect; Volunteer bias; 
Survival bias (studying survivors misses fatal cases early). 
• 
INFORMATION BIAS: Error in measuring exposure or outcome. Types: Recall bias (cases 
remember exposure better than controls — common in case-control); Interviewer bias 
(differential probing of cases vs. controls); Misclassification bias — differential or non-
differential. 
• 
CONFOUNDING: A third variable associated with both exposure and outcome distorts the 
true relationship. Example: Coffee-CHD association confounded by smoking. Control: 
Restriction, matching, stratification, multivariate analysis, RCT randomization. 
195


--- PAGE 208 ---

swaDesh
• 
HAWTHORNE EFFECT: Participants behave differently when observed (affects 
experimental studies). 
• 
DETECTION BIAS: Cases more intensively investigated, revealing associations 
artefactually. 
• 
PUBLICATION BIAS: Positive results more likely to be published — inflates apparent effect 
sizes in meta-analyses. 
Bias Reduction Strategies 
• 
Double-blinding, standardized questionnaires, multiple control groups, validation studies, 
sensitivity analysis 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Types of Bias — 'SIC-HPD' 
  S — Selection Bias (Berkson's, Volunteer, Healthy Worker) 
  I — Information Bias (Recall, Interviewer, Misclassification) 
  C — Confounding 
  H — Hawthorne Effect 
  P — Publication Bias 
  D — Detection Bias 
   
  Memory hook: 'Studies In Communities Have Problems Daily' — SICHPD 
  Key formula: OR = ad/bc (from 2×2 table) 
 
Q2. Issues and Implementation of Randomized Controlled Trial for a New 
Vaccine ⭐⭐⭐ [2013] 
A Randomized Controlled Trial (RCT) is considered the gold standard for evaluating vaccine efficacy 
because randomization controls for both known and unknown confounders. However, conducting an 
RCT for a new vaccine involves complex logistical, ethical, and statistical challenges. 
Definition 
An RCT is a prospective, experimental study design in which participants are randomly allocated to 
receive either the intervention (vaccine) or a control (placebo or comparator vaccine), and outcomes 
are compared between groups. 
Phases of Vaccine Development 
• 
Phase I: Safety in healthy volunteers (20-100 participants). Dose-finding, immunogenicity 
testing. 
• 
Phase II: Expanded safety, immunogenicity, dosing in 100-500 participants. Identifies 
common adverse events. 
196


--- PAGE 209 ---

swaDesh
• 
Phase III: Large-scale efficacy RCT (thousands of participants). Measures vaccine efficacy 
(VE) in real-world-like conditions. 
• 
Phase IV: Post-marketing surveillance. Detects rare adverse events, long-term protection. 
Key Steps in Implementing a Vaccine RCT 
• 
1. Protocol Development: Define primary outcome (disease incidence), secondary outcomes 
(immunogenicity, adverse events), sample size, endpoints. 
• 
2. Sample Size Calculation: Based on expected disease incidence, desired VE to detect, 
alpha (0.05) and power (80-90%). 
• 
3. Ethics Approval: Institutional Ethics Committee (IEC) review. Helsinki Declaration 
compliance. CTRI (Clinical Trials Registry — India) registration mandatory. 
• 
4. Randomization: Simple, stratified (by age/sex), or cluster randomization. Allocation 
concealment is critical. 
• 
5. Blinding: Double-blind preferred (neither participant nor assessor knows allocation). 
Reduces ascertainment and performance bias. 
• 
6. Intervention: Standardized vaccine administration, cold chain maintenance (2–8°C), lot 
number recording. 
• 
7. Follow-up: Active and passive surveillance for primary outcome. Adverse Event Following 
Immunization (AEFI) monitoring per WHO/CDSCO guidelines. 
• 
8. Data Management: Intention-to-treat (ITT) analysis is primary; per-protocol as secondary. 
• 
9. Vaccine Efficacy Calculation: VE = (1 – RR) × 100 = (Attack Rate Control – Attack Rate 
Vaccinated) / Attack Rate Control × 100. 
• 
10. DSMB (Data Safety Monitoring Board): Independent monitoring for safety; may 
recommend early stopping. 
Ethical Issues in Vaccine RCTs 
• 
Placebo use is ethical only if no established effective vaccine exists 
• 
Equipoise: Genuine uncertainty about intervention benefit must exist 
• 
Informed consent: Community engagement and literacy-appropriate consent process 
• 
Post-trial access: Successful vaccine should be made available to placebo group (Helsinki 
Declaration) 
🧠 MNEMONIC — Vaccine RCT Steps — 'PRSE-BFIDA' 
  P — Protocol & Sample size 
  R — Randomization & Registration (CTRI) 
  S — Subject recruitment & Stratification 
  E — Ethics approval (IEC, Helsinki) 
  B — Blinding (double-blind preferred) 
  F — Follow-up & AEFI monitoring 
197


--- PAGE 210 ---

swaDesh
  I — ITT Analysis 
  D — DSMB monitoring 
  A — Analysis & VE calculation (VE = 1-RR × 100) 
   
  Memory hook: 'Please Run Studies Ethically — Best For India's Diseases Always' 
 
Q3. Critically Appraise Randomized Controlled Trial ⭐⭐⭐ [2020] 
Critical appraisal of an RCT involves systematically evaluating the validity, results, and applicability 
of a trial. The CONSORT (Consolidated Standards of Reporting Trials) checklist and CASP (Critical 
Appraisal Skills Programme) RCT checklist are international standards used for this purpose. 
Framework for Critical Appraisal — Three Questions 
• 
A) Are the results VALID? (Internal validity — minimize bias) 
• 
B) What are the RESULTS? (Effect size, precision) 
• 
C) Are the results APPLICABLE? (External validity — generalizability) 
A) Validity Assessment 
• 
Was randomization appropriate? — Check: Sequence generation (computer random 
numbers, lottery); Allocation concealment (sealed envelopes, central allocation); Balance of 
baseline characteristics between groups. 
• 
Were all participants accounted for? — <20% dropout acceptable; Intention-to-treat (ITT) 
analysis performed? 
• 
Was blinding maintained? — Double-blind preferred; Outcome assessor blinding minimum 
• 
Were groups treated equally? — Co-interventions, contamination, compliance assessed? 
• 
Was the follow-up adequate? — Duration sufficient to detect meaningful outcomes? 
B) Results Assessment 
• 
Effect Size: Relative Risk (RR), Odds Ratio (OR), Number Needed to Treat (NNT), Vaccine 
Efficacy (VE) 
• 
Precision: 95% Confidence Intervals — narrow CIs indicate more precise estimates 
• 
Statistical Significance: p-value <0.05 conventional threshold, but CI more informative 
• 
Clinical Significance vs. Statistical Significance: Small but statistically significant effects may 
lack clinical importance 
• 
NNT = 1 / Absolute Risk Reduction — lower NNT means more beneficial treatment 
C) Applicability Assessment 
• 
Are my patients similar to trial participants? (generalizability) 
198


--- PAGE 211 ---

swaDesh
• 
Was the intervention feasible in my setting? 
• 
Were all clinically important outcomes measured? 
• 
Are benefits worth harms and costs? 
CONSORT Checklist Highlights 
• 
Title and abstract: RCT clearly identified 
• 
PICO clearly defined 
• 
Sample size justification provided 
• 
Randomization and allocation concealment described 
• 
Blinding status and limitations stated 
• 
Flow diagram (CONSORT flow chart) showing participant progress through trial 
• 
ITT analysis performed and stated 
🧠 MNEMONIC — RCT Appraisal — 'VRA' Framework 
  V — Validity (Internal): Randomization, Blinding, Dropout, ITT 
  R — Results: RR, NNT, 95% CI, p-value 
  A — Applicability: Generalizability, Feasibility, Costs 
   
  For Validity, remember 'RABBIT': 
  R — Randomization proper? 
  A — Allocation concealment? 
  B — Blinding maintained? 
  B — Baseline characteristics balanced? 
  I — ITT analysis done? 
  T — Trial completion adequate? 
 
Q4. Enumerate Various Biases in Research. Explain Influence & How to 
Reduce ⭐⭐⭐⭐ [Apr/May 2026 — NEW] 
Bias is defined as any systematic error in the design, conduct, analysis, or reporting of a study that 
results in a mistaken estimate of an exposure's effect on disease risk. Unlike random error (reduced 
by increasing sample size), bias cannot be corrected by statistical methods alone — it must be 
prevented during study design. 
Classification of Biases 
• 
I. SELECTION BIAS — Error in how participants are enrolled 
• 
   a. Berkson's Bias: Hospital controls are sicker than the general population, leading to 
spurious associations 
199


--- PAGE 212 ---

swaDesh
• 
   b. Healthy Worker Effect: Employed workers are healthier than general population — 
underestimates occupational hazards 
• 
   c. Volunteer Bias: Volunteers tend to be healthier and more health-conscious than non-
volunteers 
• 
   d. Loss-to-Follow-up Bias: Those who drop out differ systematically from completers 
(common in cohort studies) 
• 
   e. Survival Bias: Studying survivors of a condition misses fatal cases, underestimating 
exposure effects 
• 
   f. Neyman Bias: Prevalent cases exclude those who died or recovered quickly — selective 
survival 
• 
II. INFORMATION BIAS — Error in measuring exposure or outcome 
• 
   a. Recall Bias: Cases remember past exposures more vividly than controls (especially in 
case-control studies). Example: Mothers of children with birth defects over-report medication 
use. 
• 
   b. Interviewer Bias: Interviewers probe cases more intensively than controls when aware 
of study hypothesis 
• 
   c. Misclassification Bias — Non-differential: Exposure misclassification equal in both 
groups; biases OR toward null. Differential: Unequal misclassification; can bias in either 
direction. 
• 
   d. Social Desirability Bias: Participants report behaviors they think are expected (e.g., 
over-reporting vaccine compliance) 
• 
   e. Surveillance Bias (Detection Bias): More exposed individuals are examined more 
frequently — artificially inflating detection 
• 
III. CONFOUNDING — Mixing of effects 
• 
   Definition: A confounder is associated with both the exposure and outcome and is not on 
the causal pathway. Example: Smoking confounds alcohol-oral cancer association. 
• 
   Types: Positive confounding (inflates association) and negative confounding (dilutes 
association) 
• 
IV. OTHER BIASES 
• 
   Hawthorne Effect: Subjects change behavior when observed 
• 
   Publication Bias: Positive studies preferentially published — inflates systematic review 
estimates 
• 
   Lead Time Bias: Earlier diagnosis appears to extend survival without actually doing so 
(common in screening evaluation) 
• 
   Length Time Bias: Screening preferentially detects slowly progressive (less aggressive) 
disease 
200


--- PAGE 213 ---

swaDesh
• 
   Allocation Bias: Non-random treatment allocation in non-RCT studies 
How Biases Influence Study Results 
• 
Selection Bias → Spurious associations or masking of real associations 
• 
Recall Bias → Over-estimation of OR in case-control studies 
• 
Confounding → Either inflation or dilution of true effect 
• 
Publication Bias → Overestimation of treatment effects in meta-analyses 
• 
Lead Time Bias → Artificial extension of survival in screening programs 
Strategies to Reduce Bias 
• 
Study Design Phase: Randomization (controls all known and unknown confounders); Strict 
inclusion/exclusion criteria; Prospective design; Pre-registration of study protocol 
• 
Data Collection Phase: Blinding of participants, interviewers, and outcome assessors; 
Standardized questionnaires; Objective biomarkers over self-report; Training and calibration 
of data collectors 
• 
Analysis Phase: Stratification (Mantel-Haenszel); Multivariate logistic/Cox regression; 
Propensity score matching; Sensitivity analysis 
• 
Reporting Phase: STROBE (observational), CONSORT (RCT), PRISMA (meta-analysis) 
checklists; Funnel plots for publication bias in meta-analysis 
🧠 MNEMONIC — All Biases — 'SIC + HELP' 
  S — Selection Bias (Berkson's, Healthy Worker, Volunteer, Survival) 
  I — Information Bias (Recall, Interviewer, Misclassification) 
  C — Confounding 
  H — Hawthorne Effect 
  E — Effect modification (interaction) 
  L — Lead Time Bias / Length Time Bias 
  P — Publication Bias 
   
  Memory hook: Studies In Communities HELP reduce bias 
   
  To REDUCE bias remember 'RAMP': 
  R — Randomize 
  A — Allocate concealed 
  M — Measure blindly 
  P — Pre-register protocol 
 
QUICK REVISION SUMMARY

TOPIC 27: MEASURES OF CENTRAL TENDENCY & VARIABILITY 
(Frequency: 4 Times Asked | Years: 2018, 2021, 2020) 
 
Q1. Briefly Explain Measures of Central Tendency with Example (2018) ★★★★ 
 
INTRODUCTION 
Measures of central tendency are statistical values that describe the center or typical value of a dataset. 
They summarize a large set of data into a single representative figure, facilitating comparison and 
interpretation. The three principal measures are Mean, Median, and Mode. 
 
1. ARITHMETIC MEAN (Average) 
The arithmetic mean is the sum of all observations divided by the total number of observations. 
Formula: Mean (X̄ ) = Sum of all values (ΣX) / Number of observations (N) 
Example: Haemoglobin levels (g/dL): 10, 11, 12, 13, 14 
Mean = (10+11+12+13+14)/5 = 60/5 = 12 g/dL 
• 
Best used for: Normally distributed continuous data (height, weight, BP) 
• 
Advantage: Uses all observations; mathematically tractable 
• 
Disadvantage: Affected by extreme values (outliers); not suitable for skewed distributions 
 
2. MEDIAN 
The median is the middle value when observations are arranged in ascending or descending order. It 
divides the distribution into two equal halves. 
Calculation: 
• 
Odd N: Median = value of [(N+1)/2]th observation 
• 
Even N: Median = average of (N/2)th and (N/2 + 1)th observations 
Example: Income data (INR): 5000, 8000, 10000, 12000, 50000 
Median = 3rd value = 10,000 (Mean would be skewed to 17,000 by outlier) 
• 
Best used for: Skewed distributions, ordinal data, income data 
• 
Advantage: Not affected by extreme values; easy to compute 
• 
Disadvantage: Does not use all values; less amenable to algebraic treatment 
 
3. MODE 
The mode is the value that occurs most frequently in a dataset. 
Example: Blood groups of 20 patients: A, B, O, O, AB, O, A, O, B, O — Mode = O (appears most 
often) 
• 
Best used for: Nominal data, qualitative data, most common disease/symptom 
• 
Advantage: Simple to understand; applicable to qualitative data 
• 
Disadvantage: May not be unique; not based on all values 
 
203


--- PAGE 216 ---

swaDesh
RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN MEAN, MEDIAN & MODE 
Distribution Type 
Relationship 
Symmetrical (Normal) 
Mean = Median = Mode 
Positively skewed 
Mode < Median < Mean 
Negatively skewed 
Mean < Median < Mode 
 
CHOICE OF MEASURE — PEARL TABLE 
Situation 
Best Measure 
Normally distributed quantitative 
Arithmetic Mean 
Skewed distribution / income 
Median 
Nominal/qualitative data 
Mode 
Open-ended distributions 
Median 
 
MNEMONIC 
"M-M-M — Mean Minds the Middle, Median Manages Skew, 
Mode Maps the Most" 
M = Mean (uses all, affected by outliers) | M = Median (middle, skew-
resistant) | M = Mode (most frequent) | 3Ms in Normal distribution are 
EQUAL | In Positive skew: Mode < Median < Mean (alphabetical order) 
 
 
204


--- PAGE 217 ---

swaDesh
Q2. Explain Different Measures of Central Tendency with Examples, Merits and 
Demerits (2021) ★★★ 
(This is a combined question — see Q1 above for Central Tendency. Below covers Variability in detail) 
 
Q3. Explain Different Measures of Variability with Examples (2020) ★★★ 
 
INTRODUCTION 
Measures of variability (dispersion) describe the spread or scatter of data around the central value. They 
complement central tendency measures by showing how homogeneous or heterogeneous a dataset is. 
The principal measures are: Range, Mean Deviation, Standard Deviation, Variance, Coefficient of 
Variation, and Percentiles/Quartiles. 
 
1. RANGE 
Range = Maximum value − Minimum value 
Example: BP readings 110, 120, 130, 140, 160 mmHg → Range = 160 − 110 = 50 mmHg 
• 
Merit: Simplest; quick estimate of spread 
• 
Demerit: Uses only 2 values; highly affected by outliers; does not reflect distribution of 
intermediate values 
 
2. MEAN DEVIATION (Average Deviation) 
Mean Deviation = Σ|X − X̄ | / N (sum of absolute deviations from mean divided by N) 
• 
Merit: Uses all values; easy to understand 
• 
Demerit: Algebraically less tractable due to absolute values 
 
3. STANDARD DEVIATION (SD) — MOST IMPORTANT ★ 
SD is the square root of the average of squared deviations from the mean. It is the most widely used 
measure of dispersion in biostatistics. 
Formula: SD (σ) = √[Σ(X − X̄ )² / N] 
Properties of Normal Distribution using SD: 
• 
Mean ± 1 SD → covers 68.27% of observations 
• 
Mean ± 2 SD → covers 95.45% of observations 
• 
Mean ± 3 SD → covers 99.73% of observations 
Example: Mean haemoglobin = 12 g/dL, SD = 1.5 g/dL 
Reference range (Mean ± 2 SD) = 12 ± 3 = 9–15 g/dL (covers 95% of healthy population) 
• 
Merit: Mathematically precise; basis for further statistical tests (t-test, ANOVA, CI) 
• 
Demerit: Affected by extreme values; units same as original data 
 
4. VARIANCE 
Variance = SD² = Σ(X − X̄ )² / N 
205


--- PAGE 218 ---

swaDesh
• 
Used in ANOVA and regression analysis 
• 
Demerit: Units are squared (e.g., g²/dL²), hence less interpretable directly 
 
5. COEFFICIENT OF VARIATION (CV) 
CV = (SD / Mean) × 100%  — a dimensionless measure, useful for comparing variability between groups 
with different units or different means. 
Example: Comparing variability of height and weight in a population — CV standardizes 
comparison across different scales. 
• 
Merit: Unit-free; allows comparison between datasets 
• 
Demerit: Not applicable when mean is zero or near zero 
 
6. PERCENTILES AND QUARTILES 
• 
Percentile: Value below which a given percentage of observations fall 
• 
Q1 (25th percentile), Q2 (50th = Median), Q3 (75th percentile) 
• 
Inter-Quartile Range (IQR) = Q3 − Q1: Most robust measure; used in Box plots; not affected by 
extremes 
Public Health Application: Growth charts use 3rd, 10th, 50th, 90th, 97th percentiles for child 
nutrition assessment (WHO/IAP standards). 
 
MNEMONIC 
"R-V-SD-C-P" → "Ravi Visits Standard Deviation's CV 
Party" 
R = Range | V = Variance | SD = Standard Deviation (MOST IMPORTANT) 
| C = Coefficient of Variation (unit-free, comparative) | P = Percentiles/IQR 
(robust, used in growth charts) | SD 1-2-3 covers 68-95-99.7% (Rule of 68-
95-99)

TOPIC 28: MEDICAL SOCIOLOGY / SOCIAL STRUCTURE 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked — NEW | Years: May 2025, Dec 2024, 2015) 
 
Q1. Medical Sociology — Definition and Significance (May 2025) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
DEFINITION 
Medical Sociology is the branch of sociology concerned with the study of social factors, social 
relationships, social structures, and social institutions as they relate to health, illness, medical care, and 
the healthcare system. It examines how society shapes and is shaped by health phenomena. 
The term was first coined by Charles McIntire (1894). It gained academic recognition in the 1950s–60s 
through the works of Talcott Parsons ('The Social System', 1951) and Robert Straus. 
 
SCOPE OF MEDICAL SOCIOLOGY 
• 
Sociology OF medicine: Studies doctors, nurses, hospitals, doctor-patient relationships as social 
phenomena 
• 
Sociology IN medicine: Applies sociological methods to solve medical/public health problems 
• 
Social determinants of health: How class, caste, gender, and poverty influence health outcomes 
• 
Health behaviour: Why people seek or avoid healthcare (Illness Behaviour Model, Health Belief 
Model) 
• 
Medicalization of social problems: Defining social issues (alcoholism, obesity) as medical 
conditions 
 
SIGNIFICANCE OF MEDICAL SOCIOLOGY IN PUBLIC HEALTH 
1. Understanding disease distribution: Social stratification explains differential morbidity/mortality 
patterns across SES groups 
2. Doctor-patient relationship: Sociological analysis improves communication and adherence 
3. Health behavior change: Identifies social barriers to healthcare utilization and compliance 
4. Healthcare organization: Helps design effective hospital structures, referral systems, team 
dynamics 
5. Planning preventive services: Social diagnosis is integral to community health programmes 
(PRECEDE-PROCEED model) 
6. Cultural competence: Understanding beliefs, stigma, and taboos improves community 
acceptance of health interventions 
7. Policy formulation: Identifies social determinants requiring intersectoral action (e.g., NHP 2017, 
SDGs) 
 
TALCOTT PARSONS' SICK ROLE THEORY 
Rights of the Sick Person 
Obligations of the Sick Person 
Exempted from normal social roles 
Must seek professional medical help 
Not held responsible for illness 
Must want to get well and cooperate with 
treatment 
207


--- PAGE 220 ---

swaDesh
 
SOCIAL DETERMINANTS OF HEALTH (WHO Dahlgren-Whitehead Model) 
• 
Individual factors: Age, sex, hereditary factors 
• 
Individual lifestyle: Diet, exercise, smoking 
• 
Social & community networks: Social support, norms, peer influence 
• 
Living & working conditions: Employment, education, sanitation 
• 
General socioeconomic, cultural & environmental conditions: GDP, policies, governance

Q2. Role of Culture and Social Structure in Health and Disease (Dec 2024) ★★★ 
*NEW* 
 
SOCIAL STRUCTURE — DEFINITION 
Social structure refers to the organized pattern of social relationships and social institutions that together 
constitute society. Key components include social class, caste, gender, family structure, religion, and 
occupational roles. 
 
ROLE OF SOCIAL STRUCTURE IN HEALTH 
1. Social Class and Health 
The Black Report (UK, 1980) and WHO Commission on SDH (2008) document clear inverse relationships 
between social class and health outcomes: 
• 
Lower socioeconomic class → higher infant mortality, malnutrition, communicable diseases, 
mental illness 
• 
Higher class → better access to healthcare, nutrition, and health information 
• 
In India: SC/ST populations carry disproportionate burden of TB, malnutrition, and maternal 
mortality 
 
2. Family Structure 
• 
Nuclear family: Reduced caregiver support for elderly, children; isolation; mental health stress 
• 
Joint family: Better social support; shared childcare; but higher communicable disease 
transmission 
• 
Single-parent families: Higher poverty rates, poorer child health outcomes 
 
3. Gender and Health 
• 
Women: Higher morbidity but lower mortality (female survival advantage) 
• 
Patriarchal structures: Delayed healthcare for women; undernutrition; poor reproductive health 
• 
Men: Higher risk-taking behaviour; higher mortality from injuries, CVD, substance abuse 
 
ROLE OF CULTURE IN HEALTH AND DISEASE 
1. Cultural Beliefs and Healthcare Seeking 
• 
Illness explanatory models: Supernatural causation → faith healers preferred over modern 
medicine 
• 
Stigma: Mental illness, HIV/AIDS, TB — cultural stigma delays diagnosis and treatment 
• 
Traditional medicine: Ayurveda, Unani, home remedies — can complement or delay allopathic 
care 
 
2. Dietary Practices 
• 
Religious food taboos: Vegetarianism, fasting → nutritional deficiencies (iron, B12) 
• 
Cultural preferences: High salt diet (hypertension), excess ghee/sweets (diabetes/CVD) 
• 
Colostrum discarding, late introduction of complementary foods → malnutrition in infants 
209


--- PAGE 222 ---

swaDesh
 
3. Cultural Practices and Disease 
Cultural Practice 
Health Impact 
Female genital mutilation 
Infections, obstetric complications 
Child marriage 
Adolescent pregnancy, maternal mortality 
Purdah/seclusion 
Vitamin D deficiency, osteomalacia 
Sickle cell (certain tribes) 
Protective against malaria but causes disease 
Tobacco/pan chewing 
Oral cancer, COPD 
Hand washing before meals 
Prevention of enteric infections 
 
MNEMONIC 
"CAGE" for Culture's role in Health 
C = Cultural beliefs shape healthcare seeking | A = Alimentary (dietary)

TOPIC 29: HEALTH EDUCATION — Planning, Steps, Methods, 
AETCOM 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked — NEW | Years: Nov 2023, Dec 2024, May 2024) 
 
Q1. Briefly Describe Steps in Planning a Health Education Session in Community 
Setting (Nov 2023) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
DEFINITION 
Health education is the process of providing individuals and communities with knowledge, skills, and 
motivation to voluntarily adopt behaviours conducive to health. Planning a health education session is 
essential for achieving measurable outcomes. 
 
STEPS IN PLANNING A HEALTH EDUCATION SESSION 
STEP 1: Community / Situation Analysis (Needs Assessment) 
• 
Identify the health problem and target population 
• 
Assess existing knowledge, attitudes, and practices (KAP survey) 
• 
Identify cultural, social, and environmental factors 
• 
Methods: Community survey, focus group discussion (FGD), key informant interview, records 
review 
 
STEP 2: Define Objectives (SMART Objectives) 
• 
Specific, Measurable, Achievable, Relevant, Time-bound 
• 
Cognitive objectives: Knowledge gain (e.g., 'audience will be able to list 3 symptoms of TB') 
• 
Affective objectives: Attitude change (e.g., 'audience will value hand hygiene') 
• 
Psychomotor objectives: Skill acquisition (e.g., 'demonstrate correct ORS preparation') 
 
STEP 3: Identify the Target Audience 
• 
Primary audience: Those whose behaviour you want to change (e.g., mothers of under-5 
children) 
• 
Secondary audience: Influencers (e.g., community leaders, ANMs, teachers) 
• 
Consider: Literacy level, language, age, cultural background 
 
STEP 4: Select Content (Subject Matter) 
• 
Based on assessed needs and defined objectives 
• 
Sequence: Simple to complex, known to unknown, familiar to unfamiliar 
• 
Apply KISS principle: Keep It Short and Simple 
 
STEP 5: Select Teaching-Learning Methods (TLM) 
Method 
Best Use 
211


--- PAGE 224 ---

swaDesh
Lecture / Talk 
Large group, initial awareness 
Group Discussion 
Attitude change, problem solving 
Demonstration 
Skill teaching (ORS, hand washing) 
Role play / Drama 
Behaviour change, social issues 
IEC materials (posters, pamphlets) 
Reinforcement, mass outreach 
Audio-visual aids (video, film) 
Illiterate populations, mass messages 
 
STEP 6: Select and Prepare Teaching Aids (AV Aids) 
• 
Choose appropriate to literacy, culture, setting 
• 
Ensure visibility, simplicity, and cultural sensitivity 
 
STEP 7: Implementation (Conduct the Session) 
• 
Introduction: Establish rapport, state objectives (5 minutes) 
• 
Main content: Present information using chosen methods (20–30 minutes) 
• 
Interaction: Q&A, demonstrations, group activity (10 minutes) 
• 
Conclusion: Summary, key messages, call to action (5 minutes) 
 
STEP 8: Evaluation (AETCOM Framework) 
• 
Process evaluation: Was the session conducted as planned? 
• 
Impact evaluation: Short-term knowledge/attitude change (pre-post test) 
• 
Outcome evaluation: Long-term behaviour change and health status 
 
ELEMENTS OF EVALUATION (AETCOM — Types and Elements) 
Element of Evaluation 
What is Assessed

Were objectives appropriate to needs? 
Effectiveness 
Were objectives achieved? 
Efficiency 
Was it cost-effective?

Short-term change in K/A/P?

Is the effect maintained?

Was the target population reached?

Q2. Enumerate Teaching Learning Methods for AETCOM in CBME Curriculum 
(Dec 2024) ★★★ *NEW* 
 
WHAT IS AETCOM? 
AETCOM stands for Attitude, Ethics, and Communication module — a mandatory component of 
Competency-Based Medical Education (CBME) curriculum introduced by MCI (now NMC) in India from 
2019 for MBBS students. It aims to produce doctors with professional values alongside clinical 
competence. 
 
OBJECTIVES OF AETCOM 
• 
Develop professional attitudes: Compassion, empathy, integrity, respect 
• 
Ethical decision-making: Bioethical principles (autonomy, beneficence, non-maleficence, justice) 
• 
Communication skills: Doctor-patient communication, breaking bad news, informed consent 
• 
Professionalism: Punctuality, appearance, teamwork, accountability 
 
TEACHING-LEARNING METHODS FOR AETCOM 
1. Case-Based Discussion (CBD) 
• 
Real or simulated patient cases involving ethical dilemmas 
• 
Example: Case of refusing treatment, end-of-life decisions, informed consent issues 
• 
Encourages reflective learning and ethical reasoning 
 
2. Role Play / Simulation 
• 
Students enact doctor-patient scenarios 
• 
Develop communication skills — history taking, delivering bad news (SPIKES protocol), 
obtaining consent 
• 
Standardized Patient (SP) models used in OSCEs 
 
3. Problem-Based Learning (PBL) 
• 
Self-directed learning triggered by patient problems 
• 
Develops critical thinking and collaborative skills 
 
4. Small Group Discussions (SGD) 
• 
Reflective discussion of professional dilemmas in groups of 8–12 students 
• 
Facilitator guides without imposing opinions 
 
5. Community Postings / Service Learning 
• 
Exposure to rural/urban community health setting 
• 
Builds empathy, social accountability, understanding of social determinants 
 
6. Reflective Writing / Portfolio 
213


--- PAGE 226 ---

swaDesh
• 
Students write reflections on clinical encounters, ethical dilemmas 
• 
Promotes metacognitive awareness of professional development 
 
7. Expert Lectures and Panel Discussions 
• 
Medical ethicists, social workers, patients share perspectives 
• 
Broadens understanding of healthcare from multiple viewpoints 
 
8. Videos and Film-Based Learning 
• 
Films/documentaries on ethical issues (e.g., Tuskegee study, euthanasia debates) 
• 
Followed by structured debriefing 
 
ASSESSMENT IN AETCOM 
Assessment Method 
What it Evaluates 
Mini-CEX (Clinical Evaluation Exercise) 
Communication and professionalism in clinical 
encounter 
360-degree feedback 
Attitude assessed by peers, faculty, patients 
Portfolio assessment 
Reflective growth over time 
OSCE stations 
Structured skill assessment — consent, 
communication 
Log book entries 
Completion of required attitudinal activities

Practice Skills Requiring Virtue & Empathy"

TOPIC 30: VULNERABLE POPULATIONS — Health Problems and 
Challenges 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked — NEW | Years: Oct 2025, Dec 2024) 
 
Q1. Health Problems of Vulnerable Populations (Oct 2025) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
DEFINITION 
Vulnerable populations are groups with increased susceptibility to poor health outcomes due to biological, 
social, economic, or environmental disadvantages. They are at higher risk of experiencing health 
disparities and face barriers to accessing appropriate healthcare. 
 
CATEGORIES OF VULNERABLE POPULATIONS 
Category 
Examples 
Demographic vulnerabilities 
Children under 5, elderly (>60 years), 
adolescents 
Social vulnerabilities 
Scheduled Castes/Tribes, migrant workers, 
homeless, prisoners 
Health-related vulnerabilities 
Persons with HIV/AIDS, mental illness, 
disabilities 
Economic vulnerabilities 
BPL families, landless labourers, unemployed 
Gender-based vulnerabilities 
Women (especially pregnant), LGBTQ+ 
populations 
Geographic vulnerabilities 
Tribal/remote populations, disaster-affected 
areas 
 
1. CHILDREN UNDER 5 YEARS — Health Problems 
• 
Malnutrition: Severe Acute Malnutrition (SAM), stunting, wasting — India has highest burden 
• 
Communicable diseases: Pneumonia (leading cause of U5 mortality globally), diarrhoea, 
malaria 
• 
Vaccine-preventable diseases: Polio, measles (in unimmunized children) 
• 
Perinatal causes: Preterm birth, birth asphyxia, sepsis 
• 
MNCH programmes: IMNCI, NHM, POSHAN Abhiyaan, Pradhan Mantri Surakshit Matritva 
Abhiyan 
 
2. ELDERLY (>60 YEARS) — Health Problems 
• 
NCDs: Hypertension, type 2 DM, COPD, IHD — multiple comorbidities 
• 
Falls and fractures: Osteoporosis, loss of balance (leading cause of disability) 
• 
Cognitive decline: Dementia, Alzheimer's disease — rising burden 
• 
Mental health: Depression, loneliness, elder abuse 
• 
Polypharmacy: Multiple medications → adverse drug interactions 
215


--- PAGE 228 ---

swaDesh
• 
Programmes: IGNOAPS, National Programme for Health Care of Elderly (NPHCE) 
 
3. WOMEN — Health Problems 
• 
Maternal mortality: MMR 97/100,000 (India, SRS 2018-20); obstetric complications 
• 
Anaemia: Prevalent in 52% of pregnant women (NFHS-5); causes adverse perinatal outcomes 
• 
Reproductive health: RTI/STI, cervical cancer, PCOD, infertility 
• 
Gender-based violence: Domestic violence — affects physical and mental health 
• 
Undernutrition: Triple burden of malnutrition in women 
 
4. TRIBAL / SCHEDULED TRIBE POPULATIONS — Health Problems 
• 
High burden of malaria (P. falciparum predominant in tribal areas) 
• 
Sickle cell disease: Prevalent in Chhattisgarh, Odisha, Maharashtra tribal belt 
• 
Malnutrition: Severe; linked to food insecurity and traditional dietary practices 
• 
Poor healthcare access: Geographical barriers, shortage of health workers (IPHS norms not 
met) 
• 
Fluorosis: Endemic in tribal regions of Rajasthan, AP, Telangana 
 
5. PERSONS WITH HIV/AIDS — Health Problems 
• 
Opportunistic infections: TB (commonest OI in India), PCP, cryptococcal meningitis 
• 
Social stigma: Discrimination, loss of employment, isolation 
• 
Mental health: Depression, suicidality high in PLHIV 
• 
Nutritional deficiencies: Weight loss, wasting syndrome 
• 
Programmes: NACP, NACO, free ART through 800+ ART centres 
 
6. MIGRANT WORKERS — Health Problems 
• 
Communicable diseases: TB, STI/HIV (high-risk behaviours en route) 
• 
Occupational hazards: Silicosis, heat stroke, injuries (construction, mining) 
• 
Mental health: Separation anxiety, stress, alcoholism 
• 
No access to healthcare: Not covered by local health systems; poor social protection 
• 
Highlighted during COVID-19 pandemic — reverse migration crisis 2020

Q2. Ethical Considerations While Conducting Research in Vulnerable 
Populations (Dec 2024) ★★★ *NEW* 
 
WHY SPECIAL ETHICS IN VULNERABLE POPULATION RESEARCH? 
Vulnerable populations have diminished capacity to protect their own interests, are subject to coercion, 
have limited literacy, or are institutionalized. Special ethical safeguards are required to prevent 
exploitation while allowing these groups to benefit from research. 
 
FOUNDATIONAL ETHICAL FRAMEWORKS 
Framework / Declaration 
Key Contribution 
Nuremberg Code (1947) 
10 principles; voluntary consent mandatory; no 
coercion 
Declaration of Helsinki (1964, revised 2013) 
Ethical principles for medical research; 
vulnerable populations need extra protection 
Belmont Report (1979) 
3 principles: Respect for persons, Beneficence, 
Justice 
ICMR Guidelines India (2017) 
Context-specific guidelines for research in India 
 
BELMONT PRINCIPLES APPLIED TO VULNERABLE POPULATIONS 
1. Respect for Persons (Autonomy) 
• 
Informed Consent: Must be voluntary, comprehensible, and documented 
• 
Assent + Consent: For children — assent from child + consent from parent/guardian 
• 
Simplified consent: For illiterate participants — witnessed thumb impression; vernacular 
language explanation 
• 
Capacity assessment: For mentally ill, cognitively impaired — assess decision-making capacity 
• 
Re-consent: Required if study design changes significantly 
 
2. Beneficence and Non-Maleficence 
• 
Risk-benefit analysis: Benefits must outweigh risks, especially for those who cannot advocate 
for themselves 
• 
Minimal risk principle: For children and mentally ill — interventions must be of minimal risk 
unless direct benefit 
• 
Post-trial access: Participants should have access to beneficial treatments discovered during 
research 
 
3. Justice 
• 
Fair selection: Vulnerable populations should not be chosen merely for convenience (easy to 
recruit) 
• 
Equitable access: Benefits of research should be accessible to the community studied 
• 
Avoid double standard: Standards of care in research should not be lower in LMIC than in 
developed countries 
217


--- PAGE 230 ---

swaDesh
 
SPECIFIC CHALLENGES 
• 
Therapeutic misconception: Participants may confuse research with treatment 
• 
Undue inducement: Monetary incentives may coerce economically vulnerable 
• 
Language barriers: Consent forms in local language mandatory 
• 
Power imbalance: Doctor-patient relationship may inhibit refusal 
• 
Confidentiality: HIV status, mental illness — strict data protection required 
 
SAFEGUARDS IN PRACTICE 
8. IEC/IRB/Ethics Committee review mandatory before study commencement 
9. Independent patient advocates for mentally ill/incapacitated 
10. Community Advisory Board (CAB) for tribal/marginalized group research 
11. Data safety monitoring board (DSMB) for trials involving vulnerable groups 
12. Standard operating procedures for emergency withdrawal from study 
 
MNEMONIC 
"ETHICAL RESEARCH" mnemonic for key principles 
E = Ethics Committee Approval mandatory | T = Therapeutic misconception 
— address it | H = Helsinki Declaration principles | I = Informed Consent 
(capacity-sensitive) | C = Confidentiality and data protection | A = Autonomy 
(assent + consent for children) | L = Language-accessible materials | + R = 
Risk-Benefit ratio must favour participant | E = Equitable selection (not just 
convenient) | S = Standard of care — no double standard | E = Exit rights — 
right to withdraw anytime | A = Advocate (independent) for incapacitated | R 
= Re-consent for protocol changes | C = Community Advisory Board for 
marginalized | H = Helsinki + Belmont + ICMR 2017 guidelines

TOPIC 31: FOOD SAFETY / FOOD FORTIFICATION 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked | Years: Nov 2023, Dec 2024, 2022, 2020) 
 
Q1. Describe the Methods of Food Fortification (Nov 2023) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
DEFINITION 
Food fortification is the practice of deliberately increasing the content of essential micronutrients 
(vitamins, minerals) in a food item so as to improve the nutritional quality of the food supply and to provide 
a public health benefit with minimal risk to health. — WHO/FAO, 2006 
 
RATIONALE FOR FOOD FORTIFICATION IN INDIA 
• 
India has the world's highest burden of micronutrient deficiencies — termed 'Hidden Hunger' 
• 
National Family Health Survey (NFHS-5, 2019-21): Anaemia in 67% children, 57% women 
• 
Cost-effective: One of the most cost-effective public health nutrition interventions (Copenhagen 
Consensus) 
• 
Sustainable: Requires no change in dietary habits or individual behaviour 
 
TYPES / METHODS OF FOOD FORTIFICATION 
1. Mass Fortification (Population-Level) 
• 
Mandatory addition of micronutrients to staple foods consumed by entire population 
• 
Reach: Maximum population coverage 
Examples in India: 
• 
Salt iodization (since 1962): Potassium iodate added to all edible salt → eliminates IDD (Iodine 
Deficiency Disorders) 
• 
Double Fortified Salt (DFS): Iodine + Iron added → addresses both IDD and anaemia 
simultaneously 
• 
Wheat flour fortification: Iron + Folic acid + Vitamin B12 added (FSSAI standards) 
• 
Rice fortification: Fortified rice kernels (FRK) blended at 1:100 ratio — Iron, Folic acid, Vitamin 
B12 
• 
Edible oil fortification: Vitamin A + Vitamin D added (mandatory per FSSAI for refined oils) 
• 
Milk fortification: Vitamin A + Vitamin D (FSSAI mandated) 
 
2. Targeted Fortification 
• 
Fortification of specific foods for specific population subgroups at risk 
Examples: 
• 
Complementary foods for infants and young children (e.g., Cerelac fortified with micronutrients) 
• 
Supplementary Nutrition Programme (SNP) foods under ICDS — fortified Bal Shakti, Nutrimix 
• 
Mid-Day Meals under PM POSHAN Abhiyan — fortified rice and cereals 
• 
Therapeutic foods for SAM children: RUTF (Ready-to-Use Therapeutic Food) 
 
219


--- PAGE 232 ---

swaDesh
3. Market-Driven/Voluntary Fortification 
• 
Food manufacturers voluntarily add micronutrients to commercial products 
Examples: 
• 
Fortified breakfast cereals, biscuits, energy drinks 
• 
Reaches higher SES groups; subject to FSSAI regulations 
 
4. Biofortification 
• 
Breeding crop varieties with higher intrinsic micronutrient content through: 
• 
Conventional plant breeding (e.g., Orange-fleshed sweet potato — Vitamin A) 
• 
Transgenic methods: Golden Rice (beta-carotene — Vitamin A) 
• 
Agronomic practices: Selenium fertilization of soil 
Examples in India: 
• 
Iron-biofortified pearl millet (bajra) — HarvestPlus programme 
• 
Zinc-biofortified wheat varieties released by ICAR 
• 
Advantage: Reaches remote populations without food distribution systems; sustainable 
 
REGULATORY FRAMEWORK — INDIA 
Regulation 
Content 
Food Safety and Standards Act 2006 (FSSAI) 
Mandates standards for food fortification in 
India 
FSSAI Fortification Standards 2018 
Standards for wheat, rice, oil, milk, double-
fortified salt 
Prevention of Food Adulteration Act (PFA) 
1954 
Prohibits adulteration; quality standards for 
food 
+F logo (FSSAI) 
Identifies fortified food products for consumer 
recognition 
 
CHALLENGES IN FOOD FORTIFICATION 
• 
Stability: Micronutrients degraded by heat, light, and storage 
• 
Bioavailability: Added nutrients may have lower bioavailability than intrinsic 
• 
Cost: Increases food production cost (though minimal for staples) 
• 
Monitoring: Ensuring compliance across decentralized food supply chains 
• 
Consumer acceptance: Fortified rice kernels may be removed during washing by consumers 
 
MNEMONIC 
"MMTB" — 4 Types of Food Fortification 
M = Mass Fortification (staples: salt, rice, oil, flour — largest reach) | M = 
Market-driven / Voluntary (commercial foods) | T = Targeted (vulnerable 
groups: ICDS, MDM) | B = Biofortification (crop breeding: Golden Rice, iron 
bajra) | Key nutrients in India: IIFAB = Iodine-Iron-Folate-A-B12 | Regulated 
by: FSSAI 2006 | +F logo marks fortified foods 
 
 
220


--- PAGE 233 ---

swaDesh
Q3. Define Food Adulteration. Standards for Quality. Describe Provisions of PFA 
Act (2022, 2020) ★★★ 
 
FOOD ADULTERATION — DEFINITION 
Food adulteration is the act of intentionally debasing the quality of food by adding, substituting, or 
abstracting substances that are injurious to health or reduce its quality/nutritive value, or by concealing 
inferior quality or adding substandard substances. 
Per PFA Act 1954, Section 2(i): An article of food is deemed to be adulterated if: 
• 
Any substance is added which reduces its quality or nutritive value 
• 
Any substance is mixed which is injurious to health 
• 
The food is derived from diseased animals 
• 
The food is prepared, packed, or kept under insanitary conditions 
 
TYPES OF ADULTERATION 
Type 
Examples 
Intentional adulteration 
Adding chicory to coffee, water to milk, chalk 
powder to flour 
Incidental adulteration 
Pesticide residues in vegetables, aflatoxins in 
groundnuts 
Metallic adulteration 
Arsenic in water, lead in coloring agents 
Packaging adulteration 
Substandard packing material leaching into 
food 
 
COMMON ADULTERANTS IN INDIA 
Food Item 
Common Adulterant 
Milk 
Water, starch, urea, detergent, melamine 
Ghee / Butter 
Vanaspati (hydrogenated fat), animal fat, starch 
Turmeric powder 
Lead chromate, chalk powder (metanil yellow 
dye) 
Black pepper 
Papaya seeds, dry papaya berries 
Chilli powder 
Red brick powder, artificial dyes 
Tea leaves 
Exhausted tea, iron filings, artificial coloring 
Honey 
Sugar syrup, jaggery solution 
Pulses / Dals 
Metanil yellow dye, kesari dal (Lathyrus sativus 
— causes lathyrism) 
 
PREVENTION OF FOOD ADULTERATION ACT, 1954 — KEY PROVISIONS 
Administration 
• 
Central Committee on Food Standards (CCFS): Sets national food standards 
221


--- PAGE 234 ---

swaDesh
• 
Food (Health) Authority: Established at state level for enforcement 
• 
Food Inspector: Appointed under Section 9 to collect samples 
 
Sample Collection (Section 10) 
• 
Inspector purchases sample in presence of vendor; quantity specified 
• 
Sample divided into 3 equal parts → labelled, sealed 
• 
One part sent to Public Analyst, one to vendor, one retained for reference 
• 
Public Analyst reports within 45 days 
 
Penalties (Sections 16-17) 
Offence 
Penalty 
Sale of adulterated food causing injury/death 
Imprisonment 3 years to life; fine ≥ Rs. 5,000 
Sale of adulterated food — no injury 
Imprisonment up to 6 months; fine up to Rs. 
1,000 
Misbranded food 
Imprisonment up to 6 months; fine up to Rs. 
1,000 
Repeat offences 
Enhanced punishment; cancellation of licence 
 
Note: PFA Act 1954 was replaced by the Food Safety and Standards Act 2006 (FSSA) and its 
implementing body FSSAI (Food Safety and Standards Authority of India). PFA Act remains 
historical context for examinations.

TOPIC 32: HUMAN DEVELOPMENT INDEX (HDI) 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked | Years: May 2025, 2018, 2011) 
 
Q1. Human Development Index — Definition, Importance and Status of India (May 
2025) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
BACKGROUND AND DEFINITION 
The Human Development Index (HDI) is a composite statistical index developed by UNDP (United 
Nations Development Programme) to measure and rank countries' achievements in social and economic 
development. It was conceptualized by Pakistani economist Mahbub ul Haq and Indian Nobel laureate 
Amartya Sen, and first introduced in the Human Development Report 1990. 
The HDI moves beyond GDP to assess human capability and well-being comprehensively, 
encompassing three fundamental dimensions of human development: Health, Education, and Standard 
of Living. 
 
DIMENSIONS AND INDICATORS OF HDI 
DIMENSION 
INDICATOR 
GOALPOSTS (Min–Max) 
Health (Long & Healthy Life) 
Life Expectancy at Birth (LEB) 
20 years – 85 years 
Education (Knowledge) 
Mean Years of Schooling 
(MYS) Expected Years of 
Schooling (EYS) 
0–15 years 0–18 years 
Standard of Living (Decent 
Living) 
Gross National Income (GNI) 
per capita (PPP $) 
$100 – $75,000 
 
CALCULATION OF HDI 
• 
Dimension Index = (Actual value − Minimum value) / (Maximum value − Minimum value) 
• 
HDI = (Health Index × Education Index × Income Index)^(1/3)  [Geometric Mean] 
• 
Range: 0 to 1 (higher = better development) 
 
HDI CLASSIFICATIONS (UNDP) 
Category 
HDI Score Range 
Very High Human Development 
0.800 and above 
High Human Development 
0.700 – 0.799 
Medium Human Development 
0.550 – 0.699 
Low Human Development 
Below 0.550 
 
STATUS OF INDIA — HDI 2024 (Human Development Report 2024) 
• 
India's HDI: 0.644 (Medium Human Development category) 
• 
Global Rank: 134 out of 193 countries (HDR 2024) 
223


--- PAGE 236 ---

swaDesh
• 
India's Life Expectancy at Birth: 67.7 years 
• 
Mean Years of Schooling: 6.6 years 
• 
Expected Years of Schooling: 12.6 years 
• 
GNI per capita (PPP): $8,475 
• 
India's HDI has improved from 0.434 (1990) to 0.644 (2024) — significant progress over 30 
years 
 
IMPORTANCE OF HDI 
13. Comprehensive measurement: Captures multidimensional nature of development beyond GDP 
14. Policy tool: Identifies gaps in health, education, or income for targeted policy interventions 
15. International comparison: Allows benchmarking against similar and higher-ranked countries 
16. Track progress: Monitors progress toward SDG targets (Goal 3, 4, 1, 10) 
17. Highlights inequalities: Gender HDI (GDI), Inequality-adjusted HDI (IHDI), MPI — reveal 
disparities 
18. Motivates intersectoral action: Links health, education, and economic ministries for holistic 
development 
 
INEQUALITY-ADJUSTED HDI (IHDI) 
• 
IHDI adjusts HDI for inequality in distribution across population 
• 
India's IHDI (2024): 0.475 — significantly lower than HDI (0.644) 
• 
The difference (HDI − IHDI = 26.2%) represents the 'loss' due to inequality 
• 
Highlights that India's average development masks large within-country inequalities 
 
RELATED INDICES

empowerment, labour market

standards

dimensions

TOPIC 33: LIFESTYLE / PRIMORDIAL PREVENTION / NCDs 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked | Years: May 2024, 2021, 2015, 2013) 
 
Q1. Primordial Prevention — Discuss (May 2024) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
DEFINITION 
Primordial prevention refers to the prevention of emergence and establishment of social, economic, and 
cultural patterns of living that are known to contribute to elevated risk of disease. It is aimed at preventing 
the conditions that generate risk factors, before those risk factors themselves develop. 
The term was coined by Strasser (1978) and introduced into cardiovascular epidemiology. It represents 
the ZEROTH level of prevention — acting even before primary prevention is needed. 
 
LEVELS OF PREVENTION — COMPARATIVE TABLE 
Level 
Target 
Mechanism 
Example 
Primordial 
Entire population; 
healthy people 
Prevent risk factors 
from emerging 
Ban tobacco 
advertising; urban 
green spaces 
Primary 
People at risk (risk 
factors present) 
Prevent disease onset 
Vaccination; health 
education 
Secondary 
Early/subclinical 
disease 
Early detection and 
treatment 
Screening 
(mammography, PAP 
smear) 
Tertiary 
Established disease 
Limit disability, prevent 
complications 
Cardiac rehab, diabetic 
foot care 
 
OBJECTIVES OF PRIMORDIAL PREVENTION 
• 
Prevent the emergence of risk factors for NCDs in populations where they have not yet 
appeared 
• 
Counteract social trends favouring adoption of high-risk lifestyles 
• 
Create health-supportive environments through policy and structural changes 
 
STRATEGIES AND MEASURES FOR PRIMORDIAL PREVENTION 
1. Healthy Public Policy 
• 
Tobacco control: Cigarette and Other Tobacco Products Act (COTPA) 2003 — ban on tobacco 
ads, plain packaging policy 
• 
Alcohol policy: Pricing strategies, restrictions on alcohol availability, drunk driving laws 
• 
Food policy: Front-of-pack nutrition labelling, regulation of trans fats (FSSAI limits <2% trans fat 
in oils), sugar tax proposals 
• 
Environmental policy: Air quality standards (NAAQS), pollution control, clean fuel policies

2. Urban Planning and Built Environment 
• 
Creation of parks, walking and cycling tracks → promotes physical activity 
• 
Smart City Mission India: Green spaces, pedestrian-friendly infrastructure 
• 
Reducing commuter dependence on motorized transport → reduces air pollution and sedentary 
behaviour 
 
3. Health Education and Mass Communication 
• 
Mass media campaigns: Quit tobacco campaigns (mCessation, iQuit), 'Fit India Movement' 
• 
School health programmes: Instill healthy habits from childhood — diet, exercise, stress 
management 
• 
Social media health promotion for adolescents 
 
4. Economic Measures 
• 
Sin taxes: Higher taxes on tobacco and alcohol (GST 28% + cess on tobacco products) 
• 
Subsidies for healthy foods: PM POSHAN Abhiyan — free nutritious mid-day meals 
• 
Food labelling laws: Ensure consumers can make informed choices 
 
5. Community-Level Interventions 
• 
Community kitchen gardens → healthy diet promotion 
• 
Workplace wellness programmes → NCD risk factor reduction at point of employment 
• 
Faith-based organization engagement → cultural change agents
