TOPIC 34: OPERATIONAL RESEARCH 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked | Years: 2011, 2018) 
 
Q1. Discuss Procedures Adopted for Operational Research in Health Services 
(2011) ★★★ 
Q2. Discuss the Role of Operational Research in Public Health (2018) ★★★ 
 
DEFINITION 
Operational Research (OR) in public health is the search for knowledge on strategies, interventions, tools, 
or policies that can enhance the quality, effectiveness, or coverage of programmes in which the research 
is being done. It evaluates whether programmes and services work under real-world conditions. 
Also defined as: 'Research into the identification and solution of operational problems in health 
programmes.' — WHO 
 
CHARACTERISTICS OF OPERATIONAL RESEARCH 
• 
Applied research: Done within the programme being studied, not in a laboratory 
• 
Programme-oriented: Aims to improve programme performance 
• 
Uses existing infrastructure: Utilizes programme data, staff, and resources 
• 
Rapid turnaround: Results needed quickly to inform operational decisions 
• 
Multi-disciplinary: Involves clinicians, epidemiologists, social scientists, managers 
 
STEPS / PROCEDURES IN OPERATIONAL RESEARCH 
Step 1: Problem Identification and Prioritization 
• 
Identify operational problem: High default rate, poor coverage, drug resistance, cold chain 
failure 
• 
Prioritize: Magnitude (prevalence/incidence), severity, community concern, feasibility of study 
• 
Involve programme managers, field workers, beneficiaries in problem identification 
 
Step 2: Literature Review and Preliminary Assessment 
• 
Search existing evidence: Has this problem been studied before? What solutions were tried? 
• 
Map current programme performance: Routine data, HMIS, NIKSHAY (for TB), etc. 
 
Step 3: Formulate Research Question and Objectives 
• 
Clear, focused research question: PICO format (Population, Intervention, Comparison, 
Outcome) 
• 
Objectives: Primary (essential) and secondary (exploratory) 
 
Step 4: Study Design Selection 
228


--- PAGE 241 ---

swaDesh
Design 
When Used 
Descriptive (survey, cross-sectional) 
Assess magnitude of operational problem; 
needs assessment 
Analytical (cohort, case-control) 
Identify risk factors for poor outcome (e.g., TB 
default) 
Interventional (quasi-experimental) 
Test new intervention within programme setting 
Mixed methods (qual + quant) 
When process and outcome data both needed 
Record review 
When programme data exists and needs 
analysis 
 
Step 5: Ethics Committee Approval 
• 
Even OR requires IEC approval: Programme data involves patient confidentiality 
• 
Informed consent: Usually waived for record reviews; required for interviews 
 
Step 6: Data Collection 
• 
Use of existing records: Patient registers, programme reports, HMIS 
• 
Primary data: Interviews with patients, health workers, programme staff 
• 
Tools: Structured questionnaires, checklists, observation forms 
 
Step 7: Data Analysis and Interpretation 
• 
Quantitative: Descriptive statistics, regression analysis, survival analysis 
• 
Qualitative: Thematic analysis, content analysis 
• 
Interpretation in context of programme operations 
 
Step 8: Dissemination and Implementation 
• 
Research-to-policy: Rapid dissemination to programme managers 
• 
Feedback to field: Key findings shared with health workers 
• 
Scale-up: Successful interventions integrated into national programme guidelines 
 
ROLE OF OPERATIONAL RESEARCH IN PUBLIC HEALTH 
19. Quality improvement: Identifies bottlenecks in service delivery (e.g., why ANC coverage is low) 
20. Programme evaluation: Assesses effectiveness of ongoing health programmes (RNTCP, IPPI, 
NHM) 
21. Resource optimization: Identifies cost-effective use of limited resources 
22. Evidence-based policy: Provides local evidence to adapt global guidelines to Indian context 
23. Human resource performance: Studies attrition, motivation, and performance of health workers 
24. Drug resistance: OR studies on MDR-TB, AMR — essential for treatment protocol updates 
25. Health systems strengthening: Identifies weak links in supply chain, referral, data quality 
 
EXAMPLES OF OR IN INDIA

TOPIC 35: COMMUNITY DIAGNOSIS 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked | Years: 2014, 2018) 
 
Q1. Discuss Community Diagnosis and Treatment with an Example (2014) ★★★ 
Q2. Explain Community Diagnosis with an Example (2018) ★★★ 
 
DEFINITION 
Community diagnosis is the process of identifying the major health problems of a community, the factors 
responsible for them (epidemiological, social, environmental, biological), and the community's resources 
and capacity to deal with them. It is the collective counterpart of individual patient diagnosis. 
John Gordon (1952) defined it as: 'An assessment of the health of a community, analogous to the clinical 
diagnosis of an individual patient.' 
 
OBJECTIVES OF COMMUNITY DIAGNOSIS 
• 
Identify the priority health problems of the community 
• 
Determine the distribution and determinants of disease 
• 
Identify available health resources and service gaps 
• 
Provide a baseline for evaluating health programmes 
• 
Plan rational, evidence-based health interventions 
 
COMPONENTS OF COMMUNITY DIAGNOSIS 
1. Community Profile ('The Patient') 
Parameter 
Data to Collect 
Demographic data 
Population size, age-sex distribution, growth 
rate 
Geographic data 
Area, topography, urban/rural classification 
Socioeconomic data 
Literacy rate, income levels, occupational 
distribution 
Environmental data 
Water supply, sanitation, housing, pollution 
Health service data 
PHC/CHC coverage, manpower, bed 
availability 
 
2. Health Status Assessment ('Symptoms and Signs') 
• 
Mortality indicators: CDR, IMR, MMR, U5MR, cause-specific mortality rates 
• 
Morbidity indicators: Disease incidence/prevalence, disability rates, DALY 
• 
Nutritional status: Prevalence of malnutrition (SAM, MAM), anaemia rates 
• 
Service utilization: ANC coverage, immunization rates, skilled birth attendance 
 
231


--- PAGE 244 ---

swaDesh
3. Epidemiological Analysis ('Diagnosis') 
• 
Person: Who is affected (age, sex, caste, SES) 
• 
Place: Where disease occurs (geographic clustering, urban-rural differences) 
• 
Time: Secular trends, seasonal patterns, epidemic curves 
 
4. Identification of Causative Factors ('Aetiology') 
• 
Biological: Pathogens, vectors, nutritional deficiencies 
• 
Behavioural: Open defecation, unsafe water use, tobacco use 
• 
Social: Poverty, illiteracy, caste discrimination, gender inequality 
• 
Environmental: Sanitation, air quality, occupational exposures 
 
5. Resource Assessment ('Management Capacity') 
• 
Health facilities: PHC, CHC, DH — availability and utilization rates 
• 
Human resources: Doctors, nurses, ANMs, ASHAs per population 
• 
Community resources: NGOs, self-help groups, Panchayati Raj institutions 
• 
Funding: Government schemes, insurance coverage (PMJAY, state schemes) 
 
SOURCES OF DATA FOR COMMUNITY DIAGNOSIS 
Source 
Data Obtained 
Census of India (every 10 years) 
Population demographics, literacy, housing 
NFHS (every 4-5 years) 
Reproductive health, nutrition, immunization 
SRS (Annual) 
IMR, MMR, CDR, TFR 
HMIS/DHIS2 (Monthly) 
Health service utilization data 
Community surveys 
Primary data on local health problems 
Disease notification registers 
Communicable disease incidence (TB, malaria, 
HIV) 
 
COMMUNITY TREATMENT — COLLECTIVE INTERVENTIONS 
Based on community diagnosis, community-level treatments ('prescriptions') include: 
26. Health promotion: IEC/BCC activities for behaviour change (e.g., hand washing campaigns) 
27. Specific protection: Mass immunization campaigns (IPPI, Mission Indradhanush) 
28. Environmental interventions: Water supply improvement, sanitation (SBM), vector control 
29. Nutrition programmes: ICDS, PM POSHAN, anaemia management programmes 
30. Healthcare service strengthening: Deploying additional health workers, improving supply chains 
 
EXAMPLE: Community Diagnosis of a Village in Rural Andhra Pradesh 
• 
Community: Village of 5,000 population, 1 PHC catering to 25,000 people 
• 
Health data: IMR = 55/1000; stunting = 45%; Open defecation = 60%; Malaria cases = 200/year 
• 
Community diagnosis: High IMR due to malnutrition + diarrhoeal diseases + poor access to SBA 
232


--- PAGE 245 ---

swaDesh
• 
Treatment: 1. VHND activation; 2. SBM toilet construction drive; 3. POSHAN nutrition camps; 4. 
Malaria eradication programme; 5. Mobile medical unit deployment 
 
MNEMONIC 
"DHEART" — Steps of Community Diagnosis 
D = Demographic/Community Profile | H = Health Status Assessment (IMR,

TOPIC 36: QUALITATIVE RESEARCH / FOCUS GROUP 
DISCUSSION (FGD) 
(Frequency: 3 Times Asked | Years: 2022, 2020, 2014) 
 
Q1. Describe the Importance of Focus Group Discussion in Qualitative Research 
(2022) ★★★ 
 
QUALITATIVE RESEARCH — OVERVIEW 
Qualitative research is a systematic, interpretive approach to understand social phenomena — exploring 
why and how people think, feel, and behave — rather than measuring the frequency of occurrence 
(quantitative). It generates rich, in-depth, contextual knowledge. 
 
WHAT IS FOCUS GROUP DISCUSSION (FGD)? 
A Focus Group Discussion is a qualitative data collection method involving a moderated group discussion 
among 6–12 purposively selected participants sharing common characteristics, facilitated by a trained 
moderator using a semi-structured discussion guide, to explore perceptions, beliefs, attitudes, and 
experiences on a specific health topic. 
The method was developed by Robert Merton (1950s) and popularized in public health research by 
Richard Krueger. The 'focus' refers to narrowing the discussion to a specific topic. 
 
CHARACTERISTICS OF AN FGD 
Parameter 
Specification 
Group size 
6–12 participants (optimum 8) 
Number of groups 
Minimum 3–5 FGDs; until theoretical saturation 
Duration 
60–90 minutes per session 
Selection 
Purposive sampling — homogeneous within 
group 
Moderator 
Trained facilitator (not dominant; neutral) 
Assistant 
Note-taker + audio/video recorder 
Setting 
Comfortable, neutral, confidential location 
 
IMPORTANCE / ADVANTAGES OF FGD IN QUALITATIVE RESEARCH 
31. Group synergy: Interaction among participants generates ideas and perspectives not emerging 
in one-to-one interviews — the sum is greater than the parts 
32. Rapid data collection: Multiple perspectives gathered simultaneously in 60–90 minutes 
33. Cultural relevance: Uncovers indigenous health beliefs, stigma, taboos — crucial for culturally 
appropriate interventions 
34. Hypothesis generation: Identifies hypotheses for subsequent quantitative studies (mixed 
methods research) 
234


--- PAGE 247 ---

swaDesh
35. Programme development: Used to develop IEC materials, design interventions, and assess 
acceptability of health programmes 
36. Participant empowerment: Gives voice to marginalized communities — women, tribal 
populations, PLHIV 
37. Validation: Can validate quantitative survey findings ('Why do people not use condoms?') 
38. Cost-effective: Less expensive than individual in-depth interviews with same number of 
participants 
 
PROCESS OF CONDUCTING AN FGD 
39. Preparation: Define research question; prepare discussion guide (5–7 open-ended questions); 
select moderator 
40. Sampling: Purposive sampling — homogeneous groups sharing common characteristics (e.g., 
all ANMs, all mothers of SAM children) 
41. Informed consent: Individual written consent; assure confidentiality; permission for recording 
42. Conduct: Introduction → warm-up question → main questions → probing → summary and 
closure 
43. Recording: Verbatim transcription; field notes of non-verbal cues and group dynamics 
44. Analysis: Thematic analysis; member checking; triangulation with other methods 
 
TYPES OF FGD QUESTIONS (Krueger's Sequence) 
Question Type 
Purpose 
Opening question 
Introductions; put participants at ease (non-
threatening) 
Introductory question 
Introduce the broad topic 
Transition question 
Move from general to specific topic 
Key questions 
Core research questions (3-5); most important; 
most time spent 
Ending question 
Summary; final thoughts; ensure all views 
heard 
 
LIMITATIONS OF FGD 
• 
Dominant participants: One person may influence others (groupthink) 
• 
Social desirability bias: People may not disclose sensitive views in a group 
• 
Moderator bias: Inadequately trained moderator may lead the group 
• 
Not generalizable: Small, purposive sample; findings not statistically representative 
• 
Recording difficulties: In noisy settings; participant reluctance to be recorded 
 
APPLICATIONS OF FGD IN PUBLIC HEALTH 
Application Area 
Example 
Needs assessment 
FGD with women to understand barriers to 
institutional delivery 
235


--- PAGE 248 ---

swaDesh
Programme evaluation 
FGD with ASHAs on challenges in JSSK 
implementation 
IEC material development 
FGD to assess comprehensibility of health 
posters 
Health belief exploration 
FGD with tribal communities on malaria 
treatment-seeking 
Policy input 
FGD with community leaders for input into 
NHM planning 
 
MNEMONIC 
"FGD GROUPS" — Key Features and Importance 
F = Facilitator-led (trained moderator, neutral) | G = Group size 6-12 
(optimum 8) | D = Discussion guide (5-7 semi-structured open questions) | 
G = Group synergy generates richer data | R = Rapid data collection (60-90 
min) | O = Open-ended questions → explore WHY | U = Uncover beliefs,

(popularized in public health)

TOPIC 37: TRIPLE BURDEN OF DISEASE / ONION PEEL 
PHENOMENON 
(Frequency: 1 Time — NEW | Year: Apr/May 2026) ★★★★ PREDICTED HIGH PRIORITY 
 
Q1. Discuss Triple Burden of Disease and Onion Peel Phenomenon (Apr/May 
2026) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
PART A: TRIPLE BURDEN OF DISEASE 
DEFINITION 
The Triple Burden of Disease refers to the simultaneous existence of three major categories of health 
challenges that Low- and Middle-Income Countries (LMICs) like India face concurrently: 
45. Communicable diseases (traditional infectious diseases) 
46. Non-Communicable Diseases (NCDs / chronic diseases) 
47. Injuries (unintentional and intentional) 
India, like most LMICs, is undergoing an epidemiological transition but has NOT completed it — resulting 
in a 'triple burden' or 'double burden overlaid with injuries'. 
 
BURDEN 1: COMMUNICABLE DISEASES 
• 
India has the world's highest TB burden: 2.8 million new cases/year (Global TB Report 2023) 
• 
Malaria: 1.6 million cases (2022); 83% of South-East Asian burden 
• 
Diarrhoeal diseases and respiratory infections: Leading causes of under-5 mortality 
• 
Dengue, chikungunya: Re-emerging vector-borne diseases 
• 
HIV/AIDS: 2.4 million PLHIV (NACO 2021); concentrated epidemic 
• 
Hepatitis B & C: 40 million hepatitis B carriers; major cause of cirrhosis and hepatocellular 
carcinoma 
 
BURDEN 2: NON-COMMUNICABLE DISEASES 
• 
Cardiovascular diseases: Responsible for 27% of all deaths in India (GBD 2019) 
• 
Type 2 DM: 101 million diabetics (IDF Atlas 2021) — highest in world 
• 
Cancer: 14.6 lakh new cases/year; leading: cervical, breast, oral 
• 
COPD and asthma: 55 million COPD cases; second highest global burden 
• 
Mental health: 197 million affected (NMHS 2015-16); depression, anxiety, schizophrenia 
• 
NCDs cause 65% of total deaths in India — premature NCD mortality at age 30–70 years: 26% 
 
BURDEN 3: INJURIES 
• 
Road traffic injuries: India = 11% of global road accident deaths; 150,000+ deaths/year 
• 
Drowning, burns, falls: Major causes of disability-adjusted life years (DALYs) lost 
• 
Occupational injuries: Construction, mining, agriculture — unregulated informal sector 
• 
Violence and self-harm: Suicide rate 12/100,000 (WHO 2019); suicide is leading cause of death 
in 15–29 age group 
237


--- PAGE 250 ---

swaDesh
 
CONSEQUENCES OF TRIPLE BURDEN FOR INDIA'S HEALTH SYSTEM 
Challenge 
Impact 
Health system overload 
Hospitals dealing with TB, diabetic foot, and 
trauma simultaneously 
Vertical programme conflicts 
Separate programmes for NCD and CD 
compete for same staff and resources 
Epidemiological transition in progress 
Urban-rural differential: Cities face NCD 
burden; rural areas face CD burden 
Financing gap 
Out-of-pocket expenditure remains high (47.1% 
of total health expenditure, NHE 2020-21) 
Workforce shortage 
Doctor-population ratio 1:834 (India, 2021) — 
far below WHO norm of 1:1000 
 
PROGRAMMES ADDRESSING TRIPLE BURDEN 
• 
NHM: Umbrella programme addressing all three burdens — RMNCH+A, NPCDCS, NCD clinics, 
trauma care 
• 
Ayushman Bharat — PMJAY: Secondary/tertiary care for NCDs and injuries 
• 
NPCDCS: National Programme for Prevention and Control of Cancer, DM, CVD, Stroke 
• 
National Road Safety Policy and Motor Vehicles Act 2019 amendments: Injury prevention 
 
PART B: ONION PEEL PHENOMENON 
DEFINITION AND CONCEPT 
The Onion Peel Phenomenon is a metaphor used in epidemiology and public health to describe the multi-
layered causation of disease. Just as peeling an onion reveals successive layers, investigating disease 
causation reveals successive, increasingly deeper layers of determinants — from immediate biological 
causes to distant social and structural determinants. 
 
LAYERS OF THE ONION PEEL MODEL 
Layer (Peel) 
Type of Determinant 
Example (for TB) 
Layer 1 (Outermost) Biological/Proximate cause 
Mycobacterium tuberculosis 
infection 
Layer 2 
Individual risk factors 
Malnutrition, HIV co-infection, 
diabetes 
Layer 3 
Behavioural risk factors 
Crowded housing, smoking, poor 
ventilation 
Layer 4 
Social determinants 
Poverty, illiteracy, stigma, lack of 
healthcare access 
Layer 5 
Structural determinants 
Caste system, gender 
discrimination, urban overcrowding 
Layer 6 (Core/Root) 
Political/Economic determinants 
Poverty policy, healthcare 
financing, governance

--- PAGE 251 ---

swaDesh
 
SIGNIFICANCE OF ONION PEEL MODEL IN PUBLIC HEALTH 
48. Multi-level causation: Reminds us that disease is not merely biological but socially determined 
49. Comprehensive intervention: Peeling to the core reveals that addressing only the outermost 
layer (treating the pathogen) is insufficient for sustained disease control 
50. Social Determinants of Health framework: Consistent with WHO's CSDH model (Dahlgren-
Whitehead, 2008) 
51. Policy implications: Inner layers (structural, political) require intersectoral action, not just health 
sector response 
52. Epidemiological teaching: Useful pedagogical tool to illustrate web of causation, eco-
epidemiology 
 
COMPARISON WITH WEB OF CAUSATION 
Onion Peel Model 
Web of Causation (MacMahon) 
Hierarchical layers from proximate to distal 
Complex network of interrelated factors 
Emphasizes structural and social causes 
Emphasizes multiple interacting biological and 
social factors 
Linear (deeper = more fundamental) 
Non-linear, interactive 
Good for policy communication 
Good for analytical epidemiology

COPD) | India = C still significant + N rising + I increasing = TRIPLE burden 
| ONION PEEL LAYERS: B = Biological (pathogen) | I = Individual risk

TOPIC 38: BEHAVIOURAL PROBLEMS — Types and Measures for 
Improvement 
(Frequency: 1 Time — NEW | Year: Apr/May 2026) ★★★★ PREDICTED HIGH PRIORITY 
 
Q1. Discuss Different Types of Behavioural Problems and Measures for their 
Improvement (Apr/May 2026) ★★★★ *NEW* 
 
DEFINITION 
Behavioural problems (also called behavioural or mental health disorders) are patterns of behaviour that 
deviate significantly from cultural or developmental norms, cause distress or functional impairment to the 
individual or others, and are persistent rather than transient. In public health, 'behavioural problems' also 
encompasses health-damaging behaviours at the population level. 
 
CLASSIFICATION OF BEHAVIOURAL PROBLEMS 
A. CHILDHOOD BEHAVIOURAL PROBLEMS 
1. Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder (ADHD) 
• 
Characteristics: Inattention, hyperactivity, impulsivity; onset before age 12 
• 
Prevalence: 5–8% of school-age children globally; boys > girls (3:1) 
• 
Types: Predominantly inattentive, predominantly hyperactive-impulsive, combined 
• 
Impact: Academic failure, social difficulties, school dropout 
• 
Management: Behavioural therapy (CBT), parent training, methylphenidate (pharmacotherapy), 
school accommodations 
 
2. Conduct Disorder (CD) 
• 
Persistent pattern of aggression, rule violation, antisocial behaviour 
• 
ICD-11 criteria: >6 months; violates rights of others or social norms 
• 
Precursor to adult antisocial personality disorder if untreated 
• 
Management: Multi-systemic therapy (MST), family therapy, social skills training 
 
3. Oppositional Defiant Disorder (ODD) 
• 
Pattern of angry/irritable mood, argumentative/defiant behaviour toward authority figures 
• 
Distinguished from conduct disorder by absence of serious rule violations or aggression to 
others 
 
4. Autism Spectrum Disorder (ASD) 
• 
Neurodevelopmental disorder: Impaired social communication + restricted/repetitive behaviours 
• 
Prevalence: 1 in 44 children (CDC 2021); rising trend worldwide 
• 
Management: Applied Behaviour Analysis (ABA), speech therapy, occupational therapy; early 
intervention is critical 
240


--- PAGE 253 ---

swaDesh
 
5. Enuresis, Encopresis, Pica — Elimination and Feeding Disorders 
• 
Enuresis: Involuntary urination after expected continence age (>5 years) 
• 
Pica: Eating non-nutritive substances (clay, paper, paint) — associated with iron deficiency, 
intellectual disability 
 
B. ADOLESCENT BEHAVIOURAL PROBLEMS 
1. Substance Use Disorders 
• 
Tobacco: 13% of adolescents in India use tobacco (GATS 2016-17) 
• 
Alcohol: Early initiation associated with dependence and liver disease 
• 
Cannabis/inhalants: Most prevalent illicit substances among Indian adolescents 
• 
Management: Motivational Interviewing (MI), de-addiction programmes, NIMHANS community 
outreach 
 
2. Eating Disorders 
• 
Anorexia nervosa: Intense fear of weight gain; self-starvation; BMI <17.5 kg/m² 
• 
Bulimia nervosa: Recurrent binge eating + purging; normal weight 
• 
Rising prevalence in urban Indian adolescents; associated with social media body image 
pressures 
 
3. Deliberate Self-Harm (DSH) and Suicidal Behaviour 
• 
India: Suicide is leading cause of death in 15–29 age group (NCRB 2022) 
• 
Risk factors: Academic pressure, relationship problems, mental illness, social media 
cyberbullying 
• 
Management: Zero Suicide Framework, VENDETTA model (Vigilance-Empathy-Not leaving 
alone-Discuss-Engage-Talk-Treatment-Action) 
 
C. ADULT BEHAVIOURAL PROBLEMS (Public Health Perspective) 
1. Tobacco Use 
• 
28.6% of adults use tobacco in India (GATS 2016-17) 
• 
Causes: 30% of cancers; COPD; CVD; maternal complications 
• 
Measures: COTPA 2003; mCessation; varenicline; NRT; 5A's counselling 
 
2. Harmful Alcohol Use 
• 
14.6% of Indians are drinkers; 5% have alcohol use disorder (NIMHANS 2015-16) 
• 
Consequences: Liver cirrhosis, road accidents, domestic violence, mental illness, suicides 
• 
Management: Brief Interventions (AUDIT tool), community de-addiction centres, ASHA-based 
counselling 
 
3. Sedentary Behaviour and Physical Inactivity 
• 
34% of Indians are physically inactive (WHO 2018) 
241


--- PAGE 254 ---

swaDesh
• 
Associated with: Obesity, T2DM, CVD, depression 
• 
Measures: Fit India Movement, Khelo India, workplace wellness 
 
MEASURES FOR IMPROVEMENT OF BEHAVIOURAL PROBLEMS 
1. Individual-Level Interventions 
Intervention 
Application 
Cognitive Behavioural Therapy (CBT) 
ADHD, depression, eating disorders, substance 
use, phobias

activities

2. Family-Level Interventions 
• 
Parent Management Training (PMT): Evidence-based for ADHD, conduct disorder, ODD 
• 
Family therapy: Eating disorders, substance use in adolescents 
• 
Positive parenting: Prevent childhood behavioural problems through secure attachment, 
warmth, consistent boundaries 
 
3. School-Level Interventions 
• 
Life skills education (WHO Life Skills): Decision-making, coping, communication, critical thinking 
• 
School mental health programmes: Early identification by teachers; school counsellors 
• 
Anti-bullying programmes: Zero-tolerance policies; peer support systems 
• 
Rashtriya Kishor Swasthya Karyakram (RKSK): Adolescent health and mental wellness in India 
 
4. Community-Level Interventions 
• 
Community mental health centres: Reorientation of services toward community care (District 
Mental Health Programme — DMHP) 
• 
ASHA mental health training: MANAS programme — community outreach for CMDs 
• 
De-stigmatization campaigns: 'It's Okay to Talk' campaigns; media portrayal improvement 
• 
Peer support groups: Alcoholics Anonymous, Narcotics Anonymous, suicide survivor groups 
 
5. Policy-Level Interventions 
• 
Mental Health Care Act 2017 (India): Right to mental healthcare; advance directives; 
decriminalization of suicide attempt 
• 
National Mental Health Policy 2014: Strengthen community care; integrate mental health into 
primary care 
• 
National Programme for Mental Health (NPHM) / DMHP: District-level mental health teams 
242

swaDesh
• 
COTPA 2003 and Alcohol policies: Structural interventions for substance use behavioural 
problems 
• 
National Suicide Prevention Strategy 2022: India's first dedicated suicide prevention framework

39. CHANGING CONCEPTS IN PUBLIC HEALTH 
Q1. Describe changing concepts in public health. (Apr/May 2026) ★★★★ NEW 
 
INTRODUCTION 
Public health is a dynamic discipline that has evolved dramatically over centuries. It encompasses the 
science and art of preventing disease, prolonging life, and promoting health through organized efforts 
of society. The concept of public health has transformed from a narrow focus on sanitation and infection 
control to a broad, holistic approach addressing social determinants, equity, and global 
interconnectedness. 
1. HISTORICAL EVOLUTION OF PUBLIC HEALTH CONCEPTS 
Phase 1: Miasma Theory Era (Pre-1850s) 
The earliest concept of public health was based on the miasma theory, which attributed disease to 'bad 
air' arising from rotting organic matter and filth. 
• 
Hippocrates (460–377 BC): First linked environment to disease in 'On Airs, Waters and 
Places' 
• 
Focus was on quarantine, isolation of lepers, and sewage disposal 
• 
Limitations: No understanding of microbial causation 
Phase 2: Germ Theory Era (1850s–1900s) 
The discovery of microorganisms revolutionized public health thinking. 
• 
Louis Pasteur (1860s): Established germ theory of disease 
• 
Robert Koch (1880s): Koch's postulates — identified specific causative organisms 
• 
John Snow (1854): Cholera map — father of modern epidemiology 
• 
Focus shifted to: Vaccination, pasteurization, sanitation, and vector control 
• 
Landmark: Smallpox vaccination by Edward Jenner (1796) 
Phase 3: Social Reform Era (1900s–1960s) 
Recognition that poverty, housing, nutrition, and working conditions determine health. 
• 
Beveridge Report (1942, UK): Welfare state concept — 'cradle to grave' protection 
• 
Bhore Committee (1946, India): Recommended comprehensive health services 
• 
WHO Constitution (1948): Health defined as 'complete physical, mental and social well-being' 
• 
Focus: Maternal and child health, nutrition, occupational health 
Phase 4: Biomedical Era (1950s–1970s) 
Emphasis on technological and clinical medicine for disease eradication. 
• 
Development of antibiotics (penicillin — Fleming, 1928) 
• 
Eradication of smallpox (1980 — WHO) 
246


--- PAGE 259 ---

swaDesh
• 
Development of polio vaccine (Salk 1955, Sabin 1962) 
• 
Expanded Programme on Immunization (EPI) launched 1974 
• 
Criticism: Neglected social and environmental determinants 
Phase 5: Lifestyle / Behavioral Era (1970s–1990s) 
Rising burden of non-communicable diseases (NCDs) led to focus on lifestyle modification. 
• 
Lalonde Report (1974, Canada): Four determinants — human biology, environment, lifestyle, 
healthcare organization 
• 
Emphasis on tobacco control, physical activity, dietary changes 
• 
Health promotion emerged as a distinct field 
Phase 6: Primary Health Care Era (1978–onwards) 
International recognition that equitable access to basic healthcare is a fundamental right. 
• 
Alma Ata Declaration (1978): 'Health for All by 2000' — PHC as the key strategy 
• 
8 essential elements of PHC: MEDICINE (Maternal & child care, Education, Drugs essential, 
Immunization, Control of endemic diseases, Intersectoral collaboration, Nutrition, Endemic 
disease treatment) 
• 
Ottawa Charter (1986): Health promotion — 5 action areas 
Phase 7: Health Promotion and New Public Health Era (1986–2000s) 
Shift from disease prevention to active promotion of health at population level. 
• 
Ottawa Charter (1986) — 5 action areas: Build healthy public policy, Create supportive 
environments, Strengthen community action, Develop personal skills, Reorient health services 
• 
Concept of 'Healthy Cities' — WHO initiative (1987) 
• 
Jakarta Declaration (1997): Health promotion into 21st century 
Phase 8: Social Determinants Era (2000s–present) 
Recognition that non-medical factors (income, education, employment, housing) profoundly determine 
health outcomes. 
• 
Commission on Social Determinants of Health (WHO, 2005–2008) — chaired by Michael 
Marmot 
• 
Marmot Review (2010): 'Fair Society, Healthy Lives' 
• 
Conclusion: Social gradient of health — lower socioeconomic position = worse health 
outcomes 
• 
Emphasis on health equity, intersectionality, and upstream determinants 
Phase 9: Global Health and One Health Era (2010s–present) 
Globalization has made health a global concern transcending national boundaries. 
• 
Millennium Development Goals (MDGs) 2000–2015: 8 goals focused on poverty, health, 
education 
• 
Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) 2015–2030: 17 goals — SDG 3: 'Ensure healthy 
lives and promote well-being for all at all ages' 
• 
One Health concept: Human health, animal health, and environmental health are 
interconnected (COVID-19, Nipah, Ebola) 
247


--- PAGE 260 ---

swaDesh
• 
Universal Health Coverage (UHC): Everyone should receive essential health services without 
financial hardship 
2. KEY CONCEPTUAL SHIFTS IN PUBLIC HEALTH 
• 
From disease-based to health-based thinking 
• 
From individual to population/community focus 
• 
From biomedical to biopsychosocial model (Engel, 1977) 
• 
From cure to prevention and promotion 
• 
From infectious to non-communicable disease focus 
• 
From national to global health perspective 
• 
From provider-driven to people-centered care 
• 
From equity as aspiration to equity as a right 
3. MODERN PUBLIC HEALTH — KEY THEMES 
a) Epidemiological Transition (Omran, 1971) 
Describes shift in disease patterns from infectious to chronic non-communicable diseases as countries 
develop: 
• 
Stage 1: Age of Pestilence and Famine (high mortality, infections) 
• 
Stage 2: Age of Receding Pandemics 
• 
Stage 3: Age of Degenerative and Man-made Diseases (NCDs dominate) 
• 
Stage 4 (new): Age of Delayed Degenerative Diseases (India in triple burden — infections + 
NCDs + injuries) 
b) Demographic Transition 
India is undergoing demographic transition with aging population, creating new public health 
challenges. 
c) Digital Health / eHealth 
Use of ICT in health: telemedicine, electronic health records, mHealth, AI in diagnostics. 
d) Planetary Health 
Recognition that human health is dependent on the health of the Earth's natural systems — climate 
change, pollution, biodiversity loss directly affect human health. 
4. CONCLUSION 
The concept of public health has traversed a remarkable journey — from controlling miasmas to 
addressing social injustice, from managing epidemics to preventing NCDs, and from national programs 
to global health initiatives. The modern paradigm integrates biological, behavioral, social, 
environmental, and political dimensions into a comprehensive framework. The vision of 'Health for All' 
— health as a fundamental human right — continues to drive public health practice into the 21st century. 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "GEMS BPS ONE" 
248


--- PAGE 261 ---

swaDesh
 
 
40. GOOD CLINICAL PRACTICES (GCP) / INFORMED 
CONSENT 
Q1. Describe good clinical practices and its guidelines. Add a note on informed consent. 
(Apr/May 2026) ★★★★ NEW 
 
INTRODUCTION 
Good Clinical Practice (GCP) is an international ethical and scientific quality standard for designing, 
conducting, recording, and reporting trials involving human subjects. Compliance with GCP provides 
public assurance that the rights, safety, and well-being of trial subjects are protected, and that the 
clinical trial data are credible. The foundational document is the ICH E6(R2) GCP Guideline. 
1. HISTORICAL BACKGROUND 
• 
Nuremberg Code (1947): First international standards for human experimentation — voluntary 
consent is absolutely essential 
• 
Declaration of Helsinki (1964, WMA): Ethical principles for medical research — revised 
multiple times, latest 2013 
• 
Belmont Report (1979, USA): Three fundamental principles — Respect for persons, 
Beneficence, Justice 
• 
ICH-GCP E6 (1996): International harmonized GCP guideline — adopted by ICH 
(International Council for Harmonisation) 
• 
Schedule Y (India): Regulatory requirements for clinical trials under CDSCO — now replaced 
by New Drugs and Clinical Trials Rules, 2019 
2. DEFINITION AND PURPOSE OF GCP 
GCP is an international ethical and scientific quality standard that ensures: 
• 
Protection of rights, safety, and welfare of trial participants 
• 
Reliability and accuracy of clinical trial data 
• 
Compliance with applicable regulatory requirements 
• 
Credibility of data for regulatory submissions 
3. PRINCIPLES OF ICH-GCP (13 Principles) 
The ICH E6(R2) guideline outlines 13 core principles: 
• 
1. Trials conducted in accordance with ethical principles (Declaration of Helsinki) 
• 
2. Benefits and risks assessed — risk/benefit favorable before trial initiation 
• 
3. Rights, safety, and well-being of subjects take priority over science and society 
• 
4. Non-clinical and clinical information on investigational product should be adequate 
• 
5. Trials scientifically sound with clear, detailed protocol 
249


--- PAGE 262 ---

swaDesh
• 
6. Protocol approved by IRB/IEC (Institutional Review Board / Independent Ethics Committee) 
before commencement 
• 
7. Medical care and decisions for subjects made by qualified physician 
• 
8. Each person involved in conducting trial should be qualified by education, training, and 
experience 
• 
9. Freely given informed consent obtained from every subject prior to participation 
• 
10. All clinical trial information recorded, handled, stored to allow accurate reporting, 
interpretation, and verification 
• 
11. Confidentiality of records identifying subjects maintained 
• 
12. Investigational products manufactured, handled, stored in accordance with applicable 
Good Manufacturing Practice (GMP) 
• 
13. Systems with procedures implemented to ensure quality of every aspect of the trial 
(Quality Management System) 
4. KEY STAKEHOLDERS IN GCP 
a) Sponsor 
• 
Initiates, manages, and finances the clinical trial 
• 
Responsible for protocol design, investigator selection, trial monitoring, data management 
• 
May delegate functions to Contract Research Organization (CRO) 
b) Investigator 
• 
Responsible for conduct of clinical trial at a trial site 
• 
Must be qualified physician; responsible for medical decisions 
• 
Principal Investigator (PI) heads the team 
c) Institutional Review Board / Independent Ethics Committee (IRB/IEC) 
• 
Independent body constituted to ensure rights, safety, and well-being of subjects 
• 
Reviews and approves the protocol, informed consent form, and other trial documents 
• 
Provides ongoing oversight throughout the trial 
d) Regulatory Authorities 
• 
In India: CDSCO (Central Drugs Standard Control Organisation) under DCGI 
• 
In USA: FDA; EU: EMA; Japan: PMDA 
5. GCP IN CLINICAL TRIAL PROCESS 
Phases of Clinical Trial 
• 
Phase 0 (Microdosing): Exploratory, sub-therapeutic dose, pharmacokinetics 
• 
Phase I: First-in-human, safety, pharmacokinetics, dose-finding (20–80 subjects) 
• 
Phase II: Efficacy and safety, dose-response (100–300 subjects) 
• 
Phase III: Comparative efficacy with standard treatment, large RCT (1000–3000 subjects) 
• 
Phase IV: Post-marketing surveillance, long-term safety, rare adverse effects (thousands) 
6. DOCUMENTATION IN GCP 
250


--- PAGE 263 ---

swaDesh
• 
Case Report Form (CRF): Documents data on each trial subject 
• 
Investigator's Brochure (IB): Compilation of clinical and non-clinical data on investigational 
product 
• 
Trial Master File (TMF): Complete documentation of the trial 
• 
Source Documents: Original documents and records (medical records, lab reports) 
• 
Essential Documents: Minimum documents for conduct evaluation and data quality 
7. MONITORING IN GCP 
• 
On-site monitoring: Regular site visits by Clinical Research Associate (CRA) 
• 
Centralized monitoring: Remote data review 
• 
Purpose: Verify protocol compliance, data integrity, subject safety 
• 
Source Document Verification (SDV): Cross-checking CRF data with source documents 
8. AUDIT AND INSPECTION 
• 
Audit: Systematic, independent examination by sponsor to evaluate trial conduct 
• 
Inspection: Official review by regulatory authority 
• 
Both ensure GCP compliance and data integrity 
NOTE ON INFORMED CONSENT 
Informed consent is the cornerstone of ethical clinical research and medical practice. It is a process — 
not merely a signature — by which a person voluntarily confirms willingness to participate in a trial after 
being informed about all aspects relevant to their decision. 
Legal Basis 
• 
Nuremberg Code (1947): Voluntary consent is absolutely essential 
• 
Declaration of Helsinki (2013): Informed consent is mandatory 
• 
ICH-GCP Principle 9: Freely given informed consent before participation 
• 
In India: New Drugs and Clinical Trials Rules, 2019 — mandatory audio-visual documentation 
of informed consent process 
Elements of Valid Informed Consent (ELEMENTS) 
• 
Disclosure: Full information about the trial 
• 
Comprehension: Subject must understand the information 
• 
Voluntariness: Free from coercion, manipulation, undue influence 
• 
Competence: Subject must have decision-making capacity 
• 
Consent: Explicit agreement — written, signed, and dated 
Essential Information to be Disclosed 
• 
Nature, purpose, and duration of the trial 
• 
Experimental nature of the treatment 
• 
Foreseeable risks and discomforts 
• 
Expected benefits (if any) 
• 
Alternative treatments available 
251


--- PAGE 264 ---

swaDesh
• 
Confidentiality provisions 
• 
Compensation/treatment for trial-related injury 
• 
Right to withdraw at any time without penalty 
• 
Contact person for queries and emergencies 
Special Situations in Informed Consent 
• 
Minors: Assent from child + consent from legally acceptable representative (LAR) 
• 
Mentally incapacitated: Consent from LAR 
• 
Emergency research: Waiver of consent may be permitted 
• 
Illiterate participants: Impartial witness must be present; thumb impression + audio-visual 
documentation 
• 
In India (NDCT Rules 2019): Mandatory audio-visual recording of consent process for all trials 
Re-consent 
New information arising during trial that may affect willingness to continue must be communicated, and 
re-consent obtained if significant. 
CONCLUSION 
Good Clinical Practice provides the ethical and scientific framework ensuring that clinical trials are 
conducted with integrity, protecting human subjects while generating reliable data. Informed consent, 
as the bedrock of GCP, ensures that participation in research is truly voluntary, informed, and dignified. 
Adherence to GCP principles is mandatory for regulatory approval and reflects the highest standards 
of research ethics. 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "WISE PRIDE" for GCP Principles 
W – Well-being of subjects takes priority I – IRB/IEC approval mandatory S – Scientific 
soundness of protocol E – Ethical principles (Helsinki) P – Physician responsible for 
medical care R – Records maintained accurately I – Investigational product per GMP D – 
Data confidentiality ensured E – Education/qualification of personnel 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "DECVC" for Informed Consent 
D – Disclosure of information E – Elements communicated clearly C – Comprehension 
ensured V – Voluntariness (no coercion) C – Competence of subject to decide 
252


--- PAGE 265 ---

swaDesh
 
 
41. EMPORIATRICS / TRAVEL MEDICINE 
Q1. Define Emporiatrics. Describe burden and determinants of health risk during travel. Mention 
preventive measures. (Apr/May 2026) ★★★★ NEW 
 
DEFINITION 
Emporiatrics (Travel Medicine) is the branch of medicine concerned with the prevention and 
management of health problems of international travelers. The term is derived from the Greek word 
'emporos' meaning traveler or merchant. It is a multidisciplinary specialty incorporating aspects of 
infectious disease, tropical medicine, preventive medicine, public health, and occupational medicine. 
The International Society of Travel Medicine (ISTM) defines it as: 'The science and practice of 
prevention and management of health problems of international travelers.' 
GLOBAL BURDEN OF TRAVEL-RELATED ILLNESS 
• 
Approximately 1.4 billion international tourist arrivals annually (pre-COVID, 2019) 
• 
20–70% of short-term travelers to developing countries report travel-related illness 
• 
1–5% require medical attention during or after travel 
• 
0.01–0.1% require emergency medical evacuation 
• 
Leading causes of death in travelers: Cardiovascular events (50–70%), injuries/accidents 
(25%), infections (<1%) 
• 
Traveler's diarrhea: Most common travel-related illness (20–50% of all travelers) 
• 
Malaria: Most common preventable death in travelers 
• 
Respiratory infections: Second most common complaint 
DETERMINANTS OF HEALTH RISK DURING TRAVEL 
A. Traveler-Related Factors 
• 
1. Destination: 
– 
High-risk destinations: Sub-Saharan Africa, South Asia, Southeast Asia 
– 
Risk varies by country, region, rural vs. urban, season 
– 
Endemic diseases: Malaria, dengue, typhoid, yellow fever, cholera 
• 
2. Purpose of travel: 
– 
Tourists vs. backpackers vs. business travelers vs. visiting friends and relatives (VFR) 
– 
VFR travelers at highest risk — often forgo pre-travel advice, stay in local households 
– 
Adventure travelers, missionaries, healthcare workers at special risk 
• 
3. Duration of travel: 
– 
Longer duration = greater cumulative exposure and risk 
– 
Short trips (<2 weeks) vs. long-term (months): Different risk profiles 
• 
4. Season of travel: 
253


--- PAGE 266 ---

swaDesh
– 
Monsoon season increases risk of vector-borne diseases (malaria, dengue) 
– 
Winter increases risk of respiratory infections 
– 
Hajj/Kumbh Mela: Mass gatherings — unique disease risks (meningococcal disease, 
MERS) 
• 
5. Accommodation and living standards: 
– 
Budget travelers staying in local housing at higher risk 
– 
Air-conditioned hotels with purified water — lower risk 
• 
6. Food and water practices: 
– 
Consumption of raw/undercooked food, street food, tap water — major risk for diarrheal 
diseases 
• 
7. Sexual behavior during travel: 
– 
Casual or commercial sex increases risk of HIV, STIs, Hepatitis B 
– 
Tourism linked to sex tourism in certain destinations 
B. Host-Related Factors 
• 
Age: Elderly and young children are more vulnerable 
• 
Immune status: Immunocompromised (HIV, transplant, chemotherapy) at very high risk 
• 
Pregnancy: Malaria especially dangerous; live vaccines contraindicated 
• 
Underlying conditions: Cardiovascular disease, diabetes, pulmonary disease increase risk 
• 
Previous travel and immunity: Prior exposure may confer partial immunity (VFR travelers) 
• 
Genetic factors: G6PD deficiency affects choice of malaria prophylaxis 
C. Agent / Exposure-Related Factors 
• 
Vector-borne: Malaria (Anopheles), Dengue/Zika/Chikungunya (Aedes), Yellow Fever 
• 
Food/waterborne: Typhoid, cholera, Hepatitis A, traveler's diarrhea (ETEC) 
• 
Airborne: Tuberculosis (crowded transport), influenza, meningococcal disease 
• 
Contact/sexual: HIV, STIs, Hepatitis B and C 
• 
Environmental: Altitude sickness, heat stroke, sunburn, jellyfish, snakebite 
• 
Injury/accidents: Road traffic accidents (RTAs) — leading cause of morbidity/mortality 
D. Altitude-Related Factors 
• 
High altitude (>2500m): Acute Mountain Sickness (AMS), High Altitude Cerebral Edema 
(HACE), High Altitude Pulmonary Edema (HAPE) 
• 
Risk increases with rate of ascent 
• 
Ladakh, Himalayas, Andes frequently implicated 
E. Jet Lag and Circadian Disruption 
• 
Rapid crossing of time zones disrupts circadian rhythm 
• 
Symptoms: Fatigue, insomnia, cognitive impairment, gastrointestinal upset 
• 
Worsened by eastward travel 
F. Deep Vein Thrombosis (DVT) / Economy Class Syndrome 
• 
Prolonged immobility during long-haul flights (>4 hours) 
• 
Risk factors: Prior DVT, obesity, pregnancy, clotting disorders, oral contraceptives 
• 
Pulmonary embolism (PE) is life-threatening complication 
254


--- PAGE 267 ---

swaDesh
COMMON TRAVEL-RELATED ILLNESSES 
• 
Traveler's Diarrhea (TD): Most common; ETEC most common cause; 'Boil it, cook it, peel it or 
forget it' 
• 
Malaria: Most common vaccine-preventable death; P. falciparum most dangerous 
• 
Dengue: Aedes mosquito; increasing in tropics/subtropics 
• 
Typhoid Fever: Salmonella typhi; vaccine available 
• 
Hepatitis A: Feco-oral route; vaccine available 
• 
Yellow Fever: Vaccine required for entry to endemic countries 
• 
Meningococcal Disease: Required vaccination for Hajj pilgrims 
• 
COVID-19 / Influenza: Respiratory infections in mass gatherings 
• 
Rabies: Animal bites; pre-exposure prophylaxis for high-risk travelers 
PREVENTIVE MEASURES 
1. Pre-Travel Consultation 
All travelers should receive personalized pre-travel health advice at least 4–6 weeks before departure: 
• 
Risk assessment based on destination, purpose, duration, and host factors 
• 
Vaccination review and administration 
• 
Malaria chemoprophylaxis prescription if needed 
• 
Travel health kit preparation 
• 
Advice on food, water, and personal safety 
2. Vaccinations 
• 
Routine vaccines: Ensure up-to-date (Tetanus, MMR, Influenza, Hepatitis B, COVID-19) 
• 
Recommended vaccines: Hepatitis A, Typhoid (Oral Ty21a or Injectable Vi polysaccharide), 
Meningococcal, Rabies 
• 
Required vaccines: Yellow Fever (mandatory for entry to endemic countries — valid certificate 
from designated center) 
• 
Special: Japanese Encephalitis (rural Asia), Cholera (oral vaccine OCV) 
3. Malaria Prevention — 4 A's of Malaria Prevention 
• 
Awareness of risk in destination 
• 
Anti-mosquito measures (ABCD): Repellents (DEET), Bed nets (ITNs), Clothing (long-
sleeved), Doors and windows screened 
• 
Antimalarial drugs (Chemoprophylaxis): Chloroquine-sensitive areas — Chloroquine; 
Resistant areas — Mefloquine / Atovaquone-proguanil (Malarone) / Doxycycline 
• 
Accurate and prompt diagnosis and treatment if febrile illness develops after travel 
4. Food and Water Precautions 
• 
'Boil it, Cook it, Peel it or Forget it' — cardinal rule for food safety 
• 
Drink only bottled, boiled, or chemically treated water 
• 
Avoid ice cubes, salads, raw shellfish, unpasteurized dairy 
• 
Carry oral rehydration salts (ORS) for management of diarrhea 
• 
Self-treatment: Azithromycin / Ciprofloxacin for traveler's diarrhea if severe 
255


--- PAGE 268 ---

swaDesh
5. Prevention of DVT 
• 
Walk and stretch frequently during long flights 
• 
Stay hydrated; avoid alcohol and sedatives 
• 
Compression stockings for high-risk travelers 
• 
Low-molecular-weight heparin (LMWH) for very high-risk individuals 
6. Altitude Sickness Prevention 
• 
Gradual ascent: 'Climb high, sleep low' principle 
• 
Acetazolamide (Diamox) prophylaxis: 125–250 mg BD starting 1–2 days before ascent 
• 
Immediate descent in case of HACE/HAPE 
• 
Portable hyperbaric chambers (Gamow bag) as temporary measure 
7. Safety and Injury Prevention 
• 
Road safety: Seatbelts, helmets, avoid driving at night in high-risk countries 
• 
Sun protection: SPF 30+ sunscreen, protective clothing, avoid peak sun hours 
• 
Animal bites: Avoid contact with animals; immediate wound washing; seek rabies prophylaxis 
• 
Swimming: Avoid freshwater bodies in schistosomiasis-endemic areas 
8. Post-Travel Screening 
• 
Travelers returning from endemic areas with fever — rule out malaria first 
• 
Incubation periods guide diagnosis: 
– 
Malaria: 7–30 days (P. falciparum) to months (P. vivax) 
– 
Dengue: 3–14 days 
– 
Typhoid: 7–21 days 
– 
Hepatitis A: 15–50 days 
• 
Screen for HIV, STIs, TB in high-risk travelers 
• 
Eosinophilia on return — think helminthic infections (Schistosomiasis, Strongyloides) 
9. Travel Health Kit (Essentials) 
• 
Prescription medications (antimalarials, antibiotics for self-treatment) 
• 
ORS packets, antidiarrheal (Loperamide) 
• 
Analgesics/antipyretics 
• 
Insect repellent (DEET ≥30%) 
• 
Sunscreen (SPF 30+) 
• 
Sterile needles and syringes (for high-risk destinations) 
• 
First aid kit, digital thermometer 
• 
Travel insurance documentation 
SPECIAL GROUPS IN TRAVEL MEDICINE 
• 
Pregnant travelers: Avoid malaria-endemic regions; no live vaccines; chloroquine safe, 
mefloquine relatively safe after 1st trimester 
• 
Immunocompromised: No live vaccines; higher risk of all infections; meticulous food/water 
precautions 
256


--- PAGE 269 ---

swaDesh
• 
Elderly: Higher risk of cardiovascular events; ensure adequate travel insurance 
• 
Children: Malaria prophylaxis weight-based; extra attention to food/water safety 
• 
VFR travelers: Often most neglected; need same advice as first-time travelers despite 
familiarity 
CONCLUSION 
Emporiatrics or Travel Medicine is an increasingly important specialty in our globalized world. With over 
a billion international travelers annually, travel-related illness imposes a significant burden on both 
travelers and destination healthcare systems. A comprehensive pre-travel consultation addressing 
vaccinations, chemoprophylaxis, behavioral precautions, and post-travel screening can dramatically 
reduce morbidity and mortality. The key mantra of travel medicine is: 'Know before you go, protect 
yourself while there, and get evaluated when you return.' 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "WAVED SAFE" for Travel Medicine Preventive 
Measures 
W – Water and food precautions A – Anti-mosquito measures (DEET, nets) V – 
Vaccinations (routine + recommended + required) E – Emergency kit and self-treatment D 
– DVT prevention on long flights S – Safety (road, sun, animal bites) A – Altitude 
precautions (Diamox, gradual ascent) F – Follow-up (post-travel screening) E – Education 
(pre-travel counseling) 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "DEAD" for Malaria Prevention in Travelers 
D – Destination risk awareness E – Exposure prevention (nets, repellents, clothing) A – 
Antimalarial chemoprophylaxis D – Diagnosis promptly if fever develops

GRADE D TOPICS — BRIEF REVISION NOTES 
(For topics: Social Mapping, Conflict Management, Social Marketing, Bhore Committee, and 
others) 
 
37. SOCIAL MAPPING AND TRANSECT WALK (Oct 2025 — NEW) 
Social Mapping 
Social mapping is a participatory rural appraisal (PRA) tool used in community health assessment to 
create a visual representation of the community's social features, resources, and household 
characteristics. 
• 
Purpose: Identify households, schools, water sources, health facilities, roads, vulnerable 
populations 
• 
Method: Community members draw a map of their village/area on the ground or paper 
• 
Key features mapped: Households (differentiating SC/ST, BPL, pregnant women, disabled), 
water sources, latrines, roads, healthcare facilities, schools 
• 
Uses: Identifying gaps in services, planning health interventions, community health needs 
assessment 
• 
Used in: National Rural Health Mission (NRHM), ASHA activities, PLA (Participatory Learning 
and Action) 
Transect Walk 
A transect walk is a systematic walk through a community along a chosen route (transect line) to 
observe, identify, and record community resources, problems, and opportunities. 
• 
Method: Walk with community members in a straight line (transect) through different zones 
• 
Zones covered: Forest, agricultural land, settlement, common land, water bodies 
• 
Information gathered: Land use, water sources, health hazards, vegetation, infrastructure 
• 
Output: Transect diagram — visual record of observations 
• 
Differs from social map: Transect walk is linear (cross-sectional view); social map shows plan 
view 
• 
Combined use: Social map + transect walk = comprehensive community profile 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "MAPS" for Social Mapping 
M – Members draw the map collectively A – Assets and resources identified P – Problems 
and gaps highlighted S – Services and facilities located 
38. TYPES AND MANAGEMENT OF CONFLICT / JOHARI WINDOW (May 
2025 — NEW) 
258


--- PAGE 271 ---

swaDesh
Types of Conflict 
• 
Intrapersonal: Within a single individual (goal conflict, role conflict) 
• 
Interpersonal: Between two or more individuals 
• 
Intragroup: Within a group or team 
• 
Intergroup: Between different groups or departments 
• 
Organizational: Structural, functional conflicts within an organization 
Conflict Management Styles (Thomas-Kilmann Model) 
• 
Competing: Win-lose; assertive, uncooperative — used in emergencies 
• 
Collaborating: Win-win; assertive, cooperative — best for complex problems 
• 
Compromising: Partial win-win; intermediate — useful for time-limited issues 
• 
Avoiding: Lose-lose; unassertive, uncooperative — used for trivial issues 
• 
Accommodating: Lose-win; unassertive, cooperative — when relationship matters more 
JOHARI WINDOW (Luft and Ingham, 1955) 
A model for understanding self-awareness and interpersonal communication, represented as a 4-pane 
window: 
• 
Open/Arena: Known to self + known to others — free and open exchange 
• 
Blind Spot: Unknown to self + known to others — need feedback to discover 
• 
Hidden/Facade: Known to self + unknown to others — personal secrets 
• 
Unknown: Unknown to self + unknown to others — unconscious behaviors 
Goal: Expand the 'Open' area through self-disclosure and accepting feedback — improves team 
communication and trust. 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "OBHU" for Johari Window 
O – Open (self knows, others know) B – Blind spot (others know, self doesn't) H – Hidden 
(self knows, others don't) U – Unknown (neither knows) 
39. SOCIAL MARKETING IN FAMILY WELFARE PROGRAMME (2012, 2010) 
Definition 
Social marketing is the application of commercial marketing principles and techniques to promote 
voluntary behavior changes that benefit individuals and society. In family welfare, it is used to increase 
adoption of contraceptives and maternal/child health practices. 
4 P's of Social Marketing 
• 
Product: The behavior change or health product (condom, OCP, IUD) 
• 
Price: The cost (monetary, psychological, social) the person pays 
• 
Place: Where the product/service is accessible (shops, PHCs, ASHA) 
• 
Promotion: Communication channels used (IEC, mass media, IPC) 
Examples in India 
• 
Nirodh: First social marketing of condom in India (1968, HLL) 
259


--- PAGE 272 ---

swaDesh
• 
Social Marketing Company (SMC): Distributes Mala N (oral pills), Nirodh condoms at 
subsidized prices through commercial outlets 
• 
Condom Vending Machines: Accessible 24/7 in public places 
• 
Project Janani: Social franchising for reproductive health services 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "4 P's" for Social Marketing 
P – Product (what behavior/item) P – Price (cost to beneficiary) P – Place 
(accessibility/distribution) P – Promotion (communication/IEC) 
40. BHORE COMMITTEE RECOMMENDATIONS (2018, 2015) 
Background 
The Health Survey and Development Committee, chaired by Sir Joseph Bhore, was appointed by the 
Government of India in 1943 and submitted its report in 1946. It is considered the Magna Carta of Indian 
public health. 
Key Recommendations 
• 
Short-term programme: Primary Health Unit for every 40,000 population; Secondary Health 
Centre for 650,000 population 
• 
Long-term programme: Three-tier health system 
• 
Primary Health Centre: For 10,000–20,000 population — 6 doctors, 6 nurses, other staff 
• 
Secondary Health Centre: 650,000 population — 75-bed hospital 
• 
Teaching/Research Centre: For every 2 million population — 2500-bed hospital 
• 
Health workforce training: Short-service doctors (3-year degree social physicians) 
• 
Integration of preventive and curative services at all levels 
• 
Rural bias in healthcare — 'reaching health to the doorsteps' 
• 
Emphasis on social medicine — treating disease in its social context 
Significance 
The Bhore Committee recommendations formed the blueprint for India's health system. Concept of 
Primary Health Centre (PHC), integration of preventive and curative services, and decentralized health 
system all trace their origins to this report. 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "SPIRAL" for Bhore Committee 
S – Social medicine emphasis P – Primary Health Centre concept I – Integration 
(preventive + curative) R – Rural focus ("doorstep delivery") A – All three tiers of health 
system L – Long & short term programmes recommended 
41. BIOMEDICAL WASTE MANAGEMENT (2021, 2020) 
Definition 
Biomedical waste (BMW) is any waste generated during the diagnosis, treatment, or immunization of 
human beings or animals or in research activities related thereto. 
260


--- PAGE 273 ---

swaDesh
Categories of BMW (BMW Rules 2016, India — 4 color-coded bins) 
• 
Yellow Bag: Human anatomical waste, soiled waste, chemical waste, discarded medicines 
• 
Red Bag: Contaminated recyclable waste (syringes without needles, IV sets, catheters) 
• 
White/Translucent Puncture-proof Container: Used needles, syringes with needles, blades 
• 
Blue Box: Glass waste (glass slides, medicine vials) 
Treatment and Disposal Methods 
• 
Autoclave (Steam Sterilization): Red bag waste 
• 
Incineration: Yellow bag waste (anatomical waste) 
• 
Shredding + disinfection: Red bag plastics after autoclave 
• 
Chemical disinfection: Liquid waste 
• 
Deep burial: Only in rural/remote areas without CBMWTFs 
• 
CBMWTF: Common Biomedical Waste Treatment Facility — regional facility 
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "YRWB" for BMW Color Coding (Yellow, Red, White, 
Blue) 
Y – Yellow = Anatomical/soiled/chemical waste R – Red = Recyclable contaminated waste 
W – White = Sharps (needles, blades) — puncture-proof B – Blue = Broken glass 
43. EXCLUSIVE BREASTFEEDING / BFHI (2021, 2020) 
Exclusive Breastfeeding (EBF) 
• 
Definition: Feeding only breast milk (including expressed milk) with no other liquids or solids, 
not even water, except for ORS, drops, or syrups (vitamins, minerals, medications) 
• 
Duration: Recommended for first 6 months of life 
• 
Continuation: Breastfeeding with complementary foods up to 2 years and beyond 
• 
Benefits: Protects against diarrhea, pneumonia, otitis media, SIDS, reduces maternal 
breast/ovarian cancer risk 
Baby Friendly Hospital Initiative (BFHI) 
Launched by WHO and UNICEF in 1991 to promote breastfeeding globally through hospitals. Ten 
Steps to Successful Breastfeeding: 
• 
Written breastfeeding policy posted 
• 
Train all healthcare staff 
• 
Inform all pregnant women about benefits of breastfeeding 
• 
Initiate breastfeeding within 30 minutes of birth (early initiation) 
• 
Show mothers how to breastfeed and maintain milk supply 
• 
Give no other food/drink unless medically indicated 
• 
Room-in (mother and baby together 24 hours) 
• 
Encourage breastfeeding on demand 
• 
No pacifiers or artificial nipples 
• 
Foster breastfeeding support groups on discharge 
261


--- PAGE 274 ---

swaDesh
 
🧠 MNEMONIC: "WIT GIRLS S FON" for 10 Steps of BFHI 
W – Written policy I – Inform pregnant women T – Train staff G – Give no artificial food I – 
Initiate within 30 min R – Room-in 24 hours L – Let mothers know how to breastfeed S – 
Support groups on discharge S – Supply (maintain milk supply) F – Feed on demand O – 
On no pacifiers N – No supplementary feeds 
47. HERD IMMUNITY (Nov 2023 — NEW) 
Definition 
Herd immunity (community immunity / population immunity) is the indirect protection that occurs when 
a sufficient proportion of a population has become immune to an infection (through vaccination or prior 
infection), thereby reducing the likelihood of infection for individuals who lack immunity. 
Mechanism 
• 
When most individuals are immune, pathogen chains of transmission are broken 
• 
Even non-immune individuals are protected because they are less likely to encounter infected 
contacts 
• 
Critical concept: Herd Immunity Threshold (HIT) 
Herd Immunity Threshold (HIT) Formula 
HIT = 1 – (1/R₀) 
• 
R₀ = Basic reproductive number (average number of secondary cases from one primary case 
in a fully susceptible population) 
• 
Measles: R₀ = 12–18; HIT = 92–95% 
• 
Polio: R₀ = 5–7; HIT = 80–86% 
• 
COVID-19 (original): R₀ = 2–3; HIT = 50–67% 
• 
Mumps: R₀ = 4–7; HIT = 75–86% 
• 
Smallpox: R₀ = 5–7; HIT = 80–85% 
Role in Infection Control 
• 
Vaccination campaigns aim to achieve and maintain herd immunity thresholds 
• 
Polio eradication: OPV campaigns to achieve >80% coverage 
• 
Measles elimination: Requires >95% vaccination coverage (2 doses) 
• 
COVID-19: Herd immunity complicated by variants, waning immunity, and vaccine hesitancy 
• 
Failure of herd immunity: When coverage falls below HIT (e.g., anti-vaccine movements → 
measles outbreaks) 
• 
Natural herd immunity: Acquired through infection — not recommended strategy due to high 
morbidity/mortality cost 
• 
Free riders (unvaccinated individuals who benefit from others' vaccination) — creates ethical 
dimension 
 
262


--- PAGE 275 ---

swaDesh
Q37: SOCIAL MAPPING AND TRANSECT WALK 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Describe Social Mapping and Transect Walk as participatory tools in community health 
assessment. Discuss their methodology, applications, and significance in Community Medicine. 
② 1-MINUTE EXAM OUTLINE 
1. Definition of Social Mapping and Transect Walk 
2. Historical background — PRA/PLA approach 
3. Methodology of Social Mapping 
4. Methodology of Transect Walk 
5. Differences between the two tools 
6. Applications in public health / community diagnosis 
7. Advantages and limitations 
8. Use in ASHA/NHM/Village Health Planning 
9. Conclusion 
③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
Definition linking to PRA (Participatory Rural Appraisal) — Park 16th Ed, Ch. Social 
Medicine 
• 
Step-by-step methodology for BOTH tools separately 
• 
Application in Village Health Planning, ASHA role, NHM community diagnosis 
 
④ DEFINITION 
SOCIAL MAPPING (Park / JK) 
Social Mapping is a Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) technique in which community members 
collectively draw a map of their village/area, indicating the location of households, health facilities, 
water sources, sanitation, schools, and social resources. It facilitates community-led identification of 
health needs and disparities. (Park, 16th Ed; JK) 
 
TRANSECT WALK (Park / JK) 
A Transect Walk is a systematic walk by a health worker/team across an area (transect = cross-
section) along with community members, to observe, record, and discuss different zones, land use, 
ecological features, health hazards, and living conditions. It is a direct observation method 
complementing social maps. (Park; JK)

⑥ EPIDEMIOLOGICAL / PUBLIC HEALTH RELEVANCE 
These tools are foundational to community diagnosis — the first step in the epidemiological approach to 
community health. They are used in: 
• 
ASHA training and village health planning under NHM 
• 
Village Health, Sanitation and Nutrition Committee (VHSNC) planning 
• 
School health surveys, environmental health assessments 
• 
Tribal health and urban slum health programs 
• 
ICDS, Swachh Bharat, and WASH program baseline assessments 
⑦ METHODOLOGY — SOCIAL MAPPING 
◆ Step-by-Step Process 
10. Assemble community members (men, women, elderly, youth) in open space 
11. Provide chalk/sticks/colored powder or large paper 
12. Ask community to draw boundary of village first 
13. Mark houses — differentiate by caste, economic status, BPL status if needed 
14. Mark: hand pumps, wells, ponds, open defecation sites, drainage 
15. Mark: anganwadi, PHC, school, SHG meeting place, temple/mosque 
16. Discuss findings — identify underserved clusters, high-risk households 
17. Cross-verify with secondary data (census, HMIS) 
18. Prepare clean copy; use for Village Health Plan 
⑧ METHODOLOGY — TRANSECT WALK 
◆ Step-by-Step Process 
19. Select a route that cuts across different zones of the village (forest → agricultural → residential 
→ industrial, if applicable) 
20. Walk with 2–3 community members who know the area 
21. Observe and record: land use, water bodies, drainage, sanitation, housing quality 
22. Note health hazards: stagnant water (vector breeding), open dumping, industrial effluents 
23. Interview people encountered during walk about health problems 
24. Photograph hazards (if possible) 
25. Draw transect diagram: horizontal line = route; vertical zones = what is observed 
26. Compare with social map and community perceptions 
27. Compile findings for community health report

⑫ NATIONAL PROGRAM LINKAGE 
National Health Mission (NHM) — 2005 
• 
ASHA workers trained to prepare Village Health Maps 
•

⑬ RECENT GUIDELINES (2022–2024) 
• 
NHM Operational Guidelines 2022: Mandates use of social mapping in ASHA microplanning 
and Village Health Plan preparation 
•

• 
NITI Aayog 2023: Advocates community-based planning tools for Aspirational Districts 
Programme health components 
⑭ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Social Mapping and Transect Walk are powerful, low-cost, community-owned participatory tools that 
form the backbone of community diagnosis in India's primary health care system. They convert 
community knowledge into actionable health intelligence, enabling targeted, equitable, and culturally 
appropriate health planning. As India moves toward Universal Health Coverage, these tools remain 
essential for last-mile health planning, particularly in tribal, rural, and underserved areas.

Q38: TYPES & MANAGEMENT OF CONFLICT / JOHARI 
WINDOW 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Describe the types of conflict in health organizations and their management. Write a note on the 
JOHARI Window and its application in health management. 
② 1-MINUTE EXAM OUTLINE 
28. Definition of Conflict 
29. Types of Conflict 
30. Causes of Conflict in Health Organizations 
31. Stages of Conflict (Pondy's Model) 
32. Conflict Management Strategies 
33. JOHARI Window — Definition and Quadrants 
34. Application of JOHARI Window in Health Management 
35. Conclusion 
③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
Clear definition + Thomas-Kilmann 5 conflict management styles 
• 
JOHARI Window — all 4 quadrants with correct names and explanation 
• 
Practical application of JOHARI Window in health team building / communication 
④ DEFINITION 
CONFLICT (JK / Park Health Management) 
Conflict is a process that begins when one party perceives that another party has negatively affected, 
or is about to negatively affect, something that the first party cares about. In health organizations, 
conflict arises from differences in goals, values, resources, roles, or perceptions among individuals or 
groups. (Jugal Kishore — Health Management; Park — Social & Preventive Medicine) 
 
JOHARI WINDOW 
The JOHARI Window is a psychological tool developed by Joseph Luft and Harrington Ingham (1955) 
that helps individuals and teams understand interpersonal communication, self-awareness, and group 
dynamics through a 2×2 grid representing 'Known/Unknown to Self' versus 'Known/Unknown to Others'. 
(JK — Health Management) 
⑤ TYPES OF CONFLICT TABLE 
Type 
Description 
Example in Health Setting 
Intrapersonal 
Conflict within an individual — role

⑥ CAUSES OF CONFLICT IN HEALTH ORGANIZATIONS 
• 
Scarce resources (beds, equipment, budget, staff) 
• 
Role ambiguity — overlapping duties between nurses, paramedics, doctors 
• 
Communication failures — misinformation, rumours 
• 
Different professional values and goals 
• 
Hierarchical power imbalance 
• 
Personality differences and interpersonal styles 
• 
Organizational change — new policies, restructuring 
• 
Cultural and gender differences in multiprofessional teams 
⑦ STAGES OF CONFLICT — PONDY'S MODEL 
Stage 
Description 
1. Latent Conflict 
Conditions exist for conflict but not yet perceived (resource scarcity, role 
ambiguity) 
2. Perceived Conflict 
One/both parties aware of conflict situation 
3. Felt Conflict 
Emotional response — anxiety, tension, hostility 
4. Manifest Conflict 
Conflict becomes visible through behaviour — arguing, strikes, sabotage 
5. Conflict Aftermath 
Resolution or suppression — affects future relationships

⑩ GOAL OF JOHARI WINDOW IN TEAMS 
Goal: MAXIMIZE Quadrant 1 (Open Area) 
• 
Expand Open Area → reduces misunderstandings, improves trust 
• 
Shrink Blind Spot → through constructive feedback from colleagues 
• 
Shrink Hidden Area → through appropriate self-disclosure 
• 
Reduce Unknown → through training, counselling, self-exploration

Key Public Health Message 
Conflict is inevitable in complex health organizations but can be constructively managed through 
evidence-based strategies. The Thomas-Kilmann model provides practical tools for choosing 
appropriate conflict responses, while the JOHARI Window is a powerful self-awareness framework that, 
when applied in health teams, expands mutual understanding, reduces interpersonal blind spots, and 
builds the psychological safety essential for high-performing, patient-centered healthcare teams.

Q39: SOCIAL MARKETING IN FAMILY WELFARE PROGRAMME 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Define Social Marketing. Describe the principles and application of Social Marketing in India's 
Family Welfare Programme with examples. 
② 1-MINUTE EXAM OUTLINE 
36. Definition — Kotler & Zaltman / Park / JK 
37. Social Marketing vs. Commercial Marketing 
38. 4 Ps of Social Marketing 
39. Principles of Social Marketing 
40. Application in Family Welfare Programme 
41. Examples — condom social marketing, ORS, oral contraceptives 
42. National programs using social marketing 
43. Advantages and limitations 
44. Conclusion 
③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
Kotler & Zaltman definition (1971) — this is THE cited definition 
• 
4 Ps: Product, Price, Place, Promotion — with family welfare examples for each 
• 
Nirodh condom social marketing program as India's landmark example + ORS Jeevan 
Raksha 
④ DEFINITION 
SOCIAL MARKETING (Kotler & Zaltman, 1971 — cited in Park) 
Social Marketing is the design, implementation, and control of programs calculated to influence the 
acceptability of social ideas, involving considerations of product planning, pricing, communication, 
distribution, and marketing research. (Kotler & Zaltman, 1971; cited in Park's Textbook of PSM) 
 
In simpler terms: Social Marketing applies commercial marketing principles to promote voluntary 
behaviour change for social good — particularly health behaviours — rather than profit. (JK) 
⑤ SOCIAL MARKETING vs. COMMERCIAL MARKETING 
Feature 
Commercial Marketing 
Social Marketing 
Goal 
Profit / market share 
Behaviour change / social benefit 
Product 
Physical goods / services 
Behaviour, idea, or health product 
Target 
Consumers with purchasing power 
Priority populations, often 
underserved 
Success measure 
Sales, revenue 
Adoption of healthy behaviour 
Example 
Selling a soft drink 
Promoting condom use / family 
planning 
⑥ THE 4 Ps OF SOCIAL MARKETING IN FAMILY WELFARE 
271


--- PAGE 284 ---

swaDesh
P 
Concept 
Family Welfare Example

The desired behaviour or 
health product 
Condom use, OCP adoption, sterilization acceptance,

psychological) the target

Free/subsidized contraceptives; reducing social

⑦ PRINCIPLES OF SOCIAL MARKETING (JK) 
• 
Audience orientation — design programs from consumer's perspective, not provider's 
• 
Exchange theory — offer perceived benefits greater than perceived costs 
• 
Segmentation — tailor messages to specific target groups (urban/rural, age, gender) 
• 
Behaviour focus — target specific, measurable behaviour change (not just awareness) 
• 
Competition analysis — identify competing behaviours and address them 
• 
Channel optimization — use channels most accessible and credible to the audience 
• 
Continuous monitoring and adaptation — use formative, process, and outcome evaluation 
⑧ APPLICATION IN INDIA'S FAMILY WELFARE PROGRAMME 
A. Contraceptive Social Marketing (CSM) — NIRODH Programme 
• 
India was among the first countries to use condom social marketing (1968 — Nirodh 
programme) 
• 
Condoms marketed through commercial channels (shops, pharmacies, petrol stations) at 
subsidized price 
• 
Brand positioning: 'Nirodh' made condom a household word — reduced stigma 
• 
Later extended: Mala D (OCP), Copper-T (IUD) social marketing 
• 
DKT International and PSP-One ran major CSM programs in India 
 
B. ORS — Jeevan Raksha Packet 
• 
Social marketing of ORS for diarrhoea management in children 
• 
Promoted through AWW, ASHA, ANM with branded packaging 
• 
Price: minimal cost to improve compliance and perceived value 
• 
Reduced under-5 mortality from diarrhoeal diseases significantly 
 
C. Maternal Health Behaviour Promotion 
• 
Promotion of 4 ANC visits, institutional delivery, postnatal care 
• 
VHND (Village Health and Nutrition Day) — social marketing platform 
• 
JSY (Janani Suraksha Yojana) — financial incentive = 'price' reduction for institutional delivery 
• 
Poshan Abhiyaan — social marketing of nutrition behaviours 
⑨ INDIA NATIONAL PROGRAMS USING SOCIAL MARKETING

⑩ ADVANTAGES AND LIMITATIONS 
Advantages 
Limitations 
Reaches large populations cost-effectively 
Oversimplification of complex behaviours 
Uses existing commercial channels 
May create dependency on subsidized products 
Reduces stigma through branding 
May neglect poorest who cannot pay even 
subsidized price 
Measurable outcomes (sales, coverage)

⑪ RECENT UPDATES 2022–2024 
• 
Digital Social Marketing 2023: WhatsApp-based family planning counselling by ASHA workers; 
social media campaigns (Facebook, YouTube) for family welfare — reaching urban youth 
• 
Mission Parivar Vikas Review 2022: Social marketing of basket of contraceptive choices 
expanded to include injectables (DMPA-SC/Antara programme) 
• 
WHO 2022 Family Planning Guidelines: Recommends social marketing as Tier 1 evidence-
based strategy for increasing contraceptive prevalence in LMICs 
⑫ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Social Marketing has transformed India's Family Welfare Programme from a supply-driven, quota-
based approach to a demand-driven, behaviour-change model. By applying the 4 Ps framework and 
principles of audience orientation, exchange, and segmentation, India achieved significant increases in 
contraceptive prevalence and maternal health behaviours. In the digital age, social marketing must now 
harness new media platforms while ensuring equity and reaching the last-mile population.

Q40: BHORE COMMITTEE RECOMMENDATIONS 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Write about the Bhore Committee (1946): its constitution, terms of reference, short-term and 
long-term recommendations, and its impact on India's health system. 
② 1-MINUTE EXAM OUTLINE 
45. Background and constitution of Bhore Committee 
46. Terms of reference 
47. Short-term recommendations 
48. Long-term recommendations 
49. Three-tier health system proposed 
50. 'Social physician' concept 
51. Impact and legacy 
52. Criticisms 
53. Conclusion 
③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
Chairman: Sir Joseph Bhore | Year: 1943–1946 | Report submitted: 1946 
• 
Short-term: Primary Health Unit (75,000 population); Long-term: 3-tier system 
• 
'Social Physician' concept — doctor must understand social determinants of health 
④ BACKGROUND 
Historical Context (Park, Ch. Health Administration) 
After World War II, it was clear that India needed a comprehensive health policy for the post-
independence era. The British Government of India appointed the Health Survey and Development 
Committee in 1943, chaired by Sir Joseph William Bhore (ICS), to survey existing health conditions and 
make recommendations for future health development. The committee submitted its report in 1946 — 
two volumes — a landmark document that shaped independent India's health system. (Park) 
⑤ CONSTITUTION OF THE COMMITTEE 
Detail 
Information 
Chairman 
Sir Joseph William Bhore (ICS) 
Year constituted 
1943 
Report submitted 
1946 
Members 
26 members — doctors, engineers, administrators, educationists 
Known as 
Bhore Committee / Health Survey and Development Committee 
Report volumes 
4 volumes (2 main + 2 supplementary) 
⑥ TERMS OF REFERENCE 
• 
Survey existing position of health conditions in British India 
• 
Consider and make recommendations for future health development 
• 
Review health administration at Central and Provincial levels 
275


--- PAGE 288 ---

swaDesh
• 
Examine availability of medical personnel and facilities 
• 
Recommend measures to improve health of the population 
⑦ SHORT-TERM RECOMMENDATIONS (3-Year Plan) 
Primary Health Unit (Short-term) — per 40,000 population 
• 
Each Primary Health Unit to serve 40,000 population 
• 
Staff: 2 doctors, 4 PHN, 4 midwives, 4 trained dais, 2 sanitary inspectors, 2 health assistants 
• 
6-bedded hospital attached to each unit 
• 
Focus on: MCH, communicable disease control, environmental sanitation, health education 
• 
District Health Organisation to supervise primary units 
⑧ LONG-TERM RECOMMENDATIONS (Future Plan) 
Three-Tier Health System Proposed 
Tier

Facilities

•
