• 
All health services to be provided FREE to all citizens 
• 
Integration of curative and preventive services at all levels 
• 
Establishment of an All India Health Service (like IAS/IPS) 
⑨ KEY CONCEPTS INTRODUCED 
A. The 'Social Physician' Concept 
Bhore Committee emphasized that a doctor must not only treat disease but understand the social, 
economic, and environmental determinants of health. The committee recommended a 'Social 
Physician' — a doctor who is socially conscious and community-oriented. This was a revolutionary 
concept for 1946. 
 
B. Preventive-Curative Integration 
The committee strongly recommended against the prevailing dichotomy between curative and 
preventive medicine. All health units should offer both. This principle became the foundation of the PHC 
concept in India. 
 
C. Universal Health Coverage Principle (Avant la Lettre) 
All services FREE to all citizens regardless of ability to pay — a principle that foreshadowed the 
modern UHC agenda by 60 years. 
⑩ OTHER IMPORTANT RECOMMENDATIONS 
276


--- PAGE 289 ---

swaDesh
• 
Medical education reform: increase in medical colleges; 5-year MBBS course with 3-month 
social and preventive medicine posting 
• 
Nursing education: establishment of nursing schools; trained dais for all deliveries 
• 
Environmental sanitation: safe water and sanitation for all villages 
• 
Nutrition: national nutrition policy; supplementary feeding for mothers and children 
• 
Mental health: psychiatric units at district hospitals 
• 
Industrial health: factory health services 
• 
Research: establish ICMR with expanded mandate 
• 
Health statistics: vital registration system for all districts 
⑪ IMPACT AND LEGACY 
Bhore Recommendation 
What Actually Happened 
Primary Health Unit per 10,000–
20,000 
PHC per 30,000 rural population established (PHC Act 1956) 
75-bedded hospital at PHC level 
PHC with 6 beds (far less than recommended) 
Free services to all 
Partially — government facilities nominally free but quality gaps 
remain 
Social physician concept 
Included in MBBS curriculum; Community Medicine as a specialty 
All India Health Service 
Not created; health remains state subject 
3-tier system 
Modified 3-tier system formed basis of Indian public health 
infrastructure 
Doctor:population 1:1,000 
Still not achieved — India has ~1:1,511 (NHP 2017 data) 
⑫ CRITICISMS OF BHORE COMMITTEE 
• 
Recommendations were too ambitious for a newly independent, resource-poor nation 
• 
No specific recommendations for tribal and rural health disparities 
• 
Did not adequately address indigenous systems of medicine (Ayurveda, Unani, Siddha) 
• 
75-bedded PHC was never realistically achievable — led to scaling back 
• 
No explicit mention of community participation in health planning 
⑬ RELATED COMMITTEES (Contextual Knowledge)

⑭ RECENT RELEVANCE (2022–2024) 
• 
National Health Policy 2017 (reviewed 2022): Reaffirms Bhore's vision of free, comprehensive, 
integrated primary health care — through Health and Wellness Centres (AB-HWC) 
277


--- PAGE 290 ---

swaDesh

• 
NMC Act 2020: Competency-Based Medical Education includes Community Medicine — 
fulfilling 'social physician' mandate 
⑮ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
The Bhore Committee Report of 1946 remains the most comprehensive and visionary document in 
Indian public health history. Its three-tier health system, social physician concept, and universal free 
services principle laid the foundation for independent India's entire health infrastructure. Though its 
ambitious targets remain only partially achieved seven decades later, the Bhore Committee continues 
to serve as the moral compass for Indian health policy — most recently echoed in the Ayushman 
Bharat vision of UHC for 1.4 billion people.

④ DEFINITION 
BIOMEDICAL WASTE — BMW Rules 2016 (MoEF&CC) 
Any waste which is generated during the diagnosis, treatment or immunisation of human beings or 
animals or in research activities pertaining thereto or in the production or testing of biological products. 
(Bio-Medical Waste Management Rules, 2016 — Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change 
+ Ministry of Health & Family Welfare, Government of India) 
⑤ SOURCES OF BIOMEDICAL WASTE 
•

•

• 
Home health care (needles, syringes, dressings) 
• 
Ambulatory health care facilities, dental clinics

⑦ MANAGEMENT STEPS — CHAIN OF BMW MANAGEMENT 
SEGREGATION → COLLECTION → STORAGE → TRANSPORT → TREATMENT → DISPOSAL 
 
1. SEGREGATION (at point of generation) 
• 
Segregate at source into colour-coded bags/containers as above 
• 
No mixing of BMW with general solid waste 
• 
Label each bag with date, time, source department 
 
2. COLLECTION 
• 
Collected by trained sanitation workers using PPE (gloves, mask, gown) 
• 
Trolleys/carts dedicated to BMW — not used for other purposes 
• 
Bags not to be more than 3/4 full when tied 
 
3. ON-SITE STORAGE 
• 
Storage area separate from general waste, patient areas, food stores 
• 
Duration: not more than 48 hours (except with prior SPCB permission) 
• 
Refrigerated storage for anatomical waste if >48 hours 
 
4. TRANSPORT 
• 
Dedicated vehicles for BMW — labelled, GPS-tracked (from 2019 amendment) 
• 
Common Biomedical Waste Treatment Facility (CBWTF) vehicles — authorized 
• 
Manifold system — sender gives receipt, receiver signs on delivery (traceability) 
 
5. TREATMENT METHODS

⑧ BMW RULES 2016 — KEY PROVISIONS 
• 
All healthcare facilities generating BMW must register with State Pollution Control Board 
(SPCB) 
• 
Mandatory training for all healthcare staff on BMW management every 6 months 
• 
Bio-Medical Waste Management Committee at facility level 
• 
Annual report to SPCB by 31st January each year 
• 
CBWTF (Common Biomedical Waste Treatment Facility) — preferred over on-site treatment for 
smaller facilities 
• 
Bar-coding of BMW bags and real-time tracking (2019 amendment) 
• 
Incentive for safe segregation — weight-based collection charges 
• 
Prohibition: no BMW to be mixed with municipal solid waste 
⑨ BMW RULES 2019 AMENDMENT — KEY CHANGES 
2019 Amendment 
Change 
E-waste from healthcare 
Added as new category — old X-ray films, lead-lined aprons must 
be disposed via authorized e-waste recyclers 
Bar-coding 
All BMW bags must have bar codes for tracking from source to 
CBWTF 
GPS tracking 
Vehicles transporting BMW must have GPS tracking 
Pre-treatment at source 
Chemical disinfection of liquid BMW before discharge into drains 
Timelines

Sharps from immunization programmes covered under White

⑪ QUANTITIES AND BURDEN IN INDIA 
• 
India generates approximately 619 tonnes/day of BMW (CPCB 2021–22) 
• 
Only ~85% of this is treated in authorized facilities (gap of ~15% improperly disposed) 
• 
Total CBWTFs in India: 198 (CPCB 2022) 
• 
COVID-19 pandemic: BMW generation increased 3-fold in 2020–21 due to PPE waste 
• 
COVID-19 waste classified as Yellow category — incineration mandatory 
⑫ HEALTH HAZARDS OF IMPROPER BMW DISPOSAL 
• 
Needle-stick injuries: transmission of HIV, HBV, HCV to healthcare workers and rag-pickers 
• 
Environmental contamination: chemical waste → soil and water pollution 
• 
Dioxin/furan emissions: from poor incineration → air pollution, carcinogenesis 
• 
Recycling of contaminated syringes: leading to iatrogenic infections 
• 
Community exposure: burning of BMW on open ground near hospitals 
⑬ RECENT GUIDELINES 2022–2024 
• 
CPCB Annual BMW Report 2022–23: India's BMW generation 619.7 T/day; treatment capacity 
to be expanded to 800 T/day by 2025 
• 
MoHFW Revised BMW Guidance 2023: Updated PPE waste disposal protocols post-COVID; 
N95 masks in Yellow category 
• 
WHO Healthcare Waste Guidelines 2023: Recommends colour-coded segregation (consistent 
with India's 4-category system) and CBWTF model for resource-limited settings 
⑭ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Biomedical Waste Management is a critical public health and environmental imperative. India's BMW 
Rules 2016 (amended 2019) provide a robust regulatory framework with colour-coded segregation, 
authorized treatment facilities, and mandatory tracking. However, implementation gaps, especially in 
small healthcare facilities and rural areas, continue to pose infection control and environmental risks. 
Strengthening CBWTF networks, universal training, and digital tracking are key priorities to achieve 
safe, sustainable BMW management across India's 2 million+ healthcare facilities. 
⑮ RAPID REVISION SHEET — Q41

National Rural Health Mission (NRHM) 
•

•

•

⑤ NRHM OBJECTIVES 
• 
Reduce MMR to 100 per 100,000 live births (from 254, 2004) 
• 
Reduce IMR to 30 per 1000 live births (from 58, 2005) 
• 
Reduce TFR to 2.1 by 2012

Prevention and control of communicable and non-communicable diseases 
• 
Universal access to integrated comprehensive primary health care 
• 
Population stabilization, gender and demographic balance

A. ASHA — Accredited Social Health Activist

swaDesh
•

•

Improves local health facility management and accountability 
 
G. Indian Public Health Standards (IPHS)

Standards for infrastructure, equipment, staffing at PHC, CHC, DH levels 
• 
Revised 2022: updated staffing norms, equipment lists for all facility levels

④ DEFINITION 
EXCLUSIVE BREASTFEEDING (EBF) — WHO, 2001 
Feeding an infant only breast milk — no other foods or liquids, not even water — except for oral 
rehydration salts (ORS), drops, or syrups (vitamins, minerals, medicines) for the first 6 months of life. 
(WHO, 2001; Park — Nutrition and Health)

Breastmilk + water/juices; NOT other foods 
Complementary Breastfeeding 
Breastmilk + solid/semi-solid foods; usually 6–24 months 
Bottle feeding 
Formula or expressed breastmilk via bottle 
Not breastfeeding 
No breastmilk at all

•

•

⑦ BENEFITS OF EXCLUSIVE BREASTFEEDING 
Beneficiary 
Benefits 
INFANT — Nutritional 
Complete nutrition for 6 months; ideal protein:fat:carbohydrate ratio; easily 
digestible 
INFANT — 
Immunological 
SIgA, lactoferrin, lysozyme, macrophages, lymphocytes → protection 
against infections 
INFANT — Cognitive 
IQ advantage of 3–4 points; better neurodevelopment (DHA, arachidonic

↓ diarrhoea (2-3x), ARI (1.5x), otitis media, NEC, SIDS, type 1 DM, obesity

↓ breast cancer, ovarian cancer, osteoporosis, type 2 DM

packaging) 
Economic

benefit globally 
⑧ CONTRAINDICATIONS TO BREASTFEEDING 
Absolute Contraindications (WHO 2009) 
• 
Infant: Galactosaemia (cannot metabolize galactose in breastmilk) 
• 
Mother: HIV positive (in settings where safe alternatives available — WHO Option B+) 
• 
Mother: Active untreated tuberculosis (until 2 weeks of treatment) 
• 
Mother: Active herpes simplex lesions on breast 
• 
Mother: Receiving chemotherapy/radiotherapy 
• 
Mother: Certain medications (ergotamine, statins, lithium, radioactive iodine) 
 
Note on HIV in India (NACO/WHO 2022) 
In India, WHO recommends breastfeeding WITH antiretroviral therapy for HIV+ mothers (Option B+) as 
risk of artificial feeding (diarrhoea, malnutrition) outweighs HIV transmission risk in resource-limited 
settings. 
289


--- PAGE 302 ---

swaDesh
⑨ BABY FRIENDLY HOSPITAL INITIATIVE (BFHI) 
BFHI — WHO & UNICEF, 1991 
The Baby-Friendly Hospital Initiative (BFHI) was launched in 1991 by WHO and UNICEF to encourage 
hospitals to implement the Ten Steps to Successful Breastfeeding and to comply with the International 
Code of Marketing of Breastmilk Substitutes (1981). Hospitals meeting these criteria receive 'Baby-
Friendly' designation. (Park — MCH) 
 
◆ TEN STEPS TO SUCCESSFUL BREASTFEEDING (BFHI — WHO/UNICEF 1991, 
Revised 2018) 
Step 
Description 
Step 1 
Have a written breastfeeding policy routinely communicated to all staff 
Step 2 
Train all healthcare staff in skills to implement breastfeeding policy 
Step 3 
Inform all pregnant women about the benefits and management of breastfeeding 
Step 4 
Help mothers initiate breastfeeding within ONE HOUR of birth 
Step 5 
Show mothers how to breastfeed and how to maintain lactation even if they should be 
separated from their infants 
Step 6 
Give newborn infants NO food or drink other than breastmilk, unless medically indicated 
Step 7 
Practice rooming-in — allow mothers and infants to remain together 24 hours a day 
Step 8 
Encourage breastfeeding on demand (whenever infant shows hunger cues) 
Step 9 
Give no artificial teats or pacifiers (dummies/soothers) to breastfeeding infants 
Step 10 
Foster the establishment of breastfeeding support groups and refer mothers on discharge 
⑩ INDIA — MAA PROGRAMME 
MAA — Mothers' Absolute Affection Programme (2016) 
• 
Launched: August 5, 2016 by Union Ministry of Health & Family Welfare 
• 
Nodal Ministry: MoHFW; implementing agency: NHM states 
• 
Objectives: improve early initiation + exclusive breastfeeding rates 
• 
Key components: 
◦ 
Facility-based lactation management centres in medical colleges and district hospitals 
◦ 
Community mobilization through ASHA workers for counselling 
◦ 
Mass media campaign — TV, radio, social media 
◦ 
Capacity building of frontline workers 
◦ 
MAA awards to recognize best-performing states and facilities 
• 
Target: EBF rate > 69% nationally; early initiation > 50% 
⑪ COMPLEMENTARY FEEDING 
WHO / NIN Guidelines 
• 
Start complementary foods at EXACTLY 6 months (not before 4, not after 6) 
• 
Continue breastfeeding up to 2 years or beyond 
• 
Frequency: 6–8 months → 2–3 times/day; 9–11 months → 3–4 times/day; 12–24 months → 3–4 
times + 1–2 snacks 
• 
Texture progression: pureed → mashed → family foods 
• 
No salt/sugar added to foods for infants <1 year 
• 
Vitamin A supplementation (9 months, 18 months, 24 months) alongside EBF support 
290


--- PAGE 303 ---

swaDesh
⑫ RECENT GUIDELINES 2022–2024 
• 
WHO 2022 Infant Feeding Guidelines: Recommends EBF for 6 months + breastfeeding with 
complementary foods up to 2 years for all infants globally; updated HIV + breastfeeding 
recommendations (Option B+) 
• 
MAA Programme Review 2022: EBF rate improved from 54.9% (NFHS-4) to 63.7% (NFHS-5); 
MAA programme credited; target 70% by 2025 
• 
BFHI Revised Standards 2018 (implementation ongoing): Step 4 changed from '30 minutes' to 
'1 hour'; community-based Step 10 expanded 
⑬ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Exclusive Breastfeeding for six months is the single most cost-effective intervention to reduce infant 
mortality and promote child health. India's NFHS-5 shows encouraging progress (63.7% EBF), driven 
by the MAA Programme, ASHA counselling, and BFHI implementation. The Ten Steps to Successful 
Breastfeeding remain the global gold standard for hospital-level promotion. Sustaining and scaling 
these gains through community-level support, continued mass media campaigns, and strict 
implementation of the IMS Act 1992 (against breastmilk substitute marketing) is essential to achieving 
India's maternal and child health SDG targets.

Q44: LIFE TABLES AND THEIR APPLICATIONS 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Define Life Table. Describe the components and construction of a life table. Discuss its 
applications in public health and demography.

③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
All 7 standard columns of a life table — lx, dx, qx, Lx, Tx, ex (with formulae)

Distinction between Complete vs. Abridged, Cohort vs. Current life tables 
• 
India Life Expectancy at birth: 70.19 years (2021-25 SRS); Male 68.6, Female 71.8 — CITE 
THIS 
④ DEFINITION 
LIFE TABLE (Park / JK) 
A Life Table is a statistical table that shows the probability of death and survival at each age for a given 
population. It is a systematic account of how members of a birth cohort die off as they progress through 
life. It provides the basis for calculating life expectancy at any given age. (Park — Demography and 
Family Planning; JK) 
⑤ TYPES OF LIFE TABLES 
Classification 
Type 
Description 
By age grouping 
Complete Life Table 
Data for each single year of age (0, 1, 2, 3 ... 
100+) 
By age grouping 
Abridged Life Table 
Data for 5-year age groups (0, 1-4, 5-9, 10-14 ... 
85+) — more practical 
By time reference 
Cohort (Generation) 
Life Table 
Follows an actual birth cohort from birth to death 
— longitudinal; takes 100+ years to complete 
By time reference 
Current (Period) Life 
Table 
Uses age-specific mortality rates of a given 
PERIOD (e.g., 2021–25); assumes these rates 
remain constant — most commonly used 
By sex 
Combined / Separate 
Often constructed separately for males and 
females due to different mortality patterns 
⑥ COMPONENTS (COLUMNS) OF A LIFE TABLE 
292


--- PAGE 305 ---

swaDesh
Column 
Symbol 
Definition 
Formula 
Age interval 
x to x+n 
Age group (e.g., 0-1, 1-5, 
5-10) 
Given 
Number living at start 
lx 
Number alive at exact age 
x (out of 100,000 birth 
cohort — radix) 
l0 = 100,000 (radix) 
Number dying in 
interval 
dx 
Deaths between age x and 
x+n

Probability of dying 
qx

before age x+n 
qx = dx / lx 
Probability of 
surviving 
px 
Probability that a person 
alive at age x will survive to 
age x+n 
px = 1 - qx 
Person-years lived 
Lx 
Total years lived by the 
cohort in the age interval x 
to x+n 
Lx ≈ (lx + lx+n) / 2 × n 
Total future person-

Life expectancy 
ex or 
e°x 
Average number of years a

to live 
ex = Tx / lx 
⑦ CONSTRUCTION OF A LIFE TABLE (Step-by-Step) 
91. Start with age-specific death rates (ASDRs) from vital registration or census data 
92. Convert ASDRs to probability of dying (qx) for each age group using standard conversion 
formula 
93. Set radix: l0 = 100,000 (hypothetical cohort of 100,000 live births) 
94. Calculate lx+n = lx × (1 - qx) for each age group 
95. Calculate dx = lx - lx+n 
96. Calculate Lx = (lx + lx+n)/2 × n (person-years lived in interval) 
97. Calculate Tx = sum of all Lx from current age to end of table 
98. Calculate ex = Tx / lx (life expectancy at age x)

⑩ APPLICATIONS OF LIFE TABLES 
Field 
Application 
Public Health Planning 
Calculate life expectancy at birth/age; measure health status of 
population; set targets 
DALY / Burden of Disease 
Reference life table used to calculate Years of Life Lost (YLL) in DALY 
calculations (GBD Study) 
Insurance / Actuarial 
Science 
Calculate insurance premiums, annuity payments, pension planning 
Demography 
Calculate net reproduction rate (NRR); population projections; stable 
population analysis 
Health Policy 
Compare life expectancy across states/countries; track progress on 
SDGs (SDG 3.1, 3.2) 
Epidemiology 
Calculate person-years of follow-up in cohort studies; survival analysis 
Program Evaluation 
Measure impact of health interventions on life expectancy 
Family Planning 
Calculate generation length; reproductive span; population momentum 
⑪ DISABILITY-ADJUSTED LIFE YEAR (DALY) — LIFE TABLE CONNECTION 
DALY = YLL + YLD (Park / GBD) 
• 
YLL (Years of Life Lost) = number of deaths × standard life expectancy at age of death 
• 
Standard life expectancy used from reference life table (currently GBD 2019 uses frontier life 
expectancy: 91 years for females) 
• 
Life tables thus form the mathematical foundation for the global burden of disease calculations 
⑫ LIMITATIONS OF LIFE TABLES 
• 
Cohort life table: impractical — takes 100 years to complete 
• 
Period life table: assumes current mortality rates remain constant — unrealistic 
• 
Quality of life tables depends on accuracy of vital registration — poor in many LMICs 
• 
Does not account for quality of life — only quantity 
• 
Aggregated data hides variation within age groups 
• 
India: SRS-based life tables have sampling errors, especially at older ages 
⑬ RECENT DATA 2022–2024 
• 
SRS Based Abridged Life Tables 2015–19 (released 2022): India life expectancy = 69.4 years 
(M: 68.2, F: 70.7) 
• 
NITI Aayog SDG India Index 2023: Life expectancy used as key health indicator (SDG 3) — 
wide state variation (Kerala 75 years vs. UP 65 years) 
294


--- PAGE 307 ---

swaDesh
• 
Global Burden of Disease Study 2021: India YLL from CVD, respiratory disease, neonatal 
causes remain leading causes of life-years lost 
⑭ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Life Tables are the cornerstone of demographic analysis and public health planning. By converting age-
specific mortality data into intuitive metrics of survival probability and life expectancy, they enable 
policymakers, actuaries, and public health professionals to quantify the health status of populations, 
measure the impact of interventions, and plan for the future. India's improving life expectancy (41 years 
in 1951 → 70 years in 2021) reflects the cumulative impact of public health programs, and life table 
analysis remains essential to tracking and accelerating this progress toward SDG 3. 
⑮ RAPID REVISION SHEET — Q44 
QUESTION 
Life Tables and their applications 
DEFINITION 
Statistical table showing probability of death/survival at each age for a 
population (Park/JK) 
TYPES 
Complete vs. Abridged; Cohort (longitudinal) vs. Current (period) — most 
used: Current Abridged 
KEY COLUMNS 
lx (alive), dx (deaths), qx (prob dying), px (prob surviving), Lx (person-yrs), Tx 
(future yrs), ex (life expectancy) 
RADIX

LIFE EXPECTANCY

Q45: INTEGRATED VECTOR MANAGEMENT (IVM) 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Define Integrated Vector Management (IVM). Describe its components, rationale, and application 
in controlling vector-borne diseases in India. 
② 1-MINUTE EXAM OUTLINE 
99. Definition of IVM — WHO 
100. 
Rationale for IVM (why not single method) 
101. 
Five key elements / pillars of IVM 
102. 
Methods of vector control 
103. 
Application to specific diseases — malaria, dengue, filaria, JE, Kala-azar

India — National Vector Borne Disease Control Programme (NVBDCP) 
105. 
Recent WHO IVM guidelines 
106. 
Conclusion 
③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
WHO IVM definition (2004) — rational decision-making + evidence-based combination of 
methods 
• 
5 key elements of IVM: Advocacy, social mobilization and legislation; Collaboration; 
Integration; Evidence-based decision making; Capacity building 
• 
NVBDCP — year 2004 (merger); vector-specific control methods for each disease 
④ DEFINITION 
INTEGRATED VECTOR MANAGEMENT — WHO, 2004 
Integrated Vector Management (IVM) is a rational decision-making process for the optimal use of 
resources for vector control. It seeks to improve the efficacy, cost-effectiveness, ecological soundness 
and sustainability of disease-vector control by considering the use of all available methods, alone or in 
combination, and encouraging synergies with disease-specific health and environmental management 
efforts. (WHO, 2004; Park — Epidemiology of Communicable Diseases) 
⑤ RATIONALE FOR IVM (WHY NOT SINGLE METHOD?) 
• 
Single method dependency (e.g., DDT alone) leads to insecticide resistance 
• 
Environmental impact of excessive insecticide use — biodiversity loss, pollution 
• 
Cost-inefficiency of blanket spraying vs. targeted interventions 
• 
Multiple vectors may transmit same disease (e.g., dengue: Ae. aegypti + Ae. albopictus) 
• 
Community-level participation essential for sustainability 
• 
Climate change altering vector distribution — static control methods inadequate

⑨ INDIA — NATIONAL VECTOR BORNE DISEASE CONTROL PROGRAMME 
(NVBDCP) 
NVBDCP — Ministry of Health & Family Welfare, 2004 
• 
Launched 2004 by merging: NMEP (malaria) + NFCP (filariasis) + NKCP (kala-azar) + 
dengue/chikungunya control + JE control 
• 
Nodal Ministry: MoHFW; implementing states 
• 
Key malaria targets: Malaria Elimination 2030 (National Strategic Plan 2017–22) 
• 
LLIN distribution: 2.83 crore LLINs distributed in high-endemic districts (2022–23) 
• 
IRS coverage: Annual round of IRS in malaria endemic areas before transmission season 
• 
Kala-azar elimination: Target <1 case per 10,000 population in all endemic blocks by 2023 
(revised 2025) 
• 
LF Elimination: Mass Drug Administration (MDA) with DEC + Albendazole (Triple drug: DEC + 
ALB + Ivermectin — IDA in some states) 
• 
Malaria API: Reduced from 2.15 (2015) to 0.17 (2022) — Annual Parasite Incidence 
⑩ INSECTICIDE RESISTANCE — KEY CHALLENGE 
• 
Resistance to DDT, HCH, pyrethroid insecticides reported in An. culicifacies and Ae. aegypti 
• 
Resistance monitoring mandatory before insecticide rotation 
• 
Insecticide resistance management: rotation, mixture, mosaic approach 
• 
NIMR (National Institute of Malaria Research) — apex body for insecticide resistance monitoring 
⑪ RECENT GUIDELINES 2022–2024 
• 
WHO Global Vector Control Response (GVCR) 2017–2030 (reviewed 2022): Recommends IVM 
as the strategic framework for all vector-borne disease control; target: 75% reduction in VBD 
mortality by 2030 
• 
NVBDCP Annual Report 2022–23: India malaria cases 2.27 lakh (2022) down from 10 lakh 
(2018); Malaria deaths 16 (2022) vs. 77 (2018) 
• 
WHO Dengue Guidelines 2023: Wolbachia release and sterile insect technique under 
evaluation as next-generation IVM tools 
⑫ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Integrated Vector Management represents the scientific and operational evolution from single-tool 
vector control to a rational, evidence-based, multi-pronged strategy. India's NVBDCP, guided by IVM 
principles, has achieved dramatic reductions in malaria, progressed toward kala-azar elimination, and 
maintained dengue/chikungunya surveillance systems. In an era of insecticide resistance, climate-
driven vector expansion, and emerging vector-borne threats, IVM's emphasis on ecological soundness, 
community participation, and inter-sectoral collaboration is more critical than ever. 
⑬ RAPID REVISION SHEET — Q45

④ DEFINITION 
SOCIO-ECONOMIC STATUS (SES) — Park / JK 
Socio-Economic Status is a composite measure of an individual's or family's economic and social 
position relative to others, based on income, education, occupation, and other social variables. It is a 
key determinant of health — higher SES correlates with better health outcomes, access to healthcare, 
nutrition, and sanitation. (Park — Social Medicine; JK) 
⑤ IMPORTANCE OF SES MEASUREMENT 
• 
SES is a major social determinant of health — influences morbidity, mortality, nutrition, 
healthcare access 
• 
Stratify study population in community diagnosis and research 
• 
Compare health outcomes across SES groups 
• 
Target health programs to low-SES groups 
• 
Monitor social inequity in health outcomes 
• 
Required in epidemiological studies, community health surveys, MBBS community medicine 
practicals

⑪ APPLICATIONS IN COMMUNITY MEDICINE 
• 
Community diagnosis — stratify population by SES; identify disparities 
•

Cross-state comparison of health outcomes by SES class 
⑫ LIMITATIONS OF SES SCALES 
• 
Income data unreliable — many rural families have irregular/seasonal income 
• 
BG Prasad: income alone is reductive — ignores education, occupation, social capital 
• 
Kuppuswamy: not suitable for rural populations — formal employment categories inapplicable 
•

•

• 
Do not capture non-material aspects of SES (social networks, political power)

•

(CPI-based) 
• 
NFHS-5 (2019-21): Uses wealth quintiles (poorest, poor, middle, rich, richest) as SES proxy for 
national health surveys — more robust for large surveys than single-scale classification 
⑭ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Socio-Economic Status scales are indispensable tools in community medicine research and practice. 
For urban India, Kuppuswamy's three-variable scale remains the gold standard. For rural populations, 
Uday Pareek's scale with its rural-specific variables (land, cattle, farm power) provides greater validity. 
BG Prasad's simplicity makes it ubiquitous for rapid surveys. All scales require annual income updating 
for continued validity. As India's health equity agenda deepens, robust SES measurement is essential 
to identifying and addressing the social determinants that drive health disparities.

Q47: HERD IMMUNITY — DEFINITION AND ROLE IN INFECTION 
CONTROL 
 
① QUESTION RESTATED 
Define Herd Immunity. Explain the concept, how it is achieved, its role in infection control and 
vaccination programs, and its relevance to COVID-19. 
② 1-MINUTE EXAM OUTLINE 
117. 
Definition of Herd Immunity — WHO/Park 
118. 
Concept and mechanism 
119. 
Herd Immunity Threshold (HIT) 
120. 
Basic Reproduction Number (R0) 
121. 
Formula: HIT = 1 - 1/R0 
122. 
How herd immunity is achieved — vaccination vs. natural infection 
123. 
Role in eradication and elimination 
124. 
COVID-19 and herd immunity 
125. 
Limitations and exceptions 
126. 
Conclusion 
③ EXAMINER VALUE POINTS BOX 
EXAMINER EXPECTS: 
• 
Herd Immunity Threshold formula: HIT = 1 - 1/R0 — with example calculations 
• 
R0 values for common diseases: measles 12-18, COVID-19 delta ~5, COVID-19 omicron 
~8-15, polio 5-7, smallpox 5-7 
• 
Distinction between herd immunity by vaccination (preferred) vs. natural infection; 
COVID-19 context; limitations in non-uniform populations 
④ DEFINITION 
HERD IMMUNITY (WHO / Park) 
Herd Immunity (also called Community Immunity or Population Immunity) is the indirect protection from 
a communicable disease that occurs when a sufficient proportion of a population has become immune 
to the infection — through vaccination or prior infection — thereby reducing the likelihood of infection 
for individuals who lack immunity. (WHO; Park — Epidemiology; JK) 
 
In operational terms: when a critical proportion of the population is immune, an infected individual is 
unlikely to encounter susceptible persons, so transmission chains break down and the disease cannot 
sustain itself in that population. 
⑤ MECHANISM OF HERD IMMUNITY 
How It Works — Step by Step 
127. 
In a fully susceptible population, an infected case transmits to R0 (Basic Reproduction 
Number) secondary cases 
128. 
As the proportion of immune individuals (p) increases, the effective reproduction number 
(Re) decreases 
129. 
Formula: Re = R0 × (1 - p) 
305


--- PAGE 318 ---

swaDesh
130. 
When Re < 1, each case produces less than one secondary case → outbreak decays 
and stops 
131. 
The minimum proportion immune needed to reduce Re below 1 is the Herd Immunity 
Threshold (HIT) 
132. 
HIT = 1 - (1/R0) 
⑥ BASIC REPRODUCTION NUMBER (R0) AND HIT 
Disease

HIT Required (%)

Example Calculation — Measles: 
R0 for measles = 15 (average). HIT = 1 - (1/15) = 1 - 0.067 = 0.933 = 93.3%. This means at least 
93.3% of the population must be immune to prevent sustained measles transmission. This is why 
measles vaccination coverage must exceed 95% to maintain herd protection. 
⑦ HOW HERD IMMUNITY IS ACHIEVED 
Method 
Description 
Advantages 
Disadvantages 
Vaccination 
Immunizing a critical 
proportion of the population 
Safe, controlled, 
achievable without 
disease 
morbidity/mortality 
Requires high coverage; 
vaccine hesitancy; 
waning immunity 
Natural Infection 
Allowing infection to spread 
until sufficient immunity 
develops 
Generates often 
stronger T-cell immunity 
UNETHICAL — massive 
morbidity, mortality, long 
COVID, variants; NOT 
recommended by WHO 
 
WHO Position (2020, reaffirmed 2022): 
WHO explicitly states that achieving herd immunity by allowing infection to spread is scientifically 
problematic and ethically unacceptable. Herd immunity should only be achieved through vaccination, 
not through natural infection. This statement was directly relevant to COVID-19 'Great Barrington 
Declaration' debate. 
⑧ ROLE IN INFECTION CONTROL AND VACCINATION PROGRAMS 
306


--- PAGE 319 ---

swaDesh
Role 
Example 
Disease eradication 
Smallpox eradicated globally (1980) through vaccination-induced herd 
immunity; estimated HIT achieved with ~80% coverage 
Disease elimination 
Polio elimination in India (2014) — vaccine-derived herd immunity in high-
burden districts 
Outbreak prevention 
High measles vaccination coverage (>95%) prevents school outbreaks 
Protecting vulnerable 
groups 
Those who cannot be vaccinated (newborns, immunocompromised) 
protected by surrounding immune population 
Universal Immunization 
Programme 
India's UIP targets herd immunity thresholds for diphtheria, tetanus, 
pertussis, measles, polio, hepatitis B, HIB, etc. 
Mission Indradhanush 
Intensified immunization to achieve >90% coverage → approach HIT for 
vaccine-preventable diseases 
⑨ COVID-19 AND HERD IMMUNITY 
COVID-19 Herd Immunity — Special Context 
• 
Original SARS-CoV-2 (Wuhan strain): R0 ~2–3; HIT ~50–67% 
• 
Delta variant: R0 ~5–6; HIT ~80%; made original HIT targets insufficient 
• 
Omicron variant: R0 ~8–15; HIT ~88–93%; practically unachievable by vaccination alone given 
waning immunity 
• 
India COVID-19 vaccination: >220 crore doses administered (Jan 2024); >67% fully vaccinated 
• 
Hybrid immunity: combination of vaccination + natural infection provides stronger/more durable 
immunity than either alone (Lancet 2022) 
• 
BUT: Omicron's high transmissibility and immune evasion mean classical herd immunity unlikely 
for COVID-19 — focus shifted to reducing severe disease, hospitalization, death rather than 
preventing transmission 
• 
Lessons from COVID-19: Herd immunity is a dynamic, not static threshold — evolves with 
variant emergence, waning immunity, and vaccination coverage 
⑩ LIMITATIONS AND EXCEPTIONS 
• 
Non-uniform distribution: herd immunity requires UNIFORM distribution of immune persons — 
clustering of unvaccinated (anti-vaccine communities) breaks herd protection locally (measles 
outbreaks in Amish communities, USA) 
• 
Waning immunity: vaccine-induced immunity waning (COVID-19, pertussis) → effective 
coverage drops below HIT even with initial high vaccination 
• 
Variant emergence: new variants with higher R0 raise the HIT, potentially negating previously 
achieved herd immunity 
• 
Some diseases cannot achieve herd immunity: Tetanus (no person-to-person transmission), 
Rabies, Leprosy, STIs in high-risk groups — different control strategies needed 
• 
Indirect transmission routes: vector-borne diseases (malaria, dengue) — herd immunity concept 
does not apply in classical sense

⑬ RECENT GUIDELINES 2022–2024 
• 
WHO 2022: Reaffirmed herd immunity by vaccination as the ONLY ethical approach; updated 
COVID-19 vaccine recommendations with booster doses for waning immunity 
• 
India NIP 2023: National Immunization Programme expanded — HPV vaccine for girls (9-14 
years) added in 2023 → contributes to herd immunity against HPV-related cancers 
• 
Lancet Infectious Diseases 2022: Hybrid immunity (vaccination + infection) provides strongest 
and longest-lasting protection against COVID-19 — informs booster dose policy 
⑭ CONCLUSION 
Key Public Health Message 
Herd immunity is one of the most powerful concepts in infectious disease epidemiology and public 
health. When achieved through vaccination at or above the Herd Immunity Threshold (HIT = 1 - 1/R0), 
it protects the entire population including those who cannot be vaccinated. India's success in polio 
elimination, smallpox eradication, and the global COVID-19 vaccination campaign all demonstrate herd 
immunity in action. However, COVID-19 has taught us that herd immunity is dynamic — variant 
emergence, waning immunity, and uneven vaccination coverage can erode it. The lesson for public 
health is clear: sustained, equitable, and high-coverage vaccination is the only scientifically sound and 
ethically acceptable path to population immunity. 
⑮ RAPID REVISION SHEET — Q47 
QUESTION 
Herd Immunity — Definition and Role in Infection Control 
DEFINITION 
Indirect protection when sufficient proportion of population immune — 
transmission chains break down (WHO/Park) 
FORMULA 
HIT = 1 - (1/R0) | Re = R0 × (1 - p) | When Re <1 → outbreak stops

R0 = 12-18 → HIT = 92-95% (highest of common vaccines)

Original R0 ~2-3 (HIT 50-67%) | Delta R0 ~5-6 (HIT 80%) | Omicron R0 ~8-15 
(HIT 88-93%)

immunity

QUESTION 1 — TRIPLE BURDEN OF DISEASE & ONION PEEL
PHENOMENON
10 Marks | Draw both diagrams
Introduction
India, like many LMICs, faces a Triple Burden of Disease — simultaneously combating
communicable diseases, non-communicable diseases, and injuries/nutritional deficiencies.
This epidemiological paradox is elegantly explained by the Onion Peel Phenomenon.
I. Triple Burden of Disease — Definition & Components
Tier
Category
Examples
Tier 1
Communicable Diseases
TB, Malaria, HIV/AIDS, Cholera, Dengue
Tier 2
Non-Communicable Diseases
CVD, Diabetes Mellitus, Cancer, Hypertension
Tier 3
Injuries & Nutritional Deficiencies
Road traffic accidents, Malnutrition, Anaemia
II. Onion Peel Phenomenon
As a country develops economically, disease layers are peeled away like an onion — outer
layers (communicable diseases) are shed first, revealing inner layers (NCDs). Developing
310


--- PAGE 323 ---

swaDesh
countries like India get stuck between layers, suffering from all simultaneously.
DIAGRAM — Onion Peel Model (draw concentric circles in exam):
    [Outermost Ring] — INJURIES & EMERGING INFECTIONS
        [Middle Ring] — COMMUNICABLE DISEASES (TB, Malaria, HIV)
            [Inner Ring] — NON-COMMUNICABLE DISEASES (CVD, DM, Cancer)
                [Core] — MALNUTRITION & MICRONUTRIENT DEFICIENCY
Arrow note: As development increases → outer layers shed, but core persists
III. Causes in India
Push Factors (Sustaining Old Burden)
Pull Factors (Creating New Burden)
Poor sanitation & unsafe water
Urbanisation & sedentary lifestyle
Malnutrition & immune compromise
Processed food, tobacco & alcohol use
Inadequate vaccine coverage
Ageing population (demographic shift)
Rural poverty & illiteracy
Stress, pollution, road traffic
IV. Policy Implications
Universal Health Coverage (UHC) — Ayushman Bharat
Integrated Disease Surveillance Programme (IDSP)
NPCDCS for NCDs + NVBDCP for vector-borne diseases
National Nutrition Mission (POSHAN Abhiyaan)
Conclusion
The triple burden demands integrated, multi-sectoral health responses rather than vertical
disease-specific programs. India's SDG 3 target of UHC by 2030 must address all three tiers
simultaneously.
★ DISTINCTION TIP: Mention Omran's Epidemiological Transition (Stage 3–4) and the Lee &

paradox.

QUESTION 4 — GOOD CLINICAL PRACTICES (GCP) & INFORMED
CONSENT
10 Marks | Components table + Consent flowchart
Introduction
Good Clinical Practices (GCP) are internationally recognised ethical and scientific
quality standards for designing, conducting, recording, and reporting clinical trials —
ensuring participant safety and data integrity (ICH-E6 Guidelines).
I. GCP — ICH-E6 Core Principles (10)
1. Scientific soundness of the clinical trial protocol
2. Prior ethics committee (IEC/IRB) approval
3. Qualified and experienced investigator
4. Freely given, informed consent of participants
5. Medical care provided to participants
6. Confidentiality of records maintained
7. Investigational product accountability
8. Quality systems and Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs)
9. Risk-benefit assessment before and during trial
10. CDSCO (India) regulatory compliance
II. Informed Consent — Definition
Definition: Informed consent is a voluntary, competent, comprehending agreement by a
participant to undergo a medical procedure or research, after being provided full
information in a language they understand, free from coercion.
III. Process of Informed Consent — Flowchart
315


--- PAGE 328 ---

swaDesh
DIAGRAM (draw as vertical flowchart in exam):
Step 1 — DISCLOSE: Provide full, complete information about the study
    ↓
Step 2 — COMPREHEND: Ensure participant understands (read-back, Q&A session)
    ↓
Step 3 — VOLUNTARY: Free from coercion, undue influence or inducement
    ↓
Step 4 — COMPETENT: Decision-making capacity confirmed
    ↓
Step 5 — DECISION: Signature / Thumb impression + Witness signature
    ↓
Step 6 — RIGHT TO WITHDRAW: At any time, without penalty or loss of benefits
IV. Special Situations in Informed Consent
Situation
Approach
Illiterate participant
Read aloud + impartial witness signature
Minor (<18 years)
Parental consent + child assent (≥7 years)
Emergency
Waiver of consent possible — document reasoning
Mentally incapacitated
Legal guardian / Legally Authorised Representative (LAR)
Therapeutic misconception
Clearly distinguish research from routine treatment
Conclusion
GCP and informed consent are the twin pillars of ethical clinical research. India's ICMR
Guidelines (2017) and Schedule Y of Drugs & Cosmetics Act mandate these for all clinical
trials.
★ DISTINCTION TIP: Mention Nuremberg Code (1947), Declaration of Helsinki (1964,

ethics: Respect for Persons · Beneficence · Justice.

QUESTION 5 — MAMMOGRAPHY SCREENING — EVALUATING
EVIDENCE
10 Marks | Lead time bias diagram + Screening criteria table
Introduction
The claim that mammogram screening prolongs survival in breast cancer must be
critically evaluated using epidemiological tools — distinguishing true benefit from
statistical artifacts like lead-time bias and length bias.
I. Framework — Evaluating the Evidence (Bradford Hill's Criteria Applied)
Criterion
Applied to Mammography
Strength of association
RR of mortality reduction ~20–30% in RCTs
Temporality
Screening precedes survival improvement
Consistency
Multiple RCTs: Swedish Two-County Trial, HIP Trial
Biological plausibility
Earlier stage detection → better prognosis (biologically sound)
Dose-response
Regular screening → better outcomes than irregular screening
Experiment (RCT)
Gold standard — compare screened vs unscreened groups
II. Critical Biases to Rule Out
1. Lead-Time Bias
DIAGRAM (draw two timelines in exam):
Unscreened: [Clinical Diagnosis]——————[Death] = Survival 5 years
Screened:  [Screen Diagnosis]————————————[Death] = Apparent survival 9 years
           |<— Lead time (4 yrs) —>|
True survival is the same; screening only advances the diagnosis date (lead time)
2. Length Bias: Screening preferentially detects slow-growing tumours (better prognosis).
Screened cases appear to survive longer — not because of screening benefit, but due to tumour
biology favouring less aggressive cancers.
3. Overdiagnosis Bias: Detection of lesions that would never have caused symptoms or death
— leads to unnecessary treatment.
317


--- PAGE 330 ---

swaDesh
III. Correct Study Design to Confirm Benefit
RCT (Gold Standard) → Measure DISEASE-SPECIFIC MORTALITY (not 5-year survival) →
Adjust for Lead Time & Length Bias → Valid Conclusion
Key rule: Use MORTALITY as endpoint — NOT survival rate — to avoid lead-time bias
IV. Wilson & Jungner Screening Criteria — Applied to Mammography
Criterion
Mammography Status
Important health problem
Yes — breast cancer is leading cancer in women
Acceptable treatment available
Yes — surgery, chemo, radiotherapy
Detectable latent stage
Yes — DCIS and early invasive cancer detectable
Suitable and acceptable test
Yes — but radiation exposure is a concern
Cost-effective
Debated — NNS (Number Needed to Screen) is high
Conclusion
Mammography screening has modest evidence for reducing breast cancer mortality. Routine
screening is recommended for women 50–69 years (USPSTF), with shared decision-making for
40–49 years. Benefits must be weighed against false positives and overdiagnosis.
★ DISTINCTION TIP: Mention NNS (Number Needed to Screen) as a measure of screening
benefit. Reference USPSTF Guidelines and the Cochrane Review controversy on mammography.
This shows critical appraisal skills.

QUESTION 7 — BIAS IN RESEARCH STUDIES
10 Marks | Master table + Directional effect diagram
Introduction
Bias is any systematic error in the design, conduct, or analysis of a study that causes a
distortion of the true association between exposure and outcome. Unlike random error,
bias is directional and cannot be reduced by increasing sample size alone.
I. Classification of Bias
320


--- PAGE 333 ---

swaDesh
Type
Subtypes & Examples
Reduction Strategy

II. Directional Effect of Bias
DIAGRAM (draw as bidirectional arrow in exam):
BIAS TOWARDS NULL <————————————> BIAS AWAY FROM NULL
Attenuates true effect                       Exaggerates true effect
→ Type II Error (False Negative)          → Type I Error (False Positive)
III. Strategies to Reduce Bias
Stage
Strategy
Design stage
Randomisation, blinding (single/double/triple), proper control selection
Conduct stage
Standardised protocols, trained data collectors, blinded outcome assessment
Analysis stage
Multivariable regression, propensity score matching, sensitivity analysis
Reporting stage
CONSORT checklist (RCTs), STROBE checklist (observational studies)
Conclusion
Recognising and controlling bias is central to producing valid research. The GRADE
framework provides a structured approach to rate certainty of evidence from high to very low,
321


--- PAGE 334 ---

accounting for bias, inconsistency, and indirectness.

QUESTION 9 — NON-PARAMETRIC TESTS OF SIGNIFICANCE
10 Marks | Master selection flowchart + Comparison table
Introduction
Non-parametric tests (distribution-free tests) are statistical methods used when data does
not meet the assumptions of parametric tests — specifically normality of distribution,
323


--- PAGE 336 ---

swaDesh
homogeneity of variance, and interval-level measurement.
I. When to Use Non-Parametric Tests
Ordinal data (Likert scale, rankings)
Non-normal distribution (skewed data)
Small sample sizes (<30)
Nominal data analysis
Presence of outliers distorting the mean
II. Master Selection Flowchart
DIAGRAM (draw as branching flowchart in exam):
What type of data and question?
    ↓
2 Groups, Independent        → Mann-Whitney U Test
2 Groups, Paired/Related     → Wilcoxon Signed-Rank Test
>2 Groups, Independent      → Kruskal-Wallis H Test
>2 Groups, Repeated Measures → Friedman Test
Categorical Association        → Chi-square / Fisher's Exact Test
Ordinal Correlation            → Spearman's Rank Correlation (ρ)
III. Key Non-Parametric Tests — Quick Reference
Test
Parametric Equivalent
Use Case
Mann-Whitney U
Independent samples t-test
Compare medians of 2 independent groups
Wilcoxon Signed-Rank
Paired t-test
Before-after data, same subjects
Kruskal-Wallis H
One-way ANOVA
Compare 3 or more independent groups
Friedman Test
Repeated measures ANOVA
3+ related groups or time points
Chi-square (χ²)
—
Association between categorical variables
Fisher's Exact Test
Chi-square
Small expected cell count (<5)
Spearman's ρ
Pearson's r
Ordinal correlation between two variables
Sign Test
Paired t-test
Direction of difference only (less powerful)
324


--- PAGE 337 ---

swaDesh
Run Test
—
Testing randomness of a sequence
IV. Advantages & Limitations
Advantages
Limitations
No distributional assumptions required
Less statistical power than parametric tests
Handles ordinal and nominal data
Loss of information (uses ranks, not actual values)
Robust to outliers
Less precise when parametric assumptions are actually met
Useful for small sample sizes
Fewer options for complex multi-variable analysis
Conclusion
The choice between parametric and non-parametric tests depends on data type, distribution, and
sample size. When in doubt, non-parametric tests are safer and more conservative, ensuring
validity of conclusions in medical research.
★ DISTINCTION TIP: Mention the Central Limit Theorem — when n ≥ 30, distribution
approaches normality even for non-normal populations. Quote Siegel (1956) as pioneer of non-
parametric statistics. State when chi-square assumptions are violated, use Fisher's Exact Test.
QUESTION 10 — CAUSATION VS ASSOCIATION & STUDY DESIGNS
10 Marks | Bradford Hill criteria + Evidence pyramid diagram
Introduction
Epidemiology seeks to move beyond mere statistical association to establish causation —
a rigorous process guided by Bradford Hill's Criteria (1965) and validated by
appropriate study designs arranged in a hierarchy of evidence.
I. Key Differences — Association vs Causation
Feature
Association
Causation
Definition
Statistical relationship between two
variables
One variable directly produces the other
325


--- PAGE 338 ---

swaDesh
Direction
May be bidirectional
Must be unidirectional (cause precedes
effect)
Confounding
May be explained by a third variable
Independent of confounders
Measure
Correlation coefficient, p-value
RR, OR, AR — assessed by Bradford Hill
criteria
Example
Stork births & human birth rates
(spurious)
Smoking → Lung cancer (established
causal)
II. Bradford Hill's Nine Criteria for Causation (1965)
#
Criterion
Explanation
1
Strength
High RR/OR — stronger association more likely causal
2
Consistency
Replicated by multiple studies in different populations
3
Specificity
Cause leads to one specific effect
4
★ Temporality
Cause must precede effect — ONLY absolutely essential criterion
5
Biological gradient
Dose-response relationship (more exposure → more disease)
6
Plausibility
Consistent with known biological mechanisms
7
Coherence
Does not conflict with known natural history
8
Experiment
Removal of cause reduces disease (RCT evidence)
9
Analogy
Similar cause-effect relationship known elsewhere
III. Evidence Pyramid — Study Designs
DIAGRAM — Draw as triangle/pyramid in exam (apex = highest evidence):
[APEX]    Systematic Review & Meta-Analysis — Highest evidence; pooled RR/OR; measures
CAUSATION
           Randomised Controlled Trial (RCT) — Gold standard; eliminates confounding; gives RR,
NNT
           Cohort Study (Prospective) — Best observational for causation; gives RR, Attributable Risk
           Case-Control Study — Good for rare diseases; gives OR; association → causation
           Cross-Sectional Study — Measures prevalence & association; cannot establish causation
[BASE]    Ecological / Descriptive Studies — Hypothesis generation only; lowest causation value
326

IV. Choosing the Right Study Design
Goal
Best Design
Measure
Rapid association measurement
Cross-sectional study
Prevalence Ratio, OR
Rare disease causation
Case-Control study
Odds Ratio (OR)
Multiple effects of one exposure
Cohort study (prospective)
Relative Risk (RR), AR
Establish causation definitively
RCT
RR, NNT, ARR
Best overall evidence synthesis
Systematic Review + Meta-analysis
Pooled RR/OR, I²
Conclusion
Establishing causation is the ultimate goal of epidemiological research. Bradford Hill's criteria
provide a rigorous framework, but no single criterion is sufficient alone — except temporality.
The RCT remains the gold standard for causal inference in clinical medicine.
★ DISTINCTION TIP: Mention counterfactual framework of causation (Rubin's potential
outcomes). Quote Evans' Postulates for infectious disease causation (modernised Koch's
Postulates). Distinguish necessary cause, sufficient cause, and contributory cause.
DISTINCTION ANSWER WRITING — UNIVERSAL RULES
1. BOX your definitions — the examiner scans for them first
2. At least ONE diagram per question — even a simple flowchart adds 2 marks
3. UNDERLINE key terms throughout (not whole sentences)
4. Write a 3-line conclusion linking to national health policy / WHO / SDGs
5. Use numbered subheadings (I., II., III.) for clear structure
6. Mention at least ONE national program or international guideline per answer
7. Never leave white space — fill with a relevant table or diagram
8. Keep handwriting legible — examiners cannot mark what they cannot read
